HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Markets
 
The Dow Jones industrial average surged past the 4,000 mark, closing at 4,003.33, up 30.28 points for the day. The rally may mean a reduced chance for an economic recession and an enhanced possibility of what economists call a soft landing, or a slowing but still-growing economy with little inflation. [D1.] 

Dow 4,000 was not cause for a prolonged celebration. The immediate future of the rally may be determined by whether the conquering of 4,000 brings in additional cash from investors. The long-term market will be shaped by whether investors believe the economy will slow too much. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D1.]
The dollar drifted lower, hurt by expectations that interest rates in the United States had ended their climb. [D13.]
Gasoline prices fell on fears that the E.P.A. would grant Wisconsin's request to suspend sales of reformulated gasoline. [D13.]
The Treasury market was jolted by comments from the vice chairman of the Federal Reserve, who suggested that the Fed might not be finished raising interest rates. [D14.]
 
Companies
 
Time Warner is close to a sale of its stake in Turner Broadcasting to Turner and Tele-Communications for about $1.6 billion, several people with knowledge of the negotiations said. [D1.]
Howard Stringer is leaving CBS to join a new media venture created by three large telephone companies. Peter Lund will succeed him as president of the CBS Broadcast Group. The transition comes at a time of uncertainty about CBS. [D1.]
Higher commissions to travel agents are a carrot being used by some hotels, rental car companies and cruise lines, who hope that the agents, who face reduced commissions from many airlines, will steer more business their way. [D3.]
Nationsbank agreed to pay Keycorp $500 million for the right to service $25 billion in mortgages and some related assets. The acquisition would make Nationsbank's mortgage unit one of the biggest mortgage servicers in the country. [D3.]
Kmart says it has not been looking for a No. 2 executive. [D3.]
Zenith Electronics' top executive plans to retire this year. [D3.]
Lawyers for a class action against the tobacco industry are seeking approval for the largest notification effort ever. As many as 40 million current smokers, as well as an estimated 50 million former smokers, may be eligible to join the lawsuit. [D14.]
The chairman of Lilco said he would not accept a raise of $55,000, following criticism by Long Island officials. [B6.]
New York State suspended the license of Sonic Communications for unauthorized switching of long-distance services. [B3.]
 
The Economy
 
A new report tries to assess the performance of health plans, a measure that has been greatly lacking in the health care system. The report, which looked at 26 plans administered by 21 large health service providers, found most customers were satisfied but that there were wide variations among the plans. [D1.]
Alan Greenspan assured Republicans in Congress that he would try to protect the economy from any damage that might be caused by their proposed spending reductions. [D4.]
A former New Jersey official pleaded guilty to charges that he shared in more than $200,000 in kickbacks. Two other men were charged in the largest case so far in the Government's campaign against influence peddling in the municipal bond market. [A1.]
A bill that would set Federal standards in product injury lawsuits cleared a House committee. The bill would also require the loser in many suits to pay the winner's legal costs. [A15.]
 
Today's Column
 
Chevrolet has a low-key campaign for the Cavalier that at first sounds almost modest. The calculatedly sincere ads are aimed at Toyota- and Honda-oriented baby boomers. Advertising. [D2.]

HEADLINE: BALANCED BUDGET NEARS 67 VOTES NEEDED IN SENATE

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 23

 BODY:
Two more Democrats today joined the ranks of senators who support a balanced-budget amendment to the Constitution, and even opponents conceded that proponents were within an ace of the 67 votes needed to adopt the measure and send it to the states for ratification.
But in Congress, close is still a synonym for trailing. And even as it was clear that the amendment drive had gained new momentum, other senators widely viewed as supporters showed signs of having second, third and maybe fourth thoughts. 
 As a result, the hottest and most prolonged constitutional debate in recent history -- an argument that has smoldered though seven separate floor votes just since Ronald Reagan became President -- seemed bound for a climax next Tuesday with nobody sure of the outcome of the vote and the votes of at least five Senators up for grabs.
"Sixty-four votes I consider firm," said Senator Orrin G. Hatch, the Utah Republican who is leading the supporters.
"It'll win -- 67 or 68 votes," said Senator Bob Kerrey, the Nebraska Democrat who is a confirmed budget-cutter but believes the amendment is a bad idea.
"One vote, one way or the other," said Senator John B. Breaux of Louisiana, a usually savvy vote-counter who counts himself among the undecided Democrats.
The two Democrats who declared today, Senators Joseph R. Biden Jr. of Delaware and Tom Harkin of Iowa, opposed the amendment last year, the last time it came before the Senate.
Senator Harkin's conversion, in particular, was striking since only three years ago, he was running for President as one of the last of the New Deal Democrats.
Both Senators said their doubts had been overtaken by fears that uncontrolled Federal spending was wrecking the economy and pilfering prosperity from future generations.
Senator Biden said he had concluded that an amendment would force the Republican Congress to confront the need for broad spending cuts and tax increases, not merely cuts in unpopular social programs. But the true imperative, he said, is to prevent the Federal debt from consuming the resources for programs of every political stripe.
"Unless this thing gets focused," he said in an interview, "by the time we face the music, everything I care about is going to be gone."
Senator Harkin issued a statement saying that the annual interest payment on the $4.5 trillion Federal debt, which now eats up 28 cents of every income-tax dollar, "is crowding out our ability to invest in our people and build a better future for our kids."
"It's time that we pass on to our kids more opportunity, not more debt," he added.
The House passed the balanced-budget amendment by a vote of 300 to 132 on Jan. 26. Should it pass in the Senate, 38 states would have to ratify the amendment for it to become part of the Constitution.
Both Senators Harkin and Biden are leading liberals swimming against a conservative political tide, and both face re-election campaigns next year. But there was no clear evidence that politics was the sole deciding factor in their announcements, and Mr. Biden, for one, said calls to his office were running against his stance.
He sponsored his own balanced-budget amendment more than a decade ago, and later called for an across-the-board freeze on Federal spending. And while Mr. Harkin opposed a version of the amendment last year, he supported it when it came before the Senate in 1986.
Their announcements bring the tally of publicly declared supporters of the balanced-budget measure to 52 Republicans and 12 Democrats, a total that Senator Hatch cites as official but that nobody else believes is complete. The question today, in a Senate that teemed with speculation but precious little legislative action, was which of the undecideds had secretly cast his lot with the amendment supporters.
This is the sort of speculation that obsesses the chamber and its hangers-on in every long debate, and Democratic supporters broadly hinted that at least two of their members were all but committed to their side. Today the rumor mill ground almost to dust the names of five Democrats: Dorgan, Conrad, Ford, Breaux and Nunn.
Senator Byron Dorgan of North Dakota, who is concerned that the amendment would permit Congress to spend Social Security funds to balance the budget, was nevertheless said to favor the measure. He remained publicly undecided.
North Dakota's senior Senator, Kent Conrad, who is said to lean heavily against the amendment, paused only long enough to confirm his indecision before ducking into a meeting room off the Senate floor.
Senator Wendell H. Ford of Kentucky, who has twice supported the amendment, appeared squarely on the fence. A fellow Democratic Senator who spoke on condition of anonymity said Mr. Ford, the Senate's second-ranking Democrat, seemed to have been leaning for the amendment until he became angered after Republicans began a pressure campaign of television advertisements in his home state last week. Mr. Ford did not return a call seeking his comment.
Senator Breaux, also a past amendment supporter, is believed to be in the "yes" camp this time, too, and will announce his decision on Friday. Today he said opposition in his state was growing, mostly from groups representing elderly people and advocates of social programs that would face budget cuts.
"Most of the people for it think it's something we're going to do up here, and that it won't have any effect on them down there," Senator Breaux said. "That's not the way it's going to be." Still, he said, the question of whether to make those cuts might well be best left to the states as part of a debate over ratifying the amendment.
And then there is Senator Sam Nunn of Georgia, a conservative who is also a past supporter of the amendment, and who not only tends to weigh in at the last moment on major issues, but also to go against the grain.
Mr. Nunn had been marked down as a sure supporter by many groups that favor the amendment. Today he said he has deep concerns that the amendment would effectively give control of the budget to the Federal courts, as they would have the power to interpret it.
He also said he would try to change the amendment on the Senate floor next week to limit judicial reviews of its meaning, and suggested -- but did not say -- that he might vote against the amendment if he fails.
"I don't want the Federal courts raising taxes and cutting spending the balance the budget," he said.
Senator Paul Simon, an Illinois Democrat who is his party's chief supporter of the amendment, said later that it was "very doubtful" that the Senate would make any further changes of substance in the measure.
Senator Simon nonetheless pronounced himself "cautiously optimistic," but hastily added, "I'm not claiming victory."

HEADLINE: Dow, in a Surge, Pushes Past 4,000 Level

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
The Dow Jones industrial average closed above 4,000 for the first time yesterday, marking a milestone in a difficult year for stocks but a powerful one for the American economy.
For now, market analysts say, the surge in the Dow points to a reduced likelihood of an economic recession and an enhanced possibility of what economists call a "soft landing," or a slowing but still-growing economy with little inflation. 
 Though stocks have risen during the last two months, their longer-term performance has still lagged. During the last year, profits on the Dow have not matched the less risky returns of fixed-income investments. And some analysts say the Dow could turn down again in the next few weeks, because traders are misjudging the course of the economy.
"The market is ahead of itself in assuming that the Fed has won the battle against inflation and already brought about a soft landing," said Christine A. Callies, equity strategist for Brown Brothers Harriman & Company.
Still, the stock market rallied yesterday, closing at 4,003.33, up 30.28 points, because of encouraging remarks on Wednesday by Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve, implying that the Fed's tight-money policy may be coming to an end.
Such an easing would lower corporate borrowing costs, fatten corporate profits and imply that inflation was no longer a threat, thus preserving the value of assets like stocks and bonds.
"Many, many of our accounts were participating in this market today," said Kevin M. Haggerty, the manager in charge of equity trading for Fidelity Capital Markets, the trader for the nation's largest mutual fund.
The trading yesterday was unusually heavy, at 394.7 million shares, or more than 20 percent higher than the daily average of 325 million shares this year. Almost twice as many stocks rose as fell on the New York Stock Exchange, with 1,466 advancing and 787 declining. Ninety-nine stocks hit new highs, while only 34 hit new lows.
The broader stock indexes also fared well, with the Standard & Poor's 500 index reaching a record 486.91, up 1.84 points, topping its record of 485.22 on Feb. 16.
But the smaller-company Nasdaq composite index rose 3.42 points, to 791.35, which is still far from its peak of 803.93 reached last March 18. And the Russell 2000, one of the broadest stock indexes, rose only 1.01 point, to 254.95, still far from its record of 271.08, also reached last March 18.
The Dow faded more than 17 points yesterday afternoon, inching below 4,000 a scant two minutes before the close, after another senior Federal Reserve official, Alan S. Blinder, suggested that Mr. Greenspan was talking in "the abstract" and may have been misinterpreted by traders.
"One of the things that the surge in the Dow means is that it's probable we're not going to see a recession," in part because of the calming of the markets, said Richard B. Hoey, portfolio manager for Dreyfus Growth and Income Fund, with $1.6 billion of stocks. "Beyond that, the Dow does not mean a great deal," he added, because "this is more of a relief rally, than an enthusiasm rally. It's relief that the Fed isn't going to kill the rally."
There is little enthusiasm in the stock market, Mr. Hoey said, because investors can still make twice as much from fixed-income investments, like 7.33 percent from 10-year Treasury bonds, than they can from the Dow, whose stock prices rose only 2.9 percent in the last year and only 5.7 percent with reinvested dividends. "They're riskless returns, unlike the Dow," he said.
As if to underscore the uncertain returns of stocks, Motorola, once a market leader, fell 1 1/2, to 56 1/2, as the Dow and S.& P. 500 were establishing new records. Last week, Motorola reported that cellular telephone sales, a big product line, may be slowing.
Other technology stocks also weakened. Compaq fell 2, to 34 1/8, and Intel fell 19/16 , to 781/16 .
Still, the market's overall direction was up. The Dow was led higher by J. P. Morgan, rising 1 3/8, to 63, and Philip Morris, gaining 1 1/4, to 59 1/2.
Some market analysts say they wonder whether there is enough money in the stock market to sustain a broad-based rally. Last year the net cash inflow into stock mutual funds slowed to $119.3 billion, from $129.6 billion in 1993, according to the Investment Company Institute, a trade group. Meanwhile, the assets of money-market mutual funds shot up to $628.2 billion, from $582.8 billion in 1993.
But yesterday, traders bid up the price of shares after carefully combing the Congressional testimony on Wednesday of Mr. Greenspan.
In his prepared text and his answers to question from senators, the normally Delphic Mr. Greenspan was unusually clear and specific about what he called the "signal accomplishment" of keeping inflation below 3 percent for three consecutive years for the first time since the early 1960's. And traders concluded that the Fed was less likely to continue its policy of tight money beyond perhaps one more increase in short-term rates.
Mr. Hoey, the portfolio manager at Dreyfus, said he suspected that Mr. Greenspan's remarks were timed to calm markets made nervous by the continuing troubles in the Mexican economy and currency. By calming American markets, Mr. Greenspan helps to calm Mexico, which helps to calm America even more, Mr. Hoey said.
Despite the difficult 12 months that began with the Fed's increase in rates on Feb. 4, 1994, which led to a drop of nearly 10 percent in the Dow by April, traders bid up prices past the millennial mark in record time.
It took about 46 months to pass from 3,000 in April 1991 to 4,000 yesterday, compared with the 51 months it took to cross 3,000 from 2,000 in January 1987, and the agonizing 14 years it took to cross 2,000 from 1,000 in the long bear market of the 1970's.
Ms. Callies of Brown Brothers said the rally appeared to be part of the normal life cycle of bull markets, in which speculators rush in to bid up prices because some convenient event, like testimony of a Fed chairman, provides an excuse. "It's a routine change in sentiment," she said. "There would have been a speculative rally at some point, with or without Alan Greenspan."
Among the gainers yesterday, Boston Scientific rose 1 1/8, to 20 1/2, after its shareholders and those of Scimed agreed to a $1 billion merger. Scimed rose 2 3/8, to 67 7/8.
Morrison Knudsen rose 1 3/4, to 8 7/8, as its creditors granted it an extension on defaulted loans. And Multimedia rose 8, to 37 1/8, after it said it might put itself up for sale.

HEADLINE: CARTER RECEIVED COOLLY IN HAITI

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, Feb. 23

 BODY:
Five months after he negotiated an agreement that averted an invasion and restored President Jean-Bertrand Aristide to power, Jimmy Carter returned today to the scene of one of his diplomatic feats. But instead of receiving a hero's welcome, he was plunged into the turmoil of Haitian domestic politics.
Invited by Mr. Aristide and scheduled to receive an honorary degree at the Groupe Olivier Collaborateur University, Mr. Carter landed here to find the walls of the capital covered with graffiti denouncing him. 
 There was no official representative of the Haitian Government at the airport to greet him, but tonight Mr. Carter met with President Aristide and then went to a dinner at the United States Embassy.
Despite the graffiti, he professed to be undeterred and eager to jump back into the fray in a country he has visited nine times over the last decade. "We are very glad to be back in Haiti, a country obviously dedicated to peace, human rights and democracy," he said upon arriving.
The former President was accompanied by his wife, Rosalynn. Senator Sam Nunn, Democrat of Georgia, and Gen. Colin Powell, who helped Mr. Carter negotiate the departure of Haiti's military dictators and the entry of 20,000 American troops, are to arrive Friday.
Their visit, which is to end Saturday night, is intended to examine the most contentious issues facing Haiti. The priority, Mr. Carter said, is "to assess the progress being made, and to see if we can be of any help, in an orderly election process."
He said the delegation would also evaluate Haiti's security and economy. He stressed that "we are not here in an official capacity," but also said that he would be reporting to President Clinton "and other officials."
On Monday, after months of negotiation and a new electoral code, the Haitian Government announced that elections for more than 2,000 legislative, county and municipal posts will be held on June 4, with a runoff on June 25. Jockeying for political advantage began immediately, and accelerated when it became known that Mr. Carter intended to meet with leaders of 18 different parties.
On Tuesday, Mr. Aristide dismissed all 4 generals and 39 colonels and majors from the Haitian Armed Forces. Almost immediately, rumors began circulating that Mr. Carter would attempt to persuade the President to rescind that action.
Some diplomats here described the timing of the military purge as a warning to Mr. Carter not to meddle in Haitian affairs. "He could have announced those changes any time he wanted, but he chose to do so three days before Carter was scheduled to arrive," one diplomat said.
In addition the country has been swept by rumors that sympathizers of the military regime may attempt to disrupt the Carnival celebration this weekend. A decree issued by the Ministries of the Interior, Defense and Justice on Wednesday spoke of efforts to subvert the government, thus increasing the unease among the populace.
Early in the week, anti-Carter graffiti in Creole and English appeared on walls of buildings here, including the American Embassy, scribbled by groups of young men who fled whenever an American military patrol turned the corner. The slogans in English included obscene insults as well as: "Carter, We Don't Need You" and the ever-popular "Carter Go Home," while the Creole messages spoke of "Carter the fake democrat" and "Carter, lawyer for soldiers and thugs."
According to Haitian radio reports and some of the slogans, the graffiti appeared to be the work of the left wing of Mr. Aristide's Lavalas movement, which is suspicious of the American role here and of Mr. Carter's intentions. But some diplomats suggested clandestine pro-military groups trying to discredit Mr. Aristide were responsible.
Mr. Aristide's press secretary, Yvon Neptune, said today that while the Government was not involved in the graffiti, such expression was to be expected in a new democracy like Haiti's where "Everyone is free."
Groups supporting Mr. Aristide were particularly angered by Mr. Carter's decision to invite representatives of right-wing parties, including several that supported the dictatorship that overthrew Mr. Aristide in September 1991, to meet with him. More than a score of grass-roots organizations issued a statement this week saying that the Carter visit posed a danger to democracy.
Mr. Carter jokingly described the graffiti as part of his official welcome. He went on, however, to describe it as "a lawless act, apparently by one who is against democracy and freedom but who doesn't represent the people of Haiti."

HEADLINE: Dispute Over Nuclear Treaty Is Souring Relations Between Israel and Egypt

BYLINE: By CHRIS HEDGES, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: CAIRO, Feb. 23

 BODY:
Egypt, which in 1979 was the first Arab country to make peace with Israel, now sees an alarming deterioration of its relations with its neighbor, centered around a bitter dispute over Israel's refusal to sign the treaty to prevent the spread of nuclear weapons.
The dispute, which has resulted in sharp exchanges between the two countries, is the most serious since Egypt's condemnation of the Israeli invasion of Lebanon in 1982. Egypt's position has soured relations with the Clinton Administration, which has mounted a strong drive to renew the nuclear non-proliferation treaty indefinitely at a United Nations conference in April. And it has stymied Israel's efforts to broaden diplomatic and economic ties with other Arab countries. 
 "We do not believe that the N.P.T. extension should be linked to whether other states join to the treaty, or to a specific time frame for achieving our broadly supported non-proliferation goals, in the Middle East," a senior American diplomat said.
Egypt, along with Syria and six Persian Gulf countries, now say they will refuse to support renewal of the treaty until Israel signs. Egypt is lobbying other Arab nations to reject the treaty. It has advised Muslim countries to hold off normalizing relations with Israel. And the Israeli Prime Minister, Yitzhak Rabin, has accused Egypt of blocking Israel's attempts to establish diplomatic relations with the African nations of Tanzania, the Comoros and Djibouti.
"We shall continue to probe and seriously discuss the Israeli nuclear program," Foreign Minister Amr Moussa said after a meeting today with President Hosni Mubarak and the Israeli Foreign Minister, Shimon Peres. "We need more time, to see what we can reach."
The two sides said they would continue discussions and seek to thwart a further deterioration in relations.
But Mr. Moussa said Israel could not aspire to a wider peace while hiding what he called the only nuclear weapons arsenal in the region.
"We can't talk about a new regional order and then permit a unique nuclear program in our next-door neighbor," he said.
There is no optimism in Israel about a quick way out of this dispute. "The problem is that N.P.T. has no solution," said a senior Israeli official, who refused to be identified. "We won't sign, and they won't drop their demand that we sign."
The exchanges between Israeli and Egyptian leaders have grown heated in recent weeks. Mr. Rabin's characterization a few days ago of Egyptian tactics as "a foul wind" infuriated many officials, including Mr. Mubarak.
The confrontation has also strained Cairo's relations with Washington at a time when many in the new Congress are questioning Egypt's lavish aid package. Israel and Egypt each receive about $3 billion a year in aid.
But American diplomats said that despite ongoing discussions with the Egyptians, there was no effort to use the aid as a bargaining chip.
Israel, which refuses to confirm or deny the existence of a nuclear arsenal, is said by Western diplomats to have about 200 nuclear warheads.
If Israel signed the 25-year-old treaty, its installations would be opened for international inspection, exposing the full extent of any program, and it would have to freeze development of nuclear weapons.
Israeli officials contend that the Egyptians have seized on the issue to keep Israel isolated in the Middle East.
A United States Government report, as early as 1964, listed Egypt as the Arab country most vulnerable to a nuclear attack. Mr. Peres sought today to ally Egyptian fears.
"What we are doing should not affect Egypt, which is a good neighbor," Mr. Peres said. "It should affect countries that hope to destroy Israel -- like Iran, Iraq and maybe Libya."
Mr. Peres, in an Israeli radio interview earlier today in Jerusalem, said he had turned down a request by Mr. Moussa to visit Israel's top-secret Dimona nuclear installation.
"I told him," Mr. Peres said on the army radio, "I thought that was very dangerous. He asked me why and I said: 'What will happen if you visit Dimona and discover nothing. You'll ruin our deterrent.' "

HEADLINE: Greenspan Reassures the G.O.P. on Planned Budget Cuts

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 23

 BODY:
Continuing his campaign for deep cuts in the Federal budget, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, Alan Greenspan, reassured Republicans in Congress today that he would try to protect the economy from any damage that might be caused by their proposed spending reductions.
Mr. Greenspan also followed up on his suggestion last month that the Government's Consumer Price Index overstates inflation by recommending today that Congress appoint a special commission to pinpoint the upward bias in the inflation gauge. That way, he said, rather than altering the index itself, the Government could hold down the increase in the future cost of Social Security benefits, Federal pensions and other costs pegged to inflation in line with the commission's estimates. 
In his second day of Congressional testimony, Mr. Greenspan echoed his statement on Wednesday that the economy shows signs of cooling off, hinting that the Federal Reserve would hold interest rates steady or even ease credit if there were signs of a sharp downturn.
Supporting that viewpoint were two economic indicators released today.
The Commerce Department said business investment in factories and equipment, a major source of economic strength for the last two years, should slow in 1995 to a much more moderate pace. American business plans to increase capital spending by 6.6 percent this year, to $592.9 billion, less than half the 13.7 percent jump recorded in 1994.
In the other report pointing to a slowing economy, the Labor Department said that first-time claims for unemployment benefits by newly laid-off Americans rose 2,000 last week, to 349,000. It was the highest since the week ended Jan. 7, which was skewed by holiday fluctuations. Excluding that week, it was the highest since last July.
These were the latest among several recent signs that the expansion is finally being braked by interest rate increases.
Mr. Greenspan, in his testimony today before the House Banking Committee, sought to reassure Congress that spending cuts would not make the slowdown any worse.
It is "unquestionable," Mr. Greenspan said, that in the long run, deep budget cuts would result in lower interest rates and better economic growth. But he added that if the cuts were so severe that they slowed the economy in the short term, "we obviously will respond to that."
Mr. Greenspan was clearly trying to head off an excuse for limiting budget cuts. If the absence of Government spending threatens to deflate the economy, he suggested, the Fed could offset the effect with lower interest rates. But he stopped short of supporting the Republican plan to pass a balanced budget amendment, and expressed doubts that tinkering with the Constitution was a wise way to set the nation's fiscal policy.

HEADLINE: Panel Opposes School Lunch Program

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 23

 BODY:
A House committee voted today to scrap the national school lunch program and let states decide how to use Federal money to feed school children.
The program was created in 1946 and has become a fixture of American life, subsidizing meals served to more than 25 million children a day. 
 By a party-line vote of 23 to 17, the House Committee on Economic and Educational Opportunities today approved legislation that would turn this and other child nutrition programs over to the states, with a lump sum of Federal money, or a block grant.
Today's action came as House Republicans gained momentum in their efforts to pare back social welfare programs established over the last 60 years. Democrats protested loudly. The bill is likely to be approved by the full House, but the outlook in the Senate is unclear.
Senator Patrick J. Leahy of Vermont, the ranking Democrat on the Senate Agriculture Committee, predicted that the child nutrition proposals in the House bill would die in the Senate. "This assault on America's children will be stopped," he said.
Republicans, including Gov. John Engler of Michigan, one of the foremost advocates of block grants, contended that they were being depicted as heartless and mean-spirited.
Under the Welfare Reform Consolidation Act approved today by the House committee, states would gain vast new discretion to design their own school lunch and breakfast programs. Federal nutrition standards would be eliminated; states would set their own standards.
By a vote of 21 to 15, the committee rejected a Democratic proposal to preserve the school lunch program in its current form.
Republicans said the change to a block grant was necessary because states were wasting time and money on huge amounts of paperwork required by Federal regulations.
Representative Donald M. Payne, Democrat of New Jersey, said that if the Federal requirements were too burdensome, "let's look at eliminating the paperwork, not the meals."
Representative Marge Roukema, Republican of New Jersey, voted to replace the school lunch program with a block grant. But she said she was uneasy about one aspect of the proposal: each state would get a fixed sum of money for the year, and the amount would not increase if a state experienced recession or high unemployment.
Under current law, Federal spending for school meals increases in hard times because anyone who meets the eligibility criteria based on family income is automatically entitled to a free lunch. The House bill, part of the Republicans' Contract With America, would eliminate this guarantee.
Representative Duke Cunningham, Republican of California, defended the proposal for a block grant, saying, "I really believe states can do it much better at less cost with more efficiency."
The amount of money in the block grant would increase from $6.7 billion in 1996 to $7.8 billion in the year 2000, Republicans said. But Democrats said it would not increase as fast as it would under current law.
The House bill would allow legal immigrants to get subsidized school meals, but would deny such assistance to children who were illegal aliens.
The bill would also establish a block grant to replace the Special Supplemental Nutrition Program for Women, Infants and Children. This program, known as WIC, has been shown to reduce infant mortality by improving the health of pregnant women.
A separate part of the House bill would consolidate nine Federal child care programs and freeze Federal spending for them at the 1994 level, $1.9 billion a year. Republicans said the states could serve more people with the same amount of money by slashing administrative costs.
But Helen Blank, a policy analyst at the Children's Defense Fund, said the money was "totally inadequate" to meet a growing demand. Under welfare bills supported by lawmakers from both parties, she said, millions of welfare mothers will be required to work and will need child care.
The House committee rejected a Democratic proposal that would have required states to use competitive bidding when they buy infant formula for babies from low-income families. Such bidding procedures, required under a 1989 law, saved more than $1 billion in the WIC program last year. The savings permitted states to feed more women and children without increasing the cost of the program.
Representative Bill Goodling, the Pennsylvania Republican who is chairman of the House committee, said state officials were smart enough to use competitive bidding techniques if they were truly beneficial. But Robert Greenstein, director of the Center on Budget and Policy Priorities, who was head of the Federal Food and Nutrition Service in the Carter Administration, said, "In many states, the major infant formula companies successfully fought the use of competitive bidding before the Federal Government required its use."
Gov. Howard Dean of Vermont, a Democrat, said the bill approved today was "the most despicable, mean-spirited legislative proposal I have seen in all my years of public service." Without doubt, he said, "children will go hungry" if the bill becomes law.
Mr. Engler of Michigan, however, said he welcomed the flexibility provided by the House bill and would use it to coordinate the school lunch program with WIC and other forms of food assistance.

HEADLINE: To Some Sioux, Costner Now Dances With Devil

BYLINE: By DIRK JOHNSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: DEADWOOD, S.D.

 BODY:
Kevin Costner became a hero among American Indians five years ago for his movie "Dances With Wolves," a tribute to the Lakota Sioux culture before whites swarmed the Black Hills in the 1870's to snatch the land and search for gold.
In fact, the actor was presented by tribal leaders with a hand-woven quilt and honored with a ceremony in which a sacred eagle feather was tied in his hair, a gesture of appreciation for his sensitivity to Sioux traditions. 
 But now that Mr. Costner wants to build a huge new casino and resort here, some Sioux feel betrayed. The resort would be partly built on National Forest land that tribal leaders believe belongs to the Indians.
"After the movie, I thought he was pretty cool," said Joe Pulliam, a 26-year-old Sioux artist who lives on the Pine Ridge reservation in Shannon County, the poorest county in America. "But now, it seems like he's just another white guy coming here to take what he can get."
The complicated dispute over the land sought by Mr. Costner and his business partner and brother, Dan Costner, dates to the Fort Laramie Treaty of 1868. Under that agreement, the Sioux were granted land from the Missouri River in South Dakota west to the Bighorn Mountains in Wyoming, in exchange for a promise from the tribe to vacate vast sections of the Great Plains.
When gold was discovered in the Black Hills in the 1870's, however, settlers from the East began to swarm into Deadwood's famous gulch and other areas. And in 1877, Congress passed a law that took back the land, an act that many historians believe was intended to punish Indians for the defeat of General George Armstrong Custer and the Seventh Cavalry in the Battle of Little Bighorn a year earlier.
The Supreme Court ruled in 1980 on behalf of the tribes, ordering the Federal Government to compensate the Indians for the property loss. But the tribes have refused to accept the money, insisting that they be given National Forest lands in the Black Hills, which they consider sacred. The monetary compensation has been held in escrow, drawing interest, and is now about $380 million.
The Costners, who also own the Midnight Star casino in Deadwood, which is on private land, want to use about 600 acres of National Forest land for an 18-hole golf course for their new project. The proposed $100 million resort, to open in 1997, would also include a 350-room lodge and an outdoor amphitheater built on adjacent private land.
In exchange for the Federal land, the Costners would swap a 600-acre parcel they own in Spearfish Canyon, about 10 miles from Deadwood. The National Forest Service says it will probably approve the trade because the private land being offered is pristine and spectacular.
But the tribes contend that no National Forest land in the Black Hills should be traded, since they consider the compensation issue unresolved. Over the years, tribal leaders have tried, unsuccessfully, to persuade members of Congress to sponsor legislation that would return the Black Hills to the Indians.
Among whites in South Dakota, any talk of returning the Black Hills to Indians is overwhelmingly opposed. And they welcome the proposed Costner project, especially since jobs are scarce in this remote section of the Dakotas and could become even more scarce if Ellsworth Air Force Base in Rapid City, the largest employer in the area, is shut down in the Pentagon's effort to save money.
Some Sioux express concerns that a huge new casino would hurt business on eight Indian-run gambling halls in South Dakota. But others believe the huge Costner project would increase business for the tribal halls by attracting tourists who could then be lured into making the three-hour drive to the closest reservations.
"We've got plenty of history in Indian country, and we can compete with the casinos in Deadwood for tourism," said Wayne Barber, the general manager for the new Prairie Wind casino on Pine Ridge reservation. "They've got Wild Bill Hickok and we've got Crazy Horse. They've got Potato Creek Johnny and we've got Chief Red Cloud. We've got a herd of bison, an elk refuge, the grasslands, the Badlands."
Kevin Costner, who is working on a movie in Hawaii, could not be reached. Publicists for the actor have declined to comment. Dan Costner did not return telephone calls requesting an interview, but he released a statement earlier contending that their resort would not hurt tribal casinos and that it would have a "strong, positive, measurable impact on Deadwood and the surrounding communities' economies."
Sioux leaders have also expressed resentment over the $14 million in Federal, state and local tax breaks and incentives that have been promised for the Costner project.
"When the tribes wanted gambling, the state fought them every step of the way," said Tim Wapato, the executive director of the National Indian Gaming Association in Washington. "Along come the Costners, who don't even need the money, and they get subsidies. It seems racist to me."
The Costner resort would be named Dunbar, after the United States Army officer played by the actor in "Dances With Wolves." Mr. Costner earned an estimated $60 million from the movie. At the time, some tribal officials suggested that the actor invest some of his profits in Indian casinos. But he has not done so.
Tim Giago, the publisher of the Indian Country Today newspaper in Rapid City, said many of the Sioux people who revered "Dances With Wolves" wanted to give Mr. Costner the benefit of the doubt, speculating that he was "so busy with his movies" that he had not kept track of the dispute in South Dakota.
Doris Leader Charge, a Sioux who taught Mr. Costner some of the Lakota language and had a role in the film, remembers as a small child going to the store and interpreting for her grandmother, who spoke no English. Invariably, the girl would ask for candy. "When I get my Black Hills back," her grandmother would answer.
"Now I'm a woman of 70 myself," said Ms. Leader Charge. "And we're not any closer to getting back our Black Hills than we were then."
As for Mr. Costner, Ms. Leader Charge said that by "making a movie that told people all over the world that the Lakota people still live -- will always live," he has already given American Indians a gift more valuable than money.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Joe Pulliam, left, lives on the Pine Ridge reservation in Shannon County, South Dakota, and is among those Sioux who feel betrayed by plans for a casino in the Black Hills by the actor Kevin Costner. Mr. Costner is developing the project with his brother, Dan, shown viewing the site at right. (Photographs by Steven McEnroe for The New York Times)
 
Map of Deadwood, S.D.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Rally in Bonds Falters On Renewed Rate Fears

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The prices of Government securities rallied early yesterday, but then gave back almost all the day's gains after comments from the vice chairman of the Federal Reserve rattled some traders and investors.
The price of the 30-year bond fell 5/32 , to 10031/32 , for a yield of 7.54 percent, up from 7.53 percent on Wednesday. The rates on shorter-maturity bills increased a couple of basis points, while the yields on intermediate notes declined slightly. 
 In an interview with Market News Service, Alan S. Blinder, the vice chairman of the Federal Reserve, said he did not intend to signal in a speech last Friday that the Fed was finished raising interest rates or that its next policy move would be a cut in interest rates. Instead, he said, he could "not preclude" another rate increase.
The suggestion was not what traders wanted to hear, because the market rallied yesterday morning and on Wednesday after the chairman of the Federal Reserve, Alan Greenspan, indicated in Congressional testimony that the Fed might be finished raising interest rates for the time being. Mr. Blinder's comments last Friday seemed to support that view.
Since February 1994 the Fed has raised short-term rates seven times to slow the economy and control inflation.
Until the interview with Mr. Blinder was made public yesterday afternoon, some traders were saying that they could see no limit to the bond rally. An increase reported earlier yesterday in weekly jobless claims data added to a growing belief that the economic slowdown was under way. Initial claims rose to 349,000 for the week that ended last Saturday, up from a revised 347,000 a week earlier. For the week of Feb. 11, the claims were 338,000.
Mr. Greenspan testified before the House Banking Committee yesterday, repeating the points on monetary policy that he made to a Senate panel on Wednesday. But he did not change what he said, and appeared to have little impact on the market.
Robert Sinclair, managing director of United States Treasury trading for BA Securities Inc. in San Francisco, said he expected that with Mr. Greenspan's testimony finished and the monthly Treasury auction of two- and five-year notes ended, the market's attention would turn to economic data.
At the five-year note auction yesterday, the yield was 7.12 percent, down from 7.79 percent last month, and the lowest since Aug. 24, 1994, when it was 6.91 percent.
Mr. Sinclair said the Treasury market had been helped by the fact that there was little supply in alternative investments like mortgage securities, municipals and corporate securities. "With the yields so tight to Treasury securities, it doesn't make sense to buy the alternatives," Mr. Sinclair said. "But that will change once those other alternative markets get some supply."
Analysts say that as the market trades higher, things can get out of line, and they expect the market to give back some of its gains. "The market has gotten out over its skis," Mr. Sinclair said.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Changing Outlook on Rates Sends Dollar Slightly Lower

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
The dollar finished slightly lower against leading currencies yesterday as investors continued to adjust their outlook for lower interest rates in the United States.
 Dealers said there was also a growing sense that the Federal Reserve was not prepared to halt the currency's slide. 
 In comments before Congress yesterday, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, Alan Greenspan, reiterated his view that the United States economy was slowing. His assessment has caused investors to lower the chances of a further rise in interest rates and, therefore, the value of the dollar.
 "The dollar feels weak because there is a reassessment taking place about where U.S. rates are going to be," said Paul Farrell, vice president at Chase Manhattan Bank.
 At a time when European economies, especially Germany's, seem to be heating up, the spread between American and European rates is expected to narrow, a change that traditionally bodes ill for the dollar.
Some analysts expect the Bundesbank to raise interest rates within the next several months, given signs of rising commodity prices in Germany.
 Also weighing on the dollar was a growing conviction that the United States would not intervene on its behalf. Yesterday, Mr. Greenspan strongly underscored his desire to see a strong dollar, but dealers said his remarks had failed to impress market participants.
 "The market still has some doubts about the Fed's commitment to come in and aggressively support the dollar," said Michael Faust, currency strategist at Bailard, Biehl & Kaiser, a money managment firm in San Mateo, Calif.
In trading in New York late yesterday, the dollar slipped to 1.4675 German marks from 1.4710 marks on Wednesday. The dollar also fell to 1.2455 Swiss francs from 1.2490 francs. The British pound rose to $1.5945 from $1.5883.
The dollar also continued to drift lower against the Japanese yen, falling to 96.60 yen, the lowest level since Nov. 2, when it touched a post-World War II low of 96.12 yen. The dollar settled at 96.72 yen late yesterday, down from 97 yen.
While less of a factor yesterday, a strong desire to hold German marks influenced the dollar's inability to rally, dealers said.
 "The market is still very much enamored with the mark and that does not bode well for the dollar," said Tom Benfer, director of foreign exchange at the Bank of Montreal.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Nationsbank In Mortgage Service Deal

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The Nationsbank Corporation said yesterday that it would pay Keycorp $500 million for the right to service $25 billion in mortgages and some related assets.
The acquisition would increase the loan-servicing portfolio of Nationsbank's mortgage unit to $75 billion, making it one of the five biggest mortgage servicers in the country, the company said. 
 For big banks, loan servicing is a potentially lucrative business. The banks collect monthly payments from borrowers and pass them on to investors for a fee.
On Wednesday, the Nationsbank unit, the Nationsbanc Mortgage Corporation, agreed to buy the right to service $10 billion in mortgages from a unit of the Fund American Corporation of Norwich, Vt. The purchase price was not disclosed.
"We are very attracted to mortgage servicing right now, as it provides a good, recurring source of revenue," said Andrew Woodward, president of Nationsbanc Mortgage.
Shares of Nationsbank rose 75 cents yesterday, to $51.375, on the New York Stock Exchange.
An increasing number of mortgage companies are being acquired by bigger banks, whose lower costs allow them to make more money on the business.
Profits at mortgage companies have fallen in the last year as higher interest rates dampened demand for new home loans and shut down the refinancing of existing loans. The slowdown has depressed the prices of mortgage banks.
"If you're committed to the business, you'd rather buy at the low than at the high," said John Heffern, bank analyst with NatWest Securities. "Nationsbank has a history of making pretty savvy investments, buying when most people want to sell."
Nationsbank's deal includes paying $350 million for about 390,000 loans with a weighted average interest rate of 7.95 percent, and $150 million for related assets, including receivables and advances.
Keycorp said it was selling the business, which it put on the block in October, because of its low returns. The mortgage unit has about 500 employees.
"To provide returns comparable to our other businesses, substantial investments would have been required," Keycorp's chairman and chief executive, Victor J. Riley Jr., said.
Keycorp, based in Cleveland, is the country's 10th-largest banking company, with $66.8 billion in assets, while Nationsbank, based in Charlotte, N.C., is No. 3, with more than $169 billion in assets.

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
Taking Over Mexico

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL 

DATELINE: SAN DIEGO

 BODY:
When a government lies, its people must pay, and very soon do.
When a poor country borders on a rich country, people from the poor country often cross into the rich country to find work. Sometimes they don't have entry visas. 
 When these people illegally hunting work are presented as a terrifying menace, soon legally arrived foreigners become targets, then even certain native-born.
They are time-proven, all three of these little truths. They are also guaranteed to be ignored when they are the roots of trouble, as they are of the U.S.-Mexico intertwined double crisis of economic immigrants.
Maybe there are still parts of the U.S. where you can ignore Mexican-American realities. But not here, where the Mayor has a hot line connecting with Tijuana, nearby over the border, and certainly not now.
Without vote or debate in either country, the U.S. Government has taken over the responsibility for Mexico's economic future.
This decision was the result not only of mismanagement but falsehoods, evasions and self-delusions by the Mexican and U.S. Governments.
Originally they were designed to convince Americans that Mexico was strong enough financially and politically to enter an economic partnership with the U.S. -- the North American Free Trade Agreement -- that would profit investors and workers in both countries.
Then, as the Mexican economy unraveled, fast, the cover-up began. U.S. and Mexican officials did not pass on what they knew to the investing public or workers who were supposed to get jobs from Nafta.
How long can you cover up a collapsing peso? That can be answered precisely now -- until a few days before Mexico is to default. Then the Clinton Administration had to decide -- let the partner default and investors wait, or throw, oh, $20 billion to $50 billion into the pot to pay back Mexican and U.S. investors.
President Clinton spared Congress the trouble of a vote. Congress kissed his hands. But if Mexico goes bankrupt anyway, life will get nasty for Americans who should have revealed early warnings. So Washington decides to supervise Mexico and spend money to prop up its banks.
That puts America in charge. Mexican investors may like that -- and Mexican politicians who have been running what Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan describes as a one-party Leninist system.
But most Mexicans detest becoming wards of the U.S. or surrendering their oil as collateral. Maybe they will just go and have a revolution. Then Mr. Clinton can tell us how to get our money back.
The whole collection of Mexican and American turpitudes will result in even more Mexicans coming to work illegally in California and Texas. Since these states once belonged to Mexico, that eases whatever feeling Mexicans may have of committing a heinous crime.
But since in so many American eyes they are, why was the immigration problem never faced squarely in the Nafta or loan talks? Shyness? Not exactly. America is two-minded about Mexican immigrants. U.S. industry -- agriculture, textiles, construction -- wants them in time of labor shortages and other times wants them not.
Americans have the right to demand their Government try to stop illegals at the border. But whipped-up anti-illegal passions soon become anti-immigrant prejudices.
That is not maybe. California's plan to take health and education benefits from illegals is now in the courts. State action against legal immigrants has been ruled out by the Supreme Court. But unless the Court rules otherwise, Congress not only can act against legal immigrants but already has. A House panel has voted to deprive them of many health, education and welfare benefits.
Now we shall see whether Representative Gingrich and Senator Doletell their Republican colleagues to go along with the committee or fight for legal-immigrant rights in the name of that old American motto, what was it again, e pluribus something.
Next step: Gov. Pete Wilson of California wants a constitutional amendment to take citizenship from anybody born in the U.S. whose parents arrived without papers. Babies should be more careful -- the fourth little truth.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

SECTION: Section C; Page 1; Column 6; Weekend Desk 

LENGTH: 3201 words

HEADLINE: Bargains at Expensive Restaurants

BYLINE: By ERIC ASIMOV 

 BODY:
EVERYBODY wants to eat in expensive restaurants, but nobody wants to pay the price. At least, that's the usual stumbling block for the sizable portion of the restaurantgoing public that is unwilling or unable to empty its wallet to fill its stomach in the most refined sort of way.
But in an unlikely fit of collective wisdom, New York restaurateurs in the last few years have come up with a novel way to overcome this resistance: charge less. 
 Some of Manhattan's finest restaurants, including such luminaries as Gotham Bar and Grill, signed onto the $19.92 program (now up to $19.95), which offers a special three-course lunch for that price. Other expensive restaurants have menus in the same range, like Palio's $20 Piatto Unico menu and Jo Jo's $25 lunch deal. And some, like Follonico, offer simpler, less expensive menus at lunch than at dinner. In the latest twist, expensive restaurants are opening less-expensive, less-formal adjuncts in which a completely different menu is served, like Gramercy Tavern's Tavern Taste menu, served in its front room, Cafe Centro's Beer Bar, actually an adjoining restaurant, Cub Room's Cub Room Cafe, also an adjoining restaurant, and the Vodka Bar downstairs at American Renaissance.
It's not noblesse oblige that drives the restaurateurs, of course, but the desire to use their facilities efficiently. A full dining room is always better than a half-full one, and besides, there's always the chance that somebody paying $25 for a meal will feel luxurious and spring for a $50 bottle from the wine list, always a profit center.
Danny Meyer, an owner of Gramercy Tavern, says the inexpensive part of his restaurant allows the entire business to operate more economically. For example, he serves only the biggest veal chops with the largest fillet running through them on the main dining room's $52 menu. The chops on the end of the loin, which don't meet that standard, are on the less expensive tavern menu. Similarly, scraps and trims that are not included in the most meticulously designed dishes are saved and used in soups and salads. Little is wasted.
"It's a win-win situation for the entire restaurant," he said. "If we could sell enough food and beverage in the tavern, we could in a sense subsidize the luxury menu in the dining room."
Not every restaurant has added a financially gentle on-ramp. If $25 is your limit for a meal (not including
tax, tip and beverages), some restaurants may be impossible to enter. Others may demand clever maneuvering by the customer. Here are some principles to keep in mind:
*Lunches are almost always cheaper than dinners.
*Special deals, like pre-theater meals or Sunday night specials, abound. Restaurants use these deals to attract people during their least busy times, and there is an allure to eating at off-hours, like going to a movie in the middle of a weekday afternoon.
*Attitude is important. Feeling like an outsider is easy if you walk into an expensive restaurant and don't plan to spend a lot of money. But remember, restaurants offer special deals to attract customers. If the staff doesn't make you feel welcome, don't go back, and tell all your friends, too.
Here are 10 of the best meals I have recently found in Manhattan, not including some old favorites, like the $19.95 lunch on Fridays at Montrachet and food at the bar at Union Square Cafe. But menus and special deals change frequently, so you may find your own.
 
Beer Bar
 Met Life Building, 200 Park Avenue, at 45th Street, (212) 818-1333.
Beer Bar at lunchtime is raucous, stylish and exciting, all desirable attributes of a railroad terminal restaurant. Indeed, every few minutes rumbles emerge from underneath the brassy carpeting as trains enter and leave Grand Central Terminal next door. With its Art Deco metallic columns and paneled walls, transportation-theme mural and Bauhaus furniture, Beer Bar, the less-expensive auxiliary of Cafe Centro, seems like a culinary incarnation of the Machine Age, with all its characteristic energy and confidence.
The straightforward food fulfills the promise; nothing is fussy or complicated, just delicious. Like a wonderful roast chicken coated with herbs, the skin crackly, served with creamy garlic mashed potatoes and an arugula salad ($12.50); deliciously tender slices of veal served in a peppery stew with sauteed onions and carrots ($12.50), and a thick, juicy hamburger ($9.95) that comes with fried onions, bacon, crisp french fries and homemade ketchup. Appetizers are superfluous, but desserts are not. A frozen Kahlua parfait, served between two light coconut macaroons ($6.50), is enough to send you floating down to the Metro-North platforms.
After lunch, Beer Bar serves only appetizers, so lunch is the time for a meal. Naturally, there is a terrific beer list.
 
Cub Room Cafe
 183 Prince Street, SoHo, (212) 777-0030.
If Cub Room was intended to be the 90's embodiment of the inner sanctum of the Stork Club, the setting of some of Bette Davis's most memorable lines in "All About Eve," the Cub Room Cafe, its modest adjunct, might be the 90's embodiment of the roadside diner where Davis waited tables in "The Petrified Forest." With its wooden chairs and tables, marble tile floor and a blackboard menu for specials, the stark look is a good match for its big plates of gutsy food.
The cafe menu is large and neatly organized: sandwiches, soups, salads, sides and something called "plates," which turn out to be main courses. Among the appetizers, chicken livers glazed in balsamic vinegar and served with roasted potatoes and sweet beets ($6.95) is a hearty choice, while a classic spinach salad ($5.95) with a warm bacon vinaigrette and chopped hard-cooked eggs is expertly rendered.
Plates include a full-bodied lamb stew ($15.95), fragrant with garlic and rosemary, with cornmeal dumplings in a rich red-wine sauce, and pork pot ($12.95), an Alsatian-style dish of a pan-roasted pork loin served with apples, onions and potatoes in a vinegary broth. A pear-and-ginger crumble, with a scoop of lemon ice cream on top ($5), is a nice way to end the meal.
Smaller meals, like sandwiches (including peanut butter and banana), are offered, and the menu closes with this bit of sage advice: "Don't eat anything bigger than your head." The food is better than the joke.
 
Follonico
 6 West 24th Street, Flatiron district (212) 691-6359.
Midday bustles in the Flatiron district, but entering this wonderful trattoria is like crossing into Rome. Here the lunch menu, simpler and less expensive than at dinner, echoes the simplicity of the dining room's pale yellow walls, paneling, ruddy floor tiles, beamed ceiling and wood-burning stove. A glass of fruity Chianti (like the 1990 Monte Antico, $17 a bottle), combined with the wonderful smells of herbs and fresh bread add sensuous realism to the Italian fantasy.
Appetizers are seemingly commonplace, but each is augmented slightly, like a grilled portobello mushroom in rich white truffle oil ($7.50) or fried calamari, not so unusual until you taste the sharp deep-fried capers and greens ($6.50).
The main courses feel truly Roman. Spaghetti carbonara ($10.50) is elementally perfect, just pasta, cheese, pancetta, egg and butter with a little parsley sprinkled on, none of the cream, onions or peas that ruin so many New York versions. In Follonico's open-faced take on the porchetta, the traditional Roman sandwich, moist slices of richly flavored roast pork come on a slice of grilled bread, which soaks up the juices of the pork ($12). The whole is topped with remarkably flavorful broccoli rape, not at all bitter. Individual pizzas are crisp throughout and excellent ($9.50 to $12).
Desserts like sparkling granitas ($5.50), in flavors like pink grapefruit and orange, or a bountiful plate of biscotti and cookies ($5) make the return trip to New York much easier.
 
Gotham Bar and Grill
 12 East 12th Street, Greenwich Village (212) 620-4020.
The $19.95 lunch at the Gotham is one of the great bargains in town. Just entering the modern, spacious dining room makes you feel like one of Manhattan's chosen people, and the wonder is that getting a table is so easy at lunch.
The meal might begin with a chicken soup nothing like Mom's, made with clear chicken broth full of chunks of chicken and ham with accents of scallion and chervil, or with a creamy, intense carrot soup flavored with dill and pieces of smoked salmon that melt in your mouth. Follow that with one of Alfred Portale's signature vertical renditions, a mound of salmon, crisp around the edges, atop a foundation of tomato softened with richly flavored olive oil. And a choice of showstopper desserts: a flourless chocolate cake, still warm and oozy in the center, with a scoop of peanut butter ice cream, or refreshing sorbets like apple and blood orange.
All told, it's a remarkable opportunity to feel pampered and rich.
 
Gramercy Tavern
 42 East 20th Street, (212) 477-0777.
It's hard to imagine better inexpensive food in a more congenial place than in the tavern portion of Gramercy Tavern, in front of the handsome bar between the entry and the main dining room. With its high, wood-beamed ceiling, informal wood tables and Windsor chairs, open kitchen area, early American sideboard overflowing with seasonal fruit, flowers and herbs, and a colorful Robert Kushner mural, the tavern radiates good will and conviviality, like a wonderful community gathering place that just happens to have great food.
The tavern menu, available only in this part of the restaurant, offers an excellent selection of dishes, with appetizers like slices of portobello mushroom, grilled and served with a balsamic vinaigrette and topped with shavings of Parmesan cheese ($7.50). A glistening roast beet salad with red onion and shredded orange ($5.95), is both sweet and tart.
Main courses are beautifully thought out and presented. A tiny quail is butterflied and grilled and served on a soft bed of polenta. The rich, moist meat is set off nicely by little black olives, and the dish comes with a small green salad ($10.50). Roast loin of pork is a peasant dish, served with sweet-and-sour red cabbage spiked with bits of pancetta and apples ($13). Filet mignon is buttery tender but still beefy, served in a mild vinaigrette on a wondrous pillow of mashed potatoes and caramelized onions ($15).
The tavern menu includes an excellent selection of beers and wines by the glass. Chances are, you'll be offered the opportunity to choose desserts (and a cheese course) from the main dining room's menu, but that may elevate your bill to another level, and besides, the small tavern selection includes two superior desserts ($5 each). The upside-down pear tart is wonderful, like a pear Tatin in very light puff pastry, while oatmeal stout gingerbread is moist and delicious. It's the kind of food that makes you never want to leave.
The tavern menu is served daily from noon to 11 P.M., and since reservations are not available in the tavern area, it pays to go during off hours.
 
Jo Jo
 160 East 64th Street, (212) 223-5656.
Jo Jo, Jean-Georges Vongerichten's Upper East Side town house restaurant, manages to be informal and elegant at the same time. In the downstairs dining room, the floor is pure bistro, made of tiny tiles, but the ceiling is a rich lemon yellow, and the fresh flowers, pretty mirrors on the walls, thick linens and solicitous waitresses make you feel as if you are dressed up without wearing a tie.
Jo Jo's $25 lunch menu is a wonderful introduction to Mr. Vongerichten's light and surprising take on classic French food. The menu, which changes weekly, might include an appetizer of broiled sea scallops served over paper thin slices of gingery pink watermelon radish with a rosemary-scented vinaigrette, an exquisite steamed fillet of red snapper on greens and slices of potato in a peppery curry oil, and a baked pear enclosed in crisp pastry crust, bathed in a hazelnut creme anglaise. Do have coffee (it's included), which comes with a piece de resistance, almonds dusted with bitter chocolate.
 
Mad. 61
 10 East 61st Street, (212) 833-2200.
Downstairs at the Barneys New York uptown is a shopping center of a different kind. Around the perimeter of Mad. 61, the fine restaurant, are counters and displays showing what looks to be the bounty of a prosperous village market: olive oils in myriad shades of green, plentiful cheeses, crusty breads, wines and tempting desserts. From lunch to the beginning of dinner, you can sit at the counter or at one of the marble inlay tables and sample Mad. 61's market menu, an inexpensive way to try some remarkable food.
A simple but wonderful appetizer is a big platter of seasonal vegetables ($7.50), like carrots, yams, pearl onions, brussels sprouts and beets, roasted to the point that they are bursting with sweetness. You might want to try one of the excellent crisp-crusted pizzas, like a margherita ($14), simply pizza with tart, tangy tomato sauce with fresh basil and mellow mozzarella, but I would be hard-pressed to imagine a better, more soothing lunch on a cold day than the creamy, beige veal stew ($15), made with crushed white beans, olive oil, rosemary and generous chunks of tender veal.
The platters of tarts, cakes and cookies are enough of a preview to keep even the slimmest appetites alive for dessert. Plum polenta ($6) is an extraordinarily light cake, sweet but not overbearingly so. Sabbiosa ($6) is a luscious, light spongecake, almost biscuity in texture.
Mad. 61 has an excellent list of wines by the glass, ranging from $5.50 for a fragrant South African pinotage to $17 for a Kistler chardonnay from Sonoma County. You can also get smaller tastes of these wines before floating gently back out to the shopping lanes above.
 
Palio
 151 West 51st Street, (212) 245-4850.
What are we doing in Palio, one of the city's most expensive Italian restaurants? Why, having lunch, of course. Not in the second-floor dining room, but in the bar downstairs, under the towering cobalt-blue trellised ceiling, surrounded by the famous Sandro Chia four-wall mural of the Siena Palio. Just as striking as the mural is the fact that the bar, which bustles with excitement in the evening, is practically empty at lunch. If more people knew of the bar's $20 lunch menu, though, wild Tuscan horses would not keep them away.
The best appetizer at Palio is free, a basket of the restaurant's renowned breadsticks, rich with the flavors of olive oil and cheese, breaking with a perfectly satisfying snap in the mouth. For $20, you get a main course, a side dish of pasta and a salad. A grilled salmon steak, for example, is topped with an intense tapenade and comes with penne in a simple sauce of tomato and basil and a delicious salad of arugula and beets. A chicken breast is marinated in a balsamic vinaigrette, grilled and served with penne in a sumptuous veal reduction and a mixed green salad.
You must order strictly from the bar menu to keep prices down. An innocent request for a side order of polenta resulted in a $15 surcharge without a bat of the waiter's eye. Desserts are another $10 apiece, but then the excellent tiramisu is best split in the middle of the day, anyway.
 
Rainbow Promenade
 General Electric Building, 30 Rockefeller Plaza, at 49th Street, (212) 632-5100.
The Rainbow Room is the ultimate big-ticket dinner in New York, a night of dining, dancing and glamour 65 floors above Rockefeller Center. The Rainbow Promenade, the bar adjacent to the Rainbow Room, is a little tip of the big ticket: drinks (but no dancing), and food (though not actually "dining"). Still, there is glamour, or at least enough people in tuxedos and ball gowns to make you feel as if you've crashed somebody's wedding.
While there is an actual bar, most of the space stretching in two directions from the bar is devoted to handsome polished wood cocktail tables with Art Deco inlays. Most seats are near windows, and the view, of course, is stunning. Part of the attraction of skyscraper dining is simply the feeling of solidity, the palpable sense that even up so high, the floor, the walls and the windows are immovable. Such can-do confidence combined with a decidedly non-New York City crowd gives the Rainbow Room a real Middle America feeling.
Cocktails seem in order in this tribute to the cocktail age, and a master mixologist, Dale DeGroff, offers some inspired choices with wink-wink names like Hemingway daiquiri, blood and sand and the ever popular between the sheets (each $8.50). Rainbow Promenade serves "little meals," as the menu puts it, mostly appetizers. Some are quite good, like pastrami-cured salmon ($12), a David Burke creation, served on triangles of pita with onions and scallions and a swath of mayonnaise. Japanese hand rolls ($14), marinated strips of salmon in cones of seaweed, seemed over-priced even at this elevation, and a grilled chicken sandwich ($13.50) was drenched in mayonnaise, making the bread and tomato mushy. But a hamburger ($14) was decent and came with very good french fries.
For dessert, try a pear pithiviers ($7.50), if only for the Sisyphean task of pronouncing "pear pithiviers" after several cocktails. Inevitably, this is the moment when a women will stop at your table to ask if you'd like her to photograph your party.
 
Vodka Bar
 260 West Broadway, at Ericson Place, TriBeCa, (212) 343-0049.
This handsome bar, downstairs from American Renaissance, the striking new TriBeCa restaurant, offers a brief but notable lunch menu and a comfortable port in a storm. Along one wall is a row of marble pedestal tables with fresh orchids, a perfect contrast to their soft, billowy banquettes trimmed with gray-striped pillows that almost invite you to take a nap. Opposite is a curving bar with cobalt-blue lights hanging overhead. Bottles of vodka, some infusing with fruit, others with vegetables, stand ready to assist your journey to somnambulance.
Food is a wake-up call, especially a fresh pea soup with dill ($4) that is a striking chartreuse but tastes like the essence of peas, earthy and wonderful. Not so an arugula salad with mushrooms and shavings of Parmesan cheese ($6) that was so salty it was inedible. Homemade saffron fettuccine with seared shrimp, dill and sun-dried tomatoes ($12) was also salty, but not so much that the flavors of the pasta and the seafood broth could not be enjoyed. Simpler choices are better, like a very good hamburger on a fresh poppy roll ($7), with stacks of potato slices, marinated tomatoes and a wonderful sweet pickle.
Succinctly, desserts are fabulous, and the kitchen seems to enjoy sending out extra ones. You may feel that chocolate souffle, soft and oozy in the center, with caramel ice cream on the side ($7) would be enough for two people. And while we also ordered shredded apples, molded into a tart and baked with a wonderful vanilla center, served with blueberry sauce and cinnamon ice cream ($5), little did we know we would also be receiving a pear tarte Tatin, with raspberry sauce and apple sorbet ($5). By the time you leave, you really will need a nap.
