SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 4; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1232 words

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
Announcing for President: Old News, but Still News

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: DALLAS, Feb. 23

 BODY:
Presidential candidates these days stage elaborately choreographed nonannouncements as they seek the maximum fanfare and publicity.
First, they coyly suggest that they might run. Then they announce "exploratory committees." Then they hint even more strongly that they will run. Then, when everyone knows what they have decided, they finally make it official. 
 Take Senator Phil Gramm. The Texas Republican plans to mount the stage at College Station, Tex., on Friday morning for the biggest announcement of his political career. There will be balloons and bunting and foot-stomping partisans.
But they will have gathered to hear Mr. Gramm tell the world something that everyone with a passing interest in politics has known for months, if not years: he is running for President. Already, there are bumper stickers, buttons and stationery. There has even been a televised debate among the Republican competitors.
Still, neither Mr. Gramm, a 52-year-old second-term senator who will be the first Republican to declare his candidacy for the 1996 Presidential nomination, nor his opponents are about to forgo a tradition that dates from when there was genuine suspense over who would run for the Presidency. The only surprises these days are when candidates unexpectedly announce that they will not run, like then-Gov. Mario M. Cuomo's declaration the week before Christmas in 1991 and that of former Vice President Dan Quayle earlier this month.
"Everybody knows you're going to run the foot race but you do have to fire the starting gun," said Alex Castellanos, Mr. Gramm's media adviser. "Some of it is ritual, but it's an opportunity to focus public attention on your campaign and your message."
But Lyn Nofziger, the Republican consultant, said a candidate's time and money could be better spent. "As far as public perception is concerned," he said, "it doesn't amount to a hill of beans."
Mr. Nofziger said that at least he tried for some drama when he scripted Ronald Reagan's announcement challenging President Gerald R. Ford for the nomination in 1976. "Reagan was always careful about not saying he was going to run," he said. "So his announcements had a little bit of meaning."
Oddly enough, as the importance of announcements has diminished, candidates are trying to make them an even bigger deal. Perhaps they are mindful of the adage among politicians: The only time you are assured flattering news coverage is on the day you announce and on Election Night -- assuming you win.
No longer are candidates holding stark events in their hometowns. Mr. Gramm, for one, kicked off his announcement with a fund-raising extravaganza tonight at the Dallas Convention Center here, at which he proclaimed that he was now a "real candidate." Organizers said the event -- billed as the "official announcement dinner" -- raised $4.1 million, a record for one night.
On Friday, his first stop will be the front lawn of the Administration Building at Texas A&M University in College Station, where he taught economics. Then he and his entourage will fly to Columbus, Ga., and deliver a similar address in front of the elementary school where he failed the third grade. Then on to New Hampshire, Iowa and Arizona, where early contests will be held, for more events on Saturday.
By stretching out their announcement tours, candidates try to get more attention from the local press. "When you go to Columbus, Georgia, you're going to get three minutes on the local news in the Atlanta media market, which covers 65 percent of the state," Mr. Castellanos said. "You can't buy that kind of an opportunity to tell folks who you are and what you're for."
Charles Black, a senior adviser to the Gramm campaign, said a candidate's formal declaration also "makes it a little bit easier to get people to write checks."
Kathleen Hall Jamieson, dean of the Annenberg School for Communication at the University of Pennsylvania, said announcements can be valuable indicators of whether candidates have identified clear themes that will translate well to the public. "The rhetorical moment is important because candidates are essentially saying to the press, 'Judge me by this,' " she said. "And they are telling their loyal constituents, 'This is our message.' "
Ms. Jamieson said candidates who win their party's nomination often have messages that do not change much from their announcement to their acceptance speeches.
"Gerald Ford didn't forecast central themes in his announcement speech that he held to in the primary race against Ronald Reagan," she said. "Then, in the acceptance speech at the convention, he had to restart where he should have been six months before." Mr. Ford lost the election to Jimmy Carter.
This year, Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the majority leader, is trying something more ambitious than his announcements in his hometown of Russell, Kan., when he opened his unsuccessful Presidential campaigns in 1980 and '88. On April 10, Mr. Dole plans to start out in Topeka, where he began his career as a state legislator, and continue with a weeklong announcement trip.
But as far as television viewers are concerned, Mr. Dole began his announcement tour weeks ago. "We'll, I'm going to run. For President. In '96," he declared to David Letterman on the "Late Show."
Asked by the host if this constituted his formal announcement, Mr. Dole quipped, "This was an informal announcement." Little did Mr. Letterman know that the Senator had made a similarly unofficial declaration the previous night -- reaching for an older audience -- in a televised interview with David Frost.
William B. Lacy, Mr. Dole's deputy campaign chairman, said the dribbling out of the Senator's intentions was very calculated. "We had for some time been concerned that if we didn't send clear signals to the activists that he was running," he said, "then they might sign up with other candidates."
The event is even more important for former Gov. Lamar Alexander of Tennessee, who is less known than Mr. Dole or Mr. Gramm. He will try to steal some thunder from Mr. Gramm by announcing his candidacy in a three-day swing that begins next Tuesday on the courthouse steps in Maryville, Tenn., where his father first introduced him to a member of Congress when he was in grade school.
Arguably, the last time there was will-he or won't-he suspense leading up to a ceremonial declaration of candidacy was in 1968, when Robert F. Kennedy, after days of public agonizing, rose in the Senate Caucus Room on a Saturday morning and declared to a standing-room-only crowd: "I am announcing today my candidacy for the Presidency of the United States. I do not run for the Presidency merely to oppose any man, but to propose new policies."
The only recent candidate who did not embark on a traditional announcement tour was Ross Perot in 1992; he got more publicity, perhaps, by stating his intentions on the CNN program "Larry King Live."
Mr. Dole joked in an interview that he had told Senator Arlen Specter of Pennsylvania, who will probably have a much tougher time raising money for his race, that they should try something different: a joint announcement in Russell, Kan., since they both are from there.
He recalled Mr. Specter's replying that he might consider it if the two had a joint fund raiser. Mr. Dole said he told his Pennsylvania rival, "Well, I don't know about that."

HEADLINE: Still the Right Deal for Mexico

 BODY:
The United States' pledge to provide $20 billion in loans and loan guarantees should solve Mexico's immediate financial crisis. Yet the well-crafted agreement completed this week imposes harsh, though necessary, restrictions on Mexico's monetary and fiscal policies. That carries risks for both countries.
The United States could lose money in the unlikely event that Mexico not only defaults on loans but also somehow reneges on its agreement to give Washington first rights to its oil revenues. A bigger risk is political: Mexican politics could turn anti-American when policies promoted by the United States hammer hard at Mexico's already modest living standards. 
 The threats for Mexico are far more grave. Foreign investors could flee, despite international aid, driving the Mexican economy into the ground. Short of that, major banks could fail -- despite provisions in the agreement to address their problem -- as Mexican importers, unable to overcome a shrunken value of the peso, default on their loans. Even if the aid package works, President Ernesto Zedillo will be tied to an economy that almost surely will sink for months, if not years. That is bitter medicine for workers who have waited patiently for the promised benefits of radical market reforms to show up in their paychecks.
Notwithstanding the risks, the Clinton Administration could not have designed a much better package. The United States, along with the International Monetary Fund and other international banks, plans to pump enough dollars into Mexico to assure foreigners that their loans will be repaid. The agreement imposes only restrictions that are needed to stabilize the peso and the economy. In truth, Mexico would have had to adopt the monetary and fiscal restrictions even without pressure from Washington. The constraints mirror I.M.F. restrictions it already accepted. Mexico must adopt policies that welcome foreign investors, and that means stamping out inflation with tight budget and monetary policies.
Mr. Clinton made the deal politically palatable at home by including appropriate conditions. Mexico will pay ample interest on loans, and fees for loan guarantees. Washington will have first claim on revenues from Mexico's exports of oil should it default on the loans. By going around Congress, Mr. Clinton avoided humiliating conditions that some lawmakers wanted to impose, including instructing Mexico how to treat Fidel Castro and what type of monetary institution to adopt in place of its central bank.
President Clinton has responded effectively to prevent an economic crisis next door. The next few years for Mexico will not be easy, but they could have been catastrophic without this agreement.

HEADLINE: 'I'm Spending My Children's Inheritance'

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Older Americans Cited in Studies of National Savings Rate Slump" (front page, Feb. 21) is absurd. To indict the elderly for being big spenders and not saving, thereby setting a bad example for the baby boomers, makes no sense whatever. 
 First, spending is good for the economy: it creates jobs and contributes to economic health.
Furthermore, if the elderly are spending their incomes, isn't that a fine example of the recently elevated principle of a balanced budget -- spending real money as it comes in? What are savings for if not a rainy day? And many elderly, as the bumper sticker says ("I'm spending my children's inheritance"), have concluded that it's raining now.
But what you ignore is that the over-50 population controls most of the liquid money assets -- money market funds and certificates of deposit; some estimates say as much as 75 percent, in addition to other securities. Add to that the fact that 75 percent of everyone over age 65 is a homeowner with mostly low or no mortgages, and you have a different overall financial picture.
Many older people don't need to save because they have reserves and are asset-rich. But this is not true for the boomers who, along with their negligible savings, often borrow against pension plans and real estate. At retirement their dubious if not dismal financial picture will be quite different from this generation of the elderly.
Blaming the elderly for consuming entitlements they paid for and characterizing these benefits as society's decision "to let the elderly consume a bigger share of the nation's resources" adds further insult.
Let's not forget that the Government has been using Social Security funds to finance this nation by restricting the Social Security trust investments to only United States Government securities. Had these funds, since 1935, been more broadly invested, the rich Social Security trust would be many times richer today and more viable for the future.
Health care is cited as the main culprit in consumption by the elderly. But at the heart of the problem may be our lack of a system that promotes wellness or that operates efficiently for an aging society.
The prospects for addressing these issues are not promising. The rejection of the initiative offered by Senator Bob Kerrey's commission on entitlement spending to look at aging over the next 30 years has shown that we are not about to engage in any serious long-term planning.
 
BERNARD TARR
New York, Feb. 21, 1995
The writer, director of gerontology at Marymount Manhattan College, is host of "Update on Aging" on radio station WEVD-AM.

HEADLINE: Last Chance for Many Somalis as U.N. Phases Out

BYLINE: By DONATELLA LORCH, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MOGADISHU, Somalia, Feb. 24

 BODY:
With the last peacekeeping troops packing their bags, today was the final payday for Somalis who worked for the United Nations at the port and airport of this uneasy capital.
In a devastated economy in which most salaried jobs were with the United Nations, the lines of Somalis waiting to be paid for the last time were among the more vivid reminders of the uncertain future for those who are left behind. 
 As hundreds of Somalis milled around the port gates, scores of others -- men, women and children -- managed to get in without passes, sneaking past the Bangladeshi guards, taking one of their last chances to scavenge United Nations leftovers before any fighting between Somali clans breaks out again.
Many Somalis have set up food stalls near the piers, and young children run after trucks, trying to climb on, yelling, "Americans! Americans!" Many try to hide and spend the night in the port area, when it is easier to scavenge, United Nations peacekeepers say.
Hundreds of Pakistani soldiers with the United Nations force continued to board chartered planes today, leaving about 4,000 peacekeepers. At the port, which is expected to close to commercial traffic on Sunday in preparation for the final pullout, the last ships are loading the remaining United Nations equipment.
And a vast American-led international force of 23 ships lies offshore waiting to assist in the withdrawal in what military officials say is a limited mission with only peaceful objectives. The United Nations operation succeeded the United States-led mission that came here in December 1992 to save this East African country from the ravages of war and famine.
For the Bangladeshi soldiers who guard the Mogadishu port, removing the Somalis without passes is a job that is repeated several times a day.
"There is always a rush every day as everyone is trying to sneak in and get some money," said Col. Nur ul-Islam, the commander of the Bangladeshi battalion. "About 50 percent of them sleep here at night. It will be difficult but not impossible to close the port."
So far only about 50 American troops are on the ground here, preparing for the arrival of another 2,000 who will move in to make sure the pullout goes smoothly. The port and airport areas are critical to Mogadishu's warring clans because they will be the main entrance points for fuel and weapons shipments.
Senior military officials and Western diplomats say the clans will fight for the area, but they are optimistic that the fighting will not begin until after the American troops leave. Senior United Nations officials say they believe that the factions have relatively few weapons.
"The price of ammunition has risen very sharply, and now they will have to think twice before firing one bullet," said Victor Gbeho, the United Nations representative in Somalia.
But American military intelligence reports indicate that the two main warring clans, in particular the forces of Gen. Mohammed Farah Aidid, are moving their armored cars near both the port and airport.
There has been sporadic fighting in some neighborhoods even as both Mr. Aidid and his main rival, Ali Mahdi Mohammed, have sent messages to the United States special envoy, Daniel H. Simpson, expressing willingness to cooperate with the withdrawal. Mr. Simpson is not in Somalia but remains in touch with all sides.
While trying to insure a peaceful pullout, the American military is also preparing for the worst: a clan-based attack aimed at its own troops or a spillover from fighting between Somali factions.
Though agreements have been signed between Somali leaders, previous accords have not been observed. Mogadishu's political atmosphere is highly charged; subclan allegiances constantly shift and armed bandits can be hired for $50 a day, a senior American officer said.
On Saturday, American and Italian forces and the United Nations troops will go over the entire withdrawal operation step by step, rehearsing each move down to the level of company commander. And then Lieut. Gen. Anthony Zinni of the American Marines, commander of the international operation, is to walk through the area his marines will control, including the beach where they are expected to land.
Aboard the ships, soldiers and marines are preparing, spraying their uniforms with insect repellant, some filling their battle jackets with grenades, others practicing with what the Pentagon calls "less lethal weapons," like rubber and wood pellets, pepper spray and sticky foam.
The nontoxic foam can immobilize attackers, gluing them to the ground and to themselves. It cannot be washed off with soap or water, but requires special solvents.
Some marines going ashore in a few days have already been to Somalia, veterans of the first American landing in 1992 and of the operation that followed.
Cpl. Shane S. Borba, 24, of Fresno, Calif., was in Somalia from April to August 1993, during the hunt for Mr. Aidid, the American bombings of the faction leader's headquarters and large demonstrations against the United Nations. He is part of the 13th Marine Expeditionary Unit.
"The marines here ask me what to expect," Corporal Borba said. "I tell them, don't expect a full-blown-out war. It's a mindset. Don't go in there thinking everyone is your enemy.
"I tell them the Somalis are here near them because they want a handout -- 'Give me water, give me food,' they always say.
"Watch out for crowds. The Somalis can go from being your friends one moment to throwing rocks the next. I have nothing against them. They are very inquisitive; they want to know what is going on. I saw so many people caught in the middle of a firefight not meant for them. I saw the horror Somalis live in."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Tension is high in Mogadishu, the Somali capital, as it awaits the withdrawal of the last United Nations peacekeeping troops. Schoolchildren joined about 4,000 other residents on Thursday in a demonstration calling for the introduction of sharia, or strict Islamic law, in the southern sector of the city. (Agence France-Presse)

HEADLINE: A Building Boom Transforms the Phoenix Desert

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PHOENIX

 BODY:
A happy man, Fred Keene stood beside his yellow bulldozer the other day as it idled among the century-old saguaro cactuses of the Sonora Desert and said, "Busy, busy, busy."
"I'm working nonstop," he added. "I've turned down work. Just turned down a job Monday night -- no time to do it." 
 Mr. Keene and the Caterpillar D-9H bulldozer he bought a year ago are part of a building boom that is gobbling up the desert around Phoenix at the rate of an acre an hour. Wide swaths of homes and shops are spreading outward from the city where only cactus, jack rabbits and rattlesnakes had been before.
"In a year this will all be houses," said Mr. Keene, standing in the afternoon sun on a development site zoned for 4,500 homes. Only a new two-lane blacktop road now cuts into the desert on the eastern edge of the city, its concrete curb sides neatly finished and already marked with speed-limit signs.
Phoenix, one of the nation's fastest-growing cities, is expanding so rapidly that it can barely find enough construction workers to do the job. Builders are raising wages as they bid for one another's skilled craftsmen, and the average time it takes to complete a typical house has increased from about three months to more than five months.
"It's causing the contractor to pay far more today for the same guy that he was complaining about yesterday not knowing a hammer from a screwdriver," said David Wilkinson, an official of the local branch of Associated Builders and Contractors, an industry group based in Rosslyn, Va. "They're desperate."
They are so desperate that the top 10 contractors in Arizona spent an average of $14,000 each over the last three months on national advertising to attract workers, particularly electricians, welders, pipe fitters, masons, roofers and carpenters.
Skilled workers are in short supply almost everywhere, particularly in parts of the South and the Midwest, even though new home construction has cooled because of rising interest rates, said Charlie Hawkins, executive vice president of Associated Builders and Contractors.
But few areas are building as fast as metropolitan Phoenix, which has 2.4 million people, 400,000 more than in 1990. Arizona continues to attract many new residents and businesses from California, by cutting its taxes and keeping its regulations simple.
Critics of the pell-mell development warn of growing congestion and air pollution, rising crime and a strain on public services, but they are greatly outnumbered in the city government.
The boom extends to commercial construction, including a burgeoning computer-chip industry clustered along the edges of freeways in almost every direction, Mr. Wilkinson said. In downtown Phoenix, construction includes a science museum, an art museum, a museum of history, a new library and a new home for the city's largest daily, The Arizona Republic.
Housing permits in the Phoenix area for detached single-family houses rose from 21,896 in 1993 to 26,614 last year, the highest since its record of about 28,000 permits in 1978, said Jay Q. Butler, an analyst at the Arizona Real Estate Center.
At the same time, said RL Brown, publisher of The Phoenix Housing Market Letter, 1994 was a record year in home resales, up 20 percent from the year before, to approximately 52,000.
"Combine the two numbers and you've got a total housing velocity in this marketplace that is something staggering," he said.
The quality of construction has suffered somewhat, several analysts said, and some homes are even being turned over to buyers with interior details unfinished.
Like other opponents of unfettered growth, City Councilwoman Frances Emma Barwood warns of a degradation of the quality of life, and of the cost to the entire city of providing outlying areas with water, electricity, and police and fire protection.
She said the problems ranged from the obvious -- providing enough electricity to run hundreds of thousands of air-conditioners in this sun-baked city, where the normal temperature in July is 105 degrees -- to the hidden and unexpected. As the surface of the desert is scraped away, she said, vast underground populations of termites are being disturbed and have begun to devour the new homes that replace the desert vegetation.
"My concern is, What do we want to look like 15, 20 years from now?" Ms. Barwood said. "Do we want to be like any other city, a sea of rooftops, or do we want to have that last little bit of desert?"
But when she proposed a measure to slow construction, the City Council rejected it by a 7-to-1 vote. "It's kind of hard," she said. "I knew I didn't have the votes, but I just didn't realize I didn't have any votes."
At a heated five-hour public meeting of the Maricopa County Planning and Zoning Commission, hundreds of residents opposed a new planned community called the Villages at Desert Hills, 33 miles north of downtown Phoenix. The $2 billion project, by the Del Webb Corporation, a major developer, would carve 5,661 acres out of the desert to build 16,500 homes. These opponents lost too, and the plan will go before the county's Board of Supervisors for final approval in March.
Stanley Cook watches all of this activity from a unique perspective, and he finds it exhilarating. He is the manager of housing studies for Landiscor, a photographic mapping company, and he studies aerial photographs of the city every 90 days and charts its growth, lot by lot, house by house.
For every house that rises, hundreds of businessmen profit as the city's economy expands. Among them is Mr. Cook, who sells his photographs to various developers.
His composite maps, with their precise, sharp detail, portray the steady progress of Mr. Keene's bulldozer, and others like it, as they scrape away the cactuses in an ever lengthening radius. Skirting Indian reservations and national park land, they progress from one development to the next, leaving houses, banks, pharmacies, pet stores, video outlets and Chinese restaurants -- human civilization -- in their wake.
"It's feeding on itself," said Mr. Cook, another happy man. "We are not going to be able to stop growing."

GRAPHIC: Photos: The Foothill development is an example of housing that has gobbled up the undisturbed desert around Phoenix at a rate of an acre an hour. The Phoenix boom has put people like Scott Deskins in demand. Mr. Deskins laid roofing paper last week. (Photographs by Jeff Topping for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Economy
What's so great about a sluggish economy? Wall Street has been celebrating an apparent slowdown, sending stocks to record highs. While such a rally may seem perverse, there are sound reasons for welcoming a breather from a four-year-old economic expansion. Analysts hope slower growth will buy time for companies to train more workers, smooth out the production pipeline and add the capability to turn out a greater supply of goods and services. Economic Analysis. [Page 1.]
 
Factory orders for durable goods rose six-tenths of 1 percent in January, suggesting that the economy may not be slowing as the chairman of the Federal Reserve, Alan Greenspan, has suggested. It was the third consecutive monthly gain. [37.] 

A building boom is gobbling up the desert around Phoenix at the rate of an acre an hour. The city is expanding so rapidly that it can barely find enough construction workers to do the job. [6.]

Companies
 
A possible setback for Rupert Murdoch emerged. A top F.C.C. official said he did not know in 1985 that the News Corporation, the Australian company controlled by Mr. Murdoch, would own 99 percent of the equity in six TV stations that Mr. Murdoch acquired that year. The statement provides new evidence that Mr. Murdoch may have misled the Federal Communications Commission. [37.]
 
The Hanover Sterling brokerage firm shut down, and stocks brought public by the New York firm plunged. The firm is best known for bringing to market a company begun by Lawrence Taylor, the former linebacker for the New York Giants. [37.]
 
Westinghouse Electric signed an accord to sell China steam turbine and generating equipment. The deal marks the first time since 1989 that an American company has been allowed to sell high-technology components for a Chinese nuclear plant. [39.]
The F.A.A. commended USAir for its safety efforts. [6.]
 
International
 
China is trying to build its own commercial-aircraft industry. The goal of its big push is to compete for aircraft sales throughout Asia. And China is relying on the technical and manufacturing expertise of Western companies including Boeing, McDonnell Douglas and Airbus Industrie to do it. [37.]
 
Germany is confronting its first big strike in 11 years. The country's most powerful union, IG Metall, sought to shut down 22 companies and plants in the rich southern state of Bavaria. [37.]
 
The United States and Canada agreed to end restrictions on air travel between the countries in a broad aviation treaty. [1.]
 
Markets
 
The Dow extended its advance beyond 4,000, rising 8.41 points, to 4,011.74. Food and beverage stocks led the ascent. [38.]
 
Treasury prices were mixed after the durable goods report showed signs of continued robust economic growth. [38.]
 
The dollar weakened as the German mark continued to draw strength from declines in other European currencies. [46.]
 
Gold and other precious metal prices tumbled. Crude oil prices rebounded, and coffee prices surged. [46.]
 
Your Money
 
An end to rising interest rates would help investors. If the Federal Reserve has indeed changed its policy, it may be an opportune time to consider buying high-quality intermediate-term debt securities with maturities from 3 to 10 years or investing in foreign stock markets. But betting on the Fed should not be the main factor in forming an investment strategy. [35.]
 
The Israeli stock market has failed to reflect the strength of the country's economy. Israel has built a powerful export-driven economy that is increasingly independent of the Middle East, but analysts wonder when its stock market will turn around. [35.]

HEADLINE: House Votes to Freeze Regulations as Democrats Fail to Gain Health and Safety Exemptions

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 24

 BODY:
The House of Representatives voted today to freeze most new Federal regulations for the rest of the year, with Republicans and Democrats arguing to the debate's bitter end over whether the proposal would endanger the public.
The vote was 276 to 146, as many Democrats ultimately joined to support a bill that President Clinton has threatened to veto if the Senate passes it, saying it would "cost lives." The outlook for the legislation in the Senate is uncertain. 
 Democrats were repeatedly voted down as they tried to exempt specific health and safety rules from the bill. Representative Tom DeLay, the Republican whip from Texas and the bill's senior author, said the amendments were aimed at "frightening women and children and the elderly."
The bill includes an exemption for emergency health and safety rules. But it is far from certain how many regulations could pass through that loophole.
Thousands of regulations are issued every year to carry out laws that Congress has already passed. But the new Republican majority in Congress wants the regulatory moratorium in place while it considers whether to rewrite the underlying laws governing the process of issuing rules.
The bill approved today is one part of a broad anti-regulatory offensive being mounted by Congressional Republicans. On Monday the House is to consider a bill that would replace health considerations with economic calculations as the deciding factor in writing rules to carry out many of the nation's existing environmental laws. Later in the week, the House will consider a bill to reimburse landowners if their property declines in value because the Federal government restricts how it may be developed.
The regulations most likely to be delayed under the regulatory moratorium are those issued by agencies like the Environmental Protection Agency and the Occupational Safety and Health Administration. But the bill exempts many of the regulations that lubricate the wheels of commerce, like licenses, deposit insurance and trade rules.
Democrats argued that rules to protect public health, safety and the environment should not be delayed, that some regulations beneficial to industry or to state and local governments would be caught up in the delay, and that the legislation was written so broadly that it could entangle urgent rules in litigation.
But almost every time the Democrats offered amendments to exempt health, safety or environmental rules from the bill, Republicans countered that the legislation provided leeway for the Administration to impose regulations whenever there was an "imminent threat."
"We have fully protected health and safety," said David M. McIntosh, a freshman Republican from Indiana who headed the effort to reduce Federal regulations during the Bush Administration. "This bill will allow the Administration to take any precaution necessary to protect health and safety."
But after two days of debate, the dispute lingered over whether the language permitting health and safety regulations was as lenient as its supporters claimed.
For example, although Republicans insisted that the Agriculture Department could proceed with new rules on meat inspection despite the legislation, the department's top officials disputed that, as did representatives of the meat industry.
And although Republicans said that E.P.A. rules requiring cities to check for the parasite cryptosporidium in drinking water supplies could go ahead, the agency said it did not believe the rules could be proved to head off an imminent threat.
"Although its sponsors say it would exempt public health emergencies, the exemption only applies to situations that are immediately life-threatening," said Carol M. Browner, head of the agency. "We couldn't take action on long-term health risks such as cancer. We'd have to stop rules to control dioxin from incinerators, lead poisoning and bacterial contamination of drinking water."
Democrats did succeed in getting a specific exemption for aviation safety rules, but it was done in an unusual way. After the bill was approved, Democrats worked out a deal with ranking Republicans to write a clarifying technical amendment into the bill.
That means, for example, that the Federal Aviation Administration will be able to follow through on its plan to tighten the safety rules governing commuter airlines to make them equivalent to standards imposed on major airlines. The rules will not be held up, even though it would be difficult for the agency to prove that there is any "imminent" danger of commuter airplanes crashing under existing rules.
The Occupational Safety and Health Administration said that many of its rules, like those to protect workers from crippling injuries from repetitive motions, would not be covered because the injuries are not considered "imminent."
These injuries are a rapidly growing source of claims for workers compensation. But the Republicans said that the new rules were not so urgent that they should go forward.
Republicans said that the bill leaves in the hands of a White House official -- Sally Katzen, the director of the Office of Information and Regulatory Affairs -- the determination of whether a rule addresses an imminent threat to safety.
But Administration lawyers said they expected to face lawsuits from affected industries if they attempted to push through many rules using the safety loophole.
 
 
 
THE BILL AT A GLANCE
Regulatory Freeze
 BILL The Regulatory Transition Act of 1995 (H.R. 450)
 
 HIGHLIGHTS Establishes a moratorium on rulemaking by most Federal agencies between Nov. 20, 1994, and Dec. 31, 1995. The moratorium would be lifted earlier if comprehensive regulatory reforms are enacted. Emergency health and safety rules, criminal law-enforcement regulations, and many rules favored by businesses would be exempted.
 
 POLITICS Strongly favored by many business groups but opposed by labor and environmentalists, the rule is part of a broad assault on
Federal regulatory powers being pushed through the House of Representatives by
the Republicans, with some Democratic support.
 
 COST Businesses spend hundreds of billions of dollars each year to comply with Federal regulations, but there is no estimate of the savings due to a temporary reduction in new rules.
 
 STATUS
 
 House Passed on Friday.
 
 Senate Similar bill has been introduced.
 
 White House Calls the bill "unacceptable."

HEADLINE: Clinton, in Canada, Attacks G.O.P. on Domestic Politics

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS JEHL, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: OTTAWA, Feb. 24

 BODY:
President Clinton opened a broad-based assault here today on the Republican-led campaign against big government. He called the balanced-budget amendment a bad idea, denounced the party's attacks on affirmative action and said deep cuts in current Federal spending amounted to a war on children.
Turning to domestic politics at a news conference that was supposed to wrap up a meeting with Canadian leaders, Mr. Clinton accused Republicans of trying to divide voters along racial lines. He painted the spending cuts they have proposed as "radical right-wing measures," while back in Washington his lieutenants labeled the balanced-budget amendment "horrendous economic policy" that could deepen a recession by limiting the Government's ability to mitigate it with increased spending. 
 The President's offensive at a news conference in Parliament seemed intended to buck up wavering Democrats as the battle over the balanced-budget amendment headed for a showdown vote in the Senate next Tuesday and as the debate over affirmative action turns fierce.
But Mr. Clinton also had to bow to the realities of politics in 1995 by conceding that the White House had been forced to begin its own review of programs aimed at opening jobs and other opportunities to minorities to decide which should be defended and which might be scrapped.
"Unless I miss my guess, a bill doesn't become law until I sign it or it passes over my veto," he declared. To a reporter who suggested that his Presidency had become more form than substance, he shot back, "I don't consider myself a titular head of state, and until there is some evidence to the contrary, you shouldn't either."
Mr. Clinton's counterassault, coming a day after two Democratic Senators threw their lot behind the balanced-budget amendment and brought support close to the 67 votes necessary for its passage, was marked by unusual vigor.
Among the points on which he objected more bluntly than ever before was what he called the "astonishing" prospect that the Republican-backed measure would lead to the Federal courts' reviewing budget decisions. That concern was also expressed by Senator Sam Nunn, the Georgia Democrat who is widely believed to be the last vote needed for passage, and Republicans were discussing today whether to accommodate Mr. Nunn's request for a clause that would eliminate any role for the courts in deciding Federal budget issues.
The President also directed his top deputies to fight back against the balanced-budget measure in television appearances and from the White House, and aides said he would wage the battle through the weekend with a Saturday morning radio address and telephone calls to undecided or wavering senators.
Sketching a vision of a stingy Republican future, Mr. Clinton seized on proposals, now emerging from House committees, to pare up to $17 billion from the current budget. He said he regarded the Republican-backed efforts to scrap the national school lunch program and otherwise allow states to decide how to spend Federal money on social welfare programs as representing a war on children.
"I do not think the American people expect or support these radical right-wing measures that are coming out of these House committees," the President said. Expressing his hope that the Senate would serve as a brake on the Republican juggernaut, he declared, "We've got a constitutional system, and we've got a chance to see it work."
Asked about a report, first published today in The Washington Post, that he had ordered a review of the Administration's support for affirmative action programs, Mr. Clinton acknowledged that he had done so but said the step did not amount to an abandonment of efforts to give the disadvantaged "a leg up."
He pointed to the armed forces as an example of an institution made better and more representative by affirmative action programs. "In education, training, leadership, development, the military is a model," he said. "It looks like America, and it works."
But the President acknowledged that Republican complaints had put pressure on his Administration to decide how willing it was to defend programs whose utility might be outlived. He said he was determined to prevent the debate over affirmative action "from becoming another cheap, political, emotional wedge issue," but he said he recognized that the White House needed to think anew about "what our obligations are to make sure every American has a chance to make it."
"We shouldn't be defending things that we can't defend," he said. "So it's time to review it, discuss it and be straightforward about it."
Mr. Clinton has long made clear his reservations about a balanced-budget amendment, the current version of which would require the Federal Government to balance its books by the year 2002. In 1994 the White House battled vigorously to prevent a similar measure from winning passage.
But after the Republicans took control of Congress in November, the President began to mute his complaints, and as the House rushed to approve the measure by a vote of 300 to 132 last month, he and his aides waged only a quiet fight against it.
Even today, Mr. Clinton stopped shy of using absolutist terms to denounce the measure. His aides said the approach reflected an understanding that his power of the veto is impotent against the proposed constitutional amendment; it will take effect if it is approved by a two-thirds vote in the Senate and ratified by 38 states.
But as Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin and other top officials, including Alice M. Rivlin, director of the Office of Management and Budget, joined forces at the White House this morning to attack the proposal, Mr. Clinton's aides said he had decided to turn to a more vocal campaign as the measure nears its denouement in the Senate.
Gene Sperling, Mr. Clinton's deputy assistant for economic policy, said the President had concluded that "there was a special force in people seeing how economically dangerous the entire economic team believed a balanced budget to be."

HEADLINE: Mideast Peace Effort on Hold

BYLINE: By CLYDE HABERMAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: JERUSALEM, Feb. 24

 BODY:
Suddenly, there seem to be nothing but speed bumps on the road to Middle East peace. Big speed bumps at that.
Israel's peace talks with Syria have been in hibernation for months, and by extension that means no progress is possible between the Israelis and the Syrians' client government in Lebanon. 
 With Israel and the Palestine Liberation Organization, negotiations on expanding Palestinian self-rule are an exercise in wheel-spinning.
Perhaps worse, the two sides have reverted to behavior patterns that were supposed to have been broken with The Handshake at the White House in September 1993. Israel keeps jabbing its finger in the Palestinians' chest, telling them what to do, especially on security. This week, the P.L.O. responded just as it used to do: running around complaining what bullies the Israelis are, and asking the world to intervene, instead of straightening out differences face to face.
As a worrisome sideshow, a battle over this country's presumed nuclear arsenal has caused relations to deteriorate with Egypt, the first Arab state to make peace with the Israelis. On every front, there has been no real movement for so long that taking the pulse brings reminders of an old Groucho Marx line: Either my watch is broken or this patient is dead.
The situation is hardly so serious, Israeli officials say, playing down talk of a crisis and pointing up positive developments like the recent peace treaty between Israel and Jordan, still on track. Negotiations, they say, are in a trough, not a grave.
Still, no one here denies that the situation is discouraging. The reasons differ on each major front, but they are also tied together.
Syria, for example, followed Egypt's lead this week, citing Israel's nuclear program as the reason talks are deadlocked. That is apparently a facade. President Hafez al-Assad of Syria simply seems uninterested in a quick deal, and maybe Prime Minister Yitzhak Rabin of Israel feels he can bide his time as well.
The basic elements of a bargain have long been evident: If Syria agrees to full peace, Israel will return the strategic Golan Heights that it captured from the Syrians in the 1967 Middle East war. Now, after more than three years of talks, the Israelis say they are not sure that getting the heights back is Mr. Assad's top priority.
Perhaps, they say, he cares more about consolidating his hold on Lebanon and about persuading the United States to come up first with inducements for peace: money or removal of Syria from its lists of states that sponsor terror and traffick in drugs.
But given current realities in Washington, he seems unlikely to get much of anything. As some officials here see it, Mr. Assad may have concluded that he would do better to wait. Buying time would also let him see what happens on the Israel-P.L.O. front. If that keeps going badly, the reasoning goes, Israel might well turn to Syria in mild desperation, and that would give Mr. Assad a stronger hand.
Since they have said they are ready to give up just about all of the Golan (probably indeed all, though they will not say so publicly), the Israelis are plainly exasperated.
"I can't understand the Syrians," a senior official said. "Don't they want the Golan Heights? Don't they understand our signals?"
Israel's frustration also extends to the United States, for not doing a lot more to push the old enemies toward one another. Without American activism, nothing will happen, warn Israeli officials, some of whom single out Secretary of State Warren Christopher as having been too passive. Health permitting, Mr. Christopher is supposed to visit the Middle East in early March, his first trip here in three months.
On the P.L.O. front, it is Mr. Rabin who seems in no hurry.
Enthusiasm for the 1993 arrangements outlining the stages of Palestinian self-government has faded among both Israelis and Palestinians. But Mr. Rabin in particular looks lately like a man who wishes he never got involved in this in the first place. He is reluctant to forge ahead until he is persuaded that Yasir Arafat, the P.L.O. leader, is serious about cracking down on anti-Israel terrorists in areas now under his control, the Gaza Strip and Jericho, on the West Bank.
In the next stage, Palestinian authority is supposed to be expanded throughout the West Bank. As defined in the 1993 agreement, that means Palestinian elections and a companion redeployment of Israeli soldiers.
Before 21 Israelis were killed last month, the latest casualties in a series of Islamic suicide bombings, the shape of an agreement was emerging. Israel would withdraw from Arab cities and towns in the West Bank, but on a staggered basis -- fully in some places, partially in others and not at all in yet others. A likely candidate for the last category is Hebron, where Jews and Arabs live together explosively one year, as of Saturday, after the massacre of 29 Palestinians by an Israeli settler.
But overshadowing the negotiations are the bombs and a long border closing by Israel that is strangling the fragile Palestinian economy in Gaza and the West Bank. And so the core issues -- elections and redeployment -- are not being worked out at all.
Mr. Arafat now demands that Israel stop stalling and start pulling out its forces. He will accept a gradual withdrawal but wants a timetable, he says. Mr. Rabin is not budging, however. First combat terrorism, he says, though his standard for success is fuzzy because even he acknowledges that no one can stop every fanatic with a pack of dynamite.
With no real progress, mutual charges of bad-faith bargaining have grown increasingly loud. They are dismal echoes of the bad old days, pre-Handshake, when Israel and the Palestinians fought endlessly over which one was the bigger victim instead of trying to figure out how to live side by side.
But significantly, no one has walked off in a huff, saying peace talks have gone as far as they can go. Far from it, in fact. Both Mr. Arafat and Mr. Rabin have invested so much in the negotiations, commentators on both sides say, that it may be impossible for them to stop now without suffering perhaps fatal political blows.
A big problem, some here say, is that The Handshake and the euphoria surrounding it were overblown by the politicians as a new dawn. When imbedded enmities did not vanish, disappointment over inevitable reversals came harder than might have been the case with a softer sell.
In this view, patience is the key.
Yaron Ezrahi, a political theorist at Hebrew University, is reminded of the so-called "war of attrition" -- grinding and costly battles between Israel and Egypt in the late 1960's. Israel and the Palestinians, Professor Ezrahi says, are locked into "a peace of attrition," which may take its time and not always follow a straight path.
"It's going to move in zigs and zags for many years," he said. "There will be small conflicts and setbacks. But nobody has the power to stop the peace process and face the alternatives."

HEADLINE: Why Wall St. Cheers as Economy Slips

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR 

 BODY:
Wall Street investors broke out the champagne this week, celebrating what appeared to be an economic slowdown by setting new records in the stock market. But what's so great about a sluggish economy? And doesn't this mean a recession is just over the horizon?
Perhaps, but not necessarily. In fact, the overwhelming advantage of a slowdown now would be to bolster sharply the odds of sustaining America's four-year-old economic expansion for at least another couple of years or so. "The biggest risk," said Edward Hyman, chief economist of the International Strategy and Investment Group, "is if the economy doesn't slow down." 
 After last year's growth spurt of about 4.5 percent -- the fastest advance in a decade for the American economy -- signs of wear and tear are starting to show up across the country. While both California and the Northeast remain relatively sluggish, labor shortages are developing in scattered locations across the country; borrowing by business and consumers is up sharply; bottlenecks are emerging in several industries, and the prices of some raw materials are soaring.
Another year of such rapid growth would probably cause real strains in the industrial heartland from the Great Lakes to the Gulf Coast.
But there would be risks to any slowdown, too. Whenever growth slows substantially, almost any unexpected shock -- like the 1991 Persian Gulf war -- can be enough to tip a sluggish economy into recession.
Indeed, 7 of the 13 significant slowdowns since the Korean War led directly to recessions, in which economic activity actually declined, according to a report by Sanford C. Bernstein & Company, a New York investment firm. The six other slowdowns, however, were followed by further periods of growth that extended the expansion by a year or more.
It is not yet clear whether the recent signs of sluggishness will turn into the extended slowdown many experts now expect. And if they do, no one knows whether such a slowdown would be followed by a recession or by a resurgence of growth.
But there are sound reasons for the widespread belief on Wall Street that the economic expansion may be headed for a welcome breather rather than its last gasp. For one thing, inflation is still regarded as very well behaved. That means that the Federal Reserve does not need to worry about prices spiraling up and out of control and that it could stop raising interest rates as soon as it is convinced that growth has slowed, allowing the economy to take off again next year.
That is why investors were so relieved -- sending the Dow Jones industrial average to a record 4,011.74 yesterday -- after Alan Greenspan, the Fed's chairman, suggested on Wednesday that the central bank might be near the end of its yearlong series of rate increases and was even prepared to cut rates if necessary.
Without a slowdown, the Fed would be forced to push interest rates up to such painfully high levels that they would almost certainly bring on a recession.
So analysts are hoping that slower growth will buy time for companies to train more workers, smooth out the production pipeline and add the capability to turn out a greater supply of goods and services. The breather, if that is what it turns out to be, should also make it easier for consumers to pay for past purchases and develop an appetite for more.
"That's the long and short of why people are rooting for a slowdown," Mr. Hyman said.
Still, even when slowdowns do not end badly, the experience can be uncomfortable for a lot of people. Growth of 1 or 2 percent, while not as painful as a recessionary decline in economic output, usually leads to at least a slight rise in unemployment. Fewer new jobs are available. Makers of big-ticket items -- homes, cars, personal computers and the like -- often suffer a painful squeeze on profits.
By contrast, last year's boom lifted the spirits of consumers, inspired business with renewed confidence to invest, cut the jobless rate from 6.4 percent to 5.4 percent and put more than three million additional people to work.
Growth by leaps and bounds early in a recovery when unemployment is high and idle resources abound can be glorious for almost everybody. But when it happens well into an economic expansion -- as it did last year -- rapid growth can be too much of a good thing, producing unwelcome side effects.
That is why a successful slowdown -- often referred to on Wall Street as a "soft landing" -- can bestow real benefits by curing many problems created by an overheated economy.
The same strong factory operating rates that mean higher profits for business and the low unemployment that leads to higher incomes for workers can also create shortages of materials and strains on company finances. At worst, they push prices and wages up much faster than productivity is advancing, sowing the seeds of future inflation. Some optimists say they think the economy's capacity to grow has improved substantially in recent years, making it possible to expand over time faster than the 2.5 to 3 percent rate that the White House and most forecasters consider sustainable. But it is not just bond traders and the Federal Reserve's fears of higher inflation that stand in the way of year-in-year-out growth of 4 to 5 percent.
The ability of the economy to churn out goods and services is limited by two unavoidable governors: its labor force and its productivity. America's labor force is growing about 1 percent a year. And the average output of workers is growing a little faster, perhaps by an average of 1.5 percent or slightly more. So once the nation is fully employing most eligible workers and other valuable economic resources, its cruising speed is nowhere near last year's 4.5 percent growth rate.
"You can't grow forever at a rate twice as fast as the potential to produce is expanding," said Joel Prakken, an economist at L. H. Meyer & Associates in St. Louis.
Once the economy slows, however, it is difficult to predict whether the next move will be up or down. In the middle of the 1980's expansion, for example, growth came to a virtual standstill in 1985 and 1986. The Federal Reserve, which raised interest rates from the beginning of 1983 to the middle of 1984, stopped tightening and actually began to ease slightly. The stock market crash notwithstanding, 1987 and 1988 turned out to be boom years.
By contrast, consider how the growth of the late 1970's expansion came to an abrupt end. After a growth spurt in 1979, the Fed tightened credit and the economy slowed sharply. But inflation continued to rise rapidly and the Fed was forced to keep tightening, sending the economy into a deep recession.
Many forecasters are now betting that 1995 is more like 1986 than 1980.
"We can potentially have a situation like the mid-1980's," said Frederic Mishkin, director of research at the Federal Reserve Bank in New York. "If growth slows and we don't get more inflation, the payoff could be a longer expansion." Such an outcome, he added, was "the holy grail of monetary policy."
So is the hoped-for slowdown at hand? Despite Mr. Greenspan's encouraging words, the evidence remains maddeningly ambiguous.
"I happen to think the economy is slowing, and obviously the markets are acting like it's slowing," Mr. Hyman said. "But that view is still largely a leap of faith."

HEADLINE: U.S.-Canada Pact Ends Restrictions on Air Travel

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: OTTAWA, Feb. 24

 BODY:
The United States and Canada, after years of expanding trade, agreed today to open up their skies under a broad aviation agreement that will allow their airlines to fly any routes between the two countries.
The accord, the centerpiece of President Clinton's second and final day in Ottawa, is expected to expand passenger and cargo services significantly, generate even more business between the two countries and lead to lower fares. 
 "The only bad news is for those of you with frequent-flier accounts," the President told a breakfast of business leaders. "It means you'll earn fewer miles because it will be so much easier and quicker to get back and forth."
After many tries, the two countries finally phased out a restrictive agreement that governed air service for nearly three decades. The new pact, believed to be unique in the world, lets market demand, not government regulation, determine the number and destination of flights between the countries.
Normally, governments spend months and years negotiating specific routes on a strictly reciprocal basis. Now this laborious process has been eliminated by Canada and the United States.
Consumers will benefit not only from lower fares but a halving of travel time on many routes.
The accord, which is effective immediately, was signed in the ornate Reading Room of the House of Commons by Transportation Secretary Federico F. Pena and the Canadian Transport Minister, Douglas Young, with Mr. Clinton and Prime Minister Jean Chretien looking on.
The meeting, in which the leaders discussed global economic and political issues as well as a host of bilateral issues, went unusually smoothly, aides to both said.
This was Mr. Clinton's first state visit to Canada, and Mr. Chretien has not yet been to Washington. The coolness of the relationship to date largely reflected Mr. Chretien's desire to distance himself from his predecessor, Brian Mulroney, who prided himself on his closeness to President Reagan and President Bush. But it also reflected Mr. Clinton's preoccupation with his own domestic agenda.
Mr. Chretien jokingly conceded in a joint news conference that he is now on a first name basis with the President. "When we're alone, I don't call him William J.," the Prime Minister said. "I call him Bill."
Mr. Clinton said he would be "honored" to put bait on Mr. Chretien's fishing pole, a reference to Mr. Chretien's often-quoted comment that he never liked to go fishing with American Presidents, as Mr. Mulroney had done frequently, because "you end up looking like the fish."
The two leaders expressed particular satisfaction with their open skies agreement, which covers the largest bilateral aviation market in the world. Despite the constraints of the old agreement, 13 million people traveled across the border on commercial flights in 1993.
The new accord gives Canada's airlines immediate access to all points in the United States. American carriers, which now control 70 percent of the cross-border market, would gain similar access after a three-year phase-in.
Most Canadian traffic is at three hubs -- Toronto, Montreal and Vancouver. Because more American carriers want to serve the three hubs than the phase-in rules allow, the Department of Transportation must allocate flights.
Under the phase-in, the United States can add two airlines at Toronto in each of the next two years and four more in the third year; at Vancouver and Montreal, it can add six airlines in each of the next two years. Fourteen United States airlines have already applied for new routes to Canada.
Air Canada, the country's largest airline, announced it was planning 20 new routes between Canadian and United States cities in the next 18 months. It also said it would begin new daily flights between Ottawa and Washington, Montreal and Washington, and Toronto and Atlanta within 60 days, and it plans to expand its Toronto-New York service into an hourly shuttle.
Usually a route-allocation process starts only after an air agreement is signed. This time it began about six weeks ago and, according to Richard I. Mintz, public affairs director at the Transportation Department, the first route allocations will be announced on Monday. The reason for the speedup is to prevent Canadian carriers from getting a competitive head start.
"Aviation's Berlin Wall" is being removed, said a group known as United States Airports for Better International Air Service (USA-BIAS), representing 32 communities throughout the United States that operate some of the largest public airports in the world. It predicted that the new flights would stimulate more than $15 billion annually in new tourism, trade and other economic activity on both side of the border and create 290,000 jobs.
"There's going to be such a dramatic change that people haven't absorbed it yet," said James A. Wilding, chief executive of the Metropolitan Washington Airports Authority.
The two leaders also announced a joint border management agreement to speed frequent travelers through formalities, streamline the processing of commercial goods and free resources for use against smuggling of guns, drugs and other contraband. The accord, details of which are still to be worked out, envisages use of magnetic cards by frequent border crossers to avoid Customs delays.
"The bottom line is that Canadian and American taxpayers will be getting better value for their tax dollars," said Canada's National Revenue Minister, David Anderson.
Despite the harmony in transportation and border issues, a number of bilateral trade irritants still cloud the relationship, and apparently little progress was made in resolving them.
That the two leaders brought their chief trade negotiators into the summit talks -- Mickey Kantor for the United States and Roy MacLaren for Canada -- was an indication of the importance the Governments attach to resolving the disputes.
Already the United States has drawn up a list of potential retaliation targets to counter Canadian cultural protectionism, one of the most explosive areas of disagreement.
Rising Canadian trade surpluses, on top of a 20 percent depreciation of the Canadian dollar over the past four years, has raised American political sensitivity about Canadian market penetration.
The Canadian dollar's depreciation, taken in conjunction with the nearly 40 percent drop in the value of the peso over the past two months, which is likely to drastically cut United States exports to Mexico, has prompted some American trade groups to complain that the United States is emerging as the big loser in the year-old North American Free Trade Agreement.
A disagreement over country music has the highest political visibility.
The United States accuses Canada of "confiscatory" action in its regulatory decision last June to remove Country Music Television of Nashville from Canadian cable systems in favor of a new Canadian country music channel.
Other telecommunications issues include a Canadian proposal to tax audio and video cassettes to raise revenue for royalties to performers when their work is played in Canada. But the plan would deny American performers their share of the fees collected.
Another fight is over an 80 percent tax Canada wants to impose on Canadian editions of American publications like as Sports Illustrated that are designed in the United States and then beamed electronically to a Canadian printer.
Canada, in turn, is riled at the United States for imposing high tariffs on sugar and peanut butter and complains about Americans overfishing Pacific salmon.

HEADLINE: U.S. Treasuries Are Mixed On Latest Economic Data

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities were mixed yesterday after a Government report showed surprising signs of strong economic growth.
The report, on factory orders for durable goods, which represent products ranging from personal computers to aircraft, showed a third consecutive monthly gain. The data suggested that the economy might not be slowing at a pace analysts and traders had expected. But the rise in orders for durable goods was at a somewhat slower pace than the gains in November and December. And the durable goods report tends to be volatile, sometimes sending contradictory signals about the direction of the economy. 
 The market will be paying greater attention next week to the revision of the gross domestic product for the fourth quarter and to February sales reports from auto makers.
Yesterday, the price of the 30-year bond rose 6/32 of a percentage point, to 1015/32 . Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction from its price, fell to 7.52 percent, from 7.54 percent on Thursday. But rates on shorter- maturity issues were higher. Traders said the short end of the market was due for some correction because it had been pricing in unrealistic expectations of Federal Reserve policy.
Eugene J. Sherman, director of research for M. A. Schapiro & Company, likened the situation in the fixed-income market to the experienced hiker who has come to the realization that he has been following a false trail. "The market has rallied since the fourth week in January on the expectation that the economy is slowing and that the Federal Reserve is finished, or only one step away from ending, its tightening," he said.
In the municipal market, bonds were little changed amid expectations that demand for tax-exempt income and a decline in sales of new issues will continue to bolster prices. New bond sales are down about 50 percent.
Market participants are looking ahead to next week's sale of $ 500 million in New York State general obligation bonds, which are part of an advanced refinancing on existing debt. In addition, $ 150 million will be used for new projects. The price of the bonds will be negotiated through Lehman Brothers.

HEADLINE: 2 Key Democrats Voice Doubt on Budget Amendment

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 24

 BODY:
The campaign to add a balanced-budget amendment to the Constitution, a bare vote or two short of the 67 it needs to win in the Senate on Tuesday, seemed to stumble today on the concerns of two pivotal Democrats that the measure would shift power over Federal spending to the courts.
With five Democrats still undecided, at least publicly, the battle over the amendment is far from over. But the proposal's prospects clearly were not helped when two who have been regarded as among the most likely recruits to its cause -- Senators Sam Nunn of Georgia and Wendell H. Ford of Kentucky -- expressed deep reservations about it in speeches and interviews. 
 Mr. Nunn followed up on an earlier veiled threat to oppose the amendment by stating, in a speech on the Senate floor on Thursday night, that he would vote against it unless it was changed to prohibit the Federal courts from ruling on its meaning. To do otherwise, he said, would give the judiciary a veto over taxing and spending decisions that the Constitution vests solely in the legislative branch.
In an interview today, Mr. Ford, the Senate's second-ranking Democrat, said he shared Mr. Nunn's fears.
"I think Nunn kind of threw down the gauntlet," he said. "Judicial authority has a lot of us really concerned." And unless the amendment is altered to address those concerns, he said, "I think some of us are going to have a hard time voting for it."
Mr. Ford said he had other reservations about the measure as well, including the lack of a clause that would prevent Congress from using taxpayers' Social Security contributions to offset deficit spending. He nevertheless said he had not decided how he would vote.
The White House, sensing new weakness in a proposal that had once seemed a sure thing, roused its own slumbering lobbying campaign and began making a serious effort to defeat it.
In Ottawa, President Clinton attacked the amendment, partly on the ground that it would make deficit spending to prop up any future recession-battered economy more difficult. The amendment would prohibit deficit spending without the approval of three-fifths of each house of Congress, as against the simple majority now required.
The Administration's top economic officials pressed that point in Washington, holding a news conference, calling senators and issuing a white paper claiming to show how the 1990 recession would have been all the worse had deficit spending been barred. The state-by-state breakdown estimated that unemployment at the recession's peak, actually 7.7 percent, would instead have been between 8.3 percent and 9.4 percent, with an extra 750,000 to 2.3 million people out of work.
The chairwoman of the National Economic Council, Laura D'Andrea Tyson, suggested that backers of the amendment would walk the same high wire from which President Herbert Hoover slipped in 1930, when he refused to prime the economic pump after the stock market had crashed.
"He wanted to have a balanced budget," she said, "and all of the studies suggest that his attempts to have a balanced budget made the Depression considerably worse. We should not go back and undo a set of policies that work well."
Mr. Clinton has been telephoning wavering Democrats, but his impact is not clear. Senator Byron L. Dorgan of North Dakota, a Democrat said to be leaning toward support for the amendment, was told today to expect a call from the President. Asked whether that would sway the Senator, his spokesman, Barry Piatt, replied, "No."
"He's always glad to listen," Mr. Piatt said, "but this is not an issue he's going to decide on the basis of who exerts the most pressure."
In an interview, the chief Republican sponsor of the amendment, Senator Orrin G. Hatch of Utah, said that his side was one vote short of the 67 it needs on Tuesday and that Mr. Nunn's support was now "crucial." He plans to give Mr. Nunn a pledge, signed by the Senate majority leader, Bob Dole, and House Speaker Newt Gingrich, to address the judicial-control concerns in legislation that would implement the budget amendment.
Mr. Nunn was in Haiti and could not be reached. But his spokeswoman, Cathy O'Brien, said the Senator would not settle for less than a guarantee that was written into the Constitution.
Told of that, Mr. Hatch said he did not intend to change the amendment, partly out of fear that doing so would erode support in the House. Then he hedged, ever so slightly.
"As someone who through the years has painted himself into corners," he said, "I've learned not to do that."

HEADLINE: White House Signals an Easing on Affirmative Action

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 24

 BODY:
The White House is undertaking a top-to-bottom review of Federal affirmative action programs, scrambling to maintain its footing against Republican attacks on preferential treatment of women and minorities.
"What we need to guarantee is genuine equality of opportunity," President Clinton said today in acknowledging the review at a news conference in Ottawa, after meeting with Canadian officials. "That's what the affirmative action concept is designed to do, and I'm convinced that most Americans want us to continue to do that in the appropriate way." 
 But he added: "We shouldn't be defending things that we can't defend. So it's time to review it, discuss it, and be straightforward about it."
Mr. Clinton's announcement of the review marks the first signal that the White House intends to move away from the staunch defense of all affirmative action programs, a position that has been part of the Democratic Party's basic doctrine for more than two decades.
The Administration's review comes as Republicans are attacking such programs, charging that they amount to "reverse discrimination." Some Republican strategists are viewing the issue as a way to attract more of the white male voters who favored Republicans in the last election, especially those groups whose incomes have been stagnant in the last few years.
The issue has sowed jitters among Democrats who fear the Republican strategy may have some success, as well as among black organizations and liberal women's groups, who make up a significant part of the Democrats' base and who are concerned that ending or modifying affirmative action will halt economic gains they made in the 1970's and 1980's.
While stressing that President Clinton "stands firmly behind the principle of equal opportunity and civil rights," a senior Clinton adviser said that the Administration intended to pore over Federal affirmative action programs to see which ones might be outmoded or unfair.
White House officials said they did not know when the review of Federal programs would be completed. A recent listing of such efforts produced by the Congressional Research Service indicates that virtually every Federal agency, from the Department of Agriculture to the Department of Veterans Affairs, runs programs that give special aid to minorities and women.
Often these efforts are designed to encourage the use of minority- or women-owned banks for deposits from Federal or state agencies administering a given program, like the Supplemental Nutrition Program for Women, Infants and Children. Federal agencies are often encouraged to direct a portion of research grants to historically black colleges and universities.
Generally, the Federal laws and regulations express the desire that agencies meet undefined goals when it comes to aiding minorities or women. But sometimes these goals are expressed as specific numerical targets, prompting charges that they really amount to quotas.
For example, the Defense Department has a set goal of awarding 5 percent of the total value of its procurement, research, construction, operation and maintenance contracts to "socially and economically disadvantaged individuals."
There are no Federal laws requiring private companies to adopt affirmative action plans. But Federal judges have at times ordered companies that have been found guilty of racial or sexual discrimination to adopt them. Indeed, when the discrimination has been particularly egregious, courts have required companies to impose rigid hiring or promotion quotas to increase the number of minority or female employees or supervisors.
The Federal Government's most powerful tool to encourage private companies to hire women and members of minority groups is the requirement that any company with a Federal contract worth more than $50,000 make a "good faith effort" to meet various "goals and timetables" when it comes to the hiring of minorities or women.
While some Republicans are ebullient about the Democrats' discomfort, others worry that the issue could heighten racial divisions, open the party to charges of racism and anger moderate women, many of whom are already concerned over the Republican Party's drift to the right on abortion.
A Washington lawyer who has advised Republican lawmakers on the issue and who spoke on the condition of anonymity said: "A lot of them really don't have the stomach to take this stuff on. It will be incredibly divisive and they're worried about angering women."
Still, both sides acknowledge that the locus of the debate over affirmative action has shifted to the right. In addition to Mr. Clinton, several other leading Democrats have said that after three decades, the policy ought to be reviewed.
"It's head-spinning," said Clint Bolick, a persistent critic of race- and gender-based preference programs who is vice president of the Institute for Justice, a conservative public-policy research group. "The center of the debate had not shifted for 30 years, and now it has moved dramatically."
