HEADLINE: France Accuses U.S. of Leaking Report on American Spies

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PARIS, Feb. 24

 BODY:
The Interior Minister denied today that he was responsible for the disclosure of allegations that American diplomats had spied on the Government, and he blamed the United States Embassy and the Ambassador instead.
The assertion by the minister, Charles Pasqua, reported in Le Monde, seemed to startle the Ambassador, Pamela Harriman, and the embassy issued a withering statement rejecting the charge as "neither true nor credible." The statement also scolded Mr. Pasqua for violating diplomatic confidentiality. 
 The scandal blew up Wednesday when a French demand for the departure of the former station chief for the Central Intelligence Agency and four alleged subordinates was reported by Le Monde. American officials had hoped the affair would quickly cool off, but the statement by Mr. Pasqua and the embassy's response fanned the embers.
Mr. Pasqua, who is traveling in Africa, was interviewed by Le Monde on Thursday during a stopover in Dakar, Senegal. The affair is "serious, very serious," he told the newspaper, calling upon the United States to recall the Americans as soon as possible and denying that he had been responsible for making the affair public.
"I also asked the Ambassador to keep it confidential," Mr. Pasqua said. But, he added, she told him she "could not do it because many agencies were aware of it."
"There have been lots of leaks on the American side," he told Le Monde, which has published details and extensive excerpts of a French counterintelligence report on the alleged political and economic espionage.
That was too much for the embassy, where officials said they had not even seen the counterintelligence report, much less made it public.
"We regret very much a number of the statements made by Minister Pasqua in Dakar," the embassy statement said. "In particular, we categorically reject the allegation that we are responsible for the detailed and totally regrettable press leaks that were published in a number of French newspapers, including Le Monde. This charge is neither true nor credible.
"We also regret his inaccurate and incomplete account of his conversations with Mrs. Harriman. In any event, such exchanges by definition should have been protected by the rule of diplomatic confidentiality."
Mr. Pasqua's first conversation with Mrs. Harriman about the affair is reported to have taken place on Jan. 26, when he is said to have told her he was confident that close allies like the United States and France could quietly work things out.
Instead, after several more exchanges, the details appeared on the front page of Le Monde.
Most French commentators and politicians explain it as an attempt to divert attention from a scandal over illegal wiretapping sanctioned by Mr. Pasqua, an affair that has hurt Prime Minister Edouard Balladur's campaign to succeed President Francois Mitterrand in elections this spring.
Mr. Balladur was clearly the front-runner a month ago, but his poll ratings have fallen drastically since the disclosure that he let Mr. Pasqua talk him into authorizing a telephone tap last December of the father-in-law of a judge investigating allegations of Government corruption. A court later ordered the transcripts destroyed because the authorization had been illegal.
Mr. Balladur was defending his decision as late as last weekend, but pinned responsibility for it on Mr. Pasqua and saw his poll ratings fall even further.
Because America-bashing has been often been a political weapon for conservative French politicians in a pinch, many politicians and commentators have concluded that Mr. Pasqua must have leaked the spying charges to divert attention from the scandal.
Foreign Minister Alain Juppe, who backs Jacques Chirac, another conservative, for the presidency, said on Thursday that he was "scandalized" that the affair had been made public and called for an investigation to determine who leaked it. Mr. Chirac's chances have steadily improved as Mr. Balladur's have diminished, and the latest polls put both conservatives in a dead heat with Lionel Jospin, a Socialist, in the first round of balloting on April 23.
But Mr. Juppe also said the accused Americans should leave soon.
The State Department said Thursday that the United States had no intention of reassigning any officials before their tours were up. One of the accused finished a tour of duty at the embassy and left France before the scandal became public.
The former C.I.A. station chief and one of the other two accused embassy staff members are understood to be close to the end of their tours anyway. A fifth American is a civilian without diplomatic status whose present whereabouts are unclear.

HEADLINE: Continuing Its Advance, Dow Closes at 4,011.74

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Stocks reached record highs yesterday for a second consecutive session, led by rallies in food and beverage issues.
The Dow Jones industrial average again closed above 4,000 points, rising 8.41, to 4,011.74. For the week, the Dow gained 1.5 percent. 
 "We're feeling pretty good about things right now," said Doug Person, vice president at Bridges Investment Counsel, which manages $600 million. "Rates are stable and possibly trending lower, and it looks like the economy is slowing but not so much that we're looking at a recession."
Stocks stumbled early in the day after the Government reported that orders for durable goods rose six-tenths of 1 percent in January. The data suggested the economy might not be slowing as much as expected.
But the report failed to derail a rally that began picking up steam on Wednesday, when Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve, implied that the Fed's policy of raising short-term interest rates might be coming to an end.
An easing in monetary policy would lower corporate borrowing costs and improve profits, thus brightening the outlook for stocks. Long-term interest rates fell yesterday, with the yield on the 30-year Treasury bond slipping to 7.52 percent from 7.54 percent on Thursday.
Food and beverage shares rose yesterday as the outlook for moderate growth in the economy improved the attraction of companies that sell consumer staples like soap, soda and corn flakes. Such so-called defensive issues report stable earnings through economic booms and busts.
"If you buy into the theory that the economy is going to slow down, you want to be in those names," said Todd Clark, a senior trader at Mabon Securities Corporation.
Shares of the Coca-Cola Company rose 3/4, to 54 3/4, while those of Pepsico added 3/8, to 39 5/8. General Mills gained 1 1/8, to 61 3/8; Ralston Purina climbed 1 3/8, to 48, and the Kellogg Company rose 3/8, to 54 1/2.
Among broader market indexes, the Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks rose 1.20 points yesterday, to a record 488.11, its fourth consecutive gain. The Nasdaq composite index fell 0.27 point, to 791.08, pulled down by losses in shares of the Oracle Corporation, the Microsoft Corporation, Cisco Systems and Apple Computer.
On the New York Stock Exchange, volume was about 303 million shares, less than the three-month daily average of 316 million shares. Declining stocks outpaced advancing ones, 1,117 to 1,040.
Tobacco stocks rose a second day, after a trial on the addictiveness of cigarettes ended with a mistrial in Indiana on Tuesday. The case was the first in which a jury in a tobacco lawsuit considered evidence of tobacco's alleged addictiveness.
Shares of the Philip Morris Companies rose 1 1/4, to 60 3/4, after adding 1 1/4 on Thursday. American Brands gained 1/2, to 37 3/8, and Brooke Group Ltd. rose 1/8, to 3 1/2.
Among initial public offerings, Speedway Motorsports Inc., which owns the Charlotte Motor Speedway and Atlanta Motor Speedway, rose 1 from its pricing at 18.
Earlier in Tokyo, stocks fell sharply. The Nikkei index of 225 issues plunged 357.08 points, or 2 percent, to 17,472.94, its lowest close since Jan. 4, 1994.

HEADLINE: Officials Assess Local Impact if Military Operations Are Pared

BYLINE: By LAWRENCE VAN GELDER 

 BODY:
Dismay, despair and defiance were mingled yesterday in reaction to the prospect of community upheaval and millions of dollars of economic losses growing out of plans to close or curtail operations at military installations in New York, New Jersey and Connecticut.
Said to be on the Pentagon's chopping block were familiar names like the New London submarine base in Connecticut; the Lakehurst Naval Air Engineering Station in New Jersey, where the German dirigible Hindenburg burned in 1937; Fort Hamilton in Brooklyn, and the Rome Laboratory in Rome, N.Y., the sole active element of Griffiss Air Force Base, which was a victim of a round of base closings two years ago. 
 "I think this is a probably a very enormous mistake that the Defense Department is making, and I believe they will regret it," said New York State Assemblywoman RoAnn Destito, a Democrat whose district covers the Rome-Utica area. "It is a very integral part of not only the Department of Defense but of New York State's high-technology infrastructure."
Mrs. Destito, a member of the Legislative Commission on Science and Technology and the Economic Development Standing Committee, said a closing would affect not only 850 employees at Rome Laboratory but another 2,000 to 2,500 people through a ripple effect.
"Rome Labs has over $150 million in contracts let out in New York State," Mrs. Destito said, estimating that the economic impact of a shutdown at the laboratory would amount to $300 million spread over three or four upstate counties with a total population of 800,000.
She asserted, too, that a closing would affect corporations like Eastman Kodak, GTE, Boeing and Nynex that have local ties to the lab, as well as institutions like Syracuse University, Cornell, the Rochester Institute of Technology and Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute, which have educational alliances with the facility.
In New Jersey, Arthur Lindberg, a Navy veteran who heads a group called Save Lakehurst, applied the word "devastating" to the prospect of the relocation of equipment and loss of engineering talent that might accompany curtailment of operations at the Naval Air Engineering Station.
Situated on 7,400-acres in the Pinelands of Ocean County, the station is one of eight military bases in the state. With about 600 military and 2,700 civilian employees, Mr. Lindberg said, it pumps an estimated $75 million annually into the local economy.
The base is a research and development center for the catapult and recovery systems that are used on carriers to launch and land aircraft, and the Save Lakehurst committee argues that the base is the only place in the country where such work is carried out.
Representative Christopher H. Smith, a Republican who represents Ocean County and is spearheading the effort to keep the station open, said, "If the United States wants to have an aircraft carrier force, Lakehurst has to be sacrosanct."
The Navy's proposal to move the training school from its base at New London to Charleston, S.C., would cost 500 jobs in Connecticut, according to William D. Moore, the president of the Chamber of Commerce of Southeastern Connecticut.
He said, the loss of the school, with not only its instructors but also 3,000 students, "is going to be significant in terms of lost payroll for the community."
"It will also be significant for the loss of 500 people making permanent homes here," Mr. Moore said.
But Mr. Moore refused to abandon hope. "Simply appearing on the list of bases does not mean it will happen," he said. "We're not giving up, by any stretch of the imagination."
In 1993, New London officials, arguing that the submarine base should not be separated from the training school, which was too expensive to relocate, successfully contested the Navy's proposal to move the submarine base to Georgia. But the Charleston Naval Shipyard and Naval Station, with more than 26,000 military and civilian jobs, was ordered closed.
Mr. Moore said: "The question now becomes: If it's going to take a large expense to move to Charleston, where you have to reopen part of that base and no longer have the savings from 1993, what has changed?"
Fort Hamilton, where the 77th Army Reserve Command has headquarters, narrowly escaped the Pentagon's closing list for 1991. Yesterday, Greg Brooks, executive assistant to Howard Golden, the Brooklyn Borough President, said Mr. Golden had expressed concern over reports of an impending shutdown and was seeking confirmation that the fort was on the list.

HEADLINE: On a Familiar Texas Campus, Gramm Makes It Official

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: COLLEGE STATION, Tex., Feb. 24

 BODY:
Senator Phil Gramm formally declared his candidacy for the Republican Presidential nomination today, presenting himself as an unwavering conservative with the record and the grit to carry forward a rightward revolution that began last November.
Returning here to Texas A & M University, where he was an economics professor for 12 years, Mr. Gramm stood on the steps of the limestone administration building and delivered a message that was plainly spoken and razor-sharp. 
 "We need a leader that has the courage to tell our people the truth," he told an ebullient crowd of thousands that was stocked with placard-waving students swaying to the Aggie Band. "We need a leader who has the vision to define solutions to our problems, solutions that people can understand and can believe in. And we need a leader who is tough enough to get the job done. In the next 20 months, I hope to convince the American people that I am that leader."
The morning sun re-emerged from clouds, and the two-term Senator from Texas made it official: "With a love for America and a resolve to make her right again, I today declare myself a candidate for President of the United States."
Of nine or so Republicans who are already running for the Presidential nomination, the 52-year-old Mr. Gramm is the first to declare his candidacy formally. He has set the early pace of the campaign by building the largest war chest, including $4.1 million -- a record for a single event -- that he reports raising at a dinner in Dallas on Thursday night. And he has won nonbinding straw polls among active party members in a handful of states, a result that speaks to the merits of his early organization.
But half the voters do not yet have an opinion of him, a New York Times/CBS News Poll shows, and among those who do, the sentiment is mixed.
The poll, conducted by telephone among 586 Americans on Wednesday and Thursday, found that 14 percent viewed him favorably, 13 percent viewed him not favorably, 22 percent were undecided, and 51 percent had not heard enough about him to form an opinion.
Among Republicans, the balance of opinion is more positive, although he is still largely unknown. The poll found that 23 percent of Republicans viewed him favorably, 13 percent viewed him not favorably, 19 percent were undecided, and 46 percent had not heard enough.
The poll's margin of sampling error was plus or minus four percentage points.
Here today in College Station, about 60 miles northwest of Houston, Mr. Gramm reminded his audience of his best-known legislative achievement: his co-authorship of the Gramm-Rudman-Hollings budget-balancing law of the 1980's, which was ultimately undone by subsequent legislation. Asserting that no goal was more critical than eliminating the Federal budget deficit, he repeated an earlier pledge that he would not seek re-election as President if he failed to do so.
He did not outline specific budget cuts, saying only that there would have to be "hard choices" but telling reporters later that he would not tamper with Social Security.
Seeking to distinguish himself as the only genuine conservative in the field, Mr. Gramm gleefully recalled that he had stood alone "in the darkest hour" of the health care debate when he opposed President Clinton's proposed overhaul. "When pollsters were saying it was political suicide to take on the Clinton health care bill head on, when 20 Republican senators had signed on to a big-government compromise that raised taxes," he declared, "I stood up and said, 'The Clinton health care bill is going to pass over my cold, dead political body.' "
After pausing for the hoots and hollers, he added: "I am happy today to say that my political body is alive. The President's health care bill is deader than Elvis. And Elvis may be back, but the President's health care bill will not be back."
The Senator, whose last major speech before a national audience was a poorly received keynote address at the 1992 Republican National Convention, in Houston, took the opportunity today to stake out hard-line positions on a number of issues:
*He pledged to dismantle the welfare system, saying, "I will ask able-bodied men and women riding in the wagon on welfare to get out of the wagon and help the rest of us pull."
*He said he would "stop building prisons like Holiday Inns" and press for vigorous application of the death penalty. "If I have to string barbed wire on every closed military base in America," he said of criminals, "I am going to put these people in jail."
*He vowed to sign an executive order ending quotas, preferences and set-asides for members of minority groups. "I will fight for equal and unlimited opportunity for every American," he said, "but there will be special privilege for no one."
*And he called for a strengthening of the American military.
The event here was marked by military symbolism that posed some unstated but unmistakable incongruities on a day seized by a candidate whose draft deferments kept him out of military service during the Vietnam War. One speaker was his close friend and campaign chairman, Senator John McCain of Arizona, who was a prisoner of war in Vietnam. Members of the A & M Corps of Cadets, dressed in crisp white uniforms, formed a backdrop for the news photographers. Mr. Gramm arrived with his wife, Wendy Lee, and their two college-age sons by walking through the cadets' raised sabres.
And at the end of his speech, there was even the roar of a cannon.

HEADLINE: Orders Rose For Durables Last Month

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
In a report that analysts said raised new questions about whether the economy was slowing as the Federal Reserve's chairman, Alan Greenspan, had suggested, the Government said yesterday that factory orders for durable goods rose for the third consecutive month in January.
 According to data compiled by the Commerce Department, orders for durable goods increased six-tenths of 1 percent last month, led by stronger demand for industrial machinery and equipment. 
 The rise was at a somewhat slower pace than the gains in November and December. But analysts said the overall gain in January masked underlying strength. They added that the data did little to support Mr. Greenspan's statement, made to Congress earlier this week, that the pace of economic activity appeared to be losing steam.
 "This is the first major report since Mr. Greenspan's testimony, and it fails to corroborate his suspicion that the economy is slowing," said Paul D. Mastroddi, a managing director at J. P. Morgan & Company. "One of the main messages of the report is that business investment, one of the main sources of growth over the last year, started off 1995 on a strong note."
 David A. Wyss, chief financial economist at DRI/McGraw Hill, said the report ran "against the flow of the other data that is coming in, most of which shows slower growth."
 He was referring to softer retail sales figures, the weak January employment data and lower car sales. The housing market has also slowed recently.
 The durable goods report is among the most volatile the Government produces and by itself cannot be counted on to give a reliable picture of the economy. Still, the report put a damper on the bond market, which had been cheered by Mr. Greenspan's remarks. Bond prices were mixed yesterday, after posting gains in the three previous sessions.
 Mr. Mastroddi said that orders for nonmilitary capital goods rose 8.6 percent in January and were up 5.8 percent when the volatile aircraft component was excluded.
 "This tells me the business investment boom is continuing," he said.
 The gain was paced by orders for industrial machinery and equipment, which jumped by 7.2 percent, the first rise for this segment in three months.
 As Mr. Mastroddi suggested, the overall gain was held back by the transportation equipment category, where orders fell 5 percent. In addition to a decline in aircraft orders, demand for automobiles, ships and trains all declined. Excluding transportation, orders for durable goods increased 2.5 percent last month, after rising four-tenths of 1 percent in December.
 "At this stage of the cycle, we think most of the upward pressure is going to come from the industrial side of the economy," Joseph Liro, chief economist at S. G. Warburg & Company, said. "These durable goods numbers add weight to that view."

HEADLINE: G.O.P. Leaders Reject a Repeal Of Food Stamps

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 24

 BODY:
House Republican leaders said today that they had abandoned plans to abolish the Federal food stamp program and shift the responsibility to the states after senior Republican lawmakers from farm states rebelled.
The decision marks the first significant setback for the House Republican campaign to pare social welfare programs established by the Federal Government over the last 60 years. 
 The lawmakers from farm states demanded that the Government retain the food stamp program as the ultimate national safety net for poor people.
"The leadership has decided to keep the food stamp program at the Federal level," said Representative Pat Roberts of Kansas, chairman of the Agriculture Committee. "That's the best way to achieve our goals of providing nutrition for the hungry and eliminating fraud from the program."
The decision to preserve food stamps came a day after a House committee voted to scrap several other types of Federal food assistance, including the school lunch program, and turn the money over to the states.
If the states take greater responsibility for social welfare programs as Republicans want, there must be "a Federal social safety net to backstop that process," Mr. Roberts said.
The number of people getting food stamps soared to 27.5 million last year, from 20 million in 1990. More than half of the people getting assistance are children. The program cost $25 billion last year, up from $15.5 billion in 1990.
The Republican rebellion was led by Representatives Bill Emerson of Missouri and Steve Gunderson of Wisconsin, along with Mr. Roberts. But almost all the other Republican members of the Agriculture Committee also balked at the demand to replace the food stamp program with lump sum payments to the states.
House Republicans said that the party's floor leader, Representative Dick Armey of Texas, had insisted on such a block grant program, but that Speaker Newt Gingrich was more flexible and pragmatic.
Leon E. Panetta, the White House chief of staff, welcomed the decision to keep food stamps as a Federal program. But he said, "The same logic should apply to other nutrition programs that the Republicans are seeking to cut like school lunch, school breakfast and the Supplemental Nutrition Program for Women, Infants and Children."
The original version of the Republican Contract With America would have repealed the Food Stamp Act of 1977 and consolidated all nutrition programs in a block grant to the states. But most members of the Agriculture Committee come from agricultural districts and objected to the proposal as they studied its details.
Mr. Emerson said, "We are already delegating power to the states in other areas of welfare reform," with block grants for cash assistance and child nutrition programs, including the school lunch program. Even without food stamps, he said, the states were getting "a full plate" of new responsibilities. Mr. Emerson is chairman of the Agriculture subcommittee on nutrition.
In a separate action, the National Governors' Association today expressed concern about a Republican proposal to shift the Federal Government's main cash assistance program, Aid to Families With Dependent Children, to the states with a lump sum of Federal money. Leaders of the association -- three Republicans and three Democrats -- said in a letter to Congress that the welfare block grant, as now designed, would not adequately meet states' needs in times of recession or natural disaster.
Moreover, the governors objected to Republican plans to deny cash assistance to legal immigrants, and they said that other provisions of the bill improperly "limit state flexibility or shift Federal costs to the states."
On Thursday, the House Committee on Economic and Educational Opportunities voted to scrap the school lunch program and let states decide how to use Federal money to feed schoolchildren.
Some governors also wanted to replace food stamps with a block grant and substitute cash payments for food stamp coupons. But House Republicans rejected that idea, in part because they feared that it would increase fraud and abuse.
Mr. Gunderson, who represents many dairy producers, said, "We're not going to convert this to a cash program."
In an interview, Mr. Roberts, whose constituents produce huge amounts of wheat and feed grains, said: "Most governors would have preferred cash, but that's not a feasible answer. That's not going to happen in the food stamp program."
If cash replaced coupons, he said, there would be no guarantee that the money would be used to buy commodities or to combat hunger.
Under the new Republican proposal, food stamps will remain an entitlement program, available to anyone who meets eligibility criteria set by Federal laws and regulations. Congress would set an annual limit on spending for food stamps. Such a ceiling existed before 1990. In theory, benefits could have been reduced if the money ran out. But in practice, whenever costs threatened to exceed the limit, Congress appropriated more money.
Even though the food stamp program will remain at the Federal level, Republicans say they are determined to slow its growth. The Congressional Budget Office estimates that under current law the Government will spend a total of $150 billion on the program in the next five years. Republicans on the House Budget Committee say they want to trim this by $14 billion, or 9 percent. The savings could be achieved by restricting eligibility or by reducing the monthly allotment.
Agriculture Department officials say that overpayments to food stamp recipients cost the Government $1.8 billion a year. The overpayments result from errors by caseworkers or misstatements by applicants. Mr. Roberts said the Republican welfare bill would address these problems.

HEADLINE: A Stealth Airport for New York;
Lightly-Used Stewart Is New York's Stealth Jetport

BYLINE: By JOSEPH BERGER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: NEW WINDSOR, N.Y.

 BODY:
Once, Stewart International Airport was going to be New York City's fourth jetport, a gateway to the rest of America that would outstrip Kennedy International Airport in size and passenger volume and transform the rustic Hudson Valley into an economic beehive.
 Today, two decades after government planners announced that soaring vision and five years after passenger service began, even its ardent champions say Stewart is just a nice country airport. 
 To be sure, its 9,600 acres of rolling woods make it bigger than any American airport save those in Dallas-Fort Worth and Denver. But its one terminal is about the size of an elementary school auditorium. It is international only because it has one weekly flight to Cancun, Mexico, during the winter season. And rain or shine, travelers still have to walk across the tarmac to their airplanes.
 The lavish dreams -- and officials at Stewart now laugh at how grandiose those plans were -- evaporated with the end of the big-project era of Gov. Nelson A. Rockefeller, the fuel crisis of the 1970's and the introduction of jumbo jets. The wide-bodied jets cut into congestion at New York City's three airports and helped eliminate the need for a fourth 65 miles from Manhattan.
 Yet, to the almost 400,000 passengers who use it yearly, Stewart is one of the state's best-guarded secrets. Its direct flights to major airline hubs -- three daily flights to Chicago, three to Pittsburgh, four to Washington, two to Atlanta, one to Raleigh-Durham, N.C., and one to Orlando, Fla. -- connect travelers to practically any American city. And its terminal, small as it is, provides just about any service found at Stewart's grander cousins: a newsstand and gift shop, a cash machine, a cafe, three rental car companies, even a duty-free shop.
 The 30 departures a day are on schedule 91 percent of the time, one of the nation's best records. (Newark by comparison has a 75 percent on-time record.) The airport is less than an hour from the Tappan Zee Bridge and close to two highways, Interstate 84 and the Gov. Thomas E. Dewey Thruway. There are no traffic jams -- and accompanying nervous fits -- on the scenic roads in, and passengers can park right outside the terminal for $8 a day.
 "It's small and that makes it easier," said Maryann Ahearn, a nursery school teacher who lives in the Catskills village of Woodridge, N.Y. "It's not overwhelming."
 "It's easier to find people," her 7-year-old son, Tom, said of the relatives who visit.
 Joseph Capone, a 43-year-old telephone company technician who lives in nearby Salisbury Mills, said that when he returns from a business trip, he calls his wife from a pay telephone and "by the time I get my bags, my wife is waiting for me outside." He was waiting for a flight to Orlando to play golf.
 "If I go to La Guardia I'm leaving three hours before the flight," he said.
 Still, after a wildly successful first year in 1990-91, Stewart stopped growing. Officials of the State Transportation Department, which owns Stewart and pays private contractors to run it, say they believe they have attracted most of the potential passengers in the six counties of the mid-Hudson region.
 Now, they say, they have to work harder through travel agents and newspaper advertisements to lure people who live or work farther away -- in northern Westchester County, western Connecticut, eastern Pennsylvania and northern New Jersey.
 "It's tough to change habits," said Russell B. Vachon, the Transportation Department's director of aviation. "People around Westchester are convinced that it's easier to go south than to go north."
Mr. Vachon, 49, has been Stewart's guardian angel for two decades. He was a consultant to the former owner, the Metropolitan Transportation Authority, which acquired the deactivated Stewart Air Force Base in 1970 and seized dozens of surrounding farms for more acreage. He watched skeptically as the M.T.A. studied such quixotic fourth-airport schemes as a high-speed train from Pennsylvania Station that would whisk passengers to Stewart in a half-hour.
 "Trying to serve somebody in Manhattan out of an airport 65 miles north of Manhattan never made a heck of a lot of sense to me when you have airports much closer right there," Mr. Vachon said
 When he talks about Stewart, it assumes an almost human poignance.
 "I have my heart and soul in it," he said. "If there were not an airport there and you ask yourself where's a good place to put an airport, it couldn't be better."
 Mr. Vachon and other state officials have not stopped dreaming altogether. There are plans to more than double the size of Stewart's terminal by adding a second floor and increasing the number of gates from four to seven by early 1997. Ninety percent of the $12.2 million is coming from the Federal Government. The state also has plans to build an exit ramp off Interstate 84 that would shorten the trip to Stewart by skirting country roads.
 The enlarged airport could potentially handle a million passengers a year. But Mr. Vachon admits that whether the extra gates result in flights to more destinations depends entirely on the airlines.
 "You have to understand the way these guys think," Mr. Vachon said. "They only have so many airplanes and they're going to put them where the best payoff is." Officials of American Airlines and USAir, the two largest carriers at that airport, concur.
 Indeed, the main purpose of Stewart's expansion is not to increase flights but to relieve some of the congestion -- congestion that would be considered routine at other airports -- during peak hours and to let passengers walk directly to their plans without stepping outside.
 The people who use Stewart, passenger surveys show, arrive within an hour's drive, with the largest clusters traveling from Poughkeepsie (19 percent), Fishkill (8 percent), Hopewell Junction (6 percent), Kingston (5 percent) and other towns in Orange, Dutchess, Ulster, Sullivan, Putnam and Rockland Counties. I.B.M. executives at the area's three giant plants have long favored Stewart, but there are a lot fewer of them because I.B.M. cut its work force from 31,000 in 1985 to 10,500 last year.
 Ann Davis, an economist at Marist College in Poughkeepsie who studies the mid-Hudson economy, said that Stewart and nearby distribution plants for companies like Caldor, Grand Union and Baxter Pharmaceuticals have helped Orange County become one of the state's fastest growing. More than 78,000 tons of cargo moved into and out of Stewart last year.
 Yet, any expansion plans for Stewart, Ms. Davis pointed out, are dependent on the region's economic health. If the job market shrinks, as it did with I.B.M.'s downsizing, and business travel declines, that could hobble Stewart's growth.
 Limited growth at Stewart would be just fine with the area's environmental groups, who are pleased that noise and pollution have never approached the levels they feared. Carol Sondheimer, associate director of Scenic Hudson, a nonprofit group, said that a sizable spurt in Stewart's growth, inevitably accompanied by a sprawl of cargo warehouses, would erode the area's scenic beauty. That beauty is the basis for an industry larger than Stewart -- tourism centered around such sights as West Point and Hyde Park.
 "If you have people coming into an area where all they see is ugly boxes, that will not create a favorable impression," she said.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Stewart International Airport, one of the nation's largest in acreage, has a terminal about the size of a grade-school auditorium. There are two uncrowded baggage carousels. Arriving passengers walk from plane to terminal, but parking is easy. (Photographs by Chris Maynard for The New York Times)(pg.25)

HEADLINE: Alexander Woroniak, 76, an Economist

 BODY:
Alexander Woroniak, an expert on East European economic systems who was professor emeritus and former chairman of the Department of Economics and Business at The Catholic University of America, died on Tuesday in Georgetown University Hospital. He was 76 and lived in Washington.
The cause was complications from a fall on Feb, 14, said his wife, Alyce Ann Bergkamp-Woroniak. 
 A native of Lviv, in present day Ukraine, he received a law degree from the Yoanni Casimiri Universitatis in Lviv in 1939 and taught there until 1942. After World War II, he worked in Germany for the United Nations Relief and Rehabilitation Administration and the International Refugee Organization.
As an expert on German and Polish law and fluent in five languages, he was able to provide counsel on the resettlement of thousands of refugees, many of whom did not wish to return to lands under Communist control.
In 1950, he came to New York City and worked as an accountant while earning a master's degree in business and accounting from Columbia University. He joined Catholic University in Washington in 1958 and served as chairman of the business department from 1974 until his retirement in 1988.
The author of scores of journal articles, he wrote, with Daniel L. Spencer, "Transfer of Technology to Developing Countries" (Praeger, 1967). He served as a consultant for both Government and private organizations on the economies of the former Soviet Union and Eastern Europe countries.
His wife is the only survivor.

HEADLINE: Paul Bates, Head of Black Tank Unit, Dead at 86

BYLINE: By LAWRENCE VAN GELDER 

 BODY:
Paul L. Bates, the white colonel who commanded the first black tank battalion to enter combat in World War II, died on Tuesday at his home in Dunedin, Fla. He was 86.
The cause was cancer, said a daughter-in-law, Dorothy Bates, of Bloomfield, Mich., and Manhattan. 
 In January 1943, about two years after he joined the Army, Colonel Bates took command of the 761st Tank Battalion, all of whose enlisted men were black.
The 761st entered combat in November 1944 as part of Gen. George S. Patton's Third Army and fought for 183 consecutive days without relief, according to David Williams, a veteran of the battalion and the author in the early 1980's of "Hit Hard" (Bantam), an account of the unit.
The battalion fought in France, then in Germany, where it pierced the Siegfried Line, and then in Belgium, where it fought the Nazis in the Battle of the Bulge. It also fought in Luxembourg and Austria. In all, the 761st Tank Battalion went from Vic-sur-Seille, France, to the Enns River in Steyr, Austria, where it linked up with the Soviet Army.
The unit, whose shoulder patch had a black panther, inflicted thousands of casualties on the enemy and captured, destroyed or liberated more than 30 major towns, 4 airfields, 3 ammunition-supply dumps, 461 wheeled vehicles, 34 tanks, 113 large guns and a radio station.
Colonel Bates was the first member of the 761st to be wounded. Among its 687 enlisted men and 41 officers, 276 received the Purple Heart for wounds in action and 36 died in combat.
In 1978, after a 33-year struggle by the unit's veterans, President Jimmy Carter awarded the 761st a Presidential Unit Citation "for extraordinary heroism in action."
When the unit completed training in rigidly segregated boot camps in Louisiana and Texas, Colonel Bates refused a promotion from lieutenant colonel that would have separated him from what he regarded as one of the best tank battalions in the Army. He was eventually promoted to full colonel.
While in Texas, Colonel Bates refused to court-martial a black officer who had refused to move to the rear of a bus at Fort Hood. The officer was Jackie Robinson, who was subsequently court-martialed but not convicted and left the 761st before it went overseas. Praising Colonel Bates for his fairness and good judgment, Robinson, who would break the color line in major league baseball, recounted the incident in his autobiography.
In 1993, the unit made news because of a PBS documentary, "Liberators: Fighting on Two Fronts in World War II," in which two of its former enlisted men said the battalion had participated in the liberation of the Dachau and Buchenwald concentration camps. But Colonel Bates and other officers, noting that the unit was spread over a 50-mile front supporting the 71st Infantry Division at the time, would not confirm the claim. The unit did liberate Gunskirchen, a subcamp of the Mauthausen complex.
Colonel Bates, who was born in Los Angeles, graduated in 1931 with a degree in economics from Western Maryland College, where he was a star football player and a member of the Reserve Officer Training Corps. He worked as a high school football coach and a teacher before entering the Army as a first lieutenant in February 1941.
During World War II, he was awarded the Silver Star, two Bronze Stars in addition to the Purple Heart. In 1963, he was awarded the Legion of Merit when he retired from the Army, having served in combat commands in Europe, at the Command and General Staff College in Fort Leavenworth, Kan., and at the Pentagon.
He remained in close touch with veterans of the 761st and their families, attending yearly reunions and establishing a scholarship at Western Maryland for descendants of members of the battalion.
Colonel Bates, who will be buried on Wednesday with full military honors at Arlington National Cemetery in Virginia, is survived by his wife, the former Helen Rosen of Dunedin; his sons, Baron, of Bloomfield, Mich., and Manhattan, and Paul Jr., of Southboro, Mass., and three grandchildren.

HEADLINE: Ex-Oilman Named Environment Chief

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ALBANY, Feb. 24

 BODY:
In a move that pleased business groups but angered some of his staunchest environmental supporters, Gov. George E. Pataki today named a former oil company executive to be the state's highest environmental official.
 The nomination of Michael D. Zagata to be Commissioner of the Department of Environmental Conservation ends what had been a difficult process for Mr. Pataki, who draws broad support from both business and environmental leaders and had sought to satisfy both in his selection. 
 In Mr. Zagata, the Governor picked someone who was praised by business groups and some environmentalists as having a balanced resume of corporate and environmental experience. But the nominee was also attacked today as a disappointing choice by several major environmental organizations, including one that once named Mr. Pataki environmental legislator of the year.
 Though Mr. Pataki will set the broad outlines for environmental policy, Mr. Zagata will wield enormous influence over the department's daily operations, which include filing lawsuits against polluters, issuing permits to developers and enforcing regulations governing garbage disposal, wetlands development and air and water pollution.
 The agency employs 4,000 people and has a $300 million annual budget. Mr. Zagata, who is almost certain to be confirmed by the state Senate, will be paid $95,635 a year.
 Mr. Pataki, a Republican, has suggested that his own strong concern for both environmental and business interests is reminiscent of his political hero, Theodore Roosevelt. In an interview today, the Governor said he chose Mr. Zagata because he has worked for both industry and environmental groups, comes from New York State and is familiar with the agency, having worked there since last fall.
 Mr. Pataki said that even after Mr. Zagata emerged as the top candidate, he continued to review other candidates at the behest of environmental groups that had raised concerns about Mr. Zagata's record in the oil and natural gas industry over the past 14 years.
 "We talked to excellent people from all over the country," Mr. Pataki said. "From my standpoint, it wasn't close."
 Mr. Zagata did not return calls for comment.
 Earlier in his career, Mr. Zagata, 52, taught courses about wildlife resources at the University of Maine, worked for the Wildlife Society and was a Washington lobbyist for the National Audubon Society. He has a doctorate in wildlife ecology.
 After leaving the National Audubon Society in 1980, Mr. Zagata worked for Tenneco Inc., the Kerr-McGee Corporation, the Transcontinental Gas Pipe Line Company and, most recently, the Transco Energy Company, where he was a vice president for environmental and safety affairs. Last year, he was appointed by Gov. Mario M. Cuomo to be the state's environmental liaison to business.
 During his 14 years in the oil and gas industry, Mr. Zagata clashed with environmentalists on three issues. He endorsed oil drilling in an Alaskan wildlife refuge; he called for allowing oil companies to sink unused off-shore drilling platforms to create marine habitats, and he advocated allowing companies to build in delicate wetlands in exchange for protecting or creating equal amounts of wetlands elsewhere, a practice known as mitigation banking.
 Mr. Pataki said he was willing to consider mitigation banking in New York State on a case-by-case basis. "If there is a wetland of particular importance, you can't just say you'll get a credit for restoring something elsewhere," Mr. Pataki said. "On the other hand, I don't have an inherent philosophical objection in all cases."
 But several environmental groups said the idea would allow the destruction of sensitive habitats. "Mr. Zagata's career has been highlighted by his attempts to justify why industry should be allowed to do what they wanted to do all along, whether destroying wetlands or critical habitats," said Lee Wasserman, executive director of Environmental Advocates, the group that named Mr. Pataki environmental legislator of the year in 1989, citing his sponsorship of legislation to protect the Hudson River.
 Larry Shapiro, a lawyer for the New York Public Interest Research Group, said of Mr. Zagata, "This guy's role has been to put an environmentally happy face on environmentally destructive actions."
 But David J. Miller, regional vice president for Audubon, said Mr. Zagata "will successfully build partnerships in the protection and proper management of the state's natural resources." Another national environmental group, The Nature Conservancy, also supported Mr. Zagata's appointment.
 Daniel B. Walsh, president of the Business Council of New York State, said Mr. Zagata would bring "a pragmatic approach to balancing D.E.C.'s environmental protection mandate and its impact on New York State's economy."

HEADLINE: Why I Switched

BYLINE: By Joseph R. Biden Jr.; Joseph R. Biden Jr., a Democrat, is the junior Senator from Delaware.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
This week I announced that I would vote for a balanced-budget amendment to the Constitution when the Senate takes it up next Tuesday, even though I voted against such an amendment last year. Unlike flag burning and school prayer -- two issues that are the subjects of constitutional amendments that would limit personal freedom -- the decision to encumber future generations with financial obligations is a fundamental choice that can be legitimately addressed in the Constitution.
The question for me is not whether the budget must be in perfect balance. The question is whether we can indefinitely sustain deficits of $200 billion and more without permanently and dangerously limiting future options for our children's generation. Under current budget conditions, trying to spend our way out of recessions is no longer an option; deficits are now the norm, not a conscious policy choice. 
 In the decade after 1981, when we lost control of the deficit, the human-services share of Federal outlays was cut 20 percent. The share going for education, training, employment and social services was cut 40 percent. And those areas of the budget that need help the least and should have been cut -- tax loopholes for the privileged, exotic weapons, spending on those who make money off programs for the middle-class and poor -- were spared.
People say there is much that we can do to contain deficits short of this amendment. Let's look at the record. In 1984, I introduced a bipartisan across-the-board freeze of every Federal program. We got the same argument from many advocates for children and the elderly who now criticize the balanced-budget amendment. I argued then, and I argue now, that unless we put every program on the table, only these programs would suffer.
We then tried the Gramm-Rudman balanced-budget law, and it failed. Finally, under the leadership of President Clinton, we did do something: we approved a $500 billion deficit reduction plan.
And we lost the next election, because Republicans convinced voters that to balance the budget all we needed to do was cut foreign aid and Aid to Families With Dependent Children.
Now the positive action we took is being undone and replaced with more wasteful spending and more cuts in those areas that have already been bled dry. Just look at the recent action of the House on children's nutrition programs.
So where does all this leave us? It leaves us with $235 billion interest on the debt this year, $270 billion to be paid on the debt in 1997 and $344 billion in 2002. Where are we going to find the money to pay this interest? Surely not with new taxes on the affluent. Surely not with cuts in weapons we don't need, or with savings gained by a new national health care system. Instead, the money is certain to come from Federal programs for the most deserving -- programs for infants, children, the needy elderly and the middle class.
The balanced-budget amendment is far from perfect. We could have made it better -- by including a clear statement that the President and the courts should not interfere with Congress's constitutional responsibility for the budget process, by including my own amendment for a capital budget for Federal public-works investments, by dealing honestly with Social Security instead of using the surpluses in the trust fund to mask the size of the deficit. But these changes were rejected by Republican lawmakers. So the choice before us is either a less than perfect amendment or the status quo -- and all the consequences that flow from it.
I will vote for the balanced-budget amendment on Tuesday because it will force the Government to do what it has been unable to do: to spend only the money it takes in, unless extraordinary circumstances force us to find the resources to spend more. I will do so in the belief that respect for the Constitution will result in a new tradition of fiscal responsibility. Ultimately, this will determine the kind of nation we pass on to our children.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: FUNDS WATCH;
Foreign Holdings Can Balance Portfolios

BYLINE: By Carole Gould 

 BODY:
Mutual fund companies are looking abroad for inspiration, as these three new overseas funds show. Although investors are encouraged to own some foreign shares to balance domestic portfolios, they should expect interim volatility and plan to hold shares for the long term. That is because overseas securities are subject to additional risks, like currency and political shifts.
 The T. Rowe Price Emerging Markets Bond Fund will own high-yielding government and corporate bonds issued in Latin America, central Europe, Asia and Africa. The fund will hedge some risk by buying short- and intermediate-term bonds. 
 The Lexington Global Income Fund invests in a complete package of bonds from emerging markets, established foreign markets and the United States. The fund is a joint venture with Maria Fiorini Ramirez, who heads a global economics consulting group.
 The IAI Developing Countries Fund aims for long-term capital appreciation by owning equities of companies in developing markets that are growing rapidly. The minimum investment has been reduced temporarily to $1,000 from $5,000.

HEADLINE: Corrections

 BODY:
A front-page article on Tuesday about the savings patterns of generations of Americans misstated the views of Michael J. Boskin, who was chief economic adviser under President George Bush. He is pessimistic, not optimistic, that the aging of the baby boomers will raise the national saving rate. 
 The article also misstated the number of Americans born from 1946 through 1964, which is considered the baby-boom generation. It is 75 million, not 45 million.

HEADLINE: Credit Bhutto for Transforming Pakistan

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re Paula R. Newberg's attack on Prime Minister Benazir Bhutto of Pakistan in "The Two Benazir Bhuttos" (Op-Ed, Feb. 11):
Since Prime Minister Bhutto's reelection in October 1993, the economy and social structure of Pakistan have been remarkably transformed. Her Government has taken hard economic and budgetary decisions, risking political unpopularity by establishing fiscal discipline, and has been called a model of successful adjust ment in the developing world by the International Monetary Fund. 
 Foreign investment, responding to the economic incentives of the Government, has poured into Pakistan -- $6.5 billion from the United States in the energy sector alone. The $22 billion in foreign investment pledges in Pakistan during this Government's 15 months in office has quintupled foreign investment in Pakistan over the last quarter-century -- five times more in 15 months than in the previous 25 years combined.
The taxation system of the country has been reformed and an agricultural wealth tax imposed for the first time. New drug laws extending the death penalty to drug traffickers have been enacted. Cooperation with the United States and the world community on international terrorism has accelerated. The most recent example of this was the speedy extradition by Pakistan of Ramzi Ahmed Yousef, who is accused of plotting the attack on the World Trade Center in New York.
Ms. Newberg insinuates that Benazir Bhutto is working with Muslim extremists to deny rights to women. She and the world ought to know better.
To protect the rights of women in society, women's police stations have been created. Women judges have been appointed to the high courts, and thousands of women's health centers have been instituted all across the rural areas, training women in health, nutrition and family planning.
As for not building schools, 6,500 schools are under construction this year alone as part of Prime Minister Bhutto's social action program.
 
SYED RIFAAT HUSSAIN
Minister (Press), Pakistan Embassy
Washington, Feb. 21, 1995

HEADLINE: PENTAGON TO SEEK SCALED-BACK LIST OF BASE CLOSINGS

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 24

 BODY:
Scaling back its plans for shrinking the military, the Clinton Administration has prepared a list for its last scheduled round of military base closings that officials acknowledge will force the Pentagon to keep open more installations than it needs.
At Defense Secretary William J. Perry's direction, the armed services have recommended that about two dozen major bases be closed and more than 80 smaller installations nationwide be closed, reduced or, in a few cases, expanded slightly to accommodate troops from sites that are being closed, senior military officials said. The list is scheduled to be announced on Tuesday. 
 In the New York metropolitan area, the New London submarine base in Connecticut, the Lakehurst naval engineering center in New Jersey and Fort Hamilton in the Bay Ridge section of Brooklyn are expected to be closed or reduced in size, as is the Rome Laboratory at Griffiss Air Force Base in central New York.
California, where 22 bases have already been ordered closed, would undergo two more potential losses: the Navy shipyard in Long Beach and the Army base in Oakland. But other California bases remained safe, including McClellan Air Force Base in Sacramento, which the Air Force unsuccessfully tried to close two years ago.
"I'm profoundly disappointed," Senator Dianne Feinstein, Democrat of California, said in an interview. "That's 3,500 jobs at Long Beach and 2,000 at Oakland. I was hoping we would have a bit of respite."
But the biggest news may be what is not on the list.
The Army spared Fort Carson in Colorado and Fort Riley in Kansas, two bases with large training areas the Army says it cannot afford to lose. The Air Force kept open its five large air logistics centers but proposed shrinking them slightly.
And Portsmouth Naval Shipyard in New Hampshire dodged the knife -- partly to avoid steep job losses in the first primary state of the 1996 Presidential campaign, some military officials said.
After the last round of closings in 1993, then-Defense Secretary Les Aspin predicted the 1995 round would be even more ambitious, the "mother of all base closings." But Mr. Perry scaled back that goal in recent months after the military services told him it would be too difficult and too costly in the near term.
The current list is "about three-quarters of the 1993 round," Deputy Defense Secretary John M. Deutch said in an interview last week. "We need time to balance the base-closing costs and the base-closing savings, and complete the transfer of facilities to productive community use."
But by Mr. Perry's own admission, the military is keeping open more bases, depots, shipyards and laboratories than the shrinking post-cold-war military needs, bleeding money away from training, spare parts and new weapons. The overall size of the military has declined by 33 percent in the past eight years, but the number of installations has shrunk by only 20 percent.
Mr. Perry and other senior Pentagon officials are reviewing the recommendations, and senior aides cautioned that the Secretary might change some before he makes his final list public on Tuesday. But they said that is unlikely.
"Bill Perry and I are very much of the mind to make few changes to the service submissions," Mr. Deutch said in the interview.
The Defense Department list will then be given to a Presidential commission that was set up to review such plans, giving Congress political cover for the final decisions. The panel, formally known as the Defense Base Closure and Realignment Commission, is to hold its first meeting next Wednesday.
The closings will eliminate thousands of military and civilian jobs, and are certain to stir intense opposition among lawmakers whose districts or states are affected. Indeed, a parade of anxious lawmakers have met with Mr. Perry, Mr. Deutch and other senior Pentagon civilians in recent days to plead for installations in their states or districts.
New York's two Senators, Daniel Patrick Moynihan and Alfonse D'Amato, its Governor, George E. Pataki, and several Representatives from New York appealed today to Mr. Deutch to spare Fort Hamilton, the Rome Laboratory and Fort Drum in Watertown. Army officials said Fort Drum was not on the list.
Among the other major installations the military seeks to close or scale back are Red River Army Depot in Texas, Fitzsimmons Army Hospital in Colorado, Meridian Naval Air Station in Mississippi, Brooks Air Force Base in Texas and Kirkland Air Force Base in New Mexico.
But survivors like Fort Riley, as well as Fort Wainwright and Fort Richardson in Alaska, counted on powerful political patrons like Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the majority leader, or Senator Ted Stevens, Republican of Alaska, in addition to military utility.
Although this year is the last scheduled round, Mr. Perry has suggested that the Defense Department might want to hold another in 1997 or 1998, after the 1996 Presidential elections. But the military services are caught between a desire to slash expensive overhead for a dwindling number of warships, fighter jets and combat divisions and the hope of keeping valuable bases and services in case the military has to grow.
As in 1993, the Navy offered up the largest number of installations in this round, largely adjusting to decisions made in previous rounds. With its bases built to accommodate a 600-ship cold-war fleet, the Navy is now heading toward 346 ships and submarines, and must cut its overhead.
But even the Navy did not make cuts completely commensurate to its projected smaller size. "We don't have as big a Navy to cut from, and most of the big things are already done," Adm. Jeremy M. Boorda, the Chief of Naval Operations, said. "We're also trying to have a reasonable amount of surge capability."
Under the law governing the procedure for closing or consolidating bases, Secretary Perry will submit his list by next Wednesday to the eight-member Presidential commission, headed by former Senator Alan Dixon, an Illinois Democrat. The commission will have until July 1 to accept, reject or alter the Administration's recommendations.
Its decision will then go to Mr. Clinton, who will have 15 days to accept or reject them. If he approves them the recommendations go to Congress, where only a negative vote by both houses can stop the base-closing process.
In its last round in 1993, the commission closed 35 major bases and reduced or consolidated 27 others. More than 100 other smaller installations were closed or consolidated.
It usually takes five to six years to close a base, and in the short term is very costly. By the end of the decade, the Defense Department expects to save about $ 4 billion a year from the 70 major bases closed in the first three rounds. The department operates 470 bases and installations around the country.
Since 1990, the military has closed more than 800 installations overseas, including scores of small installations, but those do not require commission approval.
In making their recommendations, the armed services used the same eight criteria employed in previous base-closing rounds. These measures include military usefulness, operating costs, projected savings, economic impact to local communities and environmental impact.
 The article and the chart included one base erroneously in some copies; Ellsworth Air Force Base in Rapid City, S.D., was not on the Pentagon's list for closing. In addition, the chart listed another base incorrectly in some copies; Hanscom Air Force Base in Bedford, Mass., is not scheduled to close.

CORRECTION-DATE: February 26, 1995, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
An article and a chart yesterday about the Pentagon's recommendations for military base closings omitted, in some copies, one major installation that would be closed. It is Brooks Air Force Base in San Antonio.

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY DAILY BRIEFING;
State Rakes In Extra Cash

BYLINE: By CHARLES STRUM 

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
State bookkeepers got a pleasant surprise when they totaled January's tax collections, which took in $43 million more than anticipated. The difference reflected continued strength in New Jersey's economy, Brian Clymer, the State Treasurer, said yesterday. 
 So far, tax collections for the fiscal year that started in July have reached $8.4 billion, or $57.3 million more than projected. The biggest area of increase was in motor vechicle fees, which rose $10.2 million, showing continued strength in car sales, Mr. Clymer said. Corporate taxes were up by $7.9 million, excise taxes on alcoholic beverages up $2.1 million and lottery revenues up $1.6 million. There were slight decreases in other taxes, including the gross income tax, which was $50 million less than expected for January.

HEADLINE: Music Notes

BYLINE: By Allan Kozinn 

 BODY:

Focus on the Contemporary
The New York City Opera will increase its quotient of 20th-century opera during its 1995-96 season, which opens on Sept. 7 with Hindemith's rarely performed "Mathis der Maler" and also includes the American premieres of Toshiro Mayazumi's "Kinkakuji" on Oct. 19 and Jost Meier's "Dreyfus Affair" on April 2, 1996. 
 The premieres account for three of five new productions the company is offering in a two-part season that runs from Sept. 7 to Nov. 19 and from March 1 to April 21, 1996. The other new productions are Puccini's "Boheme," in a staging directed by Graziella Sciutti, and Strauss's "Rosenkavalier," a co-production with the English National Opera, the Los Angeles Opera and the Houston Grand Opera, to be directed by Jonathan Miller.
"I don't feel daring at all," said Christopher Keene, the company's general director. "I feel that this is what the City Opera should be doing. There was a long tradition of presenting contemporary work here in the early years, and even when we moved to the New York State Theater, that first season we did eight 20th-century works. We do very well at the box office with adventurous works, so long as we don't do too many performances of them. But that's equally true of 'Carmen.'"
"Mathis der Maler," composed between 1933 and 1935, is about the 16th-century painter Mathis Grunewald and is set in the context of tensions between Roman Catholics and Protestants and landowners and peasants. Banned by the Nazis, it had its premiere in Zurich in 1938 and was presented in New York City by the Hamburg State Opera in 1967. The City Opera is billing its production as the first professional staging by an American company. Rhoda Levine is the director.
"The Dreyfus Affair" has a libretto by George Whyte about the anti-Semitic trial in 1894 of Capt. Alfred Dreyfus, a French officer who was convicted of handing miltary secrets to the Germans on what later turned out to be fabricated evidence. Mr. Keene conducted the work's premiere in Berlin last season, and described its music as "eclectic, narrative and theatrical." Originally in German, it will be presented in an English version by Mr. Keene and Mr. Whyte.
The Hindemith and Meier works are part of a Freedom Cycle project being underwritten by Philip Morris Companies. City Opera describes the works in the cycle, which begins this season with "Harvey Milk," the Stewart Wallace-Michael Korie work that had its world premiere in Houston last month, as operas about "victims and victors, and the struggle for freedom from oppression." Future installments will include Hugo Weisgall's "Wall," Charles Wuorinen's "Celia, a Slave" and Anthony Davis's "Amistad."
The Mayazumi work is based on the Yukio Mishima novel "The Temple of the Golden Pavilion," about Mizoguchi, a disfigured monk who is obsessed with the Kikakuji Temple in Kyoto. It had its premiere in Berlin in 1976 and has a German libretto by Claus Henneberg. The City Opera production will use an English translation by Mr. Keene. Jerome Sirlin will design and direct the production, which is being underwritten by Music From Japan.
Mr. Keene is conducting all the new productions except "Der Rosenkavalier," which will be conducted by Alexander Sander in his company debut.
The revivals on the company's schedule are Verdi's "Attila," "Rigoletto" and "La Traviata," Mozart's "Magic Flute" and "Don Giovanni," Bizet's "Carmen," Puccini's "Turandot" and "Tosca," Gilbert and Sullivan's "Mikado," Lehar's "Merry Widow" and the Rodgers and Hammerstein version of "Cinderella."
Mr. Keene added that the company, hit hard by the recession two seasons ago, is looking at better times. In the first half of this season, it sold 82 percent of its tickets, up from 79 percent in 1993-94 and 72 percent in 1992-93.
Ticket information: (212) 496-0600.
 
4 U.S. Concerts Dropped
 The London Philharmonic had planned to give two performances each in New York and San Francisco on its way to a Far Eastern tour in mid-March. But the orchestra has dropped the American leg of the tour and will instead fly directly to South Korea for three concerts, and from there to Japan for five performances.
"No sponsor pulled out, as such," an orchestra spokesman said, "but the whole tour came together over a long period, and when we finally got through all the details, it was clear that it was not working out to be financially viable. It's very much our intention to get back to the States as soon as we can, probably for a more substantial tour."
Lincoln Center, which was to have presented the concerts on March 12 and 13, is offering refunds and exchanges at the Avery Fisher Hall box office or by mail from Lincoln Center Productions, 10 Lincoln Center Plaza, New York, N.Y. 10023, attention London Philharmonic. Information: (212) 580-5030.
 
Contest for Conductors
 The first Leonard Bernstein Jerusalem International Conducting Competition is open to conductors between the ages of 24 and 37. The preliminary screening, by videocassette, is to take place between April 15 and May 12. Regional auditions are to be held in the United States, England, Austria, Japan and Israel in June and July, and the final rounds, in Jerusalem, are scheduled to run from Oct. 2 to 14. There will be no ranking of winners and no limit to the number of laureates, each of whom is to receive a $ 25,000 cash prize, a medallion and conducting and recording opportunities.
The conducting competition is the first in a sequence of contests to be held under the Bernstein banner. In 1996 there will be singing competition, and in 1997 composers will compete. There is talk of adding a competition for educators, although a format for that has not yet been determined.
Applications for the conducting contest are available from the Leonard Bernstein International Music Competitions, 11 Rivka Street, P.O. Box 10185, Jerusalem 91101, Israel, or by telephone (011-972-2-735032) or by fax (011-972-2-716380).

SECTION: Section 1; Page 35; Column 3; Business/Financial Desk; Your Money Page

HEADLINE: Is the Fed's Promised Land Appearing on the Horizon?

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
In a characteristically careful way, Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve Board chairman, told Congress this week that the economy might be entering the moderate-growth/low-inflation mode that for a policy maker is the functional equivalent of the Promised Land.
And for the investor, that would mean an end to rising interest rates. 
 While this is welcome news, Steven Janachowski, a principal at Brouwer & Janachowski, a San Francisco investment advisory firm, said that a change in Fed policy ought not to be the primary basis for investment decisions.
"We don't believe in structuring portfolios based on an economic forecast," said Paul K. Napoli, executive vice president in charge of the personal investment division at the United States Trust Company.
"The data Mr. Greenspan is relying on are short-term, and subject to revisions," Mr. Napoli added. "So I am not so sure I would like to run out today and say this is the best time to buy stocks and bonds."
Still, Mr. Napoli and others concede that now is a particularly good time to be buying high-quality intermediate-term -- that is, three to 10 years -- debt securities. With yields on such Treasury notes hovering around or just over 7 percent, and inflation remaining around 3 percent and unlikely to go markedly higher, the real returns of 4 percent are handsome, they say.
"If you are someone who is shopping for income, this is a good time to consider getting back in," to the bond market, Mr. Janachowski said.
If the Fed has really finished raising rates, now is also an opportune moment to reconsider putting some money into foreign stock markets, the professionals say.
Many of these markets, particularly in countries with currencies linked to the dollar, have been battered in the last year, as local central banks have been forced to raise rates to keep in step with the Fed.
"If the economy really is slowing and United States rates really have peaked, then it is positive for all non-United States equity markets," said Richard H. King, who manages the $1.7 billion Warburg Pincus International Fund.
"Essentially, the world has pretty reasonable sustainable economic growth and low inflation, which is the best combination we have had in a long time," he added.
Over the last month, Mr. King said, he has been funneling money into Southeast Asian markets, including Hong Kong and Singapore. He said Australian and New Zealand equities should also stand to benefit if the Fed stopped tightening.
The Fed's quest for the Promised Land has taken many investors on a costly trip. To cool the economy and drive down inflation expectations -- to engineer, in short, a "soft landing" of the economy -- the Fed pushed up short-term interest rates seven times over the last year.
The impact of the Fed's moves was not confined to the domestic bond market, which in 1994 had its worst year since the Depression. Financial markets around the world were also rocked.
Mr. Greenspan did not rule out further rate increases, but in his testimony he made it plain that the upward phase of the interest rate cycle was nearing an end. Indeed, the Fed chairman said the central bank was prepared to ease monetary policy by lowering interest rates if outright signs of economic weakness began to crop up.
Mr. Greenspan would not have "used the 'E' word if he didn't think the Fed was now in a neutral policy mode," said Edward Yardeni, chief economist at C. J. Lawrence. "If we really are in a soft landing, we won't get any more tightening anytime soon."
The Fed's apparent change in approach "argues for a constructive attitude toward markets, but not a wild-eyed bullishness," said Neil A. Soss, a principal at Soss & Cotton, a New York money management firm.
One reason is that investors may have already missed some of the gains. The bond market has been rallying for more than three months. And various stock market indexes hit record levels before Mr. Greenspan testified last week.
Inevitably, many analysts remain skeptical, contending the Fed has much more to do to contain inflation and rein in the economy. And they worry that Mr. Greenspan's remarks may come back to haunt him.
"The chairman's statement virtually says the Fed will not respond any more until it becomes quite clear that inflationary pressures are worse," Henry Kaufman, who runs his own money management firm in New York, said.
"If that is the case, it will be the first time the Fed has moved off of a pre-emptive attack on inflation. That will raise the difficult question in the market that the Fed is being a laggard."
Mr. Kaufman views the drop in bond yields over the last few months as temporary. If money is to be committed to the fixed-income market, it should be in the short-term sector, he said, where a loss from another rise in interest rates would be less than on longer-term bonds.
Unlike the last year, though, when the bond market seemed to call the tune for stocks, Mr. Kaufman predicted that would change in 1995.
"There will be a departure from the performance of equities and bonds," he said. "This is a year when there will be continued strong profit growth, and that will be supportive of stocks. At some point, the bond market will labor, while the equity market continues to improve."
