SECTION: Section 2; Page 1; Column 1; Arts & Leisure Desk 

LENGTH: 2503 words

HEADLINE: TELEVISION;
The Man Who Keeps 'E.R.' 's Heart Beating

BYLINE: By ANDY MEISLER 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
ANY VISITOR TO THE BURBANK production offices of "E.R.," the NBC medical drama series, could quickly discern who is the guiding force behind the biggest network hit in several years.
It is not Michael Crichton, the novelist and screenwriter, who created the show, wrote its pilot episode -- and departed almost immediately to write new novels. It is not Steven Spielberg, whose Amblin Television company took the project to a big studio, Warner Brothers. It is not Leslie Moonves, the president of Warner Brothers Television, which produces the show for NBC. 
 It is a man named John Wells, a veteran television scriptwriter, most notably for "China Beach."
At any one moment, Mr. Wells is supervising the content and execution of at least four one-hour episodes in various stages of development -- from script to filming to editing to post-production. Future story lines of the series are also his responsibility: on the walls of the conference room next to his office are whiteboards covered, in felt-tip markers, with dozens of handwritten dramatic situations and plot twists, approved by him and cleared for use in episodes as yet unwritten. In the term of the trade, Mr. Wells is "E.R." 's show runner.
For the last 10 years at least, the person with that unofficial title has been the true auteur of series television. Day to day, a show runner makes all important decisions about the series' scripts, tone, attitude, look and direction. He or she oversees casting, production design and budget. This person chooses directors and guest stars, defends the show against meddling by the network or production company and, when necessary, changes its course.
Even in this notoriously collaborative medium, show runners are responsible for what viewers see on the screen. Yet the show runner's true position and influence are unknown to nearly everyone on the other side of the picture tube.
On a Monday morning about two months ago, the first task facing Mr. Wells was to prepare the season's 14th episode, to be filmed the following week. Mr. Wells had written the script; he plans on writing at least 5 during the 26-episode season.
"What's the figure?" he asked, turning to his casting director, John Levey.
"$56,709," said Mr. Levey, half-jokingly, referring to the projected cost of hiring guest stars and supporting cast. Mr. Wells next led a freewheeling discussion about the availability and desirability of several actors familiar to television audiences. Then he inquired about the status of a handful of previously used bit players whose recurring minor characters -- nurses, hospital staff members' children, police officers -- Mr. Wells had written in.
Next, Mr. Wells discussed with some of the show's assembled writer-producers and production coordinators how scenes shot on location in Chicago (the fictional setting for "E.R.") would be melded with scenes filmed on the Warners sound stage. A previously filmed episode needed "pickups" -- scenes filmed again because for various reasons they did not match the scenes surrounding them. Mr. Wells ordered them.
"O.K., let's get into this one," said Mr. Wells, opening the 70-page script. With the director, James Hayman, taking notes, Mr. Wells led a shot-by-shot dissection.
One scene involved a doctor working frantically over a patient inside a speeding ambulance. Was everyone "on the same page," Mr. Wells asked, about exactly how to film such a scene inside a stationary, hand-rocked ambulance mock-up?
In a comic subplot, a poisonous snake gets loose inside the emergency room. What kind of snake should be used, and from where and for how much can it be rented? The prop master, Rick Kerns, produced Polaroid pictures of some venomous specimens available from a local animal handler who specializes in cinematic reptile rentals. Mr. Wells selected a yellow-eyelash pit viper.
How much blood should be visible during a certain operation? Mr. Wells asked Dr. Joe Sachs, one of the show's two medical consultants. A special kind of X-ray device is used with a catheterization procedure performed in the script. Mr. Kerns reported that a medical company was flying in the appropriate machine, complete with the technician needed to operate it, gratis.
In Mr. Wells's script a new regular character, Deb, played by Ming-Na Wen, upstages a fellow medical student, John Carter (Noah Wyle), when they are presenting their surgical cases in a hospital classroom. Upon reaching that point, someone gently questioned Mr. Wells about Deb's motivation for acting the way she did.
"Is she Machiavellian?" he asked.
"No, it's more that she's narcissistic," Mr. Wells said.
 
The Job Description
Big Picture, Little Picture
 For complicated reasons having to do with economics, ego and history, the title show runner never appears in the credits of a show. Indeed, the term is rarely even written down or mentioned to outsiders.
Every show runner is listed as an executive producer on a show's credits. Yet not every executive producer is a show runner.
Often, the show runner is the person who created the series. Sometimes, though, the show's creator ranks below the show runner; other times, especially on long-running series, the creator-show runner has moved on and bequeathed power to another.
Perhaps most surprising, show runners almost always emerge from the ranks of writers -- traditionally Hollywood's most underempowered profession. Nevertheless, the show runner's sovereignty is acknowledged by nearly everyone in television.
"The show runner is the person responsible for a show -- period," said Mr. Moonves of Warner Brothers, which currently produces 23 network series. "He is the creative dictator. Ultimately, all the calls in that area are his. He is in charge of the big picture and the little picture."
Caryn Mandabach, president of Carsey-Werner, which has produced "Roseanne" and "The Cosby Show," agreed: "The buck always stops with him."
And Stuart Bloomberg, ABC's executive vice president for prime-time programming, said, "When we're considering doing a series, one of the first questions we ask is 'Who's the show runner?' "
John Wells's eclectic background seems perfect for the job of show runner -- but like most, he toiled for years before he even realized he was heading toward such a post.
Raised in Virginia, West Virginia and Colorado, he graduated from the theater program at Carnegie-Mellon University in Pittsburgh, then worked as a stage manager in regional theater and at La Mama, the Off Off Broadway company. He moved up to producing stage shows in Los Angeles, and in the mid-1980's began to write.
In 1987 he produced a low-budget movie comedy, "Nice Girls Don't Explode." After four years on the writing staff of "China Beach," learning from its show runner, John Sacret Young, he became show runner on no fewer than five unsold pilots, and on a short-lived CBS series, "Angel Street."
Last year Creative Artists Agency, which represents Mr. Wells, set up a meeting between him and another client, Michael Crichton. "We had lunch," said Mr. Wells, "and we really hit it off. I had read the script and had a lot of things to talk about, and he really agreed with what I had to say." Mr. Wells was put in charge.
 
The Long Workday
'This Is Like Flying A Jet Plane'
 Mr. Wells's busy Monday proceeded. With a select group of producers, he discussed the problems on episode 13, which was being filmed on the sound stage a few hundred yards away. Its director was a day behind schedule, a major delay on an eight-day shoot. "She knows she's in trouble," said a producer. "We don't want to put more pressure on her."
Mr. Wells, who had been viewing the footage already shot by this director on her first assignment for "E.R.," concluded that she had been trying to capture too many long, complicated scenes in one take. Instead, she should be breaking the scenes up into more easily filmable parts. A producer was deputized to go to the sound stage and gently make this "suggestion."
Immediately after this decision was made, Mr. Wells received an unscheduled visit from George Clooney, who plays Dr. Douglas Ross. Mr. Clooney, in an off-duty costume of baggy T-shirt and work pants, declined to let a reporter sit in on his conversation with Mr. Wells. Mr. Clooney emerged from the conference room 15 minutes later, apparently in a good mood.
Mr. Wells then moved to his office for lunch -- and simultaneously to view a preliminary edit of episode 12 on his office television set. Surrounded by about 10 staff members, including editors, writer-producers and the show's other medical adviser, Dr. Lance Gentile, he munched through the rough cut, which was still without music and sound effects, and made copious notes.
Then, while watching dailies of episode 13 -- this time viewing several different versions of various scenes -- he covered many more pages of his legal pad.
Mr. Wells next walked with Mr. Hayman, the director, to Mr. Levey's casting office across the Warner lot. For several hours he sat in on final readings for a half-dozen or so guest roles on episode 15.
Five or six actors read for each part. The aspirants ranged from pre-teen-age members of an inner-city acting workshop (for the part of a young gang member who terrorizes the hospital) to several familiar-looking female character actresses, including a well-known movie star of 15 or 20 years ago (for the part of a hospice nurse).
Mr. Hayman and Mr. Levey coached the actors, asking for occasional "adjustments" in their deliveries. But no final choices were made without Mr. Wells's nod.
By the end of the casting session, it was late afternoon. Mr. Wells left to watch yet another episode and indicate where music and sound effects should be added. After that were administrative tasks, and phone calls to return, stretching into the night.
"You know," said Mr. Wells a bit wearily, during a brief conversation between editing sessions, "this is like flying a jet plane. There are just so many ways to screw up.
"I think personally, the biggest mistake is trying to do it all by yourself. You have to accept total responsibility while also accepting the fact that it's a large collaborative effort. When I look at an episode and see only my own contribution, that's not a successful episode. When I can see bits and pieces of everyone's contribution, I think: 'That's a successful episode.' "
 
The Evolution
Why Rob Petrie No Longer Exists
 Indeed, it is the unusual ability to be at once a creative wellspring, a capable team leader and a bottom-line administrator that makes an effective show runner such a rare commodity. The rewards are accordingly handsome: $25,000 to $50,000 an episode, plus hefty fees for writing scripts, plus residuals, plus occasional participation in syndication profits.
Although show runners' names are no more than vaguely familiar to the public, the proven ones are well known in the television industry. Those who have established themselves on hourlong dramas include Jacqueline Zambrano ( "Under Suspicion"), John Romano ("Sweet Justice"), Patrick Hasburgh ("Seaquest DSV"), Chris Carter ("The X-Files"), Ed Sherin ("Law and Order") and Gregory Hoblit ("N.Y.P.D. Blue").
On sitcoms, which are performed like very short plays before audiences and are filmed or taped on permanent stage sets, the show runner's duties are somewhat different. Specifically, they encompass supervision of all onstage rehearsals in the five-day production cycle and the often frantic script rewrites that accompany them.
Sitcom show runners include Marc Flanagan ("Grace Under Fire"), Rob Ulin ("Roseanne"), Yvette Denise Lee ("Living Single"), Christopher Lloyd ("Frasier"), Michael Moye ("Married . . . With Children"), David Duclon ("Family Matters") and Jeffrey Lane ("Mad About You").
The average viewer's conception of how a television series is made may still be based mainly on the fictional relationships between the "head writer" Rob Petrie, the "producer" Mel Cooley and the "star" Alan Brady on the old "Dick Van Dyke Show." And it is true that many early television shows were the fiefs of actor-producers like Desi Arnaz or Danny Thomas. Others were produced by the same cigar-chomping wheeler-dealers long associated with the assembly-line production of motion pictures. By the 1960's, however, it was clear that there were increasing differences between a good television series and a successful movie.
"Movies were more and more about stars and spectacle and visual effects," said Mr. Romano, who was a Dickens scholar and an assistant professor at Columbia University before becoming a television writer. "Television, on the other hand, is seen on a small screen. It always has and always will depend almost solely on character and dialogue. And the only people who can give you that are writers."
This caused a subtle power shift, paving the way for idiosyncratic writer-producers like Norman Lear, Larry Gelbart and Stephen J. Cannell. And especially Steven Bochco -- whose "Hill Street Blues," with its story "arcs" that stretched over several episodes and its rapidly evolving regular characters, changed the medium in ways not really understood at the time.
"It meant that a show had to have a large and very competent permanent full-time writing staff," Mr. Wells pointed out. "In the old days, on shows like 'Mannix' or 'Hawaii Five-0,' the main characters didn't change much; the episodes could be written and run in any order. So producers hired maybe one or two story editors who farmed out most of the scripts to freelance writers.
"But then, as the shows became increasingly complex, working on a character level as well as a plot level, it became obvious that freelancers wouldn't be able to keep up with the characters' development. So increasingly, people were brought onto staff because they had to be right there to know all the things that were happening to the characters.
"And that led, as the stories became more complex, to the realization that what slowed shows down the most was a lack of good writers. So if you wanted a really good writer on a show, that writer increasingly demanded more creative control."
Creative control meant that the writer-producer surpassed the nuts-and-bolts-type "line" producer. As that happened, Rob Petrie's old "head writer" title became obsolete. Today, he would probably be a show runner, with hegemony over Mel and perhaps even Alan.
But as a viewer, you wouldn't know that. Because what has remained the same over the years is a vague and opaque system of on-screen credits and, for reasons that go back to the dawn of the movie era, a reluctance to give credit where credit is due.
"Let's face it," said a publicist who insisted on anonymity. "Who are the viewers most interested in? Some guy who types and sits in his office 80 hours a week, or the good-looking men and women who appear on their television sets every week?"

SECTION: Section 5; Page 4; Column 5; Travel Desk 

LENGTH: 1175 words

HEADLINE: PRACTICAL TRAVELER;
Children's Seats In Air Faulted

BYLINE: By BETSY WADE 

 BODY:
THE Federal Aviation Administration has tested forward-facing child safety seats certified for use aboard airplanes and found them wanting.
The results do not mean that parents who ordinarily take forward-facing safety seats aboard to buckle in larger children, those over the age of 2 and under about 3 1/2 years, should stop doing so: At the moment there is no substitute. It does mean that forward-facing safety seats bearing a Government seal for use in a car or plane may not safeguard their riders in a survivable air crash. Rear-facing safety seats for smaller children were judged safe. 
 In tests conducted last summer by the F.A.A. labs, five types of child restraint devices, holding test dummies, were fastened into airplane seats and put through simulated crashes. Forward-facing seats, backless booster seats and torso harnesses did not protect dummies from severe head impact. Although the report was published in September, it received little attention.
The author of the F.A.A. research, Van Gowdy, supervisor of biodynamic research at the F.A.A. Civil Aeromedical Institute in Oklahoma City, said that as long as safety seats were certified for air use after an automotive test, parents were going to presume a "significant level of safety" would be provided. "There is no evidence to support that," Mr. Gowdy reported at a conference on airline safety sponsored by the Southern California Safety Institute in Torrance, Calif., early this month.
Mr. Gowdy said existing test specifications for seat use in cars did not recreate cabin conditions.
In discussing his findings in Torrance, Mr. Gowdy noted the established fact that auto travel, by any calculation, was far more dangerous to children than air travel. According to Dr. Jeffrey J. Sacks at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, auto crashes kill or injure 79,000 children under 4 in a year. Even an airline year like 1994 may bring the death of one or two young children and perhaps six injuries.
 
Flying in a Lap
 In the F.A.A. tests, rear-facing seats, which are used for children under 20 pounds, usually about 2 years old, were effective in safeguarding riders. However, because airlines let children under 2 travel without tickets, they often sit in adults' laps instead. Safety seats may be used only for children under 2 if a child has a ticket or a flight attendant allows use of an adjoining empty seat.
At the upper end of the toddler range, children who have reached the age of 3 or 3 1/2 years old -- who must be ticketed -- can use regular airline lap belts and enjoy a degree of safety nearly comparable to adults. So the hazard continues for infants under 2 who are not placed in the rear-facing safety seats, and a new hazard has been revealed for children too large for these seats and too small to use an adult belt: the current Government-approved forward-facing safety seats do not do the job because the certifying tests are inadequate.
The conference, attended by airline employees and commercial safety trainers as well as enforcement officials, heard several times about the F.A.A.'s lack of a safety rule on infant seating.
James M. Hall, head of the National Transportation Safety Board, said that his agency, which investigates transport accidents and recommends changes, had repeatedly pressed the F.A.A. on this: "It continues to rebuff our requests that all be secured in takeoff, landing and turbulence."
Nothing prevents an airline from acting on its own, but Dean M. Resch, head of the survivability division of the F.A.A., said in an interview that if the cost of safety changes was greater than the legal liability, the airlines would not act alone. Airlines have said that if parents were compelled to buy tickets for infants, too, they might drive instead of flying. "If the public raises the issue, something will be done," Mr. Resch said. "But it doesn't mean much to anyone unless you are the parents of those kids who died."
How have safety seats for children been labeled safe for use in planes when three types cannot do the job? This question was asked repeatedly at the meeting of 300 people that heard Mr. Gowdy's report.
 
Tests Based on Autos
 The answer is that the seats are tested in devices that simulate automobile seats and belts, but without a barrier in front to represent a dashboard or a front seat. The automotive tests presume a 32-inch open space for a child's head to be flung forward into in a crash. When the child is in a forward-facing safety seat in an economy-class airplane seat, there is just 32 inches between the back of one seat and the back of the seat behind it. Films showed the dummies' heads hitting the seat ahead with tremendous force.
Mr. Gowdy undertook these tests at the request of the National Highway Traffic Safety Administration, manufacturers of child safety seats, the F.A.A., the National Transportation Safety Board and a few airline associations. The tests could not undo the certification provided by the highway safety agency. The brand names tested were given in the report, but not linked to the individual results.
Five brands of rear-facing seats performed well. One torso harness approved for airline use was too loose and too elastic and was judged unsatisfactory. A belly belt approved for use in England to hold a child less than 2 on an adult's lap was also unsatisfactory. "The impossibility of protecting a small child, by any means, sitting on the lap of an adult restrained by a seat belt was confirmed," the report said.
Seven models of forward-facing seats approved for both car and plane use were tested, plus one seat built overseas specifically for airplane use. Two failed because they could not be belted in. In the six others, the dummy's head received severe blows. When the airplane seat belt anchor was shifted 4.8 inches above the regular site and 4.5 inches to the rear, before the seat was installed, the dummy's head did not hit the seat ahead, but this adaptation cannot be made on the spot.
 
Seat Belts Found Better
 Four approved booster seats, the backless models with swing-aside shields that are often used for children over 2 because they are more compact to carry, were tested. One seat disintegrated. And in three, the dummy's head hit a locked seat back in the next row. If the child is big enough to ride in a booster seat, the report said, he or she will be better off using an adult seat belt.
A 3-year-old will get acceptable restraint in an adult belt although it will be near its minimum length, the report said.
The F.A.A., according to Fraser Jones, a spokesman, is working with the highway safety administration to revise the standards for the tests that certify the seats.
The F.A.A. report, "The Performance of Child Restraint Devices in Transport Airplane Passenger Seats" by Van Gowdy and Richard DeWeese, is available for $17.50 plus $4 shipping from the National Technical Information Service, 5285 Port Royal Road, Springfield, Va. 22161; (703) 487-4600.

HEADLINE: CLASSICAL VIEW;
The N.E.A. Is Elitist, True. It Should Be.

BYLINE: By Edward Rothstein 

 BODY:
DEBATES ABOUT THE NATIONAL endowment for the Arts tend to become fairly pious, so Americans will no doubt be hearing many grand proclamations beginning on Wednesday, when the Senate Appropriations Committee starts its hearings on the endowment. In recent months, House Speaker Newt Gingrich has attacked the Government agency for supplying "patronage for an elite group." The N.E.A. has responded by citing evidence of the spiritual, social and economic benefits of the arts for all Americans.
Both the attack and the defense are misguided. Populist conservative critics, in accusing the endowment of "elitism," are echoing a criticism the agency addressed long ago. In 1991, for example, endowment literature pointedly portrayed the agency as nonelitist, boasting that it served 170 ethnic minorities and insured a broad distribution of benefits, taking into account race, ethnicity, sex and geography. Unfortunately, those criteria have little to do with artistic judgment and much to do with social policy. When it comes to art, there is no way of judging even vastly divergent styles without some notion of differentiation and choice -- in other words, elitism. If the N.E.A. is doing its job properly, Mr. Gingrich should be praising it, not condemning it, for being elitist. 
 What's more, the endowment's defense that it is socially useful is both a distraction and a liability. In one of its publications, the N.E.A. said it supported arts education in schools because the arts improved grades, self-esteem, attendance and "social equity." But once art is treated as a form of therapy, either for society or for the individual, it must be judged as political or social policy. Some artworks have espoused the same goal, thus inviting political, not simply esthetic, criticism. The economic and social benefits of art should be secondary considerations, supplements not essentials. It is on the basis of the artworks and their importance to this country that the debate about the N.E.A. should be carried out.
A look at the N.E.A.'s music program may help clarify the issues. Mr. Gingrich's charge of elitism criticizes the audience as well as the art; he is asserting that Federal funds are supporting entertainment for the wealthy. At a time when budgets are being slashed in social services, why should the arts be immune?
But he has it wrong. Audiences at recitals, orchestra concerts and the opera are drawn largely from the middle class. And the performance of music -- art music as well as pop -- has traditionally been a way out of the lower classes for talented young people. Congress may still decide that support of music is too much of a luxury, in which case many Americans will mourn the loss and gaze in envy at more visionary civilizations.
Congress would be missing a great opportunity. Of all Government programs, the N.E.A. probably has the greatest impact, given the cost involved. Omus Hirshbein, who directs three endowment programs -- for music, opera and musical theater, and presenting institutions -- reports that less than $17 million is budgeted for music or music institutions in this fiscal year, a small fraction of the endowment's $167 million budget. The vast majority of presenting organizations, ranging from university associations to institutions like Symphony Space on the Upper West Side of Manhattan, will receive $15,000 or less; the largest grant to a single presenting agency will be $23,000. Smaller orchestras may receive a mere $5,000. The five largest orchestral grants are $236,400 apiece, amounting to less than 1 percent of each recipient's budget.
These sums have an importance far beyond their size. A grant from the N.E.A. provides an imprimatur that attracts additional support from local governments, foundations and private patrons. The endowment says each dollar it grants may be used to raise as much as $11 from other sources. And in some cases -- like grants given to composers -- the endowment plays the classic role of patron, bringing new works into existence.
The agency, then, can potentially supply crucial leadership in the music world at a very low cost. But is it up to the task? Are decisions made according to the rolling of various logs, personal or political? Can the N.E.A. act as a true patron, or must it act merely as an agency? Tone and taste become important.
THERE NOW SEEMS TO BE SOME HOPE. MR. Hirshbein, whose leadership of the performing arts program at the 92d Street Y was a boon to New York's musical life, inspires confidence. Since moving to the N.E.A. last May, he has expressed an interest, for example, in finding ways to increase touring by solo recitalists and jazz ensembles, to help underfinanced opera companies present interesting repertory, and to improve arts education by working with local school districts. He is also, he said, "looking for more efficient ways to bring composers and sponsors together."
Politics, of course, will still be an issue in the music program. It is bound to be, since the N.E.A. is by definition a political institution, meant to serve a diverse public. The question is, How can sophisticated and elite tastes be applied without falling prey to either orthodoxy or populism? Conflict is unavoidable. The hope is that the results will be both elite and thoroughly nontherapeutic.

HEADLINE: PERSPECTIVES;
A Big Brokerage Set to Descend on Midtown South

BYLINE: By ALAN S. OSER 

 BODY:
CORPORATE paths sometimes cross in unpredictable ways. Just as the Metropolitan Life Insurance Company was moving employees out of its imposing North Tower building at 11 Madison Avenue, at 25th Street, to make it available for general leasing, CS First Boston was facing a crucial decision. It had to decide where to park itself once its principal midtown lease expires in 1996.
These coinciding circumstances led eventually to a momentous handshake in January. CS First Boston, one of the city's largest brokerage and investment banking firms, agreed to lease 1.1 million square feet of space in the building for 20 years. That's more than half the building's total space. 
 The deal gives Met Life a fulltime guest in a corporate cluster it developed for its own use over the better part of the century. Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani announced the agreement Jan. 24. The final terms of the lease are still in negotiation.
For the Midtown South area in general, the deal widens the scope of business activity to include brokerage and investment banking, functions generally associated only with the midtown and downtown office districts. But it also demonstrates the key role of city government in holding large employers in the city.
CS First Boston wanted to stay in Manhattan, its officers said, but would not have been able to do so without a package of benefits it negotiated with the city's Economic Development Corporation.
"In our case, the economics of the deal meant everything," said Robert Raziano, chief financial and administrative office of CS First Boston. "Even with the city's package, premises costs would have been as much as 10 percent cheaper in the Jersey City enterprise zone. We were close to signing up to build a million square feet in what would have been a more efficient building. But we wanted the city's help to be able to stay in New York."
The city's package is linked to job growth. It will have a value in current dollars of $50.5 million over 20 years if employment grows to as many as 5,500 employees. The value of the deal is $32.8 million if employment stays at the current 3,700 level.
About 80 percent of the benefit will come in the form of exemptions from state and city sales taxes on equipment and machinery, a major factor as CS First Boston goes forward with what it says will be an investment of $300 million to prepare its 11 Madison Avenue space.
In interviews, officials of both companies elaborated on the events that got them together.
As recently as 1990, Met Life had about 6,400 people working in its 3.8-acre office cluster, east of Madison Square Park between 23d and 25th Streets, from Madison Avenue to Park Avenue. It has 3.3 million gross square feet of space on those two blocks, 1.1 million of it in the South Building, which includes the original Met Life Tower with its celebrated clock, and 2.2 million square feet in the North Building.
Of the total, 4,000 people worked in the North Building and 2,400 in the South Building. Now, total employment at Madison Square is down to 3,000, all in the South Building. A thousand employees have been moved to 200 Park Avenue, the former Pan Am building, which Met Life owns; another thousand throughout the region have taken voluntary separation. Other employees have been scattered to offices in the metropolitan area. Total Met Life employment in the metropolitan area has shrunk to 5,100.
Met Life is already far advanced on a $300 million upgrading program at 11 Madison Avenue, which was built in three stages between 1930 and 1950. It has 29 floors above street level and one of the city's longest, most elegant lobbies. The reconstruction work has included the demolition of mezzanine levels to create three 100,000-square-foot floors with 17-foot ceiling heights, well-suited for trading activities. It also includes a reconfiguration of the core of the building, removal of a bank of elevators, and updated electrical and mechanical systems.
Martin Turchin, the Edward S. Gordon & Company broker who was CS First Boston's real-estate adviser, said that there was no building currently available in midtown that met the space and configuration needs of the company. Even those developers with ready-to-build midtown sites apparently would not have been able to match the floor sizes available at 11 Madison Avenue.
CS First Boston is taking four 100,000-square-foot floors -- three through seven -- and the second floor, with 40,000 square feet. It will also have eight tower floors -- 21 through 28 -- totaling 400,000 square feet, for executives and investment bankers. And about 180,000 square feet below street level will be used for cafeterias, a conference center and other common facilities. Occupancy is expected to start early in 1997.
Almost $100 million of the projected $300 million in fit-out costs will be for the technological component of trading, sales and research. Technology requirements lift the occupancy costs of brokerage firms well above those of most manufacturing companies, Mr. Raziano said. They are usually 7 to 10 percent of operating revenues, or even higher in a bad year for the cyclical industry.
FIGURES from CS First Boston on employee dispersal underscore the centrality of Manhattan as a corporate center and of New York City as a residence for white-collar workers. Of the 3,700 midtown employees, 61 percent live in one or another of the five boroughs. Of the rest, 15 percent are in New Jersey, 9 percent in Long Island, 8 percent in Westchester, and 7 percent in Connecticut.
Jobs in the "front office" category account for 71 percent of total employment. Total annual compensation (including year-end bonuses) is typically in the range of $150,000 to $200,000. Back-office jobs account for 29 percent of the work force, with pay typically in the range of $50,000 to $75,000 a year.
In moving to 11 Madison Avenue, the company will give up nearly 500,000 square feet in Park Avenue Plaza, or 55 East 52d Street, a Fisher Brothers building, plus 100,000 square feet at Tower 49, at 12 East 49th Street; 30,000 square feet at 509 Madison Avenue, at 53d Street, and 35,000 square feet at 599 Lexington Avenue, between 52d and 53d Streets.
CS First Boston acquired the "CS" in its name in 1993, when First Boston inserted the designation in front of all its international affiliates. It stands for Credit Suisse, the Swiss bank that holds the principal ownership stake in the company.
Clay B. Lifflander, president of the city's Economic Development Corporation, said that in providing tax relief to induce large employers to stay in the city the Giuliani administration was trying to link a portion of the benefits to employment growth. Growth triggers a certain amount of the benefits.
"In the $50 million CS First Boston package, about $18 million is predicated on their growth over time," he said. Besides the sales-tax forgiveness, the benefits package includes partial real-estate tax abatements and discounts on energy costs.
Mr. Lifflander also said that in recent deals the city had managed to lower the average length of time it takes to recover forgiven taxes through the direct and indirect tax revenue generated when a company stays in the city. "In previous deals the paybacks were six to seven months," he said. "We're down to four months."
By the city's calculation, CS First Boston's presence in the city with 3,700 employees will generate $115 million a year in direct and indirect tax revenues. The cost to the city in forgone taxes is $21.8 million. Indirect revenues come largely from sales taxes generated by employee spending.
At 11 Madison Avenue, Cushman & Wakefield is the brokerage in charge of leasing the remaining 800,000 square feet. Market rents in the area for direct leases are in the low $20-a-square-foot area, said Jeffrey C. Heller, a leasing agent, but within the area nothing is comparable to 11 Madison Avenue, especially in the size of its floors.
"The building will compete with midtown," said Mr. Heller.

HEADLINE: Connecticut Q&A: Dr. Alan J. Sholomskas;
Treating Depression Among the Elderly

BYLINE: By JULIE MILLER 

 BODY:
THE signs of depression can easily be mistaken for the normal signs of aging, making it difficult to recognize the disorder and get treatment for an older person.
But an estimated 1 percent to 3 percent of elderly people experience major depression, and the figures rise to 15 percent to 20 percent among older adults in hospitals and nursing homes, said Dr. Alan J. Sholomskas, the recently named director of psychiatry at the new geriatric medical psychiatric unit at Masonic Home and Hospital in Wallingford. 
 The 15-bed unit was opened last October to meet the needs of a growing number of people over the age of 60 who have both psychiatric and medical problems.
"Such specialized care has not been available in standard psychiatric or medical-surgical units," Dr. Sholomskas said. "Instead, patients are shuttled back and forth between medical and psychiatric departments, which makes treatment complex."
Dr. Sholomskas was a consulting psychiatrist with Masonic Home and Hospital for eight years. A resident of Guilford, he has a private practice in New Haven, is an assistant professor of psychiatry at Yale University School of Medicine and is attending psychiatrist at Yale-New Haven Hospital and the Hospital of St. Raphael.
"Depression is every bit as treatable in older people as it is in the younger population, and there are reasons for treating it," Dr. Sholomskas said. "For one thing, it will improve their quality of life, which is a benefit to everyone."
In a telephone interview, Dr. Sholomskas discussed the strong ties between disease and depression and the reasons depression is overlooked in older adults. He also offered advice to family members caring for elderly relatives who may be depressed. Following are excerpts from that conversation:
Q. What are the signs of depression?
A. The main symptom is a sad mood or a loss of interest in life. This is the basic symptom. There are a number of other symptoms, although not all of them have to be present, including loss of more than 5 percent of body weight, decrease in appetite and sleeping difficulties. Some people either slow down or become agitated and overactive. Other symptoms are feelings of guilt or worthlessness, poor concentration and inability to make simple decisions. And finally, recurring thoughts of death, dying or suicidal thinking.
At least five of these symptoms must be present for at least two weeks in order to define major depression. Additional symptoms not on the basic list include anxiety, aches, pains, headaches and constipation. Hallucinations can occur when depression is so severe that a patient is out of touch with reality. Patients may hear voices telling them they have a fatal disease, they are poverty stricken or that terrible things will happen to them. The symptoms have a negative, self-punishing flavor.
Older people are less likely to report clear-cut sadness or depression. Instead, for example, a patient may say, "I feel terrible since the doctor gave me that antibiotic a month ago," when he actually feels depressed. Sometimes you may have to infer that a patient is depressed when he can't report it to you accurately. For example, a patient who has Alzheimer's disease can be depressed, but he wouldn't be able to say so. You would have to infer it on the basis of changes in behavior.
Q. Is depression a normal response to aging?
A. No. But it may be related to other problems of aging, such as loss of a spouse.
Q. Why does depression often go undiagnosed?
A. People are supposed to become more dysfunctional as they get older, so it is overlooked. Secondly, sometimes physicians feel there is no use in treating this disorder in a person who is not going to live that much longer. There's also the reluctance of elderly people to seek help, because they have grown up in a time when psychiatric help was not sanctioned.
In the general population, recent estimates are that 85 percent of depression goes untreated, so I would think it's at least that high among elderly people.
Q. What is the connection between depression and alcoholism or chronic disease?
A. Many people, either upon drinking or withdrawal from alcohol, develop depressive symptoms. Others drink as a way of alleviating depressive symptoms. Finally, there are those who have depression and drink, but the relationship isn't specific. They have different problems.
People who are chronically ill have a higher prevalence of depression than people who are not. In fact, among older people with depression, probably 80 percent or more have chronic illnesses. Also, specific chronic illnesses may contribute to depression. In Parkinson's disease, for example, depression develops as frequently as 40 percent of the time, and there is a very high prevalence associated with stroke.
Q. What factors place people at risk for depression?
A. Living alone, lack of social support, acute illness, severe pain and surgery. Loss of anything that contributes to their being involved with the world, such as vision and hearing. I would add retirement, children moving away and economic difficulties.
Limited mobility is a big factor. One man came to me depressed, and I initially thought it was because his wife had died recently. But that wasn't the case. She had been ill for years, and he felt relief after her death. But he lost his mobility when he ran his car into a telephone pole and totaled it. He was depressed because he no longer had a car.
Q. What treatments are available?
A. It depends on the patient's age, the severity of the illness, the types of stressors, whether or not the patient has a past history of depression and his ability to collaborate with treatment.
There are a number of psychotherapies. Those most useful for elderly people are short-term and behaviorally oriented. That is, they are less concerned with longstanding personality issues and the past than they are with current problems and attitudes toward those problems. Patients work on the negative thoughts that may contribute to depression.
Also, medications have been developed over the last 10 years that have fewer side effects. Electroconvulsive therapy, or shock therapy, although controversial, is the most effective treatment of all for serious depression. It can save a life.
Q. Can you explain electroconvulsive therapy?
A. Some people are so suicidal that they need to be under constant observation. Medications may take three to eight weeks to work and may not be safe to take because of co-existing medical conditions. Electroconvulsive therapy can work in as little as two weeks and is often safer. There are ways of doing it that minimize the effects of temporary amnesia that have occurred in the past. It has been refined. Two electrodes are applied to the head, and the very brief, small electrical current that is given is less than you would have if you had a brain seizure.
Finally, psychosocial treatments can be useful, such as certain types of group programs. Another example would be finding a doctor for a person who has a problem getting medical care, arranging transportation so he can get to the doctor's office, finding a nurse to make sure he takes his medications safely and someone to help him locate financial resources.
Q. Can depression spread to other family members?
A. Other people in the family may be a bit demoralized or discouraged, although they probably won't be clinically depressed. As caretakers, they may become stressed, anxious or worried. Some family members may withdraw from the depressed person and protect themselves by distancing. Others become overanxious and try to do too much for the person. Or they become angry and frustrated. On the other hand, they may just deny there is a problem.
Q. What should I do if I suspect a member of my family is depressed?
A. It depends on the relationship you have with the person and the resources available. You can contact the family doctor, who may be able to evaluate the person and decide whether to send him for more formal mental health treatment or to counsel and treat the person himself. There are psychiatrists who specialize in geriatrics. And there are geriatric assessment clinics, where an elderly person's condition can be evaluated.
Sometimes it's difficult if the person refuses treatment. You try finding a person who can exert some leverage. That may be another member of the family or the family physician. If it gets too severe, you may actually have to take the person in hand and take him to an emergency room or doctor's office. Sometimes there's not much you can do except monitor them.
There are a number of ways you can get psychiatric help. One is by calling your family physician. They usually have consultants they work with. You can call the University of Connecticut or Yale medical schools or the Mental Health Association of Connecticut. You can also call a hospital and ask for a referral.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Dr. Alan J. Sholomskas, director of psychiatry at Masonic Home and Hospital's new geriatric unit in Wallingford. (Thomas McDonald for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: New York Housing: Sellers Finally Sell

BYLINE: By NICK RAVO 

 BODY:
OWNERS of residential real estate in New York City, particularly those with co-ops, may look back on 1994 as the year when it once again became possible to sell their homes -- even studios and one-bedroom apartments. It still wasn't easy, and the big profits of the 1980's remained only a memory, but the increased demand and stable prices were a substantial improvement over the early 1990's, when market activity slowed drastically and prices fell.
The outlook for this year, however, is clouded by the specter of rising interest rates and fears that the region's economic recovery may be ending. Indeed, sales volume and prices for co-ops in Manhattan in 1994 -- though generally higher than in 1993 -- peaked last summer, according to the Real Estate Board of New York, and have been drifting downward for six months. 
 Mitigating this trend, though, is the fact that the traditional alternatives to buying real estate in the city have become unusually expensive. Market rents have rocketed in the last three years to near the peaks hit in the late 80's, largely because of the lack of new construction and a reluctance among many renters to buy. Suburban home prices, particularly in prime locations, are rising, too.
"New York City's real estate market has been making a slow, steady climb out of a recession," said Peter R. Marra, president of the William B. May Company, a Manhattan-based real estate broker.
Generalizations about real estate sales and prices in New York City are unusually risky, however. The market is as diverse as its population, and its trends are difficult to quantify. For instance, home sales prices rose by as much as 4 percent in 1994 in some parts of Queens, like Queens Village and Little Neck, according to an analysis of changes in single-family home sale prices by ZIP code in much of New York -- excluding Manhattan -- that was done for The New York Times by Case Shiller Weiss of Cambridge, Mass. In some parts of nearby Flushing, though, prices fell by as much as 8 percent.
Measuring the market is particularly confounding in Manhattan because the data are often sketchy, largely because the borough, south of 96th Street at least, is dominated by co-ops, for which sales are not publicly recorded; co-op sales are, technically, securities transactions and involve selling shares in a building rather than transferring a title to real property.
Elsewhere, the data can be even more imprecise. For example, in Brooklyn and the Bronx, where co-ops, single-family homes and condominiums are all found in large numbers, no central record-keeping of sales exists for any type of housing. In Queens, which also has a mix of co-ops, condominiums and single-family homes, only the sales of single-family homes are tracked by the New York State Association of Realtors -- but the figures are combined with those for Nassau and Suffolk Counties, and not broken out separately.
Only in Staten Island, where the market is made up mostly of single-family homes, is there reasonably exact data on sales volume. The number of homes sold there in 1994 rose 13.4 percent, to 887 from 782 in 1993, according to the New York State Association of Realtors.
Precise numbers also exist on the relatively small condominium market in Manhattan, where co-ops outnumber condominiums by about 6 to 1. According to data collected by Yale Robbins, a real estate publishing company, the number of condominiums sold in 1994 rose 16.5 percent, to 2,572, from 2,207 in 1993.
Though they are generally more expensive than co-ops, condominiums have increased in popularity since the late 80's, in large part because they have less restrictive ownership rules; in particular, they are easier for an owner to rent out. Common charges on a condominium, even after adding on property taxes, are also often lower than maintenance fees at comparable co-ops.
Further, buyers who are not citizens, or those who do not wish to divulge their finances, can buy a condominium without having to be scrutinized by a co-op board.
Elsewhere in the city, the volume of sales can be gleaned only from anecdotal evidence or, in the case of Manhattan co-ops, from reports prepared by real estate brokers surveying only a slice of the market.
In one of the larger surveys, produced by the Real Estate Board of New York, which incorporates transactions reported by several of the city's real estate brokers, the number of co-op sales in Manhattan rose by 26.8 percent, to 1,703 in 1994 from 1,343 in 1993; that number represents about 25 percent of the sales in the borough last year, according to the board.
The rise is generally attributed to relatively low prices, the increasing availability of financing, high rents for comparable apartments and a growing demand for larger co-ops because of an increase in households with children.
"Nineteen ninty-four was the year the co-op and condo market made its comeback," said Clark Halstead, founder of the Halstead Property Company, a Manhattan real estate brokerage.
Still, many apartment owners are having a difficult time selling. Laura and Gordon Hall, for example, have had their fourth-floor, two-bedroom, 1,100-square-foot apartment at 70 East 96th Street between Park and Madison Avenues on the northern edge of Carnegie Hill in Manhattan on the market for three years. They're asking $229,000; their maintenance is $1,023 a month. They have even had a few offers.
"We're optimistic," Mrs. Hall said. "We're getting more traffic. We're priced right."
As for sales price gains and losses in 1994, the picture in the city is even more difficult to capture than it is for sales volume. The annual report of the National Association of Realtors stated that the median home sales price for the city fell by 2.5 percent in 1994, to $168,700 from $173,000. That figure, though, does not include co-ops or condominiums, and it includes parts of Northern New Jersey, Long Island, Westchester County and Connecticut.
Even though the report is broad geographically and restricted to single-family homes, it does reinforce the widely held view among economists that the city's real estate market, overall, is in a period of relatively flat price appreciation, meaning a range from decreases of 1 percent or 2 percent year-to-year to increases of 2 to 4 percent, which is about the rate of inflation these days.
Part of this lackluster performance comes because of the waning influence of the baby boomers -- who as adults entered the starter-home market at the same time in the 80's and helped swell prices -- and the migration of young, educated workers and their families out of the city to the suburbs and, increasingly, other parts of the nation.
Indeed, Ms. Hall noted that should she sell her apartment she may move her family to Connecticut. "We have two kids, and there's more space, better schools," she said.
More important, perhaps, the economic recovery in the city in the last two years has been weak and appears to be fading.
"The New York City area has stalled and, frankly, the region is flirting with recession," said David G. Hensley, a vice president and economist with Salomon Brothers in New York. "The culprits are downsizing on Wall Street and in government."
The co-op market, in particular, has been the source of much negative publicity in the last five years, which has raised fears among many potential buyers. The publicity generally concerns complex financial troubles involving newly converted co-ops or large losses suffered by those who bought overpriced studios and small one-bedroom apartments in the 80's and then saw prices plunge by as much as 40 percent.
"I'll never buy in the city again," said Shelley Warren, who owns a 1,000-square-foot one-bedroom co-op at 317 East 70th Street on the Upper East Side of Manhattan. "I don't want to deal with the boards. I hear horror stories."
Ms. Warren, a garment saleswoman, has had several offers to buy her co-op for $145,000. She has, however, decided against selling -- except, perhaps, at a higher price -- because she wants to rent but can't afford a rental comparable in size and locale.
"I'm not sure what I am going to do now," she said.
Despite the lingering fear of co-ops, sale prices in 1994 appear to be rising off the bottoms hit in 1992 and 1993, as the memory of the real estate recession fades. Many co-op prices have also fallen so low that they are now cheaper to own and maintain than a rental apartment.
"Overall, we expect to see a modest increase in values, probably 1 to 3 percent," said Caroline Blakely, vice president with the National Cooperative Bank. "The hottest markets are the upper end -- the Upper East Side, Upper West Side, downtown."
These gains, however, are little more than estimates. The main method used by most brokers to measure appreciation -- comparing median sales prices in a given area -- is generally considered by economists and statisticians to be of limited use in a real estate market because its results can be skewed by a lack of market activity or a pattern of dissimilar sales in different years. The data from the Real Estate Board of New York, for example, on quarterly price appreciation for co-op apartments by room size for the entire borough is based on fewer than 100 sales in most quarters.
The Real Estate Board's data is also biased geographically, with 75 percent of the sales occurring between 57th and 96th Streets and slightly more than half coming from the Upper East Side. It also reflects sales made with the record bonuses paid out on Wall Street last spring, as well as mortgages with interest rates that were close to 20-year lows during the early parts of 1994. Since then, interest rates have increased by more than 2 percentage points, and Wall Street, pummeled by losses in the bond market last year, has cut back on bonuses and increased their pink slips.
Nevertheless, the numbers, and similar samples of broker sales figures, are the only statistics that exist for co-ops in Manhattan. According to the Real Estate Board of New York, median sale prices for studio apartments rose from $34,000 at the end of 1993 to $40,250 per room at the end of 1994; studios generally have one to two-and-a-half rooms. One-bedroom apartments rose from $51,548 to $55,000 per room; two-bedroom apartments rose from $72,889 to $78,889 per room; three-bedroom co-ops rose from $112,500 to $113,571 per room and those with four or more bedrooms fell from $187,500 to $158,750 per room.
Though the price per room for the largest apartments fell on a fourth quarter-to-fourth quarter basis, it rose -- to $172,822 from $168,182 -- when sales are compiled and compared for all of 1993 and 1994.
Brokers generally believe that the latter method gives a fuller and more accurate snap-shot of what is happening in the market these days.
"The market for luxury or family-size apartments is very competitive," said William Hunt 3d, co-founder of Hunt Kennedy Inc., a Manhattan real estate brokerage.
"More than any other area of the market, prices for luxury residences are being spiked up by a population explosion and baby boomlet," Mr. Hunt said. "New Yorkers are having children and buying more bedrooms."
For condominiums, the median price per room, according to Yale Robbins, stayed the same for studios and one-bedroom units -- about $60,000 for studios and $51,000 for one-bedroom apartments. For condominiums with two or three bedrooms, however, the per-room price increased from $94,400 to $110,000, further highlighting the demand for large apartments.
In an even more rarefied sector of the Manhattan real estate market, there were 53 town house sales in Manhattan last year compared with 46 the year before, according to Douglas Elliman, a Manhattan real estate broker. Many of them sold for $1 million to $2 million.
"When town-house sales dropped so substantially a few years ago, people came to realize that for the price of a rather expensive co-op apartment they could actually own a house," said Elizabeth Stribling, president of Stribling and Associates, a Manhattan real estate firm.
Outside of Manhattan, for a survey of price changes in single-family homes -- excluding condominiums and co-ops -- in Staten Island, the Bronx, Brooklyn and Queens, The New York Times used a computer analysis by Case Shiller Weiss. The company compares sale price differences in pairs of homes in the same area that were resold in 1993 and 1994. An average of 44 home sales were surveyed in each ZIP code.
That survey showed that prices of single-family homes rose the most, by at least 2 percent, in some instances by more than 4 percent, in Jamaica, Floral Park and a few other places in Queens as well as in parts of the Bronx and Staten Island. Prices also dropped the most in parts of Flushing -- by as much as 7 and 8 percent in two ZIP codes. They also fell by as much as 2 percent in other neighborhoods in Queens, like Long Island City, and in parts of Brooklyn.
"We see stable prices in Brooklyn and Queens," said Ms. Blakely with the National Cooperative Bank.

HEADLINE: YOUR TAXES;
For Social Security Recipients, Higher Rates Loom

BYLINE: By CAROLE GOULD 

 BODY:
AS their 1994 tax returns are prepared, many retirees will discover that a much bigger chunk of their Social Security benefits is taxable.
The situation is quite irksome to Joseph Feinberg, a retired salesman who works part-time to supplement his retirement income. He puts in roughly 20 hours a week at the Bergen Tile Corporation in Brooklyn and would be happier to work more hours to earn more -- if those extra earnings were not going to tip the Social Security scale and devour more of his benefits. 
 "That money could pay for medications or other essentials that we need, especially now that my income is more limited," he said. "If I could work another 10 hours a week without being penalized, I'd do that."
The situation is also irksome to the Republicans, newly in power, and there is talk in Washington about rolling back the tax increase, which was passed as part of the 1993 tax act and took effect last year. Better-off Social Security recipients face paying income taxes on up to 85 percent of their benefits under the new law, compared with 50 percent under the old law.
Despite the complaints, tax experts do not foresee a rollback.
There will be a big wrinkle in the debate when Congress picks up the Social Security tax issue: money generated by the increase is dedicated to the Medicare trust fund. Hence, eliminating that source of revenue would require new sources to be found, and those could prove even more politically unpopular than the higher taxes on Social Security, experts say.
"I'm predicting that when Congress gets serious about examining the proposal, by the time the Senate looks at it, they might decide it's the lesser of two evils," said Pamela Pecarich, director of tax policy for the accounting firm Coopers & Lybrand, in Washington. "It may be easier to keep the new rules intact than reduce Medicare further."
Indeed, the cost of eliminating the increase would be great. The Treasury Department has estimated that the new tax on benefits should raise $15 billion in revenue during the first five years and $48.5 billion by the year 2005. Repealing the tax increase would be the fourth most costly item in the Republicans' Contract with America, the Treasury Department has calculated.
Because doing so would yield political pluses, "they'll try to do it, but it will be very difficult," said Richard A. Grafmeyer, a tax lawyer with the accounting firm Ernst & Young, in Washington.
Retirees under age 70 who have filed for Social Security benefits and then gone back to work, even part time, have long faced tax consequences, and those basic Social Security rules on benefits are not changing. People under age 65 must give up $1 in benefits for every $2 they earn over a certain amount, which is indexed annually for inflation; in 1994, that amount was $8,040, and for 1995 it will be $8,160. Retirees aged 65 through 69 forfeit $1 in benefits for every $3 earned above $11,160 in 1994 ($11,280 in 1995). People older than 70 can earn as much as they like without relinquishing any benefits.
Currently, the Internal Revenue code further subjects retirees to income taxes on 50 percent of their benefits when their adjusted income exceeds $32,000 for couples and $25,000 for single taxpayers. (Adjusted income includes adjusted gross income, tax-free interest, one-half of Social Security benefits and certain foreign-source income.)
But the new tax law goes a painful step farther, taxing up to 85 percent of the benefits of retirees whose adjusted income surpasses a second threshold: $44,000 for couples and $34,000 for singles.
"What's unfair about the 85 percent bracket is that people collecting Social Security contributed to the fund and paid tax already on the money," said Lou Lieberman, a Long Island accountant. "Why should people be penalized because they were able to accumulate wealth?"
Still, many people overestimate the impact of the new rule because they look at the total amount of benefits taxable, not just the increase. Because the tax is highly progressive, "it hits hardest at upper-income taxpayers, not so much at people with moderate incomes" of $55,000 or less, Ms. Pecarich said.
For example, a retired couple earning $54,000 would pay only $1,550 more in tax, she figured. By contrast, a couple earning $100,000 would pay $4,900 more.
To figure the actual increase, one must work through a complicated formula.
For example, a married couple with adjusted income of $47,000 and Social Security benefits of $14,000 -- about the maximum for 1994 -- exceeded the old $32,000 threshold by $15,000. So they paid taxes on $7,000, which equals the lesser of one-half of that excess or one-half of their Social Security benefits.
Now their adjusted income exceeds the new threshold of $44,000 by $3,000. Eighty-five percent of that excess equals $2,550. Add that to $6,000 (the lesser of their prior taxable amount of $7,000, or one-half the difference between the old threshold of $32,000 and the new one, $44,000) for a total of $8,550. That's the taxable amount, because it is less than 85 percent of their total Social Security benefits, which equals $11,900.
So their new taxable amount of $8,550 is $1,550 more than the amount on which they would have paid taxes in 1993.
There are very few ways to wiggle out from the tax increase.
One option for single retirees, both of whom collect Social Security, and who are thinking of marrying is to skip the wedding. This lets them sidestep the substantial "marriage penalty" built into the new tax rule.
The penalty arises because the new, higher threshold for the tax on Social Security benefits for single filers is only $10,000 less than that for married filers, allowing single persons to earn proportionately much more than married couples before their new threshold kicks in.
For example, two single retirees each with total income of $37,000, $12,000 of which was from Social Security, would each be taxable on $3,000 of their benefits because their adjusted income, $31,000, did not exceed the new threshold for singles, Ms. Pecarich said. If they married, they would immediately pass the second-tier threshold, and $20,400 of their total Social Security benefits would be taxed, an increase of $14,400.
Another option for retirees, single or married, is to try staggering income, thus creating alternating years of lower and higher income. Depending on income level, this strategy may result in their Social Security benefits being taxed only every other year.
There are several ways to manipulate dates of income. People can time withdrawals from individual retirement accounts, redemptions of United States savings bonds or sales of appreciated property to occur in alternate years. Social Security recipients might also try to stagger the dates that any United States Treasury bills fall due.

HEADLINE: A SENSE OF PLACE

BYLINE: By Glenn Collins 

 BODY:
WELL, who's this, making a surprise visit? Why, it's Mr. Toll, the legendary New Jersey Department of Transportation answer man. He is ready to respond to all those thorny travel-related questions about New Jersey's highways and byways. Here are some of the most commonly asked questions about the state's newest controversy.
Mr. Toll, what should we make of all this publicity about a merger of New Jersey's major highways? 
 I'm so glad you asked -- because this is the hottest topic right now in New Jersey transportation. It's true: Gov. Christine Todd Whitman said she'd like to merge the state's three largest toll-road authorities into one mega-agency. This would consolidate, and run, the Garden State Parkway, the New Jersey Turnpike and the Atlantic City Expressway. And we hope all of them will be run by the Department of Transportation.
The new statewide highway, or parkway, or turnpike, or expressway -- what will it be called?
No formal name has been chosen. But several possibilities are being considered -- the New Jersey Turnparkway, the Garden State Expresspike and the Atlantic City Turnway, not to mention a dark-horse candidate, the Garden Refinery Roadway. Until the state's voters can put it to a referendum, though, Mr. Toll can reveal that the working title, here at our agency, is: the New Jersey Dotway.
Why Dotway, Mr. Toll?
You know. For D.O.T., the Department of Transportation.
Oh. Well, why can't the Governor leave well enough alone?
Governor Whitman has said that merging the parkway, the turnpike and the expressway could eliminate bureaucracy, provide economies of scale in purchasing and ultimately reduce costs to motorists.
Will highway service be improved?
You have Mr. Toll's guarantee about that. Just consider: all of those traffic jams on the turnpike, the parkway and the expressway will now be merged into one centrally consolidated, less bureaucratic traffic jam on the sole state highway, the New Jersey Dotway. This concentration will insure that congestion is both easier to identify and to avoid. Unless you are unlucky enough to be stuck right in the middle of it.
How will tollbooths be affected?
Tollbooth imbalance is a serious matter that will be dealt with quickly in the initial merger of the three current New Jersey highways. A crash program of tollbooth construction is planned for the turnpike and the expressway, which are relatively devoid of tolls, to bring them in line with the optimum average on the Garden State Parkway, where tollbooths are currently placed on approximately every 25 feet of roadway.
How soon will the merger occur?
The plan will be approved very quickly, just as soon as consultants have been selected to study the feasibility of the merger. After their report, and the selection of the all-important secondary consultants who will carefully review the recommendations of the first, or original, consultants, rapid approval will be forthcoming. Of course, the merger is contingent upon the final consultant consensus that will be achieved, thanks to the third set of consultants that will be hired to review the work of all the previous teams.
But Mr. Toll, won't motorists be confused by the signs for the old turnpike, parkway and expressway?
Certainly not, since they'll be directed to their destinations by the bright, easy-to-read new signs that will replace the state's old, familiar highway logos.
But how will motorists know how to find the new highways if the old turnpike, parkway and expressway signs are gone?
Why, just look for the new statewide logo of the New Jersey Dotway. Mr. Toll refers to Dottie, the cute, cartoon-figure boa constrictor that extends its perky coils all over the Garden State. The logo was designed by the very same sophisticated minds who gave the state those New Jersey specialty license plates showing a lighthouse emblazoned with the slogan "Shore to Please."
Mr. Toll, will the new merged highways be privatized?
There is every possibility that such an enrichment to the state coffers can be achieved. Active negotiations for a potential sale are currently being conducted with a variety of respected firms in the private sector that have often done business with the state. The field has narrowed down to some especially spirited bidders, like the Hoffa Corporation and the Wiseguy Group Ltd.
How will highway tolls be affected?
Thanks to the cost of new signs, advertising and administration costs, tolls on the new Dotway will undergo a minimal surcharge of only 50 percent during the initial, or merger, period. Eventually, thanks to the long-term demands of consolidated highway management, tolls will no more than double.
Mr. Toll, will there be resistance to the planned consolidation of the highways?
In addition to the three highways' 3,500 employees, who rightly fear job reduction during the consolidation, some New Jerseyans will object because they have a nostalgic preference for the highway names of yesteryear. Mr. Toll is delighted to reveal, however, that no opposition is expected from Local 509A of the International Brotherhood of Bridge and Tunnel Trolls. The membership has voted overwhelmingly to support the merger unless its bridges are directly threatened in the new highway era.
Will other revenue-enhancing innovations be part of the merger?
Certainly! One that can currently be revealed by Mr. Toll: The Department of Transportation will start charging standard billboard advertising rates for the annoying little stickers that people place in the tollbooth coin baskets to get free publicity for their products.
Will the three different highways be made to resemble each other?
Yes. A three-phase plan is currently contemplated for the gradual integration of the visual environment of the three formerly independent roads. In phase one, scheduled for 1995, refinery accents will be added to the green environs of the Garden State Parkway and the Atlantic City Expressway. In phase two, scheduled for 1996, the refinery corridor of the turnpike will be reforested by the State Department of Environmental Protection. And in phase three, scheduled for 1997, sea gulls, salt marshes and gambling-casino stretch limousines will be transplanted from the Atlantic City Expressway to the turnpike and the parkway.
Mr. Toll, please tell us: Why have so many previous merger attempts failed?
Because powerful vested interests have selfishly chosen to oppose such reform.
But what has now changed, to make a merger possible?
Officials and workers on every level of the New Jersey highway-authority bureaucracy have accepted the necessity of the consolidation plan, and they are delighted to sacrifice their continuing employment for the greater good of the state.
Are you kidding, Mr. Toll?
Of course. Mr. Toll has always been famous throughout the state for his wry wit -- not to mention his jocular effervescence.

HEADLINE: World Markets;
Emerging Markets Still Have Appeal

BYLINE: By Paul Lewis 

 BODY:
"POOR Mexico! So far from God and so close to the United States," said then-President Porfirio Diaz at the turn of the century when he sought sympathy for his country's predicament. Today he would have to admit that being America's neighbor has its advantages.
Now, when Mexico's economic luck runs out, Washington promptly declares a "systemic crisis" that threatens to send a recessionary shock wave throughout the developing world and mobilizes a $50 billion rescue package. The all-American $20 billion slice of that package was approved last week. And in an immediate sign of reviving confidence, private investors Wednesday only sold back to the Mexican Government $1.4 billion of short-term bonds, called tesebonos, although it offered to redeem $2 billion worth. 
 But unanswered questions remain. The International Monetary Fund has agreed to find another $17.8 billion. But where is the $10 billion that the Europeans are supposed to provide through the Bank for International Settlements, to say nothing of $3 billion promised by American and other private banks? And what about those new long-term bonds that Finance Minister Guillermo Ortiz said "the finest minds in America" were designing to replace expiring tesobonos so Mexico need not redeem them for dollars?
The Mexican Government admits that it no longer expects 1 percent growth this year, not with interest rates at well above 40 percent on an annual basis.
Finance officials are putting the finishing touches on a revised stabilization plan that will take into account that their initial estimates of the crisis were far too optimistic. A group of American economists led by Jude Wanniski and backed by such Republican heavyweights as Senator Bob Dole, the majority leader, and Jack Kemp, the Housing Secretary in the Bush Administration, want Mexico to use its emergency aid to buy its own currency and revalue the peso back to 3.5 to the dollar.
All these concerns are keeping many international investors wary of Mexico's markets, though not of other emerging markets. Judging by what fund managers say, the emerging markets game is far from over despite the reverses in Mexico.
"It's a bit early to look at equities in Mexico, but we'll probably be putting more money into other emerging markets this year," said Robert Heisterberg, senior vice president at the Alliance Capital Management group. "The developing world has the best growth."
Most emerging markets weakened perceptibly in the immediate backwash of Mexico's botched devaluation on Dec. 20, which is why the United States and the International Monetary Fund declared a "systemic crisis," fearing massive capital flight from all such markets.
But today the performances of Asian and Latin American markets are diverging.
Since Dec. 19, the day before the Mexican peso was devalued, Baring Securities' global emerging markets index has fallen 22.6 percent in dollar terms. But its Asian index is only down 3.79 percent while its European index, which covers Portugal, Turkey and Greece, is down 0.14 percent.
Its Latin American index, however, has tumbled a substantial 37.94 percent over the same period. But even within Latin America, differences are emerging.
While the Mexican portion of Baring's Latin American index is down 49.3 percent, the fall for Brazil is 38.06 percent, for Argentina 30.05 percent and for Chile, 13.47 percent. The Mexican crisis no longer looks systemic.
Taking a long view, investors who entered emerging markets early are still well ahead. The International Finance Corporation's global emerging market index, which started life at 100 in December 1984, stands today at 489.40, down some 15 percent from its September 1994 high of 623.87.
So it is not surprising to find many money managers still interested in emerging markets. Bill Wilbey, who runs Oppenheimer's Global Equity Fund, has 17 percent of his money in emerging markets. He has recently been moving back into Hong Kong and other Asian markets, though his exposure is well below the 40 percent he had there in 1993.
Justin Scott of the Putnam Group dislikes Latin America as a whole but says he will probably go back into Asia this year after reducing exposure there to 10 percent from 25 percent during 1994.
Michael Howell at Baring Securities keeps 20 percent of funds in emerging markets, mainly in Asia but also including Poland and Turkey.
Clark B. Winter, who runs the New York office of the London-based Global Asset Management fund group, says his GAM International Fund has always preferred Asia to Latin America and finds some shares there "very cheap."
For Morgan Stanley's Marianne Hay, however, the lesson of the Mexican crisis is that while emerging economies have the fastest growth and the highest profitability, their stock markets "now all offer much better value than they did a year ago."
