HEADLINE: The Best Ex-Job In the City;
After 13 Months Out of Office, Dinkins Is Basking in Affection

BYLINE: By ALAN FINDER 

 BODY:
When he occupied the cozy office in City Hall's northwest corner, David N. Dinkins was fond of saying that no job was better than being Mayor of New York City. But in the 13 months since he left office, Mr. Dinkins appears to have found an even better job: former Mayor.
Everywhere he goes these days, Mr. Dinkins finds himself basking in the glow of New Yorkers' affections. He cannot walk down the street or enter a restaurant without someone shaking his hand and wishing him well. 
 "You've still got my vote," a Federal Express deliveryman said as Mr. Dinkins walked on Park Avenue South on a recent afternoon.
"You look wonderful," gushed a young woman, celebrating her wedding anniversary with a lunch at a modest midtown restaurant where Mr. Dinkins was having a quick cup of soup. "You look 10 years younger."
When he stops for a red light while behind the wheel of his burgundy Ford Crown Victoria, drivers in nearby cars hit their horns and wave enthusiastically, apparently astonished that a man who once traversed the city with a retinue of detectives and aides is now traveling solo.
Mr. Dinkins, 67, takes it all in with his customary reserve, enjoying the attention quietly and politely. He never notes that it is odd that he should be feted so warmly each day by the ordinary New Yorkers who so recently turned him out of office.
He simply accepts the response graciously, as he makes his rounds in the busy and public life he has fashioned for himself since departing City Hall. His schedule allows him to make a comfortable living and still indulge in many of the things he loves most: discussions of politics and government, family, social gatherings and tennis -- though not necessarily in that order.
He teaches one day a week at Columbia University's School of International and Public Affairs, where he is a professor in the practice of urban affairs. Last fall, he taught a large lecture class on critical issues in urban policy. He has just begun a small seminar this semester on urban politics.
Mr. Dinkins also is the host, twice a week, of an hourlong interview and call-in show, "Dialogue With Dinkins," on WLIB-AM (1190). He presents his views on a range of issues, especially the administration of his successor, Rudolph W. Giuliani. Never a flamboyant speaker or prone to go for the political jugular, Mr. Dinkins nonetheless manages mildly to second-guess Mr. Giuliani quite often on the air.
Recently, for example, he criticized Mr. Giuliani's Police Commissioner, William J. Bratton, for saying that police officers could frisk someone who was drinking alcohol on a street corner. "The law as he states it I believe to be inaccurate," Mr. Dinkins said.
The former Mayor also works as a consultant for Ronald O. Perelman, the financier who is the chairman of the Revlon Corporation. Mr. Dinkins's office on Madison Avenue is next door to another politician-turned-consultant, Andrew J. Stein, the former City Council president.
Mr. Dinkins also sits on four corporate boards, including the American Stock Exchange and New World Communications, a company controlled by Mr. Perelman that owns television stations. He is a member of at least a dozen boards of nonprofit, charitable groups.
And while Mr. Dinkins is no longer a candidate for elected office, he is still out on the "rubber-chicken circuit," the litany of receptions, dinners, cocktail parties, benefits, fund-raisers and award ceremonies that are the staple of an elected official's existence. At least once or twice a day, he makes an appearance or gives a speech at a social gathering.
He recently presented an award to Kitty Carlisle Hart at a theater ceremony and enjoyed a dinner party afterward at the Russian Tea Room. He attended a ceremony at a recreation center in the Rockaways that was renamed in his honor, and he spoke at a party honoring a judge who is an old friend.
On many nights, he crams two or three parties into his schedule, which he fusses over with the attention that some people give to their stock portfolios.
"I genuinely like people," Mr. Dinkins said, explaining his active social calendar. "That which is a burden for some is a pleasure for me."
He even returned briefly to Gracie Mansion not long ago to attend a cocktail party for a retiring radio reporter. It was impossible to tell, by his practiced ease in chatting with other guests, whether he felt any discomfort at being back in the mansion that was his home from 1990 to 1994.
Mr. Dinkins and Mr. Giuliani joked about his presence. "You're welcome anytime," Mr. Giuliani said, before adding, "as a guest."
Mr. Dinkins's subdued grace no doubt obscures deeper emotions aroused by his narrow defeat in November 1993 to Mr. Giuliani, a white Republican who outpolled Mr. Dinkins, the city's first black Mayor -- a Democratic incumbent in an overwhelmingly Democratic city.
Mr. Dinkins's transition back to private life seems to have gone smoothly. For much of the last decade -- first as Manhattan Borough President and then as Mayor -- he was chauffeured by drivers or detectives. Caricatures of his mayorality often featured the aide who trailed behind him, carrying Mr. Dinkins's neatly pressed suit jacket on a hanger.
The retinue is gone now. His police security was withdrawn six months after he left office, as was Edward I. Koch's, under city tradition. Mr. Dinkins now drives himself. "It's like riding a bicycle," he said.
But he is also clearly disturbed that the city does not provide security to its former chief executives. He just will not say so directly.
"This is not the time for me to talk about it," he said. "I'll do that one of these days, when it's clear that it does not apply to me, when I'm talking about Rudy and his successors."
During a recent radio show, Mr. Dinkins was talking about Mr. Giuliani's attempts to shut down the city's fire-alarm boxes. His guest, Councilman Sheldon S. Leffler, a Queens Democrat who is the chairman of the Public Safety Committee, noted that the Council had approved a bill to block Mr. Giuliani's proposal by a vote of 47 to 2.
"With this Mayor," Mr. Leffler said of Mr. Giuliani, "you have to say he has the courage of his convictions. Forty-seven members of the Council disagree, but he wants to go ahead."
Mr. Dinkins then offered his own view. "Are you familiar with the story of the mother watching her son parading at West Point? She said, 'Look, everyone's out of step except my son.' "
Mr. Dinkins offered many other criticisms of Mr. Giuliani on his show, during his Columbia classes and in an interview. He said that Mr. Giuliani had apparently failed to include any black or Hispanic people among his core advisers; doing otherwise, he said, could have given him a broader perspective. And he said that Mr. Giuliani had been poorly served by Deputy Mayor John S. Dyson, who made several remarks that were widely considered to be racially charged.
Mr. Dinkins said, too, that Mr. Giuliani made a mistake by scheduling his inauguration last year on a Sunday morning, when many New Yorkers, including many black Protestants and Catholics, were in church.
He said the Mayor could save some vital social services from budget cuts by pressing the police and firefighters' unions for money-saving concessions. And he suggested that Mr. Giuliani should modulate his overall tone.
"I think that he would be better advised not to be combative on as many issues as he is, which doesn't mean that he is wrong on every issue," Mr. Dinkins said.
Mr. Dinkins carefully monitors the press, eager to protect his legacy. He has been quick to fault the press when he has thought it has been inattentive to his contributions. Mr. Dinkins noted on a recent radio show, for example, that newspaper articles on a dispute between Mr. Giuliani and the Council over creating an independent agency to monitor police corruption sometimes mentioned that it was the Mollen Commission that first recommended such a step.
"They seem rarely to go the next step to say that the Mollen Commission was appointed by Dinkins," he said.
Mr. Dinkins also appears to share the view of some of his political supporters, who believe the press, and the newspapers in particular, have treated Mr. Giuliani more kindly than they did him.
On his radio show not long ago, a caller said that some politicians and newspaper editorial boards had recommended a state takeover of the city's budget when Mr. Dinkins had been in charge, but that no such calls have been made while Mr. Giuliani struggled to balance the budget.
"It's plain and simple racism," the unidentified caller said.
Mr. Dinkins responded with characteristic indirectness. "I have said many times -- I don't dwell on it -- that racism exists in our society, but that most people are not racists," he said.
Then he added, "I think Rudy has had a free ride with the press, but I think it's coming to an end."

HEADLINE: Older Suburbs Struggle to Compete With New

BYLINE: By KAREN DE WITT, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LAKEWOOD, Ohio

 BODY:
The grand mansions along Lake Erie here are still splendid, and the modest homes farther from the lakefront still neat and well kept. Even the affluent in this suburb next door to Cleveland send their children to its public schools. But all is not as stable as it seems.
Like hundreds of older suburbs across the nation, Lakewood is fighting to maintain its middle-class manner. But even as it struggles to fend off problems associated with cities -- rising crime, dwindling services and fading schools -- Lakewood finds that newer, more outlying suburbs are luring away middle-class home buyers and with them the tax base needed to stave off decline. 
 Even though last fall's Republican triumphs may have marked the rise of America's suburbs to political preeminence, the traditional antagonism between city and suburb has been joined by rising tensions between old and new suburbs. And so far, the older suburbs appear to be the losers.
"We still have this fantasy image of the suburbs of the 1950's, which tends to mean Ozzie and Harriet and the nuclear family in the backyard," said Peter Dreier, the E. P. Clapp distinguished professor of politics at Occidental College in Los Angeles, who was a senior adviser to Raymond L. Flynn when he was Mayor of Boston. "But all suburbs are not the same. Gritty, blue-collar suburbs and older suburbs are having the same problems as the inner city."
The 1990 census found for the first time that scores of suburbs farthest from their metropolitan cores were drawing the bulk of new industrial and commercial investment and the kind of residents who provide healthy tax bases. The center cities and nearby suburbs like Lakewood were the losers. Lakewood has not suffered the fate of East Cleveland, Cleveland's first suburb, once the home of John D. Rockefeller and now now pockmarked with boarded-up factories and rubble-choked lots.
But it is falling behind places like Rocky River, five miles farther west, where Michael McIntyre, a lifelong Lakewood resident, recently moved with his wife, Elizabeth.
"Our concern with Lakewood was taxes," said the 30-year-old Mr. McIntyre, a reporter with The Cleveland Plain Dealer. "Lakewood is an aging community that had a lot of character, but there are lots of people and not a lot of businesses to support the tax base."
Lakewood is Cleveland's second oldest suburb, with half of its housing built before 1918. The median home price is $73,000, offering the cheapest suburban housing in the Cleveland metropolitan area. Rents too are low, $382 for a one-bedroom unit, which attracts Cleveland residents looking for affordable suburban living. Half of Lakewood residents are renters. With 5.5 square miles and 60,000 residents, Lakewood is the most densely populated city in Ohio, with 10,858 people per square mile, compared with Cleveland's 6,566 per square mile. While the city's population declined from 70,000 in 1980, the number of low-income households headed by women is on the rise, undermining the tax base. Nearly 10 percent of the residents now receive some form of welfare. And social problems like pregnancies among unwed teen-agers are not as rare in the local high school as they once were. Older residents voted down tax levies for the local schools four times since 1993, helping create a projected $1 million deficit for the 1995-96 school year.
Once solidly white, Lakewood has managed to integrate itself with few problems and now has 4 percent minority residents. An aggressive program of backyard trash pickup, parenting programs for teen-agers, and tough housing inspectors who cite residents for even one broken porch baluster, have helped it keep its reputation as a good place to live. Most of the crime is juvenile delinquency; there has been only one homicide in five years.
The strains being felt here have their echoes across the country, particularly around older industrial cities in the the northern tier of states from Massachusetts to Minnesota. Officials in places like Lowell and Somerville outside Boston, Cicero and Harvey outside Chicago, the New Haven area and the suburbs around Minneapolis are worrying whether people, wealth and political power will continue to move ever outward and what the consequences might be for the areas left behind.
"We know we're in competition with those newer suburbs, so we're doing everything we can to maintain our housing stock, our services and our public schools," said Jo Ann Boscia, president of the Lakewood City Council.
Thomas E. Bier, director of the Housing Policy Research Program at Cleveland State University, sees no halt to the outward movement. The most extensive research on urban-suburban migration studies have been done in the Cleveland area by the university's urban center, using not only national census data but data from city and county agencies as well. In a 1993 study, Mr. Bier noted that home sellers moved farther from Cleveland and its near suburbs at five times the rate of those moving into the area.
David Harbarger, Lakewood's Mayor, said he considered the issues for people deciding where to move in the suburbs were equally schools, safety and city services. "If any one area is in decay," he said, "the others can't hold it up."
Paula Reed, a local real estate agent who described herself as a "died-in-the-wool Lakewoodite" said she was considering moving because the community had turned down the school tax levies.
"We've got two children, 10 and 13, and their most important school years are coming up," she said. "The people in Lakewood can't afford not to keep the schools up. If we can't afford to attract young families who can afford to keep these properties up, we're dead."
Fueling the outmigration in the Cleveland metropolitan area, said Timothy Hagan, president of the Cuyahoga County Commissioners, is the state legislature's recent decision to expand Interstate 90 to eight lanes from four lanes westward from just inside the Cuyahoga County line, about eight miles west of Lakewood.
"The suburban communities are calling for increasing the capacity of the highways to make it easier for those commuters to go from suburbs to suburb and from the fringe of one county to another county," said Mr. Hagan. "With those counties' expansion come sewers and water and spending that moves farther and farther away from the mother city, the county seat, the engine that generated this growth."
Ms. Boscia drove a visitor around on a recent overcast winter day, pointing out the private beach and beachfront homes that gave Lakewood the feel of a Sausalito on the frozen lake, as well as the two-family houses, or doubles, in the heavily ethnic, blue-collar Birdtown area.
For the moment, Lakewood remains stable. But the whether it will stay that way is doubtful. No limit to the movement outward is in sight, most analysts say.
"Who can begin to say where it ends," said Mr. Bier. "It's not unusual in the greater Los Angeles area to drive 100 miles to work every day. The outer reaches of Cleveland are 25 miles or so. You're living in the country at 35 miles so that by L.A. standards, we could continue for decades going farther and farther out. The whole landscape is moving outward and that's not good for cities or for their older suburbs."

HEADLINE: Little-Noticed Court Gains Scrutiny in Custody Fight for Twins

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PLYMOUTH, Mass., Feb. 22

 BODY:
In a decision that opens a window onto the uncommon flexibility within the most personal of courts, a county family judge has ordered the separation of identical twin girls, temporarily giving custody of one to her mother and the other to her father.
The 10-year-old girls, Tara and Sheena Raymond of Wareham, had been living with their father, Dana Raymond, since the parents divorced two years ago. After their mother, Jean Ardizoni, sued to modify the custody arrangement, both parents, their lawyers and expert witnesses went to Plymouth County Probate and Family Court here on Feb. 14, prepared for a scheduled two-day trial. 
 Instead, Judge James R. Lawton ordered that the girls be picked up from their elementary school, then talked with them privately for 20 minutes. He ordered that Sheena live with her mother until a hearing on June 30, canceled the trial and left that day for a 10-day vacation in Florida.
Judge Lawton's decision, viewed as Solomon-like by some and cruel by others, offered a glimpse into the normally private world of Probate and Family Court, where decisions on the most emotional and intimate of matters are handed down daily by judges guided more by their personal values than by strict legal guidelines common in criminal and civil courts.
Court officials say that judges must use every tool they can to clear their increasingly heavy dockets. The case is one of thousands choking the state's probate courts, which settle disputes over trusts and estates as well as personal issues like domestic abuse, child support, guardianship, the right to die and commitment of mentally ill to institutions.
Judge Lawton, in an interview with The Associated Press, said he "simply wanted to do what the girls said they wanted."
"I was not performing surgery on Siamese twins," he said.
Where some legal experts and court administrators see flexibility, John E. Murphy, the lawyer for the twins' father, said he saw caprice. He said consistency and predictability were "the whole idea of the law."
"Then you go into court and there's chaos," Mr. Murphy said. "In probate court the judge can go on his whim of the day."
Dorothy Gill, who represents Ms. Ardizoni, 34, said the judge's action was appropriate. "What the judge did was listen to the children and fashion something that addressed their concerns," she said.
Had the girls committed a crime or been the victims of one, their names and faces would have remained secret. But in Massachusetts and other states, family court records are open to the public unless sealed by the judge. Although the press normally does not cover such cases, this one has drawn widespread attention to the court as well as the family.
Although the blue-eyed, blond twins still see each other at school, they have said they were surprised and upset at being separated.
Mr. Raymond, a 44-year-old unemployed cable splicer, said he was enraged by the decision and by the cancellation of the trial. "The politicians, somebody, has to look at the immense power one man or one woman has to destroy a family," Mr. Raymond said. "We waited about 10 months for this trial. You have the right to a fair trial, the right to be heard."
Rosemarie Haigazian, a lawyer on Martha's Vineyard who practices family law and has represented children in divorce and custody cases, said decision making in probate court differed from that in other types of legal cases.
"In civil cases, there are dollar amounts and often the help of a jury," she said. "In criminal cases there are standards of what constitutes a crime and often the help of a jury. In probate cases, it falls to the judge to take the facts, which are many and conflicting, and the law, which is voluminous, mix them together and come up with a decision."
Other than state guidelines for child support, there are no rules to guide family judges. For example, Judge Catherine P. Sabaitis, who had handled the Raymond case earlier, refuses to talk to children in divorce and custody cases, because her philosophy is that children should be shielded from adult matters, said several lawyers who have presented cases before her. Judge Lawton, on the other hand, counsels new judges to listen to children.
John E. McNichols, court administrator for the Massachusetts Probate and Family Court Department, said such broad discretion for judges was essential.
"Can one establish guidelines to deal with that highly emotionally charged an issue?" he said. "I don't think so. If that were the case, you could put a computer behind the bench."
In a society that increasingly seems to look to courts -- or television talk show hosts -- to intervene in what once were private affairs, the 43 judges in Massachusetts' 14 probate courts are taking on new areas of family matters. For example, from fiscal year 1990 to 1994, paternity suits in state family courts soared to 17,808 from 7,575.
"Too many people rely on the courts to fix things. Maybe that's just the way society has changed over the past 20 to 30 years," Mr. McNichols said.
Only a few of the state's most senior family judges have secretaries. Others rely on harried typing pools. Most court orders are handwritten. A staff of six law clerks was recently approved for family court judges: in the state's criminal courts about 40 clerks serve about 70 judges.
Meanwhile, Tara and Sheena Raymond still attend the same class at Minot Forest Elementary School in Wareham and take karate lessons together three times each week. They say they used to play tricks on their teachers and friends before Tara's braces made it easier to tell them apart. Sheena is scheduled to get braces soon and they said they'll resume the mischief then.

HEADLINE: Paterson, Used to Struggling, Struggles for Control

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PATERSON, N.J., Feb. 25

 BODY:
A short walk from the spot where a young drug suspect was shot by a rookie police officer earlier this week lies a stretch of abandoned textile mills, one after the other, dozens of mottled brick carcasses that once nurtured this city and clothed the nation.
Most everyone here learns in school that Paterson was a workhorse of the Industrial Revolution. There were so many thousands of looms earlier in the century that the din of machinery forever clogged the local air. It was a sound -- thwack, thwack, thwack -- that meant jobs. 
 The people in Paterson sometimes referred to the mills when they talked about the protests, looting and racial tensions stirred after the white housing police officer, Ronald Cohen, shot the unarmed black teen-ager, Lawrence Meyers, during the drug arrest on Tuesday. He died on Friday night.
"Everybody out here is struggling," said Raymond Brownfield, 22, an unemployed cook who was among the scores of young black men taunting police officers in riot gear on Thursday night. "It's hard to survive, to make ends meet. This used to be a real city of opportunity -- Silk City! -- but now there's nothing. Everything used to be here. It's a 'used to be' city. That's all."
With 140,891 people, Paterson represents a certain kind of urban America -- too large to readily make the transition to prettified suburbia, too small to demand the money and attention given to neighbors like Newark or New York City to overcome poverty, drugs and failing schools.
Though the city has rebounded slightly from the early 1980's, when it was called one of the poorest in America, the unemployment rate is stuck at 11 percent. The rate is 13.7 percent for black residents, according to the 1990 Census, and is certainly much higher for young black men. (National unemployment rates in December were 9.8 percent for blacks and 34.6 percent for blacks ages 16 to 19.)
The numbers are troubling to many here, but some local officials say they can also be misleading. To Councilman Thomas C. Rooney Jr., 67, a former Mayor who has lived his whole life in Paterson, jobs and history have little to do with what happened this week.
"What we saw in the last few days is a total contempt for law on the part of those who were making this minor riot," he said. "This has nothing to do with economics. It is the absolute breakdown of law, and that is a major, major difference."
To bolster his argument, Mr. Rooney points to the city's immigrant population, mostly from Latin America and the Middle East, which has transformed neighborhoods that last thrived in the days when Allen Ginsberg was a child here. He says they are evidence that anyone can make it in Paterson.
Many of the black adults who tried to soothe the anger of Mr. Meyers's friends and schoolmates disagree, insisting that it is impossible to separate Mr. Meyers's death and the resulting outcry from what they say are the discrimination and hardships faced by blacks in Paterson.
Martin Barnes, a former City Councilman and mayoral candidate who is black, said the young black residents who demonstrated this week would feel more confident that the judicial system would properly investigate the killing if they knew that more black people were in positions of power.
He pointed out some statistics: although the city is more than 36 percent black, fewer than 20 percent of the police officers are black, and virtually none are supervisors. Only in the last few years have black and Hispanic residents taken control of the City Council, which now has four Hispanic members, three blacks and two whites. The Mayor is white.
And, Mr. Barnes noted, even though blacks have arrived in City Hall, the municipal government is so hard-pressed that there is little money for social-service programs. Property values have declined and taxes have risen in a cycle worsened by abandonment and arson. The city's long-term debt has climbed sharply. The school system has performed so poorly that the state took it over in 1991. And Paterson, unlike the state's other large cities, Newark and Jersey City, has no big corporations to help out.
"Now we've gotten to the point where we've gotten some control of the political system, but we are still not doing anything because there is nothing there," Mr. Barnes said. "They've already milked it all gone, and that's when it all of a sudden got over to the blacks. They gave it to us when there was nothing left to give."
Kim Freeman, 35, a teacher's aide who is also black, said many of the parents and grandparents of the young black residents upset by the shooting of Mr. Meyers first came to Paterson from the South after World War II.
"Most people I know, their parents came up here to work in the factories," Mr. Freeman said. "Paterson was a magnet, the first industrialized city in the United States. Now it's a jobs depression. There are no jobs, no education and no housing, and that's a recipe for chaos."
One reason for the drop in fortunes is the steady decline in manufacturing, which has existed here since Paterson was founded by Alexander Hamilton in the 1790's. Hamilton envisioned factories generating power from the Great Falls, the 77-foot-high waterfall on the Passaic River, so he set up the Society for Establishing Useful Manufactures, hoping to break the country's dependence on English manufacturing.
By the end of the 19th century, Paterson was the one of the nation's leading centers for textile and locomotive manufacturing. By the 1950's, though, many of those enterprises had left, fleeing labor unrest and high costs. About 36 percent of Paterson's jobs were in factories in 1990, as opposed to 48 percent in 1975, officials said.
But some companies have remained and prospered, and others keep moving in, searching for cheap space and ready labor. That is why, despite the depressing economic numbers, some city boosters are upbeat.
"In the last year, I have seen a tremendous upturn," said Deborah Hoffman, executive director of the Paterson Economic Development Corporation, a nonprofit group. "Many communities don't welcome manufacturing. We do. When they are pushed out of the suburbs, we welcome them in Paterson."
Some businesses new to Paterson, like the Co-op Screw Manufacturing Company, said they were pleased by the city. Others, like Jean Wash, which dyes garments, said crime was a problem, as was finding qualified workers.
"I need people to run the machines, and I need girls to fold the garments," said Maurice Taieb, president of Jean Wash. "Girls I have no problem hiring. But people who are responsible to run the machines, I can't find those people. I went to the employment agency, and the people they sent were bad, not reliable. They didn't come on time."
Hispanic residents in Paterson, who are mostly from Puerto Rico, the Dominican Republic, and increasingly, Peru, appear to have largely stayed out of the dispute over Mr. Meyers's death. But Hispanic officials, like Councilwoman Maria Magda O'Keefe, said they shared many of the concerns that their black counterparts have over issues like housing and drugs.
Ms. Magda O'Keefe said Paterson had been good to many immigrants, who were starting to move from working-class or poor neighborhoods to areas that were once solely white. "We are getting better places, better homes, many are becoming homeowners," she said.
But both Ms. Magda O'Keefe and Councilman Rooney, who is known for his fiscal conservatism, said they feared that the publicity that Paterson had received this week might threaten efforts to revitalize the city. Mr. Rooney said he had received several calls from businesses worried about the violence.
"This is going to make it far more difficult for us to attract business to Paterson to create jobs," he said. "This is going to hurt all these young people because the jobs that could have been here won't come because of their actions."
"I am not optimistic, not from what I see," he added. "How could you be?"

HEADLINE: ON PRO HOCKEY;
In Quebec, Sale Rumors and an Arena Agenda

BYLINE: By Joe Lapointe 

DATELINE: QUEBEC

 BODY:
The early-season success of the Quebec Nordiques masks the never-ending intrigue in this special hockey city.
The owner, Marcel Aubut, who once traded Eric Lindros to two teams in the same day, now enjoys serious interest from potential franchise buyers in Phoenix and Denver. He wouldn't dare sell the team to two cities at the same time, would he? 
 The swirling sale rumors complement Aubut's arena-building agenda. Jean Martineau, a team spokesman, insisted the team wanted to stay in Quebec and said last week that the Nordiques wanted to finance construction through either a provincial lottery or through revenue from gambling casinos.
"That way, there's no tax money involved," Martineau said. "It's all voluntary." The arena should have 19,000 seats and 80 boxes, he said. The Nordiques are now filling 91 percent of their 15,399 seats in le Colisee.
Until an arena is built, Martineau said, the team wants a financial subsidy from the province. He did not give a dollar figure, but he said moving would abolish 600 jobs in the city and remove $10 million per year in provincial tax revenue. The overall economic impact, he said, would amount to $50 million annually. The team will lose $10 million this season, he said, and $12 million next season.
Another factor in the equation is the possibility that the 2002 Winter Olympics will be in Quebec. The city is one of four finalists, along with Salt Lake City, Ostersund, Sweden, and Sion, Switzerland. That decision will be made on June 18. A new arena would enhance Quebec's appeal.
"It's always a big fight here," Martineau said, referring to the political and economic considerations. "Mr. Aubut wants to keep this franchise in Quebec. If the officials don't have the vision to keep the franchise, it will be sad."
The team is also considering a new design of its uniform, and even that sartorial change is intertwined in the socio-political-economic fabric. The current version features several fleurs-de-lis, the insignia that adorns the provincial flag.
The Nordiques are considering using fewer of them in the new design, although they deny that the insignia will be eliminated altogether. When the Minnesota North Stars redesigned their uniforms three seasons ago, they dropped North from their name and then moved to Dallas a year later. With another referendum on provincial sovereignty approaching in Quebec, what French politician voting on arena financing would want to take the blame for the creation of the "Phoenix Sudiques"?
Furthermore, a move of the Nordiques could complicate National Hockey League expansion plans. The next two, four or six teams should sell for at least $50 million apiece, and probably more.
If Aubut moves into one of those markets, his N.H.L. partners don't get to share an expansion windfall. During the recent lockout, the wealthier N.H.L. teams promised to explore revenue-sharing possibilities. Perhaps Aubut's current dealings will encourage them to hasten their efforts.
On the ice, the robust Nordiques are enhancing their value to potential buyers as they prepare for their second of four games with the Pittsburgh Penguins on Monday night, at home.
In the first meeting last Tuesday night in Pittsburgh, the Penguins won by 5-4, pushing Quebec into second place in the standings of the Northeast Division, the Eastern Conference and the 26-team league.
Quebec's roster is the second-youngest in the league. The coach and the general manager -- Marc Crawford and Pierre Lacroix -- are both in their first seasons on the job. Thus far, the trade with Toronto for Wendel Clark appears to be the cement that has bonded this multilingual, multinational roster to play as a unit.
"I'm just one small part," said Clark, second in team scoring with 19 points on 10 goals and 9 assists, before Saturday night's game with Boston.
He said Crawford "is the main reason for the turnaround" because he has "full control.
"Everyone is following one game plan and respecting him," Clark said.
Clark speaks only high-school French, but he said the adjustment to his new culture has been "easy, if you're patient."
"It takes a little longer in restaurants," he said. "I'm picking it up as I go along."
Are the players -- from French Canada, English Canada, Russia, Sweden, the United States, Finland and Germany -- bothered by constant rumors of a new residence?
"Winning is the easiest way to solve all problems and end all rumors," Clark said. "If you win, the boat is rocked less. If you lose, the boat is rocked more."

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
'I've Walked in Their Shoes'

BYLINE: By JENNIFER FREY, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: POMPANO BEACH, Fla., Feb. 25

 BODY:
He started around 8:30 A.M., shortly after his wife, Lana, left for work. For Curt Blefary, the drink of choice was whisky, a blend called Philadelphia. He took it with barely a splash of water and a handful of ice. By noon, he would have finished more than a quart.
 With the drink came the bitterness, and with the bitterness, the depression. While he drank, Blefary read the sports sections, checking, enviously, on the progress of his former big league teammates and friends. Gene Michael was running the Yankees. Bob Watson was in Houston. Frank Robinson in Baltimore. He would see no-name players -- guys who had less talent and less notoriety than he'd had -- get hired as coaches or instructors. 
 And he would sit home, alone and unemployed, his left hip crumbling from a disease exacerbated by the alcohol, and wonder why it had never worked out for him.
 "It was the same every day," Blefary said recently, sitting in the same easy chair where he used to pass his mornings. "I'd sit there drinking and drinking. I was in a total depression. I blamed everybody for my problems except myself."
 Nearly 10 months ago, Blefary started another morning with another drink, this time not even waiting for his wife to leave. But after a few moments, he looked at the drink in his hand and started to cry. Then he walked into the bathroom and told Lana that he was going to seek help.
 With the assistance of Sam McDowell, a former Cleveland Indians pitcher and a part of the Baseball Assistance Team that aids past players with problems, Blefary enrolled in a rehabilitation program on May 13. He was released 28 days later and has been sober since.
 "I was a drinker for 33 years," said Blefary, who is now 51. "I started when I was 18. By Triple-A, I was drinking hard liquor. In the big leagues, I was out of control."
 An outfielder and a catcher, Blefary was the American League rookie of the year in 1965, when he hit .260 with 22 home runs and 70 runs batted in for the Baltimore Orioles. He hit more than 20 home runs in each of his first three seasons, then tailed off slowly over the next five years, playing for four other franchises -- including the Yankees in 1970 and part of 1971 -- before being released by the Atlanta Braves in the spring of 1973. He was not yet 30 years old.
 "I had a problem when I was playing, but nobody ever had the guts to tell me," Blefary said. "Back then, people were more likely to bring you another drink than ask if you had a problem."
 In many ways, the game has not changed. Tonight in Port St. Lucie, minor league and replacement players for the Mets will be required to sit through an hourlong presentation about the dangers of drugs and alcohol. Absent from the game when Blefary played, the presentation is now an annual ritual. Often, though, the warning is not heeded.
 "We have sessions to help the players understand how to deal with a lot of issues -- alcohol education programs, drug abuse information, sessions about relationships with women," said Steve Phillips, the Mets' director of minor league operations. "What we have to do at the minor league level is respond to what we see going on at the major league level."
 On the major league level, the Mets have watched Darryl Strawberry and Dwight Gooden, two former rookies of the year, two members of their 1986 World Series champion team, battle with substance abuse problems that have resulted in both being suspended from major league baseball. In a report published last week, Strawberry and Gooden argued that their problems stemmed from being part of what Strawberry called a baseball "life style."
 Blefary understands that. He, too, was a rookie of the year. He, too, won a World Series early in his career, playing for the Baltimore team that defeated the Los Angeles Dodgers in 1966. And, he, too, got caught up in the party.
 "You're a celebrity," said Blefary. "Yes, there were some who didn't, but there were lots who did. It was always there."
 Blefary can remember the spring day in 1965 when he showed up at breakfast still drunk, and was so sick on the bus ride to the park that he made the driver pull over so he could throw up. He remembers countless days when he sat in the whirlpool, sometimes even dunking his head, trying to get himself straight before a game.
 He picked his spots, drinking less before day games or doubleheaders, but he did not stop.
 Bitter when his playing career came to a premature end and his reputation as a drinker and carouser helped prevent him from getting a coaching job, Blefary continued to drink after he left the game. The years passed, and the drinking got worse. His kids visited more and more infrequently. He got laid off from a job driving a truck, then worked at a temporary agency for $4.25 an hour. He hated it, and he drank more.
 For a long time, he tried to fool himself into thinking he had gone straight, attending Alcoholics Anonymous meetings for nearly 11 years. He never took them seriously.
 "I'd go and try to point out all the things wrong with everyone else there," he said. "Now I have a sponsor I talk to every day. I understand."
 Blefary drinks cola now, and concentrates on recovering from hip replacement surgery performed by a local doctor, Paul Meli, who donated his time because Blefary long ago lost his insurance. He dreams about being given a second chance in baseball, about one of his old friends calling and offering him that coaching job. He talks about working with kids, giving motivational speeches that warn about the dangers of alcohol.
 And he still reads the newspapers, paying close attention to the stories about Gooden and Strawberry. He sees a lot of himself in those two, and it worries him.
 "It seems like everybody is telling them that it's going to be all right and they believe it," Blefary said. "Well, it ain't going to be all right, not if they're still in denial. I've walked in their shoes. I've been there. I know."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Curt Blefary, a former rookie of the year and a recovering alcoholic, has a warning for today's major leaguers. (Ozier Muhammad/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Sanctions on Trade With Beijing Begin As Deadline Passes

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: BEIJING, Sunday, Feb. 26

 BODY:
Trade sanctions on more than $1 billion in Chinese goods, the largest punitive trade measure ever imposed by the United States, technically went into effect today as negotiations to avert a trade war passed a deadline set three weeks ago.
With trade negotiators from both sides still working in Beijing, and with prospects that the talks could continue even as the sanctions begin to be applied on Monday, neither Government openly declared that a trade war was on. 
 Nevertheless, both sides emphasized three weeks ago that American sanctions on items such as handkerchiefs and cellular telephones and Chinese counter sanctions would automatically go into effect if an agreement was not reached by 12:01 A.M. Eastern time today.
There were indications that the American strategy was to allow the passing of the deadline in hopes that this would build pressure on the Chinese and demonstrate Washington's resolve to achieve most of its demands. Washington wants the Chinese Government to begin shutting down the more than two dozen factories, most of them in southern China, that manufacture pirated versions of copyrighted software, music and videos.
Chinese officials announced early today that they had shut down two such factories, including a well-known company that was illegally copying American movies, including "Jurassic Park." United States negotiators are believed to be pressing for a broader enforcement program as well as for opening the Chinese market to the American goods that are often pirated here.
In Washington, Clinton Administration officials said that United States trade representative, Mickey Kantor, had called a news conference for early Sunday afternoon in Washington.
The passing of today's dealine created a scene of significant confusion in Beijing as a game of brinkmanship was played out behind closed doors and over secure telephone lines between Washington and Beijing.
 In response to demands from the United States that it enforce copyright, trademark and patent laws, China has publicized a series of crackdowns on the sale of illegal compact and laser disks in the last week, confiscating pirated disks and arresting retailers.
But American officials have said they want to see a stronger symbol of China's commitment to enforce the laws, by going to the source of pirated copyrighted goods, and not just to those who market them. There are 29 factories known to be capable of reproducing compact and laser disks, most of them in Guangdong Province, near Hong Kong.
Perhaps the best known of all is the Shenfei Laser and Optical System Company, a maker of audio and video disks that is frequently cited by United States negotiators as a flagrant copyright violator. The closing of Shenfei was announced this morning.
One American official said last week that he suspected Beijing had been reluctant to prosecute Shenfei because its management includes relatives of senior Chinese leaders. The nation's paramount leader, Deng Xiaoping, made a well-publicized visit to Shenfei when he toured southern China in early 1992.
Whatever the reason for waiting until the last minute, the authorities appeared to be trying to dispel doubt about China's commitment to further action.
"Investigations have found that the Shenfei Company made illegal visual disks of the 'Jurassic Park' and other audio laser products for certain foreign businessmen," the news agency reported. "Its products were found not only available on the Chinese market but also on some overseas markets."
The report continued, "It has been a consistent stand of the Chinese Government to protect copyrights and to crack down on piracy."
The current round of talks began on Feb. 14, 10 days after Mr. Kantor, announced that sanctions would begin against China if no agreement was reached by today.
The talks intensified last week when the deputy trade representative, Charlene Barshefsky, flew here from Washington, but in daily comments before and after negotiating sessions neither side revealed exactly what separated them.
In other trade negotiations in recent years, American and Chinese officials have employed a variety of tactics and bluffs, sometimes delaying final discussions until minutes before a deadline. All the while, other gestures have signaled hardening or softening at various points in the talks.
In what appeared to be one such carefully timed action, the Beijing Intermediate People's Court held a hearing on Friday in a case against two Beijing video stores. Eight Hollywood studios had accused the shops of illegally copying 18 films. A judge postponed the case until next month, requesting more evidence.
The film studios have urged American negotiators to concentrate on Chinese factories that copy Hollywood movies and export them across Asia, a practice that potentially costs the studios millions of dollars in lost sales.
Under pressure from the computer software industry, the American negotiators have also asked China to persuade Chinese companies and officials to pay for computer software, beginning with China's trade ministry, which the Americans say uses hundreds of pirated copies of Microsoft software.
"It's not surprising that there are differences in Sino-U.S. trade and economic relations," Wu Yi, China's trade minister, was quoted as saying in a New China News Agency report on Saturday. "What's important is that the controversies should be settled through consultations on an equal footing."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Charlene Barshefsky, left, the United States deputy trade representative, and Bill Monroe, an embassy official, were escorted to their car by a hotel guard yesterday on their way to trade talks with Chinese officials. (Reuters) (pg. 6)

HEADLINE: February 19-25: Mexican Relief;
The Austerity Cure May Be As Lethal As the Illness

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

 BODY:
Bailing out Mexico was hard enough for President Clinton, who bypassed Congress after he discovered that loaning $20 billion to a foreign nation that can't pay its bills is not part of the Contract with America. But the bailout will be even harder for the Mexicans.
 When the deal was signed last week, it included American requirements that Mexico keep its interest rates sky-high -- they were 50 percent last week -- balance its budget and get Washington's approval for almost any major economic move.The fear now is that the cure could be as debilitating as the disease, that Mexico may be plunged into a recession. 
 Meanwhile, the Administration began sending quiet signals to its candidate to head the new World Trade Organization, Carlos Salinas, the former Mexican president now blamed for the peso crisis, that he might start thinking about another job. DAVID E. SANGER

HEADLINE: We Should Call It Profit Care, Not Managed Care

 BODY:
An article on Jan. 29 about a San Francisco hospital's adjustment to the new era of managed care drew a range of letters, most from physicians, citing the moral difficulties, both for individual doctors and for American society, of keeping an eye on the bottom line when a suffering patient awaits help.

To the Editor: 
 From the land of earthquakes and floods another potential disaster appears to be in the making. Under the guise of "capitation" hospitals will be known as "health companies" and "cost centers."
Once again the fact that health spending currently accounts for 14 percent of our gross domestic product is raised without explaining that care is not rationed here as it is in those countries to which we are forever being compared. Young men and women come to the United States from all parts of the world for training in our post-graduate medical system. We must be doing something right.
The proposed "managed care" in the capitation system will limit patients' choice of doctors and have their in-hospital care standardized using "clinical pathways" that outline details and duration of care according to each illness. Any experienced doctor can tell you that good care must be individualized and cannot follow a standard plan put forth by some partially educated person.
"Capitation" in no way decreases administrative costs, which now account for between 30 and 60 percent of the health care dollar in the United States, and certainly does nothing for those citizens currently uninsured! Managed care and rationed care are synonymous and it is important for all patients and potential patients to realize this.
 
WARREN W. FRANCIS
Providence, R.I., Jan. 30
 
The writer is a clinical associate professor of surgery at Brown University.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 24; Column 6; Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: We Should Call It Profit Care, Not Managed Care

 BODY:
An article on Jan. 29 about a San Francisco hospital's adjustment to the new era of managed care drew a range of letters, most from physicians, citing the moral difficulties, both for individual doctors and for American society, of keeping an eye on the bottom line when a suffering patient awaits help.

To the Editor: 
 The brave new world you described may provide a quick fix for the rapidly disappearing fee-for-service form of medicine, but the evolving managed-care plans are unlikely to be sustainable. Hospitals with excess beds, tertiary care units, high-priced specialists and high-tech equipment are increasingly being taken over and managed by large for-profit H.M.O.'s and insurance companies controlled by highly paid managers and chief executives.
Competition among managed-care providers has resulted in ever-larger mergers in order to effectively contract with companies seeking ever-cheaper health plans for their employees. A past history of providing continuous medical care to an individual for 15 or 20 years does not guarantee that any particular doctor-patient relationship will continue; every week I see people being changed from one "plan" to another by their companies.
Pressure to create profits has not only caused hospitals to economize by closing floors and dismissing nurses, but it increasingly places physicians at risk as "gatekeepers." Ordering fewer tests, consultations and hospitalizations increases profit margins. But gatekeeping also creates adversarial relationships between health care providers and patients. In addition, as your article pointed out, managed care competition makes hospitals less likely to put resources into community programs. In their zeal to economize, "health companies" not only exhibit little compassion for their patients, but in attempts to maximize profits by avoiding high-risk and seriously ill individuals, they destroy the very purpose of health insurance.
The corporatization of medicine may provide "less of the art of medicine and more of the science of medicine," but eventually the societal penalties resulting from forming a second-class group of citizens based on sickness, disability, serious illness and uninsured status will prove these "health companies" to be disastrous for medical care in the 21st century.
 
RICHARD H. LANZEROTTI
San Francisco, Feb. 1
 
The writer is an oncologist and internist.

HEADLINE: Budget Amendment Will Handcuff Presidents

 BODY:
To the Editor:
 Newt Gingrich's "Beyond the 100 Days" (Op-Ed, Feb. 22) celebrates halftime achievements of the Republican Contract With America. Mr. Gingrich is right to celebrate now. Who knows how much will be there for him to celebrate at the end of the 100 days, what with the Senate's Democrats slowly waking up and even moderate Republicans (of whom there are a few left) having second thoughts about some of the radical ideas coming sometimes from both sides of the House? 
 The Contract With America deserves, however, an attempt at dispassionate analysis. Unquestionably, the first 50 days produced some legislation that was badly needed, like bringing Congress under the same regulations that business has to adhere to. The line-item veto could have potentially good effects, if used by a responsible President -- and it is the voters' job to put responsible Presidents in the White House.
 The importance of these laws passed pales, however, compared with the potentially calamitous harm that other parts of the contract can inflict on current and future generations.
 I would like to discuss only one, but arguably the most dangerous piece of legislation, the balanced budget amendment. Should it become part of the Constitution, it will tie the hands of political leaders in future recessions and prevent them from stimulating the economy, as Ronald Reagan did in the 1981-82 recession.
 Then Mr. Reagan gave a new dimension to the definition of fiscal stimulus, by increasing the deficit to unprecedented heights. While his motive in doing so had probably more to do with the cold war than the recession, the beneficial effect on the economic situation was undeniable.
 During the next recession in 1990-92, President Bush already had the stimulus of a large fiscal deficit at his disposal and was able, together with lowering of interest rates by the Federal Reserve, to more or less master the recession by the time President Clinton was inaugurated.
 The proponents of the balanced budget amendment would deny this powerful tool for the prevention of depressions to future generations of political leaders. At the same time they are telling us that we should trust future politicians to have the courage to break a constitutional mandate. The kind of courage that current leaders, according to their own admission, do not possess. Actually they use their own lack of courage as their principal argument for the passage of the amendment.
 Future generations of voters will curse them for this amendment, and if 1994 is any indication, will punish their own generation of politicians for the calamities they will have to suffer.
 
CHARLES A. LAWNER
Tarrytown, N.Y., Feb. 23, 1995

HEADLINE: House Hunters' Friend: Buyer Brokers

BYLINE: By PENNY SINGER 

 BODY:
WHEN Denise and Fred Weiss started looking for a house last spring, instead of going to a traditional realtor, they enlisted the services of a buyer broker.
"At the time we were in a rental apartment in Riverdale with our two young sons, and it was tight," said Mrs. Weiss who is 29. "Interest rates were low, so we figured it was a good time to get into the housing market. When we discussed it with our families, my brother-in-law, who is an appraiser, suggested that we go with a buyer broker." 
 At the time, Mrs. Weiss said, "I didn't know the difference between a buyer broker and a regular real estate broker until my brother-in-law explained that a buyer broker would work on our behalf, in our best interests, while other real estate agents work strictly on behalf of the seller."
Mrs. Weiss got in touch with Laurie Bell, a licensed real estate agent at Balch Realty, an exclusive buyer agency, with offices in Bronxville and Brewster.
"We had a long talk," Mrs. Weiss said. "I told her what we were looking for and how much we could afford to spend. That was in May, and in June we put a bid on the second place Laurie showed us, a two-bedroom, two-bath condo in Bedford Mews, and on Sept. 1 we moved to Bedford. We love it. It couldn't be better. There is a playground and a swimming pool, and my husband has an easy commute on the Saw Mill River Parkway to his business in the Bronx. We are very happy."
The advantages of using a buyer broker are many, Ms. Bell said.
"For one thing the home-buying process is made much simpler," she said. "For example, a buyer broker will search through an inventory of listings and show only those that closely meet the requirements of her client. The Weisses bought the second house they saw. And a buyer broker will also do the negotiating."
Not to be overlooked is that there is no charge for the services of a buyer broker. "Our compensation comes from the realtor's commission on the transaction, which is paid by the seller," she said.
The principals who founded the Balch agency are real estate appraisers, not real estate sales men and women, said Peter B. Bell, chairman of the board and Ms. Bell's husband.
"We've been appraisers since 1986, trained in being objective in assessing properties, so the role of buyer broker is a logical one for appraisers," Mr. Bell said. "Yet it took a long time for the concept of the residential buyer broker to make it from California, where it started, to the Northeast. Now there are buyer brokers in every state. We work with 12 different multiple-listing agencies in several states."
In the last six months, he added, the Balch agency has completed 40 transactions for clients. "One was for a $750,000 home in Scarsdale for a client that was referred to us by another buyer broker, Robert Daly of Carlsen's Country Heritage Realty in Washingtonville, which is in Orange County."
Mr. Daly said: "My client wanted to be closer to a new big job he took in Yonkers. Up here, we're near West Point. We have been seeing a very fast-growing market with numbers of clients coming from Westchester and the metro area who still commute to Westchester and into Manhattan."
Mr. Daly, who said he became a buyer broker after years of dealing with property acquisition for clients, added: "As a buyer broker I am the agent of the buyer and obligated to represent the best interest of my client throughout the transaction. People like that; business is good."
Ira Fischer, a real estate lawyer in White Plains, recently formed the Home Buyers Agency in White Plains exclusively as a buyer broker. "As a real estate lawyer, I know how to get the best terms for a client," Mr. Fischer said. "I can critique the situation like an engineer who is called in to inspect a home for a client."
Mr. Fischer, who relies on listings in the multiple-listing service in Westchester, said: "Basically, simply what I am doing is showing clients how to buy real estate. Most people don't have the foggiest idea. What I find interesting is that most of my first clients have been lawyers, who somehow understand the underlying concept better than most people."
Mr. Bell agrees. "At least 30 percent of our clients are professionals," he said, "mainly doctors and lawyers, people who realize the value of advocacy."
The buyer-broker method has been the routine way to buy commercial property for years, observed P. Gilbert Mercurio, executive vice president of the Westchester County Board of Realtors, a trade group of 4,000 members.
"The buyer broker is still a fairly novel concept in this area in the home-buying sector, but an alternative that will appeal to certain consumers," Mr. Mercurio said. "However, I don't see buyer broker agencies in conflict with sales agents in real estate offices. Sales agents in those offices will simply work with buyer brokers as easily as they would with anyone else."
Antoinette Emerson, the president of the Westchester County Board of Realtors, anticipates an increase in buyer broker agencies.
"I don't see them as adversaries of the agencies we already have but as another way to stimulate all the overall home-buying industry," Ms. Emerson said. "As a matter of fact, it is conceivable that some of our member firms will be adding buyer-broker services."
Because the concept is of such interest to the home-buying public, Mr. Mercurio said, the role of the buyer broker will be a topic of a seminar at the Real Estate Expo '95, which will be taking place next Sunday at the County Center in White Plains.
"Representatives of the Balch agency will be conducting the seminar," Mr. Mercurio said. "The expo, which is now in its 11th year, is a tremendous opportunity for first-time home buyers or for anyone in the home-buying market to get their hands on information. It's worth attending for the guide alone. There's no puffery in it. We put out a solid 'how to' handbook, which contains 25 articles written by experts in the field that cover all aspects of home buying."
Expo exhibitors include realtors, mortgage bankers, mortgage brokers, real estate lawyers, appraisers, home inspectors, "and just about everyone associated with every aspect of buying and selling real estate in Westchester," Mr. Mercurio said, adding that seminars are also held throughout the day.
"Last year, more than 10,000 people attended the show. It's a hot ticket, and the price is right. Admission is free, and so is all the information that attendees will receive."

HEADLINE: The Controversy Over Plans To Trim the State's Budget

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER 

 BODY:
CLUTCHING their handmade suits and dresses, the old women at the Home for the Aged Blind in Yonkers were both proud and frightened -- proud that they had learned to sew again, although this time it was without their eyes, but worried that they would soon be shut out of the day-care program that taught them their new skills.
Under Gov. George E. Pataki's proposal to close a $5 billion budget gap and cut taxes, day-care programs like theirs, along with home-care programs for the elderly, would lose some of their financing. 
 Throughout Westchester in recent weeks, health-care officials, educators, providers of legal services for the poor and other social service administrators have been attempting to assess the impact of the Governor's budget proposal, unveiled on Feb. 1. Mr. Pataki has proposed a $32.4 billion budget for the 1995-96 fiscal year, down $1.1 billion from the previous year, which would reduce taxes for most residents.
At Purchase College and elsewhere in the county, there was outrage about how those tax reductions would be achieved. Nearly 200 students demonstrated at the Purchase campus on Feb. 14, blocking the main entrance and tying up traffic on nearby roads. They were objecting to proposed increases in tuition and cuts in student aid.
There have also been demonstrations by students and university staff and faculty members in Albany, and more are planned.
In White Plains, at the offices of Westchester-Putnam Legal Services, Michael D. Hampden, the executive director, has been struggling with proposed cuts in his agency's budget and the potential loss of a homelessness-prevention program for welfare recipients threatened with eviction. The budget in Albany, Mr. Hampden said, was being balanced at the expense of the poor.
In Hastings-on-Hudson, Dr. Vincent T. Beni, Superintendent of Schools, was complaining about the budget plan's potential impact on education. He said Mr. Pataki wants to freeze state aid to schools at current levels, place a three-month moratorium on the purchase of school buses, cap spending for special education and halt state participation in new building programs for 15 months, among other things.
Like other school administrators, Dr. Beni, who is also president of the Chief School Administrators of Southern Westchester, was worried about how to balance his budget at a time when enrollment was increasing.
Joseph J. Burns, president and chief executive officer of Visiting Nurse Services in Westchester, in White Plains, said: "Everyone's complaining. Albany will need the wisdom of Solomon to see who gets what."
But no one was saying anything at the County Department of Social Services, which oversees Westchester's welfare program and stands to suffer some of the deepest cuts. Statewide, the Governor's plan calls for cutting $343 million in welfare programs and $1.2 billion from Medicaid, based upon projected growth in those categories next year -- some of the most significant cuts to social service programs in the state's history.
When asked his opinion of the proposed cuts, Norman Shaw, a Department of Social Services spokesman, said, "No comment until more is learned about Mr. Pataki's budget." There was the same message at the offices of County Executive Andrew P. O'Rourke, where the word from a spokeswoman was that the budget "was being analyzed." Mr. O'Rourke, a Republican, said that he was generally supportive of the Governor's intent to reduce taxes.
A survey of a representative sample of cities and towns in the county showed that the municipalities could lose up to 10 percent in transportation aid for road improvements. But Mayor Frances S. Gibbs of Peekskill said, "We really just don't know the impact yet."
Meanwhile, Democratic state legislators from Westchester have been listening to the concerns of their constituents, and they vowed to return to Albany tomorrow after their winter recess with strong messages for the Republican administration.
After meeting with constituents for 13 hours last week, State Assemblywoman Sandra R. Galef, a Democrat from Ossining, said she would fight for health care for the elderly, tuition assistance for undergraduates and avoiding caps on public school expansion plans.
"What is clear to me," Mrs. Galef said, "is that a lot of people are going to get hit in Westchester. It is also clear that the Governor doesn't care about the elderly, even though this population is growing," she said.
Mr. Pataki wants to cut Meidcaid-financed adult day-care programs, forcing many older residents into already crowded nursing homes. He has also proposed eliminating at-home care for people, mostly the elderly, who need services like shopping, housekeeping or cooking, if such a cut would not result in a person's going to a nursing home. All other care would be limited to 25 hours a week. Mr. Burns, at Visiting Nurse Services in Westchester, said, "The elderly are going to die on the vine in their little apartments with no one to even boil an egg for them."
William R. Smith, executive director for St. Cabrini Nursing Home in Dobbs Ferry and the immediate past chairman of the New York Association of Homes and Services for the Aging, called the elderly "the voiceless minority, the forgotten people." He estimated that in Westchester there were about 1,100 older people in need of day-care services.
But State Senator Nicholas A. Spano, a Republican and Conservative from Yonkers, said: "The people of New York told us they want less government. They agreed cuts were necessary, but now they're saying, 'Don't cut us.' The reality is hitting home, and it's hard to take."
He said the lobbying efforts and demonstrations notwithstanding, the "bottom line is that there isn't much money for restorations."
Administrators at colleges and universities in the county, however, were hoping that Governor Pataki would restore at least some of their aid. They continued to lobby hard against the cuts, which they say would hurt poor and minority students the hardest.
At Iona College in New Rochelle, Brian Monahan, a vice president and director of enrollment, said reductions in the state-financed student-assistance program would "make it difficult for our needy students to continue their studies."
The maximum tuition assistance would drop by $500 to $1,000 from $4,050, under the new budget proposal and would affect hundreds of students at Iona, he said. Tuition at the private college, which has 6,000 students, is $10,900 a year.
At Purchase College, Renneth Sorhaindo, director of the education opportunity program, said cutbacks would affect students who fall below admission standards and poverty guidelines. About 170 equal opportunity students each year receive about $1,216 to pay for room, board and books. Under the program, the students are also eligible for tutoring assistance.
Walter H. Liebman, a spokesman for the college, said many of the 4,000 students enrolled "would be hurt in unimaginable ways" by the Governor's budget. Tuition there is $2,650.
At public schools across the county, administrators predicted that the budget that Governor Pataki had devised to reduce state taxes would increase taxes elsewhere.
Dr. Beni in Hastings said that the Governor "was careful not to call it cuts," but freezing state aid to schools without factoring in inflation amounted to a cut.
"And who will pay for it?" he asked. "The bottom line is, the local taxpayer." He predicted that school boards would tap the property owner to pay for building expansions and other programs.
At Westchester-Putnam Legal Services, Mr. Hampden was facing $88,000 in proposed cuts to his agency, whose total yearly budget is $1.9 million, along with the loss of the Homelessness Prevention Program, which he called "a moneymaker for the state," because of the high cost of sheltering the homeless.
He said there was a "complex mix going on around the country now of sociological biases against the poor -- it's almost in vogue."
Anne Erickson, a lobbyist for the Greater Upstate Law Project, which represents legal service organizations, said the state was "pushing more and more families toward the edge."
"Everyone is walking away from their obligation to help those in need," she said. "This is a mean-spirited budget."
But in Albany, Claudia S. Hutton, a spokeswoman for the State Division of the Budget, said that even with the proposed cuts New York would still be "the No. 1 spending state in the nation for Medicaid."
Miss Hutton also said that with the cuts the state would still have "the most generous needs-based financial aid program for students in the nation" -- $562 million, which is more than twice what each of the next two highest-spending states, Illinois and California, spend on student aid.
