SECTION: Section 7; Page 2; Column 1; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 1654 words

HEADLINE: Why Law Is No Fun Anymore

BYLINE: By Jeremy Waldron; Jeremy Waldron is a professor of law at the University of California, Berkeley. His most recent book is "Liberal Rights: Collected Papers 1981-91."

 BODY:

OVERCOMING LAW
By Richard A. Posner.
597 pp. Cambridge, Mass.:
Harvard University Press. $39.95. 
THE Chief Judge of the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit has given his new book, "Overcoming Law," an odd title -- odd because we think judges should study and apply the law, not try to overcome it. But Richard A. Posner is no ordinary judge. Before his appointment to the bench in 1981, he was a professor of law at the University of Chicago, and for years he has been one of the most articulate spokesmen for the scholarly movement known as "law and economics."
The law and economics movement analyzes legal problems -- like who has the responsibility to foresee the possibility of an accident, or how a contract should be interpreted when its provisions are unclear -- in terms of the costs and benefits faced by rational, self-interested individuals. Alternative solutions are evaluated by asking which set of incentives and disincentives will promote the most efficient use of the resources involved. Should a construction company be liable for the enormous losses incurred when an employee accidentally cut through a power cable and closed down Newark International Airport in January? The answer, the economist would say, should depend largely on whether the cost to the airport of providing a backup power supply would have exceeded the cost to the contractor of having to determine where the cables were before he began his excavations.
Judge Posner's book applies that method to the legal system itself. It is about the economics of practicing and studying law: the incentives and disincentives faced by judges, lawyers and law professors. The book describes the legal profession as a collapsing cartel, a decadent guild coming to terms with market competition. To those lawyers who complain that their practice is no fun anymore, Judge Posner responds: "Naturally it is less fun. Competitive markets are no fun at all for most sellers; the effect of competition is to transform most producer surplus into consumer surplus."
Similarly, he describes judges and law professors not as paragons of judicial reason but as leisure seekers. The jurisprudential distinction between the decision in a case and the dictums (the wider arguments for the decision that have no standing as precedents) means that a leisure-seeking judge can avoid writing an opinion of his own; he can simply concur in another judge's vote without being bound, in a later case, by the other's detailed opinions. As for law professors, Judge Posner thinks they behave like wannabe judges, "writing, in the guise of articles, alternative judicial opinions" that no one takes any notice of, and for which they need take no responsibility.
Much of Judge Posner's discussion is penetrating as well as mischievous, but one wonders what it would portend for the legal system as a whole. Does "overcoming law" mean replacing the tired apparatus of courts, clerks and casebooks with cost-benefit analysis and panels of economic experts? Judge Posner says no: the rule of law is "a public good of immense value," with its norms of impartiality and predictability.
Indeed, the author is much more explicit in this book than he has been in the past in books like "The Problems of Jurisprudence" about the limitations of the economic approach, at least in determining substantive justice. Judges, he says, "should be wary of embracing totalizing visions that like Gradgrind's Benthamism in 'Hard Times' reduce individual human beings to numbers or objects."
Economics cannot determine ultimate assignments of rights, he insists. It cannot tell us, for example, whether the right over a fetus's life should be assigned to the mother or the fetus, "any more than the economist can decide whether the goal of our society should be to maximize the wealth of the United States or the wealth of the entire world." An economist can explain a lot about the structures of cost and benefit associated with a given set of rights, and he can sometimes discern which assignment of rights would be more efficient. But Judge Posner readily concedes that efficiency is not the be-all and end-all: "Our liberal intuitions are as deep as our utilitarian ones."
Many of the most striking arguments in "Overcoming Law" reflect the concerns of a liberal (or a libertarian?), not an economist. "Our statute books overflow with vicious, exploitive, inane, ineffectual and extravagantly costly laws," he writes. We are "the most penal of civilized nations," with our exploding prison population, our thirst for execution, the severe penalties we impose, often "for intrinsically minor, esoteric, archaic or victimless offenses." Judge Posner condemns the practice of handcuffing and thus humiliating anyone who is taken into custody: "The arrest of Joseph K in the first chapter of 'The Trial' is immensely more civilized than any arrest would be likely to be in the land of freedom at the threshold of the 21st century."
He compares -- startlingly -- our heavy use of pretrial detention, often for years in overcrowded jails, with conditions in Nazi Germany. Even his colleagues on the bench do not escape the comparison: "We should not suppose that our judges have a margin of moral superiority over our political leaders greater than the margin that the German judges of the Third Reich had over their Leader." The main reason, he maintains, that the United States has never had a judiciary in complicity with a Hitler is that it has never had a Hitler to be in complicity with. But the United States, he notes, has had slavery and laws against miscegenation and Red scares and the wartime internment of Japanese-Americans, "and most of our judges went along with these things without protest."
These are remarkable observations. But his remedy for the evils just cited would seem to be more law -- a greater emphasis on legality, rights and justice. What, then, is the "law" to which the title of the book refers -- the law that needs to be overcome? It is, according to Judge Posner, "a professional totem signifying all that is pretentious, uninformed, prejudiced and spurious in the legal tradition." The most important word here is "uninformed." The theme that dominates this book more than any other is the need for what the author calls "pragmatism" in the way voters, lawyers, judges and professors think about the law.
Pragmatism, in Judge Posner's hands, is not just a vacuous commendation of common sense, but an unflinching emphasis on future consequences -- the effects of our rules, rights and principles -- to the exclusion, if necessary, of precedent, analogy and doctrine, as these are usually understood in the legal world. Ronald Dworkin, perhaps our foremost philosopher of law, has made much of the fact that legal argument pays tribute to the past even as it seeks to do justice for the future. Judges, he says, continue to wrestle with the meaning of precedents, in a way that goes far beyond figuring out what people might have been counting on, and Mr. Dworkin seeks to explain this in terms of the community maintaining its "integrity."
For Judge Posner, however, Mr. Dworkin's analysis is superfluous, because the reverence for precedence represents the discredited worst in our judicial practice, not something worth retaining. Think of the uninformed way in which jurists use the method of analogy: "If they have a case involving the appropriate system of property rights for a new natural resource, say petroleum, they examine decided cases dealing with 'similar' resources, say water or rabbits. They do not go out and talk to petroleum engineers, ecologists or natural-resources economists."
JUDGE POSNER'S most scathing and extensive remarks are reserved for his former colleagues in the law schools. I will not go into details, for some of the chapters are little more than book reviews that have already been published. Many of them will date quickly, and they give the whole thing the feel of a miscellany rather than, as the author hopes, a sustained argument "meant to be read consecutively."
Nevertheless, Judge Posner does present a clear, unrelenting and salutary theme. Legal scholars ought to make much greater use of empirical evidence in suggesting judicial and legislative strategies to achieve a better or a fairer society. Law schools need to foster the basic research necessary for the development of analytical frameworks for organizing and evaluating this evidence. The law and economics movement is one such framework, but Judge Posner is open to the idea that there are others.
The title of scholar, he insists, is one that has to be earned -- particularly by those who expect to be listened to more than the ordinary citizen in their comments on public affairs. For Judge Posner, the title is not conferred simply by the weight of one's footnotes -- particularly when the footnotes in most law reviews consist of references to the writings of other law professors who have produced similarly uninformed work. The price of scholarship is a willingness to study the empirical reality of issues, and either to acquire the intellectual tools that are necessary to make that study useful or to engage seriously with scholars in other disciplines (economics, sociology, public studies) who already have them.
Will this advice be taken seriously? Judge Posner believes there is an increasing political gap between law professors and the judiciary, and I doubt whether the "leftward-leaning lawyers" who, he says, dominate constitutional scholarship will be disposed to pay much attention to the methodological advice of a Reagan appointee. It will be a pity, though, if this book is ignored or proscribed. It is by far the best -- the most scholarly, the most thoughtful, as well as the sharpest and most provocative -- of the current crop of commentaries on the plight of law today.

686 of 735 DOCUMENTS

The New York Times

PUB-DATE: February 26, 1995

SECTION: Section 6; Page 26; Column 1; Magazine Desk 

LENGTH: 3108 words

HEADLINE: ON THIN ICE, ONE LAST TIME;
Will Steger

BYLINE: By By Jon Bowermaster; Jon Bowermaster is co-author, with Will Steger, of "Crossing Antarctica."

 BODY:
INSIDE WILL STEGER'S WOOD-BURNING SAUNA, at the edge of Pickett Lake in Ely, Minn., in January, it's 150 degrees. Outside it's 10 below. Steger splits the difference, lying silently on a bench next to the pulsing stove, then sprinting out onto the frozen lake and diving through a rough-cut hole in the ice.
After a roll in the snow, Steger re-enters the sauna, dripping wet and smiling. At 135 pounds, he's not quite what you'd expect America's best-known polar explorer to look like. Yet on March 5 he will again put his wiry, 50-year-old body to the test, leading a six-member team in an effort to be the first to dog-sled across the Arctic Ocean in one season, from Siberia to Canada by way of the North Pole. 
 As Steger sweats in the sauna, his mind is racing with the myriad organizational loose ends that plague modern-day adventuring. He still has to come up with half of the $90,000 needed to fly his crew to the starting point in the Aleutians. In a week he's off to New York for an appearance on "Good Morning America" to promote the expedition. Meanwhile, here at his 400-acre compound on the Canadian border, two tons of equipment are being packed for the 120-day trip, including an assortment of high-tech communication gear that will allow the team satellite access to the Internet, and thus to classrooms, scientists and armchair adventurers around the globe. For Steger, the thought of finally being alone on the ice -- or as alone as one can get with millions of cyberviewers following every footstep -- is the one thing that gives him solace.
"Out there," he says, pointing north, "the rhythm becomes very Zen-like. It's harsh, very demanding. But for me expeditions are like a retreat, like a religion. They are the only times I truly relax."
Despite its 21st-century accouterments, Steger's expedition marks the end of an era. Each winter more and more small teams and individuals venture to the Poles. But there are virtually no big, team-effort polar expeditions anymore: they are complex and hard to finance, and there are fewer and fewer "firsts" to be accomplished. Steger has made a name and a career out of a seemingly antiquated pursuit by pure determination. Since the mid-1960's he has covered tens of thousands of miles across the Arctic, Canada, Greenland and the Antarctic on the back of a dog sled.
His public recognition, such as it is, comes from numerous awards and support from the National Geographic Society, the Explorers Club, teachers' groups and scientific associations, and is largely a result of two big expeditions. In 1986, he was a leader of the first unsupported trip by dog team to the North Pole. Adm. Robert E. Peary claimed to have reached the North Pole in 1909, but there is still a question of whether he truly did so or only came close. Thus Steger's brutal, 55-day grass-roots effort (most of the $500,000 budget was raised in small bundles, from friends not corporations) was a relatively untrumpeted American success story. On the strength of that expedition, Steger led a five-man international dog-sled team 3,741 miles across Antarctica in 1989. The trip took seven months; the team endured temperatures that dipped to 113 degrees below zero and one storm that lasted 50 days. No one had ever tried a polar crossing of such length; it is unlikely anyone ever will again.
The coming expedition, says Steger, will be his last big one. Dubbed the International Arctic Project, it is a stab at another first -- the crossing by dog sled of the Arctic Ocean in one season. Unlike Antarctica, the Arctic is not a body of land but an ocean, frozen in winter and treacherously less so as spring advances. In a season when most men his age are worrying about slipping on the sidewalk and the most strenuous thing they do in the cold is pull-start the snow blower, Steger is packing his dog sleds for what may be the most arduous expedition of his life.
STEGER IS IN MANY WAYS A throwback: simultaneously eccentric, quick-witted and ferociously focused. He has greeted with elan the Prime Minister of Japan, chatted with the Bushes in the Rose Garden, talked politics and the environment with Al Gore. Over the years he has charmed millions of dollars from corporate sponsors and thousands of hours of volunteer time from people who identify with his overriding cause: educating the public about the frozen world.
Steger also has what might be described charitably as a playful side. He has, after a couple too many Pisco sours, lofted butter patties from his spoon toward the Mayor of Punta Arenas, Chile. On July 4, 1989, just before leaving for Antarctica, he injured two fingers by holding onto an M-80 explosive a moment too long. He has been known to weep when one of his dogs dies and to beat them for disobeying. He keeps to himself, though he rankles at the suggestion that he is solitary. "That loner tag is something the media has invented," he insists. "It makes for a better image if I'm painted as some nut case living in isolation in the woods."
Some of Steger's former teammates regard him as egocentric, hesitant to share credit, more than happy to leave details to teammates. "The heaviest thing Will and I carried across Antarctica were our egos," muses Jean-Louis Etienne, the French doctor who shared leadership of the Antarctica expedition with Steger, whom Etienne nevertheless refers to as "my brother."
Yet others insist that, in a profession brimming with egomaniacs, Steger is refreshingly low key. "He's the most unprepossessing, most humble hero of exploration I have ever met," says Bill Graves, a former editor of National Geographic who brought Steger into the Society's fold in the mid-1980's and has helped finance each of his expeditions since. "I've never known anybody -- and I've known a lot of them, having run the so-called 'nut squad' at the Geographic for many years -- with less apparent ego and more absolute determination and courage. Yet he's such a tiny little guy. Until you look at his record you can't believe he is actually 15 times the size of the rest of us."
Exploration has long been the core of Steger's life. At age 12 he volunteered to help chart the northern lights as part of the 1957-58 International Geophysical Year project. ("I'd stare at the skies, trying not to blink, and when I'd see a shooting star I'd write down the time, the speed, the color and try and map the trail.") When he was 15, he and his brother Tom built a small boat and Huck Finned down the Mississippi. In 1964, at age 20, he made a 3,000-mile kayak trip from southern Alberta to northern Alaska. The next year he made three first ascents of peaks over 18,900 feet in the Peruvian Andes. In 1969 he made a 4,000-mile kayak expedition on the Mackenzie, Peace and Yukon Rivers, taking time off from his job teaching science in St. Paul.
"We didn't put any restrictions on any of our kids," says his 75-year-old father, William. The elder Stegers remain close to all nine of their kids, but particularly Will, their second, who only recently moved the last of his belongings from their house in the suburbs of St. Paul. "We encouraged them to do whatever they wanted," says the senior William. "Will just happened to stretch every limit."
The period of his 20's also found Steger hitchhiking, hopping freights, doing drugs and bumming around Los Angeles, before finally finding his life's focus at a Zen monastery in the Sierras. In 1970 he started buying land on the edge of the Boundary Waters outside Ely, Minn., and organizing winter skills trips, initially for juvenile delinquents from the Twin Cities. Steger grew all his own food, built a small cabin by hand miles from the road, lived on less than $2,000 a year and slowly bought up neighboring land.
Today, what he calls the Homestead covers 400 acres, with a dozen small cabins, a main lodge, a two-story guest cabin and a 50-foot-tall, half-built "castle" that he dreamed up while traveling across Antarctica. (He earned the money for construction mostly by licensing his name to Target, for such items as Will Steger adhesive bandages, lunch buckets and fireplace logs.) Steger envisions the Homestead as a kind of enviro-educational think tank in the wilderness, accessible to corporations and educators.
In the late 1970's, Steger bred a sturdy breed of huskies and started taking extended trips -- with just one friend, or his second wife, Patti, or alone -- deep into the high Arctic. Those early trips began at the back door of the Homestead, and he dressed in $30 worth of Salvation Army clothes. He remembers those trips as the best days of his life, "leaving and not telling anyone, coming home and not telling anyone." One trip across the Arctic by dog sled and raft covered 7,800 miles. In 1985 he traveled another 5,000 miles from Duluth, Minn., to Point Barrow, Alaska, to test equipment and teammates for the successful run at the North Pole the following year.
"Those were the good old days," he says. "On any day I'd much rather be trying to figure out how to get a team of dogs off thin ice than having to write another letter asking for money."
Indeed, the organizational stresses have taken a toll on Steger, who admits that on many days recently he has found himself wondering why he persists. The $1.5 million expedition has consumed him for four years and cost $250,000 out of his own pocket. And with fewer firsts to be achieved, corporations see less and less reason to sponsor polar ventures. "I'm still in favor of some adventure for adventure sake," says Steger. "But adventure has changed. It demands more creativity, and more responsibility, today than it did 20 years ago. Funding expeditions has never been easy, but now you have to be a lot smarter than just saying you want to get from Point A to Point B. Tying in high technology is a must if you expect sponsorship."
Steger still has his believers. More than 50 companies have donated to this trip, from Science Diet to Lands' End to Eveready, providing everything from dog food to long underwear to batteries. Charlie Orr, chief executive of the San Francisco-based Shaklee Corporation, which has put up $300,000 for this trip, is one of Steger's most ardent supporters. "He is a quiet, compassionate individual and this is a company, I guess, that has a lot of respect for quiet passion," Orr says. "He is also a Renaissance man for the 1990's: a real explorer, adventurer, educator, athlete, humanitarian, environmentalist, a bit of a scientist, world citizen and a businessperson. There are lots of people out there with good ideas -- few see them through like Will does."
NEXT SUNDAY, THE TEAM -- four men, two women and 33 sled dogs -- will leave the Russian Arctic island of Severnaya Zemlya. They hope to reach the North Pole by April 22, the 25th anniversary of Earth Day, and then continue south across the Arctic Ocean to Resolute, the northernmost town in the Canadian Arctic. About two-thirds of the way through the dogs will be flown out; unique canoes fitted with wood-and-plastic runners will be brought in, enabling the team to traverse the last 500 miles, much of it melting ice. Such a lengthy route -- 2,000 miles -- has never before been tried in one season, because the spring thaw turns the seas into a cold, dangerous wash covered by thin crusty ice. (The emphasis here is on the longest trip, not one accomplished without support -- supplies will be dropped off to the team five times during the crossing.)
Although the prospect of failure is rarely considered, two major obstacles could stop Steger: warm temperatures, meaning lots of open water, and uncooperative winds, which could force drifting ice -- and the team -- far off Steger's course. "I've gotten used to people saying, 'You won't make it,' " Steger says. "But I've studied these routes for years in advance. I've spent long months traveling in the Arctic. I know things about these conditions, these places that others do not. It's a very feasible trip if everything goes as planned. Which, of course, it rarely does."
Steger is most concerned about the first 40 miles. "The real challenge is going to be literally getting onto the ice; at that point it is moving past the island, even at the coldest temperatures, at five to eight miles a day. We could get there and be looking at 40 miles of open ocean." Other dangers are the extreme cold (the temperature averages 40 to 50 degrees below zero), storms that could cause the ice to break up around the team while encamped at night, and the ever-present danger of losing a sled or a team member through thin ice. Errant, hungry polar bears are also a concern. Consequently, each sled carries a 12-gauge shotgun.
All of this excites Steger. "This area is the most dynamic, moving surface on earth. From day to day you don't know what to expect. It could be perfectly clear in the morning and then by afternoon you've reached another stretch of open water. In the Arctic, unlike much of Antarctica, monotony is not an issue."
The team is a mix of ages and experience levels: Victor Boyarsky, a 44-year-old Russian with a Ph.D. in mathematics and physics, accompanied Steger on the Antarctica crossing. There's Julie Hanson, 41, a longtime Outward Bound instructor in Ely; Martin Hignell, 33, a former dog trainer with the British Antarctic Survey, and Ulrik Vedel, 28, an officer with the Danish Army who has spent years patrolling Greenland. The inclusion of Takano Takako, 31, a Japanese journalist and adventurer, helps introduce the effort to a Japanese audience.
For all its dangers, the expedition is intended to help draw attention to the importance of the Arctic ecosystem and the critical role it plays in sustaining a healthy global environment. Arctic currents strongly affect the temperature and flow of the world's oceans and exert a powerful influence on the weather patterns of the entire Northern Hemisphere. Around its periphery are found the world's most productive fisheries. It is home to some of the last remaining migratory herds, the caribou. It is also rapidly becoming a repository for a wide range of industrial contaminants; the same qualities that make the Arctic unique in our imaginations -- extreme cold and long periods of darkness -- also act to extend the shelf life of pollutants that drift there.
But it is the high-tech communications ability of the expedition that has Steger most excited about this trip. When Admiral Peary arrived at the North Pole (or said he did) in 1909, the news took months to reach New York and London by telegraph. When Steger visited the North Pole in 1986, he radioed the news to Resolute, some 1,200 miles away, which contacted Steger's home office in St. Paul by telephone and fax. This time around, Steger's arrival at the North Pole will be greeted by live television cameras from "Good Morning America" and the international press.
The expedition will also be followed closely by classrooms around the world. When Steger crossed Antarctica, hundreds of thousands of students were able to follow his daily progress through computer links. Building on that success, the International Arctic Program has developed curricula and study guides, enlisted schools and teachers and set up computers and modems so that classrooms from Japan to California, the Northwest Territories to Brazil, can check in every morning for a report on the team's progress.
Each day the team will radio its position and observations -- what they're seeing and doing, how the dogs are faring, what the weather is like -- to a base in Resolute. There, the details will be posted on the Internet. Updates will also be posted on Econet and the Scholastic Network, an America Online service for teachers and students. All told, some 20 to 25 million children and adults are expected to follow the expedition by computer. For schools without high-tech equipment, E-mail reports and weekly fax updates will be available.
"The Arctic is both my teacher and my classroom," says Steger. "The ice is my blackboard. This expedition lets me use my talents as an explorer to give something back to these children." (Steger is not alone in utilizing the new link between adventurers and classrooms. Earlier this month another Minnesotan, Dan Buettner, a long-distance bicycler, began a three-month tour of the Mayan ruins of Central America; his route will be dictated to him by classrooms around the globe. They will correspond with him through his laptop.)
When off the ice, Steger visits classrooms in person. "Despite the addiction kids have with Game Boys and Power Rangers," he says, "real-life heroes still mean something to them. That's a powerful reason for me to continue these adventures. Truth is, I would not have done this trip without the Internet, without the high-tech educational component. It just wouldn't make sense; the economic climate is not right to support 'just' an adventure.' I wrote my master's thesis in education in 1970 on the basic philosophy that real education comes from within the students. A lot has changed in 25 years and I'm even more convinced that via telecommunication the world -- the kids -- are going to see what we're doing. They're going to interact with us. It's going to draw them out."
FIVE WEEKS BEFORE departing Steger kneels on the floor of his Minnesota cabin poring over maps, pinpointing the few white spots he has yet to visit.
He readily admits this will be his last big expedition. He is tired of the nonstop pace, the constant money worries and the necessity of controlling his wilder urges out of allegiance to his corporate financers. Twice married and twice divorced, Steger would like to wed again soon. And he wants to spend more time around the house. "Pursuing these firsts introduces a lot of tension into your life," he says. "But in order to raise the money and interest the media, you've got to take on big challenges, attempt big things. Virtually everything we've done in the past 10 years has been a first. I want my next challenges to be building a garden, writing a book, kayaking around Ellesemere Island. To be honest, I'm sick of firsts."
He pauses for a moment, listening to the wind outside. "I'm making a 90 degree turn after this one. I'm giving my dogs to my teammates. I want to get away from the 'dairy farm' aspect, the responsibility of the office and employees. I want to start over again. Build a small team of dogs, go out and travel by myself or with one other person. I want to go on teaching. And I want to live a quieter life. I'm very happy to step out of the limelight. I've had my time."

HEADLINE: How One U.S. City Lends a Hand to Russia

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Your article on the growing health problems of Russians (front page, Feb. 19) describes just one of many areas of difficulty in Russian life. Illustrative of all of Russia, the focus of the article was Tula, the sister city of Albany. 
 Tula has recently received a small Agency for International Development grant to plan a demonstration managed health care project, with technical assistance from the Albany-Tula Alliance, a three-year-old, volunteer not-for-profit corporation, in partnership with Community Health Plan, a health maintenance organization, and the Albany Medical Center. We hope funds will be available to allow development of a clinic in connection with Tula's Hospital No. 1. It is one way to support Russia's fledging democracy and to help people in great need.
The cost to the United States to assist Russia and other former Soviet lands to attain democracy and participation in the global economy is minuscule compared to war or even preparation for war. Additionally, bringing Russia into the global economy opens new markets for American products. We are all winners if Russia succeeds in this conversion, but that country needs help.
The Albany-Tula Alliance has had successful educational and cultural exchanges, has connected more than 200 families in Tula with more than 200 families in the Albany region and has worked on economic development to benefit both regions.
Not surprisingly, those of us who have volunteered to help others, as our country was helped in its beginning stages by foreigners, have had our lives enormously enriched.
 
CHARLOTTE S. BUCHANAN
Chairwoman, Albany-Tula Alliance
Albany, Feb. 21, 1995

HEADLINE: Corporations Have Little to Teach Schools

 BODY:
To the Editor:
What is most intriguing about a new survey showing that "employers are divorcing themselves from the schools" is the reaction to this finding on the part of those who conducted the survey and you (front page, Feb. 20). Both take it for granted that the "divide between the schools and the workplace" is "troubling" and even "alarming." 
 This reaction, however, implies that our schools exist chiefly to turn out adequately skilled workers whose labors will help companies triumph over their counterparts in other countries.
This view -- which sees education as an "investment," children as workers in training, and corporate profitability as the touchstone against which to judge the success of our schools -- has led to a degree of business involvement in educational reform that may be far more alarming than its absence.
When corporate groups such as the Committee for Economic Development speak out, their suggestions include more carrot-and-stick controls, more standardized testing and curriculums, more hours in the school day and days in the school year spent doing more drill-and-skill lessons in narrowly conceived academic curriculum.
Never mind that this is precisely the pedagogical prescription that has led to the current state of affairs in which students drop out, tune out or act out -- and even the grade-grubbing "successes" have little interest in learning per se.
There is minimal overlap between business's agenda, which is predictably geared to its own financial success, and such goals as helping students to see intellectual exploration as intrinsically desirable, to become lifelong learners and questioners, compassionate and cooperative people who value democratic participation and social justice.
For those with such a vision of education, less interference by a constituency obsessed with "competitiveness" may be just what the teacher ordered.
 
ALFIE KOHN
Montclair, N.J., Feb. 21, 1995
 
The writer is author of "No Contest: The Case Against Competition."

HEADLINE: Financiers With a Real Place in History

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS 

 BODY:
The crisis at Barings, a British investment banking firm that has endured for nearly 230 years, is in keeping with its history. More than most firms, it has shown a willingness to invest abroad, often in markets that its competitors had deemed far too risky.
It was Barings that arranged the financing for the United States' purchase of the Louisiana Territory from France in 1803. It was a transaction that seemed far too dangerous to some. "We all tremble about the magnitude" of the loan, a Barings executive wrote that year. 
 In recent years, Barings has staked out a position as a leader in research and trading in emerging markets, providing comprehensive evaluations of Latin American and Asian markets as those markets have zoomed and crashed. And it was a Barings trader in Singapore who was reported to have made the big bet -- on Japan -- that brought on the present crisis.
As recently as two weeks ago, the firm was hiring investment bankers for its emerging-markets group. It planned to open offices this year in Russia and Turkey.
Founded in 1762 by Francis Baring, the great-great-great-grandfather of Peter Baring, the current chairman, Barings has remained under family management for virtually its entire history. It is a history that has been close to the British establishment. It was a big financier of Britain's war against France in the Napoleonic age, and it remains a manager of part of Queen Elizabeth's fortune.
But that history has been shaky at various times, most notably just more than a century ago.
In 1890, Barings went broke largely as a result of bad loans to Argentina, a country it expected would grow prosperous. The firm was reorganized only with the support of the Bank of England, which had to keep loans outstanding to the firm until 1895, when they finally were repaid.
That collapse cost the leading members of the family dearly. Edward Charles Baring, the first Lord Revelstoke, was running the firm at the time and plunged heavily into Argentina. Most of his assets, including his art collection, were liquidated.
At the time, sympathy for the Barings was limited, recalled Philip Ziegler in his book, "The Sixth Great Power," which chronicles the history of the firm. Members of the family had been known for haughtiness.
"Fancy the Barings being brought so low," Randolph Churchill, the father of the future Prime Minister, wrote in 1890. "Lord Revelstoke will not be able to ride the high horse as much as he used to."
But, Mr. Ziegler wrote, "though their judgment had been faulty, their probity was undoubted, and the courage and resolution with which they accepted responsibility for the disaster and set about retrieving it, won the admiration of their sourest critics."
In 1890, The New York Times wrote that Barings was "the greatest banking house of all the world," whose "signature has stood always and everywhere as an absolute guarantee." Today, it clearly would not rank so high, but its crisis still is important.
If Barings is unable to continue in business, it will be a blow to emerging markets, many already reeling from the Mexican economic crisis. Barings is a leading investment bank in a great number of emerging markets and has played an important role in raising capital for many of those countries. Other firms may not be as eager as they would have been a year ago to rush in to fill the void with emerging markets' having lost some of their luster.
Those markets, however, were not where Barings' problem arose. It was in Japan, one of the world's most developed and liquid markets. That very liquidity evidently allowed a trader to make huge bets that Japanese stocks would rise this year. And it was those bets that may end the story of one of the most remarkable families in world financial history.

HEADLINE: U.S. Force Steps Up Haiti Arms Seizures

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, Feb. 26

 BODY:
With responsibility for the security of Haiti scheduled to be transferred to the United Nations late next month, American troops here are accelerating efforts to seize weapons and quash threats that might interfere with the "seamless transition" they have promised worried Haitian officials.
During the last month, the number of military street patrols here has multiplied, and their scope and visibility has expanded. Searches for weapons appear to be more aggressive. In another show of force, two former officials who are widely known as leaders of the Tontons Macoute, the paramilitary militia of the former Duvalier family dictatorship, were summoned to American military headquarters where -- in the words of one diplomat -- an American general "read them the riot act." 
 The change came after months of pleading by both the Government of President Jean-Bertrand Aristide and by United Nations officials, all of whom have expressed concern that the American troops were being withdrawn without finishing their mission of creating a more "stable and secure environment" in Haiti.
The more energetic stance also coincided with the arrival in mid-January of a new American military commander, Maj. Gen. George A. Fisher, and elements of the unit he leads, the 25th Infantry Division.
The American-led multinational force, which began landing here last Sept. 19 under the agreement that restored Mr. Aristide to power after three years in exile, is scheduled to be replaced by March 31 by nearly 7,000 United Nations peacekeepers. About 2,500 American troops, most of them from the Hawaii-based 25th Infantry Division, will remain as part of that force, which will be commanded by another American, Maj. Gen. Joseph Kinzer.
The more visible American military presence on the streets of Port-au-Prince, the capital, is in part a response to the increase in common crime that followed the collapse of the Haitian army and police last fall as President Aristide sought to weaken an institution that was responsible for his exile and establish a new civilian police force. The first American commander here, Lieut. Gen. Hugh Shelton, sought to keep his troops away from anything that smacked of police duties, saying "we are not rent-a-cops," but General Fisher seems to have "a more flexible view of things," one diplomat here said.
"Given the fact there really isn't an armed threat to the Government right now, the biggest threat to the economy and the stability of the situation is crime, and so one of our goals is to suppress crime," General Fisher said in an interview at the American military headquarters here. He added, however, that "the front line of defense in fighting crime" remains the interim Haitian police force and international police monitors.
Initially, American troops in Haiti were concentrated almost exclusively in Port-au-Prince and in Cap-Haitien, the country's second largest city. But in anticipation of the United Nations takeover, more troops are being moved to the countryside, where 70 percent of the population lives and where clashes between Aristide supporters and paramilitary groups continue.
"The fact that Port-au-Prince is fairly quiet has given us the capability to put more forces out in these areas than we might have earlier," General Fisher said. With parliamentary elections scheduled for June 4 and campaigning already under way in some places, he added, "additional coverage out in the countryside is desirable during the electoral process."
In both urban and rural areas, American troops have also paid return visits during the last month to about 70 Haitian military installations that American troops first searched last fall. In several cases, such as a raid conducted Jan. 27 on the former headquarters of the Haitian Navy, American troops reported finding large caches of weapons.
It is not clear whether the weapons had been there all along or whether they were moved to the base after the initial searches, General Fisher said. But given the "great concern in the Government and the population about weapons," he said, he wanted to allay those fears. Troops also continue to respond to reports of arms caches hidden by paramilitary groups.
Following complaints that paramilitary leaders and their followers were being allowed to remain at liberty and plot against the Aristide Government, steps have also been taken to curb those activities. In an action that was widely talked about here, Franck Romain, a former mayor of Port-au-Prince, and Williams Regala, a former defense minister, were recently called into American headquarters "to discuss a matter of concern to the multinational force," as an American press release delicately phrased it.
When asked to elaborate, General Fisher said, "We get a lot of information from various sources, and if we think we see something that could be a potential problem, then we will call an individual in and talk to him, just to be sure the problem doesn't materialize."
In addition, the United States seems to have adopted a harder line against paramilitary groups like the Front for the Advancement and Progress of Haiti, or Fraph. After their arrival in September, the few American-led units in the Haitian countryside at times were reported to have treated the group -- which has been blamed for the killing and torture of thousands of people -- as an opposition political party. In several areas, these units have cooperated with its local leaders.
James Dobbins, the State Department's special coordinator for Haiti, said here last week that the United States has "never regarded Fraph as a legal or legitimate organization" and that "the U.N. doesn't either." Any American Embassy or military official who may have said otherwise in the past, he added, "was misinformed."
The more aggressive attitude and higher level of visibility by the American troops has generally pleased the Aristide Government, although one adviser to the President said that he thought a lot of the activity was "just for show." He noted that Mr. Romain and General Regala were quickly released, even though "they are two of the worst Macoutes in the country and ought to be kept under wraps."
The planned withdrawal of American troops has been a source of concern both to Haitian Government officials and to ordinary citizens, who worry that members of paramilitary groups may be tempted to test a new United Nations force that is both unknown and unproven. "To counter some of those rumors," General Fisher said, a campaign is now under way "to let people know that the United Nations is going to be very capable."

HEADLINE: U.S. AND CHINA SIGN ACCORD TO END PIRACY OF SOFTWARE, MUSIC RECORDINGS AND FILM

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: BEIJING, Feb. 26

 BODY:
The United States and China signed an agreement today to end a long-running dispute over how to protect intellectual property like movies, compact disks and computer software. The countries avoided a trade war and announced a plan to make life in China harder for pirates and easier for American investors.
The agreement includes an intensified six-month crackdown on copyright violators beginning on Tuesday, a series of task forces to collect evidence, expanded search-and-destroy powers for Chinese customs officers and the immediate removal of quotas on imports of American films. 
 United States officials described the pact as the most comprehensive and detailed copyright enforcement agreement they had ever negotiated with any country.
Negotiating tactics delayed the agreement until the last minute, and then some. The talks ended 12 days after the United States trade representative, Mickey Kantor, announced that trade sanctions on more than $1 billion of Chinese goods would begin today if no accord was reached.
The last-minute deal may well turn out to be a major victory for both the Clinton Administration and American business. But some American business leaders were cautious, saying enforcement was crucial. [Page D1.]
Indeed, the success of the agreement depends upon its enforcement by Chinese authorities, who so far have been inconsistent when it comes to Chinese copyright and trademark laws. Many United States companies with operations in China complain that local authorities often have little incentive to prosecute businesses that provide them with revenue and enjoy wide latitude on how to punish or excuse offenders.
"There is no question that the key to this agreement will be implementation," the deputy trade representative from the United States, Charlene Barshefsky, said at a news conference late tonight. "And implementation means vigilance on the part of the U.S. as well as political will on the part of the Chinese Government."
Chinese officials, who at times during the negotiations complained that some American demands would violate China's sovereignty, portrayed the agreement as an affirmation of their own commitment to protecting the rights of creative industries.
"We are determined to do well in protecting intellectual property rights, no matter whether an agreement is reached or not," said Wu Yi, China's Trade Minister.
Negotiators went 10 hours past the deadline, agreeing on the final wording of a 22-page document in English and Chinese only minutes before it was signed.
"It is a win-win agreement," Ms. Barshefsky said, asserting that the pact meant both increased access to China for American makers of films and music and improved enforcement against those who copy and sell such products illegally.
If neither side got all it wanted in the negotiations, the Chinese had more at stake. Out of $45 billion in goods traded between China and the United States last year, exports from China to the United States accounted for $37 billion.
Winning American approval on intellectual property protection is also an important step toward gaining membership in the World Trade Organization, a status that Chinese leaders have said they consider an essential element in modernizing their economy.
Ms. Barshefsky said the enforcement agreement, while not aimed at helping China gain access to the World Trade Organization, "would certainly be W.T.O.-compatible." She said the United States was ready to assist China in its application to the trade body, but she denied that there was any intention to support China's application in exchange for concessions in the enforcement agreement.
Ms. Barshefsky would not discuss concessions made by either side during the negotiations. But a person familiar with the negotiations, describing them as torturously complex, said that the final days were spent on the issue of market access for American film and music companies, which have been barred through a series of formal and informal obstacles from the China market.
Ms. Barshefsky said that all quotas would be lifted -- "starting now, today" -- and added that the Chinese had also agreed to allow the establishment of joint ventures for audio and video production, first in the large cities of Shanghai and Guangzhou and expanding to a total of 13 cities by 2000.
"Legitimate U.S. products will be able to replace pirated goods," Ms. Barshefsky said.
The person familiar with the talks, who spoke on the condition of anonymity, also said that the Americans dropped their initial demand that the Ministry of Foreign Trade and Economic Cooperation in China pay for all the pirated computer software being used inside its own offices. This person said that Chinese officials disputed the United States estimates that 95 percent of computer software used in China was pirated and asserted that it was closer to 25 to 30 percent -- and that they would take steps to lower that amount.
But the United States negotiators were apparently unyielding on their insistence that Chinese officials close down several suspected pirates among the 29 production lines capable of making compact and laser disks.
Ms. Barshefsky noted that Chinese authorities had closed seven compact- and video-disk producers in recent weeks, including the Shenfei Laser and Optical System Company in Shenzhen, which she characterized as the "most notorious" of flagrant copyright violators.
Asked if China's failure to close Shenfei would have prevented an agreement, a deputy assistant trade representative, Lee Sands, said, "That's one way of looking at it."
As part of their efforts to monitor enforcement, American officials will receive data from China each quarter on the number of establishments raided, products confiscated and fines imposed.
"There will be ample opportunity for consultation and verification," Ms. Barshefsky said.
China's cooperation with the United States on the issue of intellectual property rights grew out of a 1992 memorandum of understanding, which then led to China's establishment of stronger copyright and trademark laws.
Disagreements between China and the United States, often about enforcement of those laws, inevitably has led to public bickering. At one point, the official Chinese press personally attacked Mr. Sands.

HEADLINE: As Balanced-Budget Vote Looms, Several Senators String Out the Suspense

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 26

 BODY:
Senators get to be senators because they are good politicians, and so it is not unheard of for one of them to withhold some pronouncement on an issue until the last moment, seeking to increase influence or hog the spotlight. But seldom has a circle of attention been so crowded as the one surrounding the vote scheduled in the Senate for Tuesday.
The vote is on the adoption of a balanced-budget amendment to the Constitution, almost certainly the most important vote of the year. Sixty-seven votes, or two-thirds of the Senate, would send the current version of the amendment to the states for ratification votes and, advocates say, set the stage for a profound reshaping of Federal spending priorities. 
 Supporters of the measure say they are one or two votes short of the number they need. And as of this evening, not one but five Senators were still mulling the matter over. The five, all Democrats, are John B. Breaux of Louisiana, Wendell H. Ford of Kentucky, Sam Nunn of Georgia and Kent Conrad and Byron L. Dorgan, both of North Dakota.
"The way we put it in Western Kentucky," Mr. Ford said this week, "is, 'When you're thinkin', you're cogitatin'.' And I'm doing some real cogitating."
No doubt that is true. Still, Mr. Ford's cigarette-rasped twang masks two decades of Washington political experience that have made him the Senate's second-ranking Democrat. Mr. Breaux, the third-ranking Senate Democrat, is widely regarded as one of the country's cannier politicians, and Mr. Nunn has built a considerable reputation in no small part on his habit of being the political weight that tips the scales.
So while the 95 other senators have declared themselves on an issue that has been debated for nearly a month this year, and for many months more in the preceding dozen years, the holdouts continue to wait for a sign that in most cases only they can identify.
"We're down to the fab five, aren't we?" said Thomas Mann, the Congressional scholar at the Brookings Institution and a critic of the amendment.
"People who decide late on issues subject themselves to an unbelievable amount of pressure and scrutiny," he said. "One reason some of them hold out is that they feel that being the pivotal vote attracts some kind of political payoff," like publicity. "Others may feel they can extract something for it -- a change in the amendment; some kind of favor on the side. And others may just be paralyzed with fear."
If votes could indeed paralyze politicians, Tuesday's would be a good candidate. As now written -- there are 23 amendments to be considered before the final vote takes place -- the balanced-budget amendment would be the tightest fiscal straitjacket Congress has ever donned. It would bar the Government from running a deficit as soon as seven years hence, unless 60 percent of members voted otherwise.
Simple mathematics says that a yes vote on Tuesday is an endorsement of at least $1.2 trillion in cuts from the spending that is now anticipated through 2002 -- or, failing that, an increase in taxes.
Although the amendment is wildly popular in most opinion polls, even some Republican senators say privately that they believe its popularity stems from the fact that most voters do not appreciate how much it would affect them.
Skeptics say simple political calculus has led some Democrats to support the amendment even though they do not like it, because they face re-election next year, before its major effects would hit home. Of the 12 Democrats who publicly support the measure, three who are up for re-election in 1996 voted against it when a similar version came before the Senate last year.
The undecided Senators have no such considerations. Messrs. Breaux, Ford, Nunn and Dorgan all supported the amendment in 1994; Mr. Conrad opposed it, but he does not have to run again until 2000.
Rather, other factors -- including, of course, sincere indecision over what is right -- seem to be at work.
Mr. Nunn, a pivotal vote, has staked out his bottom line. On Thursday evening, he said he would oppose the amendment unless Congress approved a change on Tuesday that would prohibit the Federal courts from interpreting the law, and thus making their own fiscal policy.
Democrats say Mr. Nunn is unmovable on the issue and is using his leverage to get what he wants, as he has on issues ranging from confirmations of Presidential nominees to approval of missile treaties.
Mr. Breaux is officially on the fence, but almost everyone believes he will vote for the amendment if he can bring other undecideds along with him; he is believed to have held his silence in part so he can work as an honest broker with fellow Democrats.
Mr. Dorgan, too, is counted by many as a yes vote. But both he and Senator Conrad, who is a likely no vote, are said to be worried by the amendment's failure to guarantee that the annual surpluses in the Social Security trust fund -- $630 billion, with interest, between now and the century's end -- will not be used to pay down the deficit.
Economists dispute the wisdom of those fears, but the concerns have a solid political basis. In today's Bismarck Tribune, North Dakota's third-largest newspaper, the American Association of Retired Persons flatly states in an advertisement that should the amendment be adopted and approved by the states, "by 2002, each Social Security recipient in North Dakota will lose $2,025."
Mr. Ford, squarely in the middle, is said to support the amendment in principle but question how it would work in practice. He, too, worried in an interview about the Social Security fund and court interference in fiscal issues.
Most of all, he does not trust a Republican Congress to implement the amendment fairly. "The gut stopped at the middle aisle in '93," he said sourly, noting that Democrats had enacted $500 billion in new taxes and cuts then without a single Republican vote. "Now they'd be glad to work with us. So what?"
Mr. Breaux said on Thursday that he would announce his decision on Friday, then issued a press release on Friday saying he had changed his mind. Mr. Dorgan was rumored to be planning an announcement on Friday, but made none.
Mr. Conrad, on CNN, said he would vote against the amendment unless he got "assurances" on the courts and Social Security. Otherwise, he said, "it's a very, very difficult vote for me."
Difficult, yes. Whether it is impossible, voters will learn this week.

HEADLINE: Foreign Aid Budget: Quick, How Much? Wrong.

BYLINE: By BARBARA CROSSETTE 

 BODY:
As Congress moves to cut foreign aid and overhaul the apparatus that delivers it, supporters of such assistance are gathering evidence that Americans have little or no idea how small the aid budget actually is.
When confronted with figures, poll takers are discovering, American voters often seem to be instinctively more generous than their representatives in Washington. 
 A survey last month, conducted jointly by two independent policy centers -- the University of Maryland Center for International and Security Studies and the Center for the Study of Policy Attitudes -- found that Americans were wildly off the mark in guessing how much their nation spends on foreign aid.
Among the 801 people surveyed, 15 percent was the median figure given when Americans were asked about how much of the budget went to foreign aid. Other recent polls, including a Harris Poll in November 1993, indicated that Americans thought the Government devoted 20 percent or more of its spending to foreign assistance.
The figure is about 1 percent of the Federal budget. Total foreign aid -- military and non-military combined -- is now about $13 billion a year. About half that, devoted largely to human-development programs relating to health, family planning and economic self-help, is administered by the United States Agency for International Development.
When asked about an "appropriate" expenditure for aid, respondents to the January poll said about 5 percent of the budget, or about five times what is spent now.
Measured another way, by per capita gross domestic product, the United States devotes only 0.15 of its G.D.P. to foreign aid, far less than all other industrial countries. And the figure has been steadily declining in recent years.
According to the Agency for International Development and the Organization of Economic Cooperation and Development, which tracks the economies in richer nations, about $44 of the taxes paid annually by an average American family would go to foreign aid. In Denmark, the equivalent figure would be $900.
"There are four very common myths about foreign aid," said Catherine Gwin, vice president for studies at the Overseas Development Council, an independent policy and research organization in Washington.
"One is that we spend an awful lot of money," Ms. Gwin said in an interview. "All the polling data show that the gap between what we spend and what Americans think we spend is astronomical."
"The second myth is that Americans are very generous as a nation, despite the fact that the percentage we spend is lowest in the O.E.C.D.," she said.
This myth, however, does not extend to personal contributions to private groups, donations that continue to rise, said John W. Sewell, president of the policy and research organization.
"The third myth is that aid is money down a rat hole, and that there have been no successes," she said, referring to a comment on such aid made by Senator Jesse Helms, Republican of North Carolina. "And the fourth is that there is no direct, immediate payback to the U.S."
There are reasons for the "rat hole" argument, Ms. Gwin said.
"During the cold war, we allocated foreign aid money on cold war rationales and then critiqued it on the basis of development effectiveness -- so we get a Zaire," she said, alluding to United States support an anti-Communist Zairian leader who has been widely viewed as corrupt and authoritarian.
Or a Vietnam. Ms. Gwin said many supporters of aid became disillusioned after large amounts of money were spent trying to prevent the South from falling to Communism. "We have never really recovered," she said.
"During the cold war, we gave aid for one reason and then judged it for another," she said. "But in countries where the aid was given for development reasons, the anecdotes are much more positive -- and there are many of them. Americans don't know what's been happening in literacy rates, in population and fertility decline, and how much of a change has occurred."
J. Brian Atwood, the Administrator of the Agency for International Development, said in an interview that the United States "has been a leader in the world."
"For example," Mr. Atwood said, "43 percent of all the family-planning services offered to women around the world are from A.I.D."
If other donors joined the campaign with similar commitment, he said, "we can indeed achieve the goal of stabilizing world population by the year 2030.
"There is nothing right about the words 'foreign' or 'aid,' " he said. "It's not charity. It's not a gift. It's something we do in our own interests."
Whether stemming the spread of epidemic diseases, helping to create jobs that keep would-be immigrants at home or sustaining and nurturing new democracies and their shaky private-sector economies, the job of aid, Mr. Atwood said, is to keep countries from "falling off the edge," with global results. He said Americans understand that the United States cannot escape the consequences.
The development agency points to a July 1993 Gallup Poll that said only one in four Americans agreed that the United States "should avoid becoming involved with other nations as much as possible." In the mid-1980's, Gallup found that 3 in 10 Americans took that position.
"Because of the impression that has been created that our foreign aid program is nothing more than an international welfare program, it has been possible for demagogues, isolationists and populists -- some all in the same person -- to exploit that," Mr. Atwood said.

HEADLINE: Group of 7 Defines Policies About Telecommunications

BYLINE: By NATHANIEL C. NASH, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: BRUSSELS, Feb. 26

 BODY:
The Governments of the seven largest industrialized countries, seeking to find common ground on the future of the global information network, today backed broad concepts of competition, deregulation, the protection of intellectual property rights and security of data.
The statement came at the end of a three-day meeting of officials of the so-called Group of Seven countries who were trying to prepare a common policy on how to approach the future of telecommunications. 
 "We made a giant step forward," Commerce Secretary Ronald H. Brown of the United States said at a news conference today, adding that the accord among the group "far exceeded the expectation going into the meeting."
Heading into the conference, some officials believed that France and Canada, worried that the giant American telecommunications and broadcast companies would overwhelm their industries, could set major obstacles to an accord that bases the future of telecommunications on free markets and free competition.
Yet the conference ended in remarkable harmony, as the nations managed to put aside their differences. Even the French, while stressing that governments would need to monitor world information systems, supported a joint statement that effectively embraces a vigorous free-market approach.
"A year ago the question was whether we could reach this kind of agreement," an American official said. "But even they seemed to realize that you have to open these markets. You can't afford to keep them closed."
The conference was sponsored by the European Commission, the executive body of the European Union. Commission officials have strongly supported the opening of telecommunications to competition. Most of its member states, though, including Germany and France, have maintained telecommunications monopolies and do not plan to deregulate telephone and data transmission until 1998. Of all the European Union member countries, only Britain and Sweden are considered to have open markets in telecommunications.
At the conference, the first held by the group to deal with what was called the global information infrastructure, German officials clearly supported open markets. "Private sector competition must be the driving force," Gunther Rexrodt, Germany's Economic Minister, said.
The joint statement, which addressed a number of concerns raised by France and Canada, called for "ensuring universal provision of and access to services," "promoting equality of opportunity to the citizen," "promoting diversity of content, including cultural and linguistic diversity," and "recognizing the necessity of worldwide cooperation with particular attention to less developed countries." The Group of Seven countries comprises the United States, Canada, Japan, Britain, France, Germany and Italy.
Martin Bangemann, the European Community's Commissioner for Industry, said that "we must insure that the information society is not divided up into the have's and have-not's."
Indeed, a tone for the conference was set on Friday night when at the opening dinner, Thabo Mbeki, South Africa's First Deputy President, told conferees, "The reality is that there are more telephone lines in Manhattan, New York, than in the sub-Sahara Africa," adding that half of the world's population had never made a telephone call.
Governments in Europe had earlier expressed fears about deregulation. For example, some were troubled that the privatization of their state-owned telephone monopolies would create significant unemployment in a Europe that is already saddled with its highest unemployment since World War II, with 17 million people unemployed.
Dismantling those monopolies will probably mean considerable reduction in employment in those sectors, as well as fights with strong telecommunications unions. But conference attendees apparently agreed that the inflow of new investment, and the ensuing economic growth, would more than compensate for the short-term loss of jobs.
And the conference also concluded that open telecommunications markets would not eliminate cultural diversity, but promote it, as it makes access to broadcasting available to small cultural groups.
Some attendees said the meeting helped define a clear set of principles for the speedy opening of markets. Stuart E. Eizenstat, the United States Ambassador to the European Union, said he believed that as a result of the conference, some European countries would deregulate their telecommunications markets before the 1998 deadline set by European Commission regulations.
 The conference also proposed 11 pilot projects as the first concrete step in advancing the information superhighway. The projects range from establishing links among libraries, museums and classrooms around the world to sharing information on the environment, natural disasters and disaster management to building compatible links between global information systems.

HEADLINE: Japan's Ghost In China Pact;
U.S. Tries to Avoid Old Trade Mistakes

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

 BODY:
The deal struck at the last minute yesterday between China and the United States may well turn out to be a major victory for both the Clinton Administration and American business, proof that the right mix of bluster and economic incentives can convince China's leaders that their only choice is to operate by the world's trade rules, rather than their own.
But just beneath the surface, the argument over China's piracy of Whitney Houston's songs and Microsoft's MS-DOS was just the first big skirmish in a struggle that will probably last for years. It is a fight to make sure that the United States does not make the same mistakes with China that it made for decades with Japan. 
 Whether Washington prevails this time may not be clear until 2010 or so, but as Mickey Kantor, the United States trade representative, said the other day, "you have to start somewhere."
Throughout most of Japan's post-war rise, Washington found every reason possible not to make trade -- and America's own economic health -- the No. 1 issue with a close ally. The cold war was on, and the Japanese islands were America's unsinkable battleship in the Pacific. By the time anyone realized the enormity of the American mistake, it was already far too late.
In some respects, China today is about where Japan was in the late 1960's, a powerhouse of growth, a huge exporter, and the leading candidate to overtake the United States someday as the world's largest economy. To get there, Beijing's economic planners have surveyed the world and found their role model -- Tokyo.
So while the argument over the last few weeks was ostensibly about pirated copies of the movies "Jurassic Park" and "The Lion King," the real worry was about the future of McDonnell Douglas jet technology and Chrysler designs for mini-vans in a country that seemed determined to let 1,000 pirates bloom. That is why American business stuck by the Administration, even if it meant angering the same Chinese officials with whom they were striking deals left and right.
"Everyone realized that piracy is not just a problem, it is a fundamentally ingrained part of China's economic plan," said Gregg Mastel, a senior fellow at the Economic Strategy Institute in Washington and a specialist on trade with China. "And that is why so many people are skeptical that this agreement, or any agreement, can stomp this out. But you have to give the Administration credit for recognizing the problem and getting serious about it."
By sticking to its guns, the Administration has won a series of remarkably specific commitments requiring the Chinese authorities to inspect every compact disk factory and software house, and to close down the offenders or force them to license the technology they use.
It is a far more invasive procedure than China would ever allow an outside power to dictate in other realms -- human rights, for example -- and all the more remarkable because so many of the factories are enormous sources of revenue for the Chinese Army and the families of the leadership. That is why so many people inside and outside the Administration suspect that new piracy factories may spring up as quickly as old ones are closed.
But the reason the Administration won this round, at least on paper, is that it holds the keys to something Beijing's leaders want desperately: entree into the World Trade Organization, the newly created United Nations of commerce. Beijing has insisted that it be admitted on special terms, exempt from many free-trade rules because of China's status as a huge but still developing nation. Mr. Kantor has said no way.
The only choice, he has said, is for China to join "under commercial, not political terms," an issue he probably will take up in an expected trip to China next month. And without protecting the intellectual property of Americans and others who operate in China, he said, the country could kiss the W.T.O. goodbye.
"We're determined not to get into the same position we got into with Japan," Mr. Kantor said in a telephone interview yesterday. "We had a cold war strategy with the Japanese and Europeans that worked, making them bulwarks of democracy by letting them export freely to our markets even while their own were closed. But those sanctuary markets soon became a matter of entitlement. And it's been hard, to say the least, to break that."
By some measures, it may already be too late in China's case. Its trade surplus with the United States grew by 30 percent last year, to about $30 billion, making it half the size of Japan's. China already exports to the United States four times the goods, measured in dollar terms, that the United States sends back to China. The ratio of imports to exports with Japan has never come close to a gap that large.
Moreover, America's dealings with China in the last year or so have left Washington's economic leaders so bruised that they are pining for the simplicity and predictability of dealing with Japan -- preferring, in short, to clean the teeth of a wily old crocodile than an unpredictable lion. "We've done this for so long in Japan at least we know who is making the decisions and how they are likely to react," one senior Administration official said.
"In China, we get one intelligence report after another," the official added, "and we still don't really have a clue who calls the shots on these kinds of issues." The problem will only worsen, many experts suspect, if the country's political succession crisis deepens.
For Mr. Clinton, who has made international trade the linchpin of his strategy for economic expansion, there is probably limited joy in the agreement reached yesterday. He is bound to be criticized for holding China's feet to the fire on intellectual property in a way that he never did on human rights. (Mr. Kantor, however, says the new agreement will also help the human rights cause, because China has agreed to accept an unlimited number of American movies and to open more markets to American companies. The process, he says, is likely to spread understanding of democracy's reach.)
And if Mr. Clinton succeeds in changing China's economic behavior, the evidence will not be clear until well after his Presidency is over, even if he serves two full terms.
He would hardly be the first President to discover that prying open China's markets is not a big political victory in the heartland. One of Mr. Clinton's predecessors, Mr. Kantor noted at a news conference yesterday, wrote to the Chinese that if companies on both sides of the Pacific "will trade, there should be rules, so that they shall not break your laws nor our laws." The letter was sent by John Tyler in 1843, and resulted in a historic first treaty with China the next year.
It didn't help him much: Tyler failed to get his party's nomination for a second term.
