HEADLINE: A Tennessean Quietly Prepares To Make His Move for President

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 26

 BODY:
After a recent national television interview, Lamar Alexander invited his advisers to pick him apart in a New Hampshire hotel room. But he was his own toughest critic, conceding that he needed to punch up his meandering presentation and remarking dryly, "After awhile I get to the point."
The mild-mannered Mr. Alexander -- a Tennessee stalking horse of sorts -- lacks the caustic tongue of the two other leading and far better known contenders for the Republican Presidential nomination, Senators Bob Dole and Phil Gramm. 
 But because of his dogged organizational efforts and the zeal with which he has positioned himself as the Washington outsider, Mr. Alexander, who on Tuesday plans to declare his candidacy in his hometown of Maryville, Tenn., has propelled himself to join the top tier along with the two Senators.
"He's the most dangerous because no one's paying attention to him," said David Carney, a senior adviser to Mr. Dole.
The 54-year-old former Tennessee Governor describes himself as a conservative, preaching a grass-between-the-teeth populism that favors dismantling billions of dollars in Federal programs and returning them to the states.
The morning he invited his aides to critique him at the Holiday Inn in Manchester, N.H., Mr. Alexander took notes as Mike Murphy, his chief consultant, instructed him on sharpening his message: "You are different from all these guys: Businessman. Governor. Nashville. And I have a vision of where I want to take the country, which is out of D.C. Back to families. Boom. That's the mission." Mr. Alexander repeated those points at two events that day.
Still, there are important questions about whether Mr. Alexander, a millionaire and New York-educated lawyer, can convincingly present himself as an outsider -- or as a conservative. Though he does not highlight it in his campaign speech, Mr. Alexander knows his way around what he calls the "arrogant empire" of Washington.
After serving as president of the University of Tennessee, he was President George Bush's Secretary of Education for two years. He started out in Washington as a legislative assistant to his mentor, then-Senator Howard H. Baker Jr. of Tennessee, and worked in the Congressional relations office for President Richard M. Nixon.
Mr. Alexander's conservative bona fides are also suspect, because he has the reputation and record of a middle-of-the-road Republican who is not driven by ideological passion.
For instance, he raised taxes during his two terms as Governor from 1979 to 1987, a fact that he defends, saying it was "to pay teachers more for doing well and for building the best road system in the states." He says he is against abortion but does not have a well-articulated view of how the Republican Party should grapple with the issue. And some conservatives do not take seriously his call to abolish the Education Department, his old agency.
"There's some reinvention," said Edwin C. Hargrove, a political science professor at Vanderbilt University. "He's not a natural populist, but he's turned himself into one. It's a little bit of an act. He's not an ideologue like Phil Gramm. He doesn't come with a heavy set of dogma. His values are to believe in limited Government."
Mr. Alexander is hitching his candidacy to the success of popular Republican Governors, like John Engler of Michigan and Stephen Merrill of New Hampshire. He is saying, essentially, trust the governors, not Washington, to change welfare, health care and job-training programs.
He asserted at a forum in New Hampshire last week: "The question of whether you have two-year limits on welfare is not for my friends here from Washington to decide. It's for Governor Merrill and the citizen legislature and the town councils of New Hampshire to decide."
If voters accept Mr. Alexander as a conservative and a populist, another question arises: Even at a time when returning power to the states is in vogue, do Republican primary voters want a President who talks more about process and states' rights than ideology?
"I don't think a process argument that, 'I'm going to send all these programs back to the states,' is going to carry him through to get the nomination," said Robert M. Teeter, Mr. Bush's campaign chairman in 1992. "It's got a body of thought behind it, but it just doesn't strike me as a gut issue enough. The Presidency is about leadership, about setting national economic policy, leading the Congress, foreign policy -- the big issues."
Jim Pinkerton, a Republican theoretician, warned that Republican voters may want a more direct message. "Will they go for, 'Elect me, and I will kick down the welfare state,' " he said, "or Alexander, who says, 'Elect me, and I will help the Governors do it one by one by one.' "
Already, Mr. Alexander has found himself retreating a bit. His catchy rallying cry about turning Congress into something of a part-time citizens' legislature -- "cut their pay and send them home" -- played well in the 1994 campaign. But now that the Republicans have swept Congress, he has largely shelved that approach for variations with less bite, like, "We know what to do."
Despite questions about his message, Mr. Alexander, a graduate of Vanderbilt University and the New York University Law School, is widely viewed among Republicans as a shrewd, disciplined contender who has put populist imagery to good use.
After losing a bid for Governor in 1974, he tried again in 1978, donning a lumberjack shirt and walking 1,000 miles across the state to end up in the Governor's mansion. Laying the groundwork for this Presidential campaign, last summer he drove 8,800 miles around the country, trying to portray himself as a friend of ordinary Americans. He furthered that effort, and built a network of support, by hosting a satellite television show that featured Republicans around the country and was broadcast out of a studio that was once home to "Hee-Haw."
With his easygoing, if not compelling, personal style, Mr. Alexander's hope is that voters will search for a fresher face. He also hopes that a sitting governor does not jump in the race and usurp his anti-Washington message.
"If the party wants a senior, experienced leader who's made a career in Washington, then they'll choose Bob Dole," he said in an interview. "If they want somebody who's a Washington outsider, then they'll choose me."
Mr. Alexander has forced Republicans to take notice because he has surrounded himself with smart advisers and has signed an impressive roster of fund-raisers for former Presidents Bush and Ronald Reagan.
While there is no evidence in early polling that Mr. Alexander's organizational efforts have paid off, other campaigns are watching closely. Though Mr. Gramm depicts the race as between himself and Mr. Dole, Charles Black, a senior adviser to Mr. Gramm, said of Mr. Alexander, "I see him as a potential threat."
Mr. Black added: "But I'm not sure that the '94 election didn't repeal his message. It's hard to talk about cutting their pay and sending them home when them is us."
Some White House officials contend that Mr. Alexander might be the most competitive challenger to Mr. Clinton because he comes off as a steady leader who is not as driven by rigid ideology.
But the challenge for Mr. Alexander is to persuade voters that he has not reinvented himself. He said he had scoured his past and found several examples of conservatism, including his support for a moment of silence in schools and his shutdown of Tennessee's offices in Washington.
"I'm not always thought of as especially conservative," he said. "It's almost a matter of disposition and temperament." But Mr. Alexander also said there had to be room for Republican moderates. "If we become too rigid, if we seem too intolerant," he said, "if we sound like we're making lists of people we don't like, we're going to run off enough people that we'll lose the election -- and deserve to."
Mr. Alexander emphasizes his soft side -- he is an accomplished classical and country pianist -- as an apparent contrast to Mr. Dole and Mr. Gramm. Yet he has to demonstrate that he has the temperament to endure a national campaign.
On a recent morning, aides to Mr. Alexander invited a reporter to his hotel suite in New York to sit in as he made telephone calls to potential supporters. The usually calm candidate grew prickly and uncooperative, insisting that he rarely made phone calls.
And he ridiculed the widely held notion that candidates frequently use the phone to woo supporters. "Let me tell you the deal about telephone calls in Presidential campaigns," he said. "They're not the way you make political contacts."
Mr. Alexander later apologized, saying he was embarrassed by what he called his rude behavior. He said that he was not quite used to what a candidate has to go through to run a national campaign.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
Press

BYLINE: By William Glaberson 

DATELINE: MADISON, Wis.

 BODY:
ACCORDING to Frank Denton, the editor of the morning paper here in the capital of Wisconsin, people are sick of old-fashioned journalism that simply points out problems.
He says he has found a solution. "Our journalism is not here to fry people -- although we fry people all the time," Mr. Denton said. "Our main goal is in helping the public find the solutions to problems." 
 Mr. Denton's 86,000-circulation daily, The Wisconsin State Journal, is a leading practitioner of what is called civic or public journalism, an approach that is being tried by more than a dozen news organizations.
Advocates of public journalism say news professionals should actively work to improve public life rather than simply describe its breakdown. These advocates say news organizations should focus less on conflict and more on educating citizens and providing them with ways to participate in public issues.
Public journalism has begun to attract widespread attention only in the last year or so, but it already has a cadre of national spokesmen and critics. Advocates say traditional journalism has failed, while critics say the job of journalism is to report on society, not to participate in it.
That discussion often veers into the academic. But in Madison, where The State Journal has been experimenting with public journalism for more than two years, the debate is no longer theoretical. The discussion that is beginning here over the benefits and dangers of public journalism is likely to be repeated in communities across the country.
For example, the Mayor of Madison, Paul R. Soglin, said he was troubled by the methods being used by The State Journal in one of its most visible public journalism experiments, a long series called City of Hope, which has explored problems of crime, poverty, racism and unemployment in the region.
To make the process less confrontational and to increase the possibility of finding solutions, Mr. Denton said he had invited city, state and community leaders to periodic meetings at the paper while his reporters prepared their articles.
Reporters and editors discussed their findings and potential solutions at the meetings. In a sharp break with tradition, the reporters and editors gave the leaders detailed briefings on what would be published in their articles. One result was that the group called together by the newspaper convened a "jobs summit" to develop an economic development strategy for the area.
Mayor Soglin says the experiment has been awkward. The State Journal, the most powerful editorial voice in the region, has been "wearing two hats" by reporting a story it helped to create, he said. And Mr. Soglin says he worries about the "top down" nature of a process in which a group selected by editors, rather than by voters, is making policy proposals.
But mostly, he said, he worries that by mixing the roles of creating news and reporting it, the newspaper may get too much power.
Some of Mr. Denton's reporters have expressed concerns about whether they are getting too close to public figures. Mr. Denton says the paper's news professionals must never advocate one policy or another because that could hurt the credibility of the paper.
But he said that gathering influential people and giving them advance information was an innovation that made journalists less adversarial with prominent people in the community. "Instead of making them feel like targets -- and almost victims -- of our journalism we are trying to make them, ultimately, instruments of solutions," he said.
Dave Zweifel, the editor of The Capital Times, the afternoon newspaper here, says he fears that journalists lose perspective when they orchestrate the news. The Capital Times, with a circulation of 23,000 and old sympathies for Wisconsin's progressive political movement, competes for news with The State Journal, which has always been much more politically conservative. The papers have different owners but share many business operations.
Mr. Zweifel said he thought the concept of public journalism and the movement that is growing up around it have made some journalists lose sight of the most important traditions of their profession. "My idea of public journalism," he said, "is finding out what's going on and raising hell about it. But I don't think I should go down to the mayor's office and hold his hand."
Not all of The State Journal's public journalism experiments have raised hackles. The paper has sponsored a series of town meetings, public hearings and "citizen juries" in partnership with public television and radio stations in Wisconsin, a local public relations firm and the CBS television affiliate here.
Since 1992, the events, called We the People/Wisconsin, have included town hall meetings on various issues and public events in which the public, rather than journalists, question politicians. Citizen panels have grappled with policy options on complex questions like what should be done about local property taxes and revamping health care.
The newspaper and broadcast stations have coordinated coverage. Readers, viewers and listeners have been able to view the events, read about them in The State Journal and request more detail and analysis.
The series has been praised as a sophisticated effort to help people understand public issues. But while public journalism may occasionally be noble, it is not always scintillating, said Marc Eisen, editor of Isthmus, an alternative weekly in Madison. "It becomes something you respect and admire," Mr. Eisen said, "but you don't necessarily rush home to watch it."

HEADLINE: BOOKS OF THE TIMES;
For Endangered Species, Humans Present 'the Cooked Frog Problem'

BYLINE: By CHRISTOPHER LEHMANN-HAUPT 

 BODY:
NOAH'S CHOICE
The Future of Endangered Species
By Charles C. Mann and Mark L. Plummer
302 pages. Alfred A. Knopf. $24.
The biblical Noah had it easy, write Charles C. Mann and Mark L. Plummer in their new book, "Noah's Choice: The Future of Endangered Species." He had clear instructions from God, the material to build the ark, a known number of animals to save and the right amount of space to keep them. "He and his sons just stood on the gangplank and let everything in," the authors write. "When no creature was waiting outside, he shut the door and waited for the rain." 
 But today, "in the role of modern Noahs," the authors continue, we confront choices that are both momentous and tragic. We have no instructions. "We don't even know the number of potential passengers, although we know that whatever ark we choose to build will be unable to accommodate everything. What will be saved and what will be left behind? There is no automatic answer."
Mr. Mann, a magazine writer, and Mr. Plummer, an economist, together wrote "The Aspirin Wars: Money, Medicine and 100 Years of Rampant Competition." They therefore bring a broad background to the problem of endangered species. But unfortunately, the answers they provide in this book plunge us only deeper into the problem.
To illustrate the difficulties of their subject, they consider in particular the cases of the American burying beetle (Nicrophorus americanus Olivier); the Karner Blue butterfly (Lycaeides melissa samuelis); the whooping crane, or American crane (Grus americanus); the snail darter (Percina tanasi), and two birds, the black-capped vireo (Vireo atricapillus) and the golden-cheeked warbler (Dendroica chrysoparia).
These reports are lively and entertaining. Of the burying beetle they write, "The larvae rear up and stroke the jaws of their parents to get breakfast." An expert tells them: "It's traditional family values. You don't see that much with insects."
Of the whooping crane: "Unaware of us, the birds walked with the intelligent gravity of the benign aliens in a science fiction film. Their yellow gaze was cool, quick and remote. Legs bent widdershins, straightened, bent again." Descriptions of such creatures make one root all the harder for the endangered-species legislation that was intended to protect them.
Yet the authors' aim is precisely in the opposite direction, to call into question this country's approach to the problem of species extinction. They introduce the burying beetle to show how protecting a species of dubious value can impede a humanitarian project, in this case a highway to help poor Choctaw Indians get to a hospital more easily. They write about the Karner Blue butterfly to dramatize the complexity of the changes "necessary to reverse the damage we have done to biodiversity."
They touch upon whooping cranes to raise the question why species should be saved in the first place. Should we do so for their utilitarian value to humans? The authors say no, because "biodiversity as a whole has overwhelming utilitarian value, but most individual species do not." Certainly the species of monkey in which the AIDS virus originated did not prove useful to people.
Then should we save species for the sake of what the authors call the Noah principle, "because losing species that have long been on earth lessens the total value of the planet"? Again they say no, because the Noah principle creates an attitude toward all species that "thinkers since Kant have called a perfect duty."
And they continue: "The problem with creating a perfect duty to biodiversity is twofold. It is unethical, because trying to save every species perfectly would force our society to destroy many or all of its other accomplishments, an act of self-immolation that the ecologically concerned cannot force on others, who may have different but equally worthy goals. And it is impracticable, because this perfect duty is impossible to fulfill, even if our society were willing to turn back 300 years of its history."
Yet this perfect duty to species is what the authors believe has been embodied by the Endangered Species Act of 1973, which, they insist, has transformed Federal biologists into "ecological mandarins" and allowed species like snail darters, black-capped vireos and golden-throated warblers to stand in the way of worthwhile human endeavors.
What the authors would prefer is for environmental laws to use what they call Noah's choice instead of Noah's principle. They would accomplish this end by unlinking the two stages of action required by the Endangered Species Act so that the decision to list a species as endangered would not automatically lead to the design and enactment of a program to conserve that species. Instead the decision to conserve or not would be weighed against human needs through "a countrywide debate on the value of biodiversity, and a democratic vote on the means of its protection."
All of which makes good sense up to a point. The trouble is that beyond that point the authors fail to consider the root cause of environmental problems, which surely is human population growth. If people continue to proliferate and the decision to save species is balanced against basic human needs, the choice will inevitably favor people.
Without some limit on population growth, the world is bound to encounter what the authors call "the cooked frog problem," which they define this way: "Drop a frog in a pot of boiling water, they say, and it will immediately leap free. But put that same frog in a pot of cool water and gradually turn up the heat, and the frog will happily sit and be cooked to death."
Balancing species and human needs without controlling population growth sounds like a perfect way of gradually turning up the heat on the earth's species. And as the authors make so clear in this otherwise sensible book, there is no way to uncook the frog.

HEADLINE: CS First Boston's Exit Leads to New Bond Firm

BYLINE: By LESLIE WAYNE 

 BODY:
Just two weeks after the CS First Boston Corporation decided to get out of the public-finance business, some of its top employees said Friday that they were starting their own municipal bond house to be made up largely of former CS First Boston municipal bankers.
The new firm is starting with $25 million in capital and is headed by three top CS First Boston municipal bankers: Bradford Higgins, Thomas Barry and Donald Carey. The firm, called Cambridge Capital Partners, plans to build on its relationships with former CS First Boston clients and will recruit extensively, but not exclusively, among former CS First Boston employees. 
 Mr. Higgins, a former CS First Boston managing director, said the firm would not be "a second Boston or a little Boston."
"We're Cambridge and that's different," he said.
The firm will build on relationships that Cambridge's founders have maintained with municipal issuers over the years. Some of CS First Boston's biggest municipal clients are New York City and the Metropolitan Transportation Authority and the Commonwealth of Massachusetts.
"We've been working around the clock to take the franchise," Mr. Higgins said. "We want to look like the same team, but under a different name."
Earlier this month, CS First Boston announced that it was closing its municipal bond department, which had employed 110 people and usually held the fifth or sixth position in volume of bond underwritings.
In January, the Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette Securities Corporation announced that it was leaving the municipal bond business. Rising interest rates have caused fewer municipalities to issue bonds, shrinking the profits for brokerage firms. The last year was considered one of the worst in memory and there is little hope in the industry that 1995 will see a great increase in volume.
Still, Mr. Carey is confident. He said that CS First Boston's municipal department was profitable in 1994 and that he disagreed with the decision to leave the business. "It was a mistake," he said. "We're a good group who can make money even in adverse markets. Our theory about this venture is like 'Field of Dreams.' If we build the firm, the clients will come."
Mr. Carey is the son of former Gov. Hugh Carey of New York, who will be an adviser to the firm. The younger Mr. Carey would not identify the source of the $25 million in capital, but he said none of it had come from CS First Boston. The investors, which he said he would identify this week, include other Wall Street firms, dealers, banks, private investors and the firm's partners.
The firm anticipates hiring about 30 to 35 people, mainly, but not solely, from CS First Boston, he added.
"By getting out of the business, First Boston gave us an opportunity," Mr. Carey said. "It's like the person who used to date your wife or your husband before you did. When they broke up, they were doing you a favor."

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
Seizing on Pataki's Budget as Issue, Democrats Begin Assault on Cuts

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ALBANY, Feb. 26

 BODY:
As they struggle to rebuild their party from the ashes of last year's electoral defeats, New York State's Democrats have seized on an issue they believe will rally their demoralized troops: Gov. George E. Pataki's state budget.
In speeches across the state, in political literature, in television and radio commercials, Democratic leaders and their allies have begun a full-scale assault on the deep cuts in Mr. Pataki's budget, calling them a boon for the wealthy but devastating for the middle class. 
 Their strategy seems transparently simple: to reclaim the working-class and middle-income voters from upstate and suburbia who fled the Democrats last fall to help Mr. Pataki, a Republican, defeat the incumbent Democratic Governor, Mario M. Cuomo.
But the plan carries an equally clear risk. In opposing a budget that meets Mr. Pataki's campaign pledge to make government smaller, the Democrats could open themselves to charges of supporting the tax-and-spend, big-government policies that voters so soundly rejected last year.
Such concerns are shrugged off by the Democrats' leading spokesman, Assembly Speaker Sheldon Silver, who has traversed the state in recent weeks arguing that the painful details of the Pataki budget will ultimately drive voters back into the Democratic fold.
In Buffalo last Wednesday, Mr. Silver told several hundred Democratic contributors that Mr. Pataki's proposal to cut state income tax rates by 25 percent or more will benefit only the wealthy. "This is not a tax cut, this is a tax switch," he said, contending that the Pataki plan would reduce state aid to local governments and cause them to raise property taxes that fall heavily on the middle class.
The next day, Mr. Silver warned a gathering in Buffalo of Jewish community leaders that Mr. Pataki, to pay for his tax cuts, would slash government services that the middle class depends on, from urban mass transit to state colleges to suburban road repair programs.
"The wealthy as a rule don't send their children to state university," he said, warning that state college tuition could rise by $1,300 or more a year under Mr. Pataki's budget plan. Mr. Silver told the group that Mr. Pataki's cuts in health care spending show that "prolonging life is not a priority of this Governor."
Notably absent from Mr. Silver's presentations have been discussions of the poor, even though Mr. Pataki has proposed cutting $1.9 billion for social-services programs.
Rather, Mr. Silver tells his mostly middle-class audiences that welfare cuts will increase the number of homeless people living on their streets. And he warns that cutting Medicaid, the health care program for the elderly, poor and disabled, will hurt the quality of care for all people, regardless of income. "Whether a nurse shows up in 30 seconds or in 10 minutes is affected by Medicaid cuts," he says.
With voters showing little sympathy for welfare recipients, Mr. Silver's strategy of linking middle-class to lower-class interests might be the only way to protect programs for the poor this year, Democrats and political analysts say.
But analysts also said the strategy could backfire if the Democrats were cast as defending bloated and inefficient government programs. "They won't come off as credible if they come off as shills for the health care industry," said Fred Siegel, a history professor at Cooper Union in Manhattan.
To bolster Mr. Silver's campaign, a coalition led by labor unions close to the Democratic Party began running radio and television commercials this weekend that attack Mr. Pataki's proposed Medicaid cuts. The unions include Local 1199, the health and hospital workers union, and District Council 37, the municipal employees union.
One of the commercials, which will run predominantly in Republican Senate districts upstate, shows a middle-class couple in their kitchen fretting that Medicaid cuts will end home-care services for their elderly mother and force them to put her into an expensive nursing home. The camera pans to show the mother anxiously eavesdropping on the argument from the dining room.
Then, an announcer's voice says: "Tell your state legislators our families are too important. Stop Pataki's Medicaid cuts." A closing panel says in writing, "If they don't, we'll remember."
Vic Fingerhut, a Democratic pollster and media consultant who designed the commercials, said Mr. Pataki was politically vulnerable on Medicaid because, according to his polls, voters already think Republicans don't care about the elderly. "When we get him on the defensive, his numbers will go down and all Republican numbers will go down," he said.
If the figures do fall, it will mark an important initial victory for Mr. Silver, a Manhattan Democrat who is the first non-governor to lead the party in 20 years. Soft-spoken and deliberative in manner, Mr. Silver has not always seemed comfortable with his new role. Unlike Mr. Cuomo, he does not seem to relish heated debate, and he jokingly acknowledges that he does not speak in "10-second sound bites."
Colleagues who admire his leadership in the Assembly say he has been somewhat slow to reconstruct the party's machinery or to set the party on a clear new course. He has also yet to recommend a new state party chairman or begin preparations for the 1996 Presidential and state legislative races, a task that begins with repaying the party's $750,000 debt.
"The more time that is allowed to lapse here, the greater the grumbling becomes and the greater the possibility for a disharmonious process to develop," said Judith Hope, a Suffolk County Democrat, referring to the process of picking a new state chairman.
Clearly, Mr. Silver has heard some of that grumbling. In recent weeks, he has met with scores of county party leaders, assuring them that the state party will be more grass-roots oriented, that they will play a greater role in running it. "There's a sense that the party has been too dominated at the top by the Governor," he said.
He also says he is considering having two people share the party chairmanship, one from upstate and one from downstate, to allay geographic tensions. Prominently mentioned to fill those posts are Ms. Hope and John Sullivan, a lawyer from the town of Oswego on Lake Ontario.
And as if in response to critics who say he has been slow to develop a plan of attack against the Pataki budget, Mr. Silver plans in the coming week to get a Democratic tax-cut bill passed in the Assembly that he says is better for people earning under $100,000 a year than Mr. Pataki's plan.
The Assembly will also soon release an economic forecast showing that the state has more money to spend than Mr. Pataki's budget allows for -- a key step toward staking out the Democratic position in budget negotiations. And before the fiscal year ends on March 31, Mr. Silver has pledged passage of a Democratic version of the budget that shifts spending cuts away from middle-class services.
Pointing to a recent statewide poll by The Buffalo News showing that less than half of the electorate rates Mr. Pataki's job performance as good or excellent, Mr. Silver suggested that the Democrats are not as weak as they might seem.
"His honeymoon period is starting out at a pretty low level," Mr. Silver asserted. "And there's no place to go but down."

HEADLINE: METRO MATTERS;
When Welfare Is Gone: A Cautionary Message

BYLINE: By Joyce Purnick 

 BODY:
EVER since Gov. George E. Pataki announced his plan to put a 90-day limit on Home Relief benefits for childless, "employable" adults, observers of the welfare system have tried to foresee the consequences. Alarmists predict a precipitous rise in homelessness; supporters foresee a cadre of newly productive workers. But most admit they don't know what to expect.
Now comes a glimpse into the future from Michigan, which eliminated its entire home relief program -- called general assistance -- in October 1991. That made Gov. John M. Engler a role model for Republicans everywhere; Mr. Pataki even hired Michigan's budget director, Patricia W. Woodworth. 
 But what's happened to the 82,000 people who lost Michigan's support? A study by the University of Michigan's School of Social Work, to be published today, provides a cautionary message: success depends upon very careful selection of those deemed capable of becoming self-sufficient.
The study, financed by the Ford Foundation, found that while predictions of Dickensian trauma were exaggerated, most of the former relief clients are in precarious shape. Though most are trying to work, only a small fraction can find or hold a regular job. Those considered "young and able-bodied" are doing better than others, but they are hardly thriving. And a surprisingly small number -- about a quarter -- are young and able-bodied. The great majority are elderly, disabled or ill.
"We found that most recipients do not work in the labor market, have health problems and totter on the edge of subsistence," the study concluded. It tracked a random sample of 426 former recipients with face-to-face interviews over two years.
THOUGH Governor Engler tried to dismiss the study during an appearance on ABC's "Nightline" last week, a spokeswoman for his Department of Social Services, Karen Smith, later said that his administration accepted the findings. "We don't think it is flawed," she said. But she and Mr. Engler take some comfort from the finding that a third of the former recipients no longer draw Federal food stamps, concluding that they no longer need them or were never entitled to them. It may also mean, however, that they left the state or cannot prove eligibility.
In 1993, two years after benefits were stopped, the study found the former recipients arrayed like this:
*20 percent were regularly self-supporting.
*30 percent relied on different forms of government subsidies -- mostly disability benefits.
*17 percent were working in odd jobs, like raking leaves or collecting cans.
*16 percent received help from friends or family.
*4 percent were getting unemployment insurance, workers compensation or private pensions.
*12 percent had no cash support of any kind.
Canceling the program, therefore, put a greater burden on the Federal Government, which has to pay for most of the disability benefits from Social Security funds. Cancellation also put a greater burden on Detroit. The homeless population of that city grew dramatically, and JoAnne Klocke, director of the city's Homeless Coordination Department, cites cancellation of home relief as one reason. The increase was so great that even as the state saved money on general assistance, it was forced to spend more for emergency shelters.
Probably the study's most troubling discovery was that most of the former relief recipients were weak candidates for full-time jobs. Only half had high school diplomas. About 70 percent reported chronic illnesses, and many were depressed. More than three-fourths of those over the age of 40 were in poor health.
"These are not lazy people hanging out on the dole," said Mark Elliott, a program officer at the Ford Foundation. "Many are doing some kind of work. But health, mental health, physical ability or changes in the economy have diminished their ability to support themselves. We are kidding ourselves if we think a majority of these people are going to become self-sufficient."
ABOUT a quarter of the former beneficiaries were deemed "young and able-bodied" because they were under age 40 and reported no health problems. That, presumably, is the same "employable" population that the Pataki plan would support for only 90 days a year. New York City's Mayor, Rudolph W. Giuliani, would go further and keep such people off the Home Relief rolls for two years.
In Michigan, a majority of these younger, healthier people had worked at casual jobs during 1993, but only a third were employed when interviewed. Another third said they searched for cans to raise cash, and many of the rest were begging or pawning goods.
The findings are not inconsistent with the experience of Mary E. Glass, New York's State Social Services Commissioner, who ran the Westchester County workfare program for Home Relief recipients. New York now assists 311,000 households with relief payments of $350 a month and Medicaid services. The Governor intends to give only 90 days' support to those judged to be employable and to refer the rest to health, drug or training programs. How those programs will be financed is not clear.
How many are truly employable? In Westchester, Ms. Glass estimated the number at just over a third of the Home Relief population. The rest were ill, aged, incapacitated or addicted to drugs or alcohol. The Michigan study did not examine the nature of the widespread illnesses it encountered, but experts say substance abuse is a common problem among relief recipients.
Dr. Sandra K. Danziger, co-author of the Michigan study, advises New York to be very careful about how it decides whom to throw off Home Relief. "People should work," said Dr. Danziger, of the Michigan School of Social Work. "But for people who are older, or ill, or for people who were trained in manufacturing and there's no job for them, it's a very vulnerable situation."

HEADLINE: Abroad at Home;
Visigoths at the Gate

BYLINE: By ANTHONY LEWIS 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
At this early date the new Republican leadership has pushed an extraordinary volume of legislation through the House of Representatives or its committees. In that mass of bills, can a theme -- a common purpose -- be identified?
States' rights might be one. Newt Gingrich and his colleagues have talked a good deal about the need to shift power and responsibility from the Federal Government to the states. 
 But that possibility is ruled out by the pending bill to limit law suits over personal injuries. Tort law, which governs such cases, has been a matter for the states throughout our history. The Republican bill, laying down rules for the whole country in major areas of tort law, would displace state legislatures and courts: the most breathtaking nationalization of the law in a very long time.
Market economics is another possibility. "Let the market decide instead of the Government" is a favorite conservative saying.
But again, the slogan conflicts with what House Republicans have done. In voting to end the nutrition program for pregnant women, infants and children (WIC), folding it into a block grant to the states, they rejected a proposal to require competitive bids when a state buys infant formula. Such competition -- reliance on the market -- saved the Federal Government $1 billion last year in the WIC program.
Evidently one must look not at what the Republican leaders say but at what they do. These are some of the measures that have passed the House or are on their way to the floor:
* Repeal of the National School Lunch Act, which has fed hundreds of millions of children free or at low cost over the last 50 years. It would be replaced by a block grant, with less funding, that would let the states provide meals if they wished. Thousands of schools are expected to drop out of the program if the change is made.
* Ending the WIC program. Just as the school lunch act has raised nutritional standards for children, WIC has reduced infant mortality and the number of low birth-weight babies, who are more likely to have developmental problems.
* Legislation making it harder for investors to sue over claims of securities fraud. This bill is a companion to the one that would discourage personal injury suits by such steps as limiting damages.
Not yet ready for action but strongly supported by Speaker Gingrich and hence likely to move ahead is legislation aimed at the Food and Drug Administration. It would make the F.D.A. ease up on its standards for new drugs and medical devices or, alternatively, move the testing and approval functions to a new, less rigorous agency.
Another planned attack is on payments for children with disabilities, under the Supplemental Security Income (S.S.I.) program. Some 900,000 poor children suffering from cerebral palsy, mental illness or other conditions now receive a maximum of $458 a month.
Looking at that list of actions taken and planned, one can hardly miss the theme. The purpose of one measure after another is to enrich those who have money and power in our society and reduce the modest help this country gives to the poor and the weak. Manufacturers and drug companies would gain. Sick children and poor mothers would lose. But the harm would not be limited to those particular classes. All of us would be affected.
Anyone, rich or poor, may be hurt by taking an untested drug. Or by eating meat containing bacteria that cause food poisoning.
When the House last week considered a Republican bill to forbid new Federal regulations for a year, an amendment was offered to let the Agriculture Department go ahead with stricter meat inspection rules drafted after some fatal food poisonings. Republicans rejected the amendment. But they agreed to loopholes for regulations that business wants.
There are faults in our legal system and in Federal regulations. But this will not be a better country for any of us if it is an America with more defrauded investors, more injured people without legal recourse and more ill-nourished children.

SECTION: Section A; Page 14; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 613 words

HEADLINE: Unbalanced Amendment

 BODY:
Tomorrow's vote in the Senate on the balanced-budget amendment is crucial for the Republican agenda to chop Government programs into bits. The outcome is also crucial to the nation because the pernicious amendment would do enormous fiscal damage. Proponents are alarmingly within three votes of winning.
The core of the amendment would require the Government to balance its books unless three-fifths of the House and Senate vote to run a deficit. To the wavering Democrats -- John Breaux of Louisiana, Sam Nunn of Georgia, Wendell Ford of Kentucky, and Kent Conrad and Byron Dorgan of North Dakota -- here are five unassailable reasons to vote no. 
 Unnecessary. Federal deficits have indeed been too high. That poses a threat that borrowing will siphon savings away from productive private investments.
But the fact that borrowing must be contained does not imply it ought to be eliminated -- any more than family borrowing, to buy a house or pay college tuition, need be eliminated. A prudent rule would keep Federal debt growing less quickly than incomes. This rule would justify deficits of about $200 billion a year, close to current levels.
Misleading. Proponents claim the amendment would protect future generations against ruinous interest payments. True, today's children will owe taxes when they grow up to pay interest on Federal debt. But proponents ignore the fact that the tax payments will flow right back to these children as owners of Government bonds.
Unenforceable. Because key terms of the amendment -- like outlays and receipts -- are undefined, Congress will be able to manipulate and evade. Can Congress create independent agencies or find other ways to spend and borrow off the Government books? A Senate committee has already written into the legislative record, used to guide future court decisions, that the Tennessee Valley Authority would be exempt from the amendment. It should take lawyers five minutes to stretch whatever "principle" guides that exception to scores of other Government programs.
The amendment also fails to provide an enforcement mechanism. It might simply become an empty gesture or, worse yet, the courts might step in to tell Congress how much it should tax and where it should spend.
Irrational. Federal bookkeeping lumps ordinary spending with long-term public investments. Congress, forced by the amendment to cut quickly, would go after hugely expensive, though vitally important, investments, such as scientific research, costly laboratories and equipment, job training or other investments that would not produce benefits for years, if not decades.
Reckless. When the economy slows, tax revenues fall off and spending on unemployment insurance and food stamps rises. This automatic rise in the deficit, by triggering spending, serves to mitigate the slowdown. But under the proposed amendment, Congress could easily turn a mild downturn into something worse. Unless a three-fifths supermajority saves the day, Congress would have to raise taxes and cut spending in a slow economy -- the opposite of responsible stewardship.
Take another unintended consequence. When savings and loans went bankrupt during the 1980's, the Federal Government bailed out depositors with borrowed money, thereby preventing a banking panic. But under the proposed amendment, the Government could not react instantly unless a supermajority in Congress approved.
The balanced-budget amendment appeals to taxpayers who demand that the Government spend their money wisely. But Senators Nunn, Ford, Conrad, Dorgan and Breaux need to recognize that this honorable sentiment cannot be wisely embedded into the Constitution.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 4; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1303 words

HEADLINE: White House Is Joining in Efforts To Loosen the Limits on Banking

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 26

 BODY:
The Clinton Administration plans to call this week for legislation that would allow commercial banks, securities firms and insurance companies to merge, forming giant financial services companies that would offer everything from checking accounts to mutual funds and life insurance, Federal officials say.
In a speech prepared for delivery in New York on Monday and in Congressional testimony scheduled for Wednesday, Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin will urge Congress to repeal the Depression-era Glass-Steagall Act, the officials said. For more than 60 years, the law has forced financial concerns to choose between owning commercial banks or owning securities companies like brokerage firms and investment banks, but not both. 
 Mr. Rubin also plans to call for broad changes in the Bank Holding Company Act of 1956, which has effectively barred most financial concerns from owning both commercial banks and insurance companies, said the Federal officials, who spoke on condition of anonymity. Mr. Rubin's speech will represent the first time that the Administration has taken a position on eliminating the legal and regulatory barriers among financial industries.
Regulatory changes in recent years have already allowed commercial banks, like Citibank, to begin selling stocks and mutual funds on a limited basis. But the Glass-Steagall Act still bars Citibank, for example, from merging with a brokerage firm like Merrill Lynch or an investment bank like Goldman, Sachs, which provides corporate investment advice and helps companies issue stock. The Bank Holding Company Act bars Citibank fom merging with a big insurance company like Prudential.
The Administration's plan would allow such deals, provided they complied with antitrust laws. The plan would pave the way for new consolidation in the financial system.
Because many House and Senate Republicans already want to allow mergers of commercial banks and investment banks, members of Congress said the odds were high that some sort of legislation to do so would pass in this session of Congress. "I think that's very likely," said Representative Jim Leach, the Iowa Republican who is the chairman of the House Banking Committee and has already introduced a bill to eliminate Glass-Steagall.
It remains less clear whether the Administration will succeed in allowing commercial banks to merge with insurance companies. Mr. Leach has strongly resisted this idea in the past, but said in an interview on Friday night that he was willing to let his committee decide the issue.
By contrast, some Republicans want to go much further than the Administration, allowing any business, including insurance companies and corporations outside the financial industry, to merge with banks.
Supporters of greater consolidation say competition will drive down fees for everything from checking accounts to stock trading. Opponents say the giant companies that may result are unlikely to be sensitive to the needs of individuals and small businesses.
All the plans under consideration would require that federally insured deposits be used only in commercial banking, and not in securities trading or other industries. But some critics have questioned whether such rules can be enforced and warned that allowing the combination of commercial banking with other industries might result in costly Federal bailouts.
The risks of securities trading were highlighted today when Barings P.L.C., a British bank older than the United States, disclosed that a single trader had lost all the bank's capital while betting on the direction of Japanese stock prices.
Important differences remain among the Administration's plan and competing Republican proposals. The details will pit lobbyists for small banks and insurance agents against big banks, securities firms and insurance companies.
The Federal Reserve, which blocked an Administration plan last year for the consolidation of bank regulators, supports the combination of commercial and investment banks, but wants more regulatory authority over these combinations than the Administration or many Republicans want to give it.
Mr. Leach is under pressure from younger Republican members of Congress who want much broader deregulation. Representative Richard H. Baker, a Louisiana Republican who heads the House Banking Committee's panel on capital markets and securities, has already introduced a bill that would allow mergers among commercial banks, investment banks, insurance companies and even nonfinancial companies like General Motors or AT&T.
Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato, the New York Republican who heads the Senate Banking Committee, has introduced legislation in the Senate identical to Mr. Baker's.
The Clinton Administration proposal developed by Mr. Rubin amounts to a compromise between the two Republican positions. The Administration would allow banks to merge with insurance companies but not with nonfinancial companies.
Repeal of Glass-Steagall had been sought by banks for years, but blocked by Representative John D. Dingell, Democrat of Michigan, the longtime chairman of the House Energy and Commerce Committee. But when the Republicans took control of Congress, they not only removed him from the chairmanship but also gave the issue to the House Banking Committee, where support for repeal has long been strong.
Mr. Rubin is a former co-chairman of Goldman, Sachs, which opposed the repeal of Glass-Steagall until recently. But while Goldman has not taken a clear position this year, other securities firms mostly favor the position of Mr. D'Amato and Mr. Baker.
During the Depression, Congress banned commercial banks from investment banking because of a widespread perception that stock market speculation had led to the collapse of many banks. But more recent economic research has found that few if any banks failed then because of losses in the market crash. Most economists now believe that the banks failed because they lacked adequate reserves and because many people failed to repay loans.
Small banks and insurance agents have long opposed the repeal of Glass-Steagall. The small banks, which have considerable power on Capitol Hill because of their sheer numbers and the political alliances of their executives, are worried that they will not be able to compete with the giant new financial services companies. Insurance agents are frightened that banks will drive them out of business by using their branch networks to sell insurance.
Kenneth A. Guenther, the executive vice president of the Independent Bankers Association of America, which represents small banks, said, "The question now is whether you allow the common ownership of G.M., Citicorp and Merrill Lynch -- it would open the door to a level of financial concentration that this country has never seen."
But Mr. Guenther acknowledged that with the Clinton Administration lining up with Congressional Republicans, the barriers between commercial and investment banking would probably be eliminated.
The clashing positions of Mr. Leach and Mr. D'Amato partly reflect the states they represent. Iowa has hundreds of small rural banks, while New York has dozens of securities firms eager to expand.
Some big securities firms are partly or entirely owned by big insurance companies and would benefit from Mr. D'Amato's bill and the Administration's plan. But Mr. Leach's plan would only allow the new financial giants to derive up to one-tenth of their sales from insurance, a limit that is too low to accommodate insurance companies.
Mr. Rubin plans to provide an overview of the Administration's intentions to the Greater New York Savings Bond Committee on Monday, said Administration officials who spoke on condition of anonymity. He will address the House Banking Committee on Wednesday.

HEADLINE: PULSE;
Service Business

 BODY:
Revenue from service business in the United States rose to $1.6 trillion in 1992, up 59 percent from 1987, according to a report recently released by the Commerce Department. The service business industry includes the non-manufacturing establishments of the economy. The largest area of growth occurred in the prepackaged computer software business, a segement of business services, with revenues up 253 percent. Next was another computer-related business, information retrieval services, which include on-line services, and increased by 229 percent. In New York, New Jersey and Connecticut, service business revenue was up 49 percent. Health services led the way at an average revenue increase of 71 percent for the region. The home health care boom is largely responsible for the overall explosion in health services.

HEADLINE: Congressional Roundup;
Budget Amendment Slipping Among Senate Swing Votes

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 27

 BODY:
Prospects that the Senate would adopt a constitutional amendment requiring a balanced Federal budget, a measure most recently predicted to be a vote or two short of a sure thing, seemed to take a turn for the worse today, just hours before a scheduled vote on the proposal on Tuesday.
In rapid succession, three undecided Democrats -- Senators Sam Nunn of Georgia and Byron L. Dorgan and Kent Conrad, both of North Dakota -- indicated that they would oppose the amendment unless the Republicans agreed to major changes that they had so far resisted. 
 Despite Republican pleas, Mr. Nunn expressed "grave doubts" that he could support the amendment unless it barred the courts from intervening in its application. Mr. Nunn argued that without such a prohibition, the Federal courts could assume some of Congress's constitutional control of taxes and spending.
Later, after a 90-minute meeting with Republican leaders, Mr. Dorgan and Mr. Conrad said separately that they not only shared Mr. Nunn's concern but that they believed that the amendment did not properly protect the Social Security trust fund. Both men strongly indicated that they would oppose the amendment unless it was changed.
A fourth Democrat who was in the meeting, Senator Wendell H. Ford of Kentucky, also appeared to harbor deep reservations. The fifth undecided Democrat, Senator John B. Breaux of Louisiana, was with Mr. Nunn in Atlanta today and could not be reached for comment.
Taken together, the day's developments suggested that the 52 Republicans and 12 Democrats who were publicly aligned in support of the amendment would fall short of the 67 votes they would need to push it through the Senate and send it to the states for ratification.
But all that can change in a wink. Republicans who control the amendment debate may accede to the Democrats' conditions in order to pass some sort of amendment. Some Democrats may be holding out to win concessions, but in the end will vote for the amendment, recognizing that public sentiment is behind it.
At a rally of amendment supporters today, two of the Republicans, Senators Orrin G. Hatch of Utah and Larry E. Craig of Idaho, expressed scant interest in the sort of concessions that the Democrats were asking.
"They can vote the way they want to," Mr. Hatch said of Mr. Nunn and like-minded Democrats. "But they have to realize this is the most important constitutional vote of this century."
Messrs. Nunn, Ford, Breaux and Dorgan all supported the last version of the balanced-budget amendment, which narrowly failed in the Senate last spring. And all but Mr. Ford were once viewed as likely supporters of the current version.
But the Senate's political microclimate began shifting on Thursday, when, for the first time, Mr. Nunn voiced serious concerns about the amendment. Noting that its language lacked a prohibition on judicial rulings that had been part of the 1994 version, Mr. Nunn said the current measure could enable appointed Federal judges, in effect, to write their own fiscal policy, should Congress ever fail to meet the amendment's demands and be taken to court.
Republicans tried to satisfy Mr. Nunn today by proposing companion legislation that would largely limit judicial review of the budget amendment to declaratory judgments, which have no legal authority.
Mr. Hatch allowed that this would leave the nation with no legal means of enforcing the amendment's requirement that Congress enact a balanced budget, but he said that public pressure would ensure that it is obeyed.
"That's the only way it's enforceable, at the ballot box," he said. "It would be enforceable politically."
That is a mirror image of the principal argument of the amendment's supporters: that without a dictate in the Constitution itself, Congress and the public lack the political will to control the nation's spending habits.
Indeed, Senator Bob Packwood, the Oregon Republican who is chairman of the Finance Committee, said today in an address to the Group Health Association of America that to balance the budget by the year 2002, two of the nation's largest entitlement programs, Medicare and Medicaid, would have to be restructured and their rate of growth reduced by about $400 billion over the next seven years.
Medicare and Medicaid account for about $300 billion a year in Federal spending, and each is growing at a rate of about 10 percent a year.
Mr. Nunn, who was in Atlanta today for a Democratic Party function, said he would look at the Republican proposal for companion legislation, but asserted that no such statute, however well crafted, could dilute the power of a clause in the Constitution.
"There's grave doubts about whether this will work," he said. "This country started on the basis of not having taxation without representation. I don't think we want a back door now because somebody's in a hurry to pass something unwinding the Boston Tea Party."
Later today, Mr. Dorgan and Mr. Conrad cited much the same reasons in expressing varying degrees of unhappiness with the amendment.
Mr. Dorgan, who had long been seen as a likely supporter of the amendment, tempered his support in a Senate speech this evening. He indicated that he could not support the measure unless its supporters agreed to limit judicial review of its provisions. But unlike Mr. Nunn, he hinted that he would consider the companion-law approach.
Mr. Dorgan also said he wanted assurances that taxpayer contributions to the Social Security trust fund would not be tapped to reduce the budget deficit. His fear, echoed by many fellow Democrats, was that Congress would simply tap the hundreds of billions of surplus dollars now building up in Federal retirement accounts and use that money to pay Federal bills, unless that was barred.
Democrats argue that dipping into Social Security funds will leave the accounts empty, except for Federal i.o.u.'s, when baby-boom workers begin to retire in the next century.
This evening, Mr. Dorgan said he preferred to change the amendment to eliminate Social Security from his calculations, but was open to separate legislation resolving his concerns if it could be approved immediately. Otherwise, he said, "I would not be comfortable supporting a constitutional amendment knowing that, riding on that vote, was three-quarters of a trillion dollars in Social Security revenues."
Mr. Conrad took a tougher stance. After meeting with Mr. Hatch, Mr. Craig and Senator Paul Simon of Illinois, a Democrat who is among the amendment's prime supporters, Mr. Conrad said in an interview that the amendment was a "nonstarter" unless it was changed to restrict the courts' power to review it.
He also said he favored changes to prevent what he called "the looting of the Social Security trust fund" and the addition of a provision that would make it easier to run a deficit during an economic emergency, when Government spending is often the only way to pump up the economic pace.
Mr. Conrad disagreed that his demands amounted to opposing the amendment. "Those who have the destiny of this amendment in their hands need to listen and see where the votes are," he said.
But he added that while Mr. Simon seemed open to changing the amendment during their meeting, Mr. Hatch's response was "completely different."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Collapse of Barings
 How could one rogue trader bring down Barings P.L.C.? An audacious strategy by the trader, Nicholas W. Leeson, 28, led to more than $1 billion in losses for Barings, a profitable and well-regarded British investment house. [Page A1.] 
 Rogue traders are not the only ones who have destroyed investors' wealth in recent months. Money managers who play by the rules can get caught short, too, when they fall to overconfidence about their mastery of the markets. News Analysis. [D1.]
 The Bank of England made a tough call letting Barings fail. [D8.]
 The crisis hurt two American investment pools managed by Barings. The shares sank on the New York Stock Exchange. [D8.]
 
Companies
 Apple Computer settled a suit on repetitive stress injuries, after a blunder by its lawyers. I.B.M. is left as the sole defendant. [D4.]
 Chrysler will idle for a second week an assembly plant in Ontario that produces its midsize and full-size sedans. [D4.]
 Conseco plans to spend almost $728 million to buy the remaining shares of two life insurance companies it controls. [D4.]
 Royal Dutch/Shell and Exxon will proceed with a North Sea oil project after receiving approval from Britain. [D4.]
 Rupert Murdoch said Fox had fully informed the F.C.C. of all details of its ownership structure in 1985. [D22.]
 Salomon said its losses were greater for the quarter and the year than previously reported because of a bookkeeping error. [D24.]
 
The Economy
 The Treasury Secretary called for the repeal of Glass-Steagall, an act that has banned mergers of commercial banks and securities firms since the Great Depression. The proposal by the Secretary, Robert E. Rubin, touched off a political fight. [D1.]
 The I.R.S. will delay refunds for as many as seven million American taxpayers to allow time to check Social Security numbers and verify claims for the earned-income tax credit. [D1.]
 Orders for machine tools fell a sharp 31 percent in January. [D6.]
 The House is considering a bill to make it difficult for agencies to impose new rules on health, safety and the environment. [A18.]
 
International
 Mexican stocks tumbled as investors continued to flee the market, fearing the impact of Government austerity measures on business. Investors were also troubled by the announcement by Banamex, the large banking company, that it would dismiss as many as 35 percent of its 33,000 employees. [D2.]
 
Markets
 The Dow industrial retreated below the 4,000-point level, weighed down by concerns over the collapse of Barings P.L.C. [D1.]
 The dollar advanced, rebounding from early losses against the mark, which hit new highs against the pound and lira. [D19.]
 Silver prices fell sharply after USA Today said that it would test a silver-less film. Coffee prices jumped to a 16-week high. [D19.]
 Treasury prices gained, helped by a flight to quality. [D20.]
 
Today's Columns
 Bond mutual funds are making a modest comeback, after investors bailed out in droves last year. While a lot less money was flowing out of the funds this month, sales remained tepid. Some inflows seem to be in good part attributable to a strong performance by junk bond funds. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D1.]
 A young New York shop has found another voice of experience by hiring Len Sirowitz, co-chairman of Rosenfeld, Sirowitz, Humphrey & Strauss. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D23.]

PUB-DATE: February 28, 1995

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 6; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1670 words

HEADLINE: POLL FINDS PUBLIC DOUBTS KEY PARTS OF G.O.P.'S AGENDA

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE 

 BODY:
Eight weeks after Republicans assumed control of Congress and vowed to make the Government more responsive, Americans are dubious about central elements of the party's legislative agenda on issues that include welfare, crime, military spending and the Federal budget deficit, according to the latest New York Times/CBS News Poll.
Republicans are generally more supportive than Democrats and independents of what the new leadership in Congress is trying to do. But Republicans, too, express misgivings about the course taken by Speaker Newt Gingrich and his loyalists. Most Americans believe that Congress should stress jobs and crime -- issues that have not been top priorities for Republicans this year -- and health care, which has not been on the agenda of the new Congress at all. 
 While the Republican leadership has addressed the issue of crime, people took exception to major parts of the Republicans' crime policy. A substantial majority favored the ban on assault weapons that the Republicans have vowed to overturn, and most objected to a bill that has passed the House and would give towns and cities more discretion in spending money that was targeted by President Clinton specifically for more police officers.
One of the most dramatic examples of the gap between public opinion as measured by the poll and the priorities of Congressional Republicans, which are based on their reading of public sentiment, involves the proposed balanced-budget amendment to the Constitution. The amendment, already approved by the House, is scheduled for what appears to be a close vote in the Senate today.
Seventy-nine percent of Americans said they favored such an amendment. But that support shrinks to only 32 percent when Americans are asked about balancing the budget if it would require cuts in Social Security, as many analysts say. The Republicans have defeated efforts to remove Social Security from the budget-balancing equation.
The poll also found that 45 percent of Americans approved of President Clinton's overall job performance, up from 38 percent in December. One reason may be that he is getting more credit for his handling of the economy; 47 percent gave him high marks on the economy, as opposed to 41 percent last month.
Even more striking was a sharp improvement in the popularity of Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the majority leader, amid publicity over his run for the 1996 Republican Presidential nomination. With 63 percent of Americans having an opinion of Mr. Dole, 40 percent held favorable views -- double his favorable rating last month -- and 23 percent unfavorable.
Mr. Gingrich, by contrast, was not nearly as popular. Of the 55 percent who express an opinion about the Speaker, 22 percent held a favorable opinion and 33 percent unfavorable.
The nationwide telephone poll of 1,190 adults, which was taken last Wednesday through Saturday, has a margin of sampling error of plus or minus three percentage points.
More than half of Americans said they knew nothing about the Contract With America, the House Republicans' political manifesto. But the number of people who had some familiarity with the contract has risen to 45 percent, from 27 percent in December.
Even so, Americans -- Democrats and Republicans alike -- disagreed with or were divided over many particulars in the contract.
For example, while lawmakers from both parties would require that welfare recipients be ineligible after five years or less, 66 percent of Americans said people should be allowed to receive benefits as long as they are willing to work for them. And 62 percent said unmarried mothers under the age of 18 who have no way of supporting their children should be eligible for welfare. That is at odds with a bill sponsored by Republicans in the House that would prohibit using Federal money for children born out of wedlock to women under 18.
Cindy Butler, a Republican mother of three young children from Hilton Head, S.C., who was one of the respondents in the poll, said in a follow-up interview that she feared that her party's calls for ending welfare would drive unmarried mothers to have abortions. "I'm firmly against abortion, and some of these girls are going to look to abortion to solve the problem," she said. "Children come first in my book."
Here are other areas of divergence:
*Despite the reservations about dipping into Social Security, Americans were far more comfortable with balancing the budget than cutting taxes. Given an either-or choice, 55 percent favored balancing the budget and 40 percent preferred cutting taxes. Only 32 percent said they would choose a tax cut if it amounted to $500 per child, as House Republicans have proposed. But in a shift toward optimism, 57 percent thought the budget could be balanced without any new taxes on people like them; only 43 percent held such a view two years ago.
*Despite the contract's call to increase military spending, 63 percent preferred to keep such spending at the current level.
*Sixty-nine percent of Americans said it was a poor idea for the House to approve, as it did recently, a bill that would allow the police to make searches without having a warrant. Proponents of the bill argued that it would reduce the likelihood that criminals would get off on technicalities; opponents said it was a violation of people's constitutional protection from illegal searches.
*Americans expressed displeasure with the recent House vote to change a law enacted last year that gave money to towns and cities to help pay for 100,000 more police officers on the streets. The House bill would allow jurisdictions to use the money for any crime-related expense they want. Fifty-three percent said it would be better to require that the money be used for officers on the streets; 43 percent favored giving towns and cities discretion.
One of the respondents, Gary Gasque of Marion, S.C., said in a follow-up interview that he believed the House should not have voted to change the requirement for more police officers. "That was wrong," said Mr. Gasque, a Republican. "Now the money won't hit the street like it should. This is a small town, and I just bought my kid his third bike -- they keep getting stolen. People are shooting at each other four blocks from the police station here."
There seems to be general support for recent moves by Republicans and some Democrats to reconsider giving preferences to minorities. Fifty-five percent said no preference in hiring and promotion should be given to blacks today, even when there has been job discrimination against blacks in the past. And 48 percent said no preferences should be given to women, even in cases when there has been job discrimination against them in the past.
Despite the concerns about the direction of the new Congress, the public has high expectations. Fifty-four percent said the 104th Congress would accomplish more than Congress usually does in a typical session. Public approval of how Congress is handling its job is higher than last fall, but it is still only 36 percent, with 51 percent disapproval.
While Mr. Clinton's approval rating has improved slightly, 59 percent said they had no clear idea of what the President stands for. But he still gets strong marks for compassion, with 60 percent saying he cares about the needs and problems of people like them.
At this early juncture in next year's race for the Republican Presidential nomination, Senator Dole enjoys a wide advantage: 49 percent of Republicans preferred him when asked to pick from a hypothetical grouping of eight candidates.
Most Americans do not yet have an opinion of Mr. Dole's leading rivals, Senator Phil Gramm of Texas, who formally entered the race last week, and former Gov. Lamar Alexander of Tennessee, who plans to announce his candidacy today. But among those who do, sentiment is mixed.
On Mr. Gramm, 14 percent said they viewed him favorably, while 13 percent said not favorably. Twenty percent were undecided, and a majority -- 52 percent -- said they had not heard enough about him to form an opinion.
On Mr. Alexander, 4 percent expressed a favorable opinion, 3 percent an unfavorable view, 8 percent were undecided and 84 percent said they had not heard enough about him yet to form an opinion.
As for Mr. Gingrich, 51 percent of the public said he has strong qualities of leadership. But only 35 percent said he cares about the needs and problems of people like them.
 
How the Poll Was Conducted
 The latest New York Times/CBS News Poll is based on telephone interviews conducted from last Wednesday through Saturday with 1,190 adults around the United States, excluding Alaska and Hawaii.
The sample of telephone exchanges called was selected by a computer from a complete list of exchanges in the country. The exchanges were chosen to assure that each region of the country was represented in proportion to its population. For each exchange, the telephone numbers were formed by random digits, thus permitting access to both listed and unlisted numbers. Within each household, one adult was designated by a random procedure to be the respondent for the survey.
The results have been weighted to take account of household size and number of telephone lines into the residence and to adjust for variations in the sample relating to region, race, sex, age and education.
In theory, in 19 cases out of 20 the results based on such samples will differ by no more than three percentage points in either direction from what would have been obtained by seeking out all American adults.
The potential sampling error for smaller subgroups is larger. For example, it is plus or minus five percentage points for Republicans, Democrats or independents taken as a group.
In addition to sampling error, the practical difficulties of conducting any survey of public opinion may introduce other sources of error into the poll. Variations in question wording or the order of questions, for example, can lead to somewhat different results.
