HEADLINE: THE COLLAPSE OF BARINGS: THE OVERVIEW;
Young Trader's $29 Billion Bet Brings Down a Venerable Firm

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LONDON, Feb. 27

 BODY:
At the epicenter of Sunday night's spectacular collapse of a leading British investment bank stands a 28-year-old trader named Nicholas W. Leeson, a Briton who in the course of a month put $29 billion of the firm's money on the line and lost more than a billion of it.
Working in Singapore for Barings P.L.C., an investment house that had survived nearly two and a half centuries of ups and downs, Mr. Leeson eluded detection by his own firm and by outside regulators as he made an ever-mounting and ill-fated gamble on Japanese stock prices and interest rates. 
 As details of Mr. Leeson's audacious strategy became clearer today and as Barings and regulators from several countries sought to find him after his disappearance last week, the world's financial markets shuddered but ultimately calmed down.
Share prices in Tokyo fell nearly 4 percent, but stock markets in most other financial centers were not hit as hard. Shares fell a little more than 1 percent in Hong Kong. In Europe, the British pound was battered by selling, falling at one point to a record low against the German mark before recovering somewhat.
But the European stock markets were relatively calm, with London finishing down less than half a percent. In the United States, the Dow Jones industrial average fell 23.17 points, to 3,988.57, with investors using concern over Barings as an excuse to take profits, analysts said. [Page D1.]
[Tokyo stocks had recovered somewhat by midday Tuesday, posting a gain of 1.8 percent.]
In London, the British Government ordered an urgent inquiry. In an interview with The Financial Times, Peter Baring, the firm's chairman, speculated that Barings could have been brought down by a deliberate act of criminal sabotage. But he said the bank did not yet know what had happened.
"It's difficult to get a fix on the motivation of this fellow," Mr. Baring said, referring to Mr. Leeson.
Mr. Leeson does not face any criminal charges from the case so far, and it is unclear what laws, if any, he might have violated.
As bankruptcy administrators worked feverishly to salvage all or part of Barings, one question kept popping up throughout the financial world: How could a single rogue trader bring down an otherwise profitable and well-regarded institution, unnerving financial markets around the world in the process?
The loss, discovered late last week, was enough to sink Barings, despite intensive efforts by the Bank of England to arrange a rescue package over the weekend.
Mr. Leeson, who had succeeded in the arcane business of derivatives despite having failed his high-school-level math exams, disappeared from his post in Singapore last Thursday, just before the firm discovered the huge loss. He was last reported to be in Kuala Lumpur.
The governor of the Bank of England, Eddie George, who cut short a ski vacation in France on Friday night to oversee the unfolding crisis, said Mr. Leeson had amassed two huge investment positions.
One -- a bet that Japanese stock prices would rise -- involved the purchase of futures contracts representing $7 billion worth of shares. The other was a bet that interest rates would rise, and it took the form of contracts representing $22 billion worth of Japanese government bonds and currency investments.
Stocks in particular went badly wrong for Mr. Leeson as Japanese shares fell sharply last week. But the rate-sensitive investment lost money, too, Mr. George said.
Perhaps most important, nobody at Barings or at the Singapore International Monetary Exchange, where most of the trades were carried out, or at the Bank of England, Barings' primary regulator, ever questioned Mr. Leeson as he built the positions during a period of almost a month.
"There was clearly a failure of control," Mr. George said at a news conference today. "How could one trader take these huge positions and how could he succeed in concealing them as he did? They just didn't know this chap had taken on this huge position."
There have been disputes within Barings in the last several days about whether the Singapore operation was adequately supervised. Some officials questioned whether the Barings banking subsidiary, which oversees the derivatives business, had enough expertise to monitor the situation properly.
Mr. George said he did not have enough information yet to point the finger at Barings or the Singapore authorities, who would have primary responsibility for policing the trades. He said that it might be impossible to assure completely that determined individuals, if they were skillful enough, could not mislead internal and external regulators in any kind of investment field.
Mr. George said it was also possible, perhaps even likely, that Mr. Leeson had help from someone else at Barings in covering his tracks, since it would be difficult for any trader to conceal investments of the scale Mr. Leeson made. But Mr. George said he had no specific evidence that anyone else was involved.
Up until last week, Mr. Leeson had no reputation within Barings as a risk taker. Coming from a lower-middle-class family north of London, he had become quite successful since joining Barings three years ago, making big but ostensibly safe investments that sought to profit on the tiny differences between what identical financial instruments sold for in Japan and Singapore. When his computer detected such a price difference, he would buy heavily in the lower-priced market and sell instantaneously in the other, generating small but steady earnings for the firm and substantial bonuses for himself.
"He was an extremely able trader," Mr. George said.
But around Jan. 26, officials said today, Mr. Leeson inexplicably, and without authorization, changed tactics. Instead of matching buy and sell orders, he in effect became purely a buyer. His purchases accelerated over the next several weeks, notably so last week. Throughout the period Japanese stock prices were falling, creating losses that Mr. Leeson apparently tried to cover by increasing the size of his bet, Bank of England officials said.
Officials said they did not know why Mr. Leeson began making the unauthorized transactions, but they speculated that he might have been trying to cover a loss in another account.
Mr. Leeson's dealings, which teams of Barings executives, bankruptcy administrators and Bank of England officials are still trying to unravel, were primarily purchases of futures contracts on the Singapore exchange. They were considerably less exotic than the complex derivatives cooked up by some banks and investment firms.
Because the purchases required only a small down payment -- typically 10 percent or less, traders said -- Mr. Leeson was able to amass a position large enough to excite comment throughout Asian financial markets about his aggressive strategy, analysts said, even as his trading was going unnoticed by regulators. Mr. George said regulators had not yet determined how much Mr. Leeson had put down as a margin payment on his position.
There are also reports that Mr. Leeson used another complex strategy called a straddle, which amounted to a bet that the Japanese stock market index would stay essentially level. Straddles are a particularly volatile investment and can cause huge losses if the market moves significantly up or down.
It is unclear what alerted Barings to the problem in Singapore. But, as his positions deteriorated, Mr. Leeson would have faced calls for increased margin payments.
On Friday, when regulators first tried to assess the extent of the damage, they calculated that of the $650 million in losses they could identify at that point, $500 million was attributable to the bet on stock prices, with the remainder from the interest-rate bet.
If, as expected, Barings defaults on the contracts as they mature in coming weeks, the Singapore exchange and its clearing corporation would be liable to pay the counterparties -- the investors who made the opposite bet and stand to gain what Barings has lost. The Singapore exchange would then become a creditor of Barings, to be repaid based on how much Barings and its various parts bring when they are sold. Officials said the counterparties are widely dispersed.
As of Sunday night, all of the contracts remained open and vulnerable to further losses from drops in Tokyo share prices or declines in interest rates. Executives from Ernst & Young, the accounting firm appointed by a British judge late Sunday to oversee Barings, declined to say whether they were trying to sell or otherwise close out any of the positions. The judge acted Sunday after Barings was placed in administration, the rough equivalent of Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection in the United States.
Nigel Hamilton, who is heading the Ernst & Young team, said the loss stood at $900 million before the 3.8 percent fall in Japanese markets today, suggesting that the loss is now well in excess of $1 billion.
Mr. Hamilton said there had been considerable interest from banks and investment houses in Britain, the United States and elsewhere in acquiring all or part of Barings, which had been scheduled this week to announce record profits for 1994 of more than $160 million.
He declined to name any of the potential bidders. But among those that expressed some interest over the weekend were Morgan Stanley of the United States, which tried but failed to reach a merger deal last year with S. G. Warburg, another British investment firm; Deutsche Bank, Germany's largest bank, and most of the big British banking companies, including HSBC Holdings, Barclays Bank and Abbey National.
"We believe there is a high-class core business there," Mr. Hamilton said. "A number of people were quick off the mark to talk to us, and they are serious people."
But it is unclear whether Ernst & Young will be able to make any significant sales until it finds a way to cap the losses on the Singapore investments. The status of Barings bondholders and some depositors was unclear today, although Mr. Hamilton said nearly all of the firm's operations continued relatively normally today and that around $46 billion of funds entrusted to Barings' asset management subsidiary would not be affected.
Ernst & Young is trying to keep crucial personnel at Barings from quitting to take other jobs, a development that would make it harder to sell the privately held firm. Mr. Hamilton said employees had been notified recently of their 1994 bonuses but had not yet received them. Some consideration would be given to paying them, he said.

SECTION: Section D; Page 8; Column 1; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 744 words

HEADLINE: THE COLLAPSE OF BARINGS: THE DECISION;
Acceptable Failure Not Seen as a Threat to Financial Systems

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS 

 BODY:
In closing down Barings, Britain's oldest investment banking group, the Bank of England made a difficult judgment call, analysts and economists say, deciding in effect that its failure would not threaten the health of either the British or the international financial systems nor cause heavy losses to small depositors.
This is the exact reverse of the decision taken by the United States Treasury and the International Monetary Fund in December when they organized an international rescue package for Mexico after agreeing that the peso's collapse threatened to set off a huge withdrawal of capital from emerging market economies that could slow the world's economy. 
 But with no small depositors threatened with bankruptcy, apparently no danger that the Barings failure would bring down other banks and with the losses on its open Japanese stock positions almost incalculable, Britain's central bank allowed the House of Barings to go to the wall after more than two centuries in business, rather than risk public money shoring it up.
"Barings was not too big to fail," said Lisa S. Kaess of the New York investment advisory firm of Geoffrey Bell & Company.
"The Bank of England decided there is no risk of contagion, no systemic risk to the banking industry" said Robert Solomon, a former Federal Reserve economist now at the Brookings Institution in Washington.
The Bank of England's decision contrasts sharply to some of its own earlier actions, as well as to Washington's efforts in behalf of Mexico. In 1974, the Bank of England secretly began the biggest banking rescue operation in British history when it organized a "lifeboat" to save London and County Securities and other secondary banks facing collapse as a result of ill-judged property lending.
The central bank, fearing a spreading collapse among secondary banks as panicky clients switched deposits to safer institutions, arranged for Britain's biggest banks to recycle depositors' money back into the secondary banks while they were reorganized.
The big banks had a clear interest in cooperating since a collapse of the small secondary banks would have left the big banks with large bad debts on their books.
More controversial was the Bank of England's rescue of Johnson Matthey bankers in 1984 from a crisis caused by imprudent lending, which critics saw as favoritism toward this famous bullion trading house and at least partially intended to shore up the prestige of the City, London's financial district.
On the other hand, no central bank defended the Bank of Commerce and Credit International before it was shut down four years ago. And more recently there was no rescue of the big German trading company, Metallgesellschaft, which like Barings had suffered large losses in the derivatives market.
And although B.C.C.I. was not a British bank, the Bank of England was criticized by Britain's Asian community because at the time of B.C.C.I's failure a number of small British Asian banks were also forced to close.
The governor of the Bank of England, Eddie George, cut short a ski holiday to confront the crisis. Increasing the bank's reluctance to bail out Barings with public money, many analysts said, is the legacy of Britain's Conservative Government under Margaret Thatcher and its emphasis on free markets and self-reliance.
Another factor is that the Bank of England, like most other European central banks and finance ministries, did not fully share American concerns over Mexico's financial crisis and felt they had been railroaded into coming to the country's rescue.
"It would have been illogical to go for a nonmarket solution for Barings when it had favored a market solution for Mexico," a banker said.
Nevertheless, in the view of some analysts, the Barings failure will force the markets to look carefully at smaller British investment banks, notably S. G. Warburg, which is still looking for a bigger foreign partner after the breakdown of merger talks with Morgan Stanley, Kleinwort Benson and Lazard Freres.
Another consequence foreseen by some analysts is that the Barings failure, by further depressing the Japanese stock market, where the Nikkei index has already fallen 15 percent this year, may deter American investors overseas.
Last week the Securities Industry Association said American purchases of foreign securities in the third quarter of 1994 were down 89 percent from a year earlier, even before the Mexican crisis broke.

HEADLINE: 13,000 Workers On Furlough

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 27

 BODY:
Telephones went unanswered in dozens of municipal offices here today, the first of what could be as many as 10 furlough days for employees of the District of Columbia.
The office closures, affecting 13,000 of the District's 41,000 employees, were part of a plan by Mayor Marion S. Barry Jr. to save $70 million by Sept. 30. The plan, which is intended to deal with the capital's financial crisis, calls for days without pay, salary cuts, tax increases and other measures. Today's furloughs were predicted to save $3 million. 
 The District faces a projected budget deficit of $722 million, and Mr. Barry has proposed that the Federal Government take over responsibility for the city's courts, jails and Medicaid program. Congress is considering establishing a financial review board to assume full control of the city's finances.
A hearing has been scheduled for Thursday to explore possible solutions to the District's financial problems and to examine measures taken by other municipalities that have faced similar problems.
About one-third of the city's 41,000 employees, like those in the police, fire, and traffic departments, were exempted from the furlough because they provide critical services.

HEADLINE: Cotabato Journal;
Son Leads Marcos Family Into the Sunlight Again

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHENON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: COTABATO, the Philippines, Feb. 22

 BODY:
The campaign rally was over, and the women in the audience -- hundreds of them -- descended on the cherubic 37-year-old candidate they all still know by his boyhood nickname.
"Bong-Bong!" one teen-ager in the audience in this southern Philippine city screamed between giggles. "Bong-Bong, we love you!" 
 Another reached out to pinch his arm as he passed through the crowd. "Oh, my God!" squealed Bettina Lorez, 22 years old. "I touched Bong-Bong Marcos!"
The candidate, Ferdinand Marcos Jr., is the only son of the late Philippine dictator. Already a member of the Philippine House of Representatives, he is running a high-priced campaign for the Senate in elections this May, and early polls suggest that he should win.
It is the same path -- the House of Representatives, followed by the Senate -- that took his father to the presidency a generation ago. And Mr. Marcos does not deny that he might be interested in following in his father's footsteps all the way to Malacanang, the Presidential palace. "I'll concede that it's in the realm of possibility," he said.
Nine years after he was forced to join his family in humiliating exile in the United States, the younger Mr. Marcos is today the fastest-rising politician in the Philippines.
Supporters say that his quick rise is proof of the continuing power of the Marcos name, while enemies of the younger Mr. Marcos say that it is proof instead that millions of Filipinos have short memories, and that the power of celebrity overwhelms common sense in Philippine politics.
The newspaper attacks on his Senate campaign have been withering. "Like his late unlamented father, Marcos Jr. perceives falsehood as truth, slavery as freedom, and dictatorship as liberty," wrote Juan Mercado, a reporter who was imprisoned by President Marcos. "Like father, like son."
And mother, too? The President's widow, Imelda Marcos, is likely to join her son in the campaign. She is expected to declare her candidacy for a House seat from her home province in the central Philippines, less than four years after she was allowed to return from exile to face criminal charges over the theft of billions of dollars from the Philippines Treasury, charges that linger on. Mrs. Marcos, perhaps best known to the world for the closet full of footwear she left behind when the family fled country in 1986, is almost certain to win if she runs -- a "shoe-in," as Manila columnists, who love word-play, insist on noting.
Young Mr. Marcos says he has been delighted by the reception that he has received since returning to the Philippines in 1991.
"To be perfectly honest, after 7 or 8 years in the United States listening to all those reports, you halfway expected that they would find a tree and lynch you," said Mr. Marcos, who used his time abroad to study at both Oxford University and at the University of Pennsylvania. "But that wasn't the case. We received a tremendous reception."
He insists that he did not enter politics out of any desire to redeem the reputation of his father, who died in exile in Hawaii.
"I'm not going to vindicate my father's name because his name doesn't need vindication," Mr. Marcos said. "I am so confident that history will judge him well."
At the same time, Mr. Marcos makes clear that he is not running on the legacy of his father's 21 years in power -- a legacy that, to many Filipinos, was besmirched by an unquenchable thirst for power and wealth.
"Something obviously went terribly wrong," Mr. Marcos said of his father's presidency. But he suggests that the blame should go to his father's close aides and friends, and not to President Marcos himself.
"I think maybe he should have been more circumspect, less kind, less understanding to some of his friends," Mr. Marcos explained.
The 1986 revolution that toppled his father and brought Corazon C. Aquino to power, he says, was "a living hell -- it was horrible, and I am glad to say I survived with my sanity. There were times when you would say, 'Yeah, maybe we were nasty people; yeah, maybe we shouldn't be allowed to go back home.' "
He appears to have overcome his reputation in the Philippines as a spoiled brat, a reputation that he admits was well-earned.
"Of course I was spoiled," he readily concedes. "How could you not be, living in a palace?"
But if there was self-doubt, it has since disappeared, and Mr. Marcos is today a confident politician who knows how to work a crowd, using equal doses of charm and self-deprecating humor.
His campaign theme is one of Philippine nationalism, and he charges on the stump that President Fidel V. Ramos, one of the heroes of the 1986 uprising, is ruining the economy by selling off Government-owned businesses.
"We know that the only beneficiaries of the Ramos economic policies are not Filipinos," he says on the stump. "It is the foreigners that he has favored with all the largest contracts, with all the largest deals."
Yet economics -- specifically, the economics of campaigning -- is an issue that also dogs Mr. Marcos. The Government has charged that his family stole billions of dollars from the Philippine Treasury, and critics have suggested that some of that plundered wealth is being used in the campaign.
Mr. Marcos denies that there is any hidden wealth. "Where is it?" he asks. "For all the accusations hurled against us, what have they proven? Everything that we did have has been sequestered or sold off."
Mr. Marcos insists that he lives largely off the earnings of his wife, a lawyer from a wealthy Philippine family. And the Senate campaign, he says, is being underwritten by friends abroad.
"For campaign like this, we have to depend on friends," he insists. "I may not be poor, but I'm not rich."

HEADLINE: Priest Given 4 Years in Prison In Armored-Car Robbery Case

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: ROCHESTER, Feb. 27

 BODY:
An Irish priest caught with $2 million from a $7.4 million Brink's armored-car robbery was sentenced today to four years and three months in a Federal prison.
The Rev. Patrick Moloney, a Melkite Catholic who runs a New York City shelter for troubled teen-agers and illegal immigrants, bowed his head when Judge David G. Larimer of Federal District Court referred to him as a thief. "Based on your prior life, you should have known better," the judge said. 
 Father Moloney gave a thumbs-up sign to his brother and three nieces before being taken into custody. He had faced up to five years in prison. The judge opted for the minimum sentence recommended by Federal parole authorities.
Father Moloney, 62, and Samuel I. Millar, 39, a Northern Ireland rebel who served eight years for a botched bombing, were convicted on Nov. 28 of conspiracy to possess stolen money. Mr. Millar is to be sentenced on Thursday.
The authorities said they found $2 million on Nov. 12, 1993, in a Manhattan apartment used by the two men and $168,000 in a safe in the priest's bedroom. About $107,000 had serial numbers linking it to Brink's.
The rest of the money stolen at gunpoint on Jan. 5, 1993, from a Brink's depot in Rochester is missing. Investigators have said they suspect it was funneled to the Irish Republican Army.
The defense asked that Father Moloney, who was released on bail before Christmas, be allowed to serve his sentence nearer to home at a medium-security Federal prison in Allenwood, Pa. The judge said prison officials would decide where the priest would serve his time.
In appealing for mercy, Father Moloney called himself "a victim of circumstance and circumstantial evidence." More than 100 letters of support from friends, supporters and priests were sent to the judge.
In response, Chris Buscaglia, the prosecutor, said, "I have a hard time understanding the idea that because Patrick Moloney might have done some good things in the past, that entitles him to some get-out-of-jail-free card."
Acquitted after a seven-week trial were Charles M. McCormick Jr., an unemployed teacher who subleased the apartment to the priest, and Thomas F. O'Connor, an ex-Brink's guard who was said to have smuggled Mr. Millar into the United States in 1984 and was suspected of masterminding the Brink's robbery.
Mr. O'Connor, a retired police detective, befriended Mr. Millar on a trip to Belfast in 1984. He testified that at least three gunmen showed up in the depot, took him hostage and dumped him on a roadside two hours later.
Father Moloney, who emigrated from Limerick in 1955, was arrested on weapons-smuggling charges during a visit home in 1980 but was never prosecuted. His brother, John, pleaded guilty and served three years.

HEADLINE: THE NEEDIEST CASES;
Donations Lag as Campaign Nears End

BYLINE: By ABBY GOODNOUGH 

 BODY:
With one day left in its 83d campaign, The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund still has a chance to match last year's total of $4.59 million. It would have to collect another $250,000, a task that is formidable but not impossible.
Contributions dated no later than March 1 and received by March 8 will be included in this year's appeal. The total today -- $4,346,550.81 -- is the lowest since 1989, when the fund raised $4,039,728.75. The number of donors this year -- 11,820 as of last Friday -- is also unusually low. Last year, 13,500 individuals and foundations sent money to the fund. In 1993, almost 14,000 did so. 
 When the campaign ends, the money will be split among seven of New York's largest private charities. As in the past, they will use it to provide an array of programs for thousands of poor, sick, elderly and disabled people. But this year, charity officials say, the fund will be more crucial than ever in providing food, clothing, furniture and housing.
This year's fund had a promising start, and by New Year's Day it was nearly $360,000 ahead of last year's pace. But donations slowed considerably after that, and by late January, the fund was receiving only several thousand dollars a day. The decrease in donations comes as government budget cuts threaten a vast range of social services in New York, from soup kitchens to medical benefits.
The fund's most successful campaign was in 1991, when it raised $5.08 million. This year's decrease reflects a precarious economy -- many who gave wrote that they did so despite salary cuts and layoffs.
But there was no lack of spirit among contributors. After reading that the fund was behind in January, many sent second and even third donations.
"My previous donation was made with the plight of elderly poor in mind," wrote Elma Williamson of Rhinebeck, N.Y., who sent a second donation of $200. "But this I would like to dedicate to the children, in hopes that they can find a more stable existence in this chaotic world."
In letters, hundreds of donors expressed anger about plans to decrease government aid to the poor. Over and over, they recognized that the need was more pronounced than ever.
As the campaign neared an end, the letters grew more impassioned.
"Since I have sent a contribution to your fund as long as I have lived in New York I should not like to bypass a year when there seems to be more need than ever," wrote Gina Epworth of Manhattan, who sent $100 a few weeks ago.
Writing that she was sad to see the fund lagging, Janet L. Schultz of Eugene, Ore., sent $100 in early February.
"Perhaps everyone is late, as I am," she wrote. "People seem to get more caught up in their own worlds every year and I wish they'd take our President's words to heart, that we all have to work together and help."

HEADLINE: Supreme Court to Decide Case On Bias Against Legal Aliens

BYLINE: By LINDA GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 27

 BODY:
The Supreme Court agreed today to decide whether a Reconstruction-era civil rights law, originally intended to protect the legal rights of former slaves, also bars discrimination against aliens.
While the issue reaches the Court in the context of the refusal by an insurance company to write homeowner's insurance for people who are not American citizens, the case could have considerably broader implications. 
 The United States Chamber of Commerce and a coalition of large employers told the Court that an interpretation of the 1866 law as barring employment discrimination against aliens would force employers to violate that law every time they complied with a 1986 law that makes it a crime to hire aliens who lack documents authorizing them to work in the United States.
The question the Court agreed to decide covers only private and not governmental discrimination against aliens, so the case is not likely to affect such governmental measures as California's Proposition 187, which bars illegal aliens from public school, welfare benefits and non-emergency health care. The California measure is being challenged principally on constitutional grounds; Supreme Court precedents have given aliens a protected status within the 14th Amendment's guarantee of equal protection.
But the Constitution does not govern purely private behavior like employment or ordinary commercial transactions, which are covered by a number of modern and century-old civil rights laws.
In the case the Court accepted today, the law at issue is the Civil Rights Act of 1866, which is usually referred to as Section 1981, after its numerical listing in the United States Code. The law gives "all persons" the same right to "make and enforce contracts as is enjoyed by white citizens."
The United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit, in Richmond, ruled last year that this law could be invoked by an Australian citizen, a legal resident of the United States, who was refused a homeowner's policy on his Baltimore house by the Government Employees Insurance Company, a subsidiary of the Geico Corporation. The company told the man, Vincent P. Duane, that it did not write homeowner's insurance for noncitizens.
Mr. Duane sued in Federal District Court for an injunction and damages. Geico asked that the suit be dismissed on the ground that Section 1981 applied only to discrimination on the basis of race. Both the District Court and the appellate court ruled that the law also bars discrimination against aliens. Those courts' rulings made no distinction between aliens in the United States legally or illegally.
The case has not yet gone to trial, reaching the Supreme Court on the purely legal issue of the law's scope.
In its appeal, Geico v. Duane, No. 94-1139, the company argues that Congress has never indicated a belief that private discrimination against aliens was illegal. The debate over the Immigration Reform and Control Act of 1986, which made it illegal for employers to hire undocumented aliens while at the same time protecting some legal aliens against job-related discrimination, proceeded on the assumption that no laws existed on the subject, the company's brief said.
The notion that Section 1981 already prohibited discrimination against aliens "is an astonishing proposition," the brief said, "because Congress has legislated for more than a century without identifying or implementing any such national policy."
Mr. Duane argued in his brief that to interpret Section 1981 as not applying to discrimination against noncitizens, the Court would have to ignore the law's literal language, which guarantees to all the rights enjoyed by "white citizens."
In 1987, the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, in New Orleans, ruled that Section 1981 did not apply to aliens; the Supreme Court considered that decision but ultimately did not review it.
Also today, the Court heard arguments in a Federal criminal case that raises the question of whether the Government has jurisdiction under the racketeering law to prosecute a crime involving a single small company with operations essentially confined to one state.
Last year the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in San Francisco, overturned the racketeering conviction of a former Federal prosecutor who used the proceeds of narcotics dealing to finance the operation of a gold mine in Alaska.
The Racketeer Influence and Corrupt Organizations Act, the Federal law usually known as RICO, makes it a crime to invest income from racketeering activity in "any enterprise which is engaged in, or the activities of which affect, interstate or foreign commerce." The Ninth Circuit ruled in this case that the Government had failed to prove that the gold mine had more than an "incidental" effect on interstate commerce.
The ruling was startling because since the New Deal, the Federal courts have placed few if any constraints on the authority of Congress to regulate and define interstate commerce. In its appeal of that 1994 ruling, U.S. v. Robertson, No. 94-251, the Government is urging the Court to rule that any "perceptible and identifiable" impact on interstate commerce is sufficient to provide jurisdiction.
In this case, the gold mine used supplies from outside Alaska and its owner, the defendant, traveled in and out of the state, Miguel A. Estrada, an Assistant Solicitor General, told the Court.
Justice Sandra Day O'Connor asked, "Is there any business enterprise in America that wouldn't be covered" by the commerce power?
"As a practical matter, I cannot think of any business in America that wouldn't be covered," Mr. Estrada replied. He added that "in our economy in this day and age, I can't think of anything that is likely to happen in the real world" that would not have implications for interstate commerce.
The Justices appeared essentially to agree with the Government's lawyer, although one member of the Court, Justice Antonin Scalia, said in frustration at one point, "If that's what we've said, maybe we should unsay it."
Later, addressing Glenn S. Warren, the defendant's lawyer, Justice Scalia did not appear to hold out much hope that the mountain of precedent arrayed against him was about to tumble down. "You don't have a single case in which 'affecting commerce' has been interpreted by this Court as you wish us to interpret it here," Justice Scalia told Mr. Warren in a disappointed tone.
But a second interstate commerce case the Court is considering during this term may present more problems for the Government. In early November, the Court heard arguments in U.S. v. Lopez, No. 93-1260, which challenges the constitutionality of a 1990 law that made it a crime to possess a gun within 1,000 feet of a school. A Federal appeals court declared the law unconstitutional as going beyond the Government's authority to regulate interstate commerce. Today, nearly four months after the argument, that case is apparently still under active consideration, as several questions about it from the bench indicated.

HEADLINE: CUNY Professors, Fearing Worst, Rush Out Their Resumes

BYLINE: By WILLIAM H. HONAN 

 BODY:
Tenured professors, both young and old, already had begun frantically sending out resumes and scrambling to seek jobs at academic conventions, as anger and anxiety escalated about the effect of proposed budget cuts on the City University of New York.
But yesterday, their worst fears began to take shape when the board of trustees met and declared a financial emergency for CUNY's senior colleges -- a step that paves the way to lay off tenured faculty. 
 "With the kind of cuts now being talked about, I can't do my research and maintain my professional self-respect," said a 40-year-old Hunter College professor who is considered a superstar by many of his colleagues.
"I'm sending out my resume," said the professor, who spoke on condition of anonymity.
Last night's unanimous vote by the trustees echoed their declaration of a financial emergency on Jan. 30 for CUNY's six two-year community colleges. Last night, they acknowledged that the system's 12 four-year colleges are also in jeopardy because of Gov. George E. Pataki's proposed $162 million cut in state financing for the next school year.
The trustees also unanimously approved CUNY's participation in the state retirement incentive program now being discussed in the Legislature. As many as 5,000 of its 14,000 staff and faculty -- both tenured and nontenured -- would be eligible, said Jay Hershenson, director of university relations.
At CUNY, contracts with tenured faculty require that they be given 12 months of advance notice before being dismissed. Now that an emergency has been declared, presidents of the senior colleges will begin to prepare plans to cut spending.
"The budget as it currently stands will not allow us to keep everyone on the payroll currently with us," said W. Ann Reynolds, CUNY's chancellor.
The four-year colleges have a total budget of $900 million, of which $300 million comes from tuition and $600 million from the state.
The community colleges have a total budget of $319 million, in which tuition amounts to $126 million, the state contributes $119 million and the city $74 million.
In previous fiscal crises, CUNY has called for significant faculty layoffs. But this time, spokesmen for faculty members say, the issue is as much politics as economics.
Contrasting the present situation with the financial crisis of 20 years ago, Irwin H. Polishook, president of the CUNY faculty union, said: "The cause of the trouble in 1976 was the de facto bankruptcy of New York City. The trouble in 1995 is fiscal, political and ideological."
Mr. Polishook, who was in Albany yesterday lobbying against the Governor's proposed cuts, said, "Everybody up here is talking about the death penalty. If something doesn't change, we'll have a sentence of death for the university."
As to whether some professors were likely to be more in danger losing their jobs than others, Mr. Polishook said, "Those in the highest demand area are in a stronger position."
An unusually hostile atmosphere pervaded last night's board meeting. Some faculty members, wearing their colorful purple, black and blue academic gowns, and seated in the audience made out-of-order comments throughhout the first half hour of the meeting. They grew silent only when James P. Murphy, chairman of the board, threatened to have them evicted.
William Crain, chairman of the psychology department, shouted that the trustees were yielding to political pressure.
"We respectfully request a much stronger public defense of the university," he said. "There has been no defense from trustees."
After the vote, Sheldon Weinbaum, an engineering professor, said: "If they had any sense, they'd resign en masse. I mean, a 25 percent cut for CUNY? And they're just sitting there and taking it. No chutzpah."
Barbara Bowen, director of women's studies at Queens College, agreed.
"What this represents and what the trustees just ascented to was a demotion of CUNY out of being a university toward being something less than a college. They just sat in there and agreed to let CUNY go."

HEADLINE: Rubin's Plan For Banking Spurs Fight

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 27

 BODY:
Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin touched off a vigorous lobbying and political fight today by proposing the elimination of legal barriers that have separated the nation's commercial banks, securities firms and insurance companies for decades.
Big businesses in the three industries welcomed the Administration's call for Congress to pass legislation essentially abolishing the Glass-Steagall Act that forbids banks and securities firms to merge. They said it would help to make them more efficient and more internationally competitive. But small banks and insurance agents strongly criticized the plan as a threat to their operations and bad public policy. 
 Mr. Rubin said the Administration's plan would foster competition, forcing companies to offer better service to consumers and businesses at lower cost. The proposal would allow banks to diversify into new industries, while requiring that they not use federally insured deposits in those industries. The Administration contends that taking such steps will produce a healthier banking industry.
"It's absolutely essential that the banking system maintain safety and soundness," Mr. Rubin said in a speech to the New York Savings Bond Committee in Manhattan. "But where there are constraints that have become outmoded by changing conditions, then those constraints should be lifted."
The critics of the plan on Capitol Hill and at various trade groups quickly warned that allowing mergers of banks and securities firms could mean heavy losses to taxpayers if the combined companies gambled in stock markets with federally insured money.
As evidence of the riskiness of securities trading, critics like Representative Charles Schumer, a Brooklyn Democrat, pointed to the collapse on Sunday of Barings P.L.C. The British investment bank apparently lost close to $1 billion when a single trader made a wrong bet on the direction of Japanese stock markets.
"My basic concern is just as was highlighted by Barings," Mr. Schumer said. "You could end up with a crisis that makes the savings and loan crisis look tame."
Paul A. Eguale, senior vice president for government affairs at the Independent Insurance Agents of America, said that if big banks were allowed to merge with insurance companies, they would start demanding that their customers buy insurance from the affiliates as a condition for granting them loans.
"It's absolutely incredible to me that we have a Democratic Administration that does not see the dangers of allowing these large financial institutions to have cross ownerships at the expense of Main Street," Mr. Eguale said. "They're sacrificing economic interests in the name of international competition."
But Edward Yingling, chief lobbyist of the American Bankers Association, which represents banks of all sizes, said the group would now push actively for the repeal of the Glass-Steagall Act, which has banned mergers of commercial banks and securities firms since the Great Depression.
Two different Republican bills are already working their way through Congress that would also deregulate the American financial system. One, introduced by Representative Jim Leach, the Iowa Republican who is chairman of the House Banking Committee, would only allow the mergers of commercial banks and investment banks, but not insurance companies.
Mr. Leach expressed dismay today that the Administration's plan was not stricter in preventing the use of federally insured deposits in areas outside commercial banking.
Mr. Leach's bill would have the Federal Reserve regulate a holding company that would own a securities firm and a commercial bank, thereby giving the Fed new powers over the nation's securities industry. The Administration's plan, by contrast, would give the Fed a central role only in regulating financial conglomerates primarily in commercial banking. The chairman of the Federal Reserve, Alan Greenspan, is scheduled to present his views to the House Banking Committee on Tuesday.
Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato, the New York Republican who heads the Senate Banking Committee, and Representative Richard H. Baker, a Louisiana Republican who heads a panel of the House Banking Committee, have introduced identical bills in the House and Senate. They would allow nonfinancial companies like AT&T to merge with commercial banks, as well as securities firms and insurance companies.
Senator D'Amato praised Mr. Rubin's plan for its similarities to his own plan. "His support for key provisions of my bill is the first indication from this Administration that it will join in a bipartisan effort to create a more innovative and competitive financial services industry," Senator D'Amato said.
Mr. Baker said in a telephone interview on Saturday that he did not feel the Federal Government was equipped to continue regulating financial industries given the pace of change in them. "In the Banking Committee's offices, you will find memory typewriters," he said, "when we're working in a world of four-pound laptops."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin, who yesterday proposed eliminating the barriers between commercial banks and securities firms. (Associated Press) (pg. D5)

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Day 200: Negotiators Begin New Round of Talks in Changed Atmosphere

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: SCOTTSDALE, Ariz., Feb. 27

 BODY:
Negotiators for baseball's owners and players began a new round of talks today, but remained uncertain whether the presence of Bud Selig or his peace talks with Donald Fehr last week would have any impact on their effort to end the 200-day-old strike.
Two small groups led by Selig and Fehr met for four hours, spending much of their time on the core economic issues of payroll tax, salary arbitration, free agency, revenue sharing and an industry growth fund. When they emerged, they acknowledged that they remained far apart, but emphasized that the atmosphere had changed. 
 Describing the meeting as "businesslike, not hostile," Fehr said: "We're talking and the tone is civil. Hopefully, it will lead somewhere. It would be unfair to go beyond that at this point. It's too soon to draw inferences from that about substance."
While the two sides await concrete results of their supposedly changed relationship, another scenario that could develop in the next two weeks away from the bargaining table could have a far greater impact on getting the players back to work.
The general counsel of the National Labor Relations Board is close to making a decision on the union's unfair labor practice charge against the clubs. That decision could trigger a series of events that could put the players back on the field around the middle of next month.
Fred Feinstein, the general counsel, was not in his office in Washington today to comment on the status of his deliberations, but the negotiators were back at the table, only at a new site.
Selig, the acting commissioner, and Fehr, the union chief, led their teams in the first serious bargaining since the last round of talks collapsed at President Clinton's feet in the White House the night of Feb. 7. They met at a golf club on Gainey Ranch, a high-income housing community in this Phoenix suburb. The site was selected because Selig and Fehr's parents have homes in the community.
Selig had previously spent little time in talks with the players.
"I said all along if everybody thought that my being here was productive, I'd be happy to be so," Selig said. "It's very difficult to determine what that will or won't mean. I hope it will mean something productive, but only time will tell."
Selig declined to spout false optimism. "This is not going to be a quick process," he said at a news conference.
Fehr echoed that thought. "Clearly," he said, "we still have a lot of work to do. The gulf between us is wide."
In Washington, Feinstein was very likely only days away from deciding whether to issue a complaint against the owners and if so, whether to seek an injunction that would force the clubs to restore all of the work rules that existed before they tried to implement a salary cap.
People on both sides expect Feinstein to recommend both steps to the board. If the board tells him to seek an injunction, the board and the owners could be in United States District Court in Manhattan by the end of next week. A judge could then have a decision by sometime the following week, perhaps by March 15.
"If the N.L.R.B. seeks and obtains an injunction restoring the previous terms of employment," Fehr said, "I would recommend our members go back on that basis."
The owners could counter by locking out the players, but that action would be accompanied by a major legal and economic risk. If the owners locked out the players and an administrative law judge upheld the board's unfair labor practice complaint, the clubs would be liable for back pay plus interest.
Asked about the labor board's possible role in the dispute, Selig said: "If we continue to dwell on the ancillary issues, the issues other than the issues that have separated the parties, we'll never make progress. You have to focus on making a deal."
The labor board was prepared to issue a complaint against the owners earlier this month charging that their declaration of an impasse Dec. 22 was illegal. But Feinstein placed the case in abeyance when the owners agreed to withdraw the salary cap and restore what was supposed to be the status quo.
But the owners viewed the status quo, the previous work rules, differently from the union, eliminating salary arbitration and placing the negotiation of individual contracts solely in the province of the Player Relations Committee, the clubs' labor arm. That's when the union filed its second unfair labor practice charge.
The two sides recently have become embroiled in a dispute over the status of minor league players. The union has asked minor leaguers not to play in major league exhibition games, saying they would be considered replacement players if they did. The clubs have accused the union of using those players, whom they do not represent, for their own means.
A three-man union contingent met with about 340 minor leaguers in Tampa, Fla., tonight to explain the union's position. Other meetings are scheduled for West Palm Beach, Fla., Tuesday night and Phoenix Wednesday night.
Joining Fehr in the negotiating session today were Lauren Rich, a union lawyer, and three players, Jay Bell, Paul Molitor and Terry Steinbach. Working with Selig were John Harrington of the Boston Red Sox, Jerry McMorris of the Colorado Rockies, Dave Montgomery of the Philadelphia Phillies and two lawyers, Chuck O'Connor and Rob Manfred.
Jerry Reinsdorf of the Chicago White Sox, a member of the owners' negotiating committee, was at the Hyatt Regency Hotel, which is also on the Gainey Ranch grounds, but he said he was not participating in the talks.
A person on the management side said he heard that Reinsdorf was claiming to have nine owners pledged to block a settlement they didn't like. Ratification of an agreement requires 21 votes from the 28 clubs.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Donald Fehr, left, and Bud Selig spoke cautiously yesterday after four hours of talks on the economic issues. (Photographs by Associated Press)

LENGTH: 785 words

HEADLINE: The Nuclear Wedge Issue

 BODY:
Citizens should not be deceived about the real motivation behind the gathering crusade against affirmative action. The rhetoric about reverse discrimination is the 90's equivalent of blue smoke and mirrors. Everyone in American politics knows what is going on, and if three decades of racial progress are to be abandoned, let us at least be candid about it. Republican strategists and the party's Presidential candidates have spotted a nuclear-strength wedge issue for the 1996 election.
By concocting a fantasy that white males are a new victim class in the world's richest nation, they see a highway to a pre-emptive lock on the electoral vote. The tipoff came from the 1994 Congressional election, when 62 percent of white males voted for G.O.P. candidates. By sustaining those numbers the Republicans can seal off President Clinton, or any other Democratic nominee, from victory in the nation's Southern tier, including the mega-states of Texas and California. Absent a vote drain from a third-party candidate, a Democratic victory becomes virtually impossible. 
 The G.O.P.'s reigning ideologue, William Kristol, sketched the plan for building this miraculous backlash machine, and Senators Bob Dole and Phil Gramm have cranked it up. Democrats are rushing to get out of the way. Perhaps President Clinton's promised review of existing Federal affirmative action regulations will have a calming effect in his party's ranks. If not, they will break and run.
The attack on affirmative action depends on exaggerating every inconvenience suffered by a white worker or student into a massive insult against egalitarian principle. It is true that in absolute terms, males have lost a tiny portion of the economic advantage represented by all-male construction unions, firehouses and medical school classes. It is also true the contracting guidelines pioneered by President Nixon put new businesses owned by blacks, Latinos and women into a bid pool that was once a safe haven for white-owned firms.
A growing economy once cushioned the impact of such competition. But as high-salaried industrial and government jobs disappear, a sinister national tendency toward scapegoating has resurfaced. The noble bipartisan tradition of reasonable remedies for past discrimination has withered. In its place, the nation has a new class of politicians willing to argue that it is intolerable for a white male anywhere in America to taste one drop of the discrimination that over the decades has been gulped in gallons by blacks and women.
Critics of affirmative action would have a strong case if their handful of reverse-discrimination examples represented a vast national problem. But the reality is far different, and it is an apotheosis of illogic to have the United States junk 30 years of progress because a Colorado contractor lost a $300,000 guard-rail contract to a minority bidder.
Among doctors, lawyers, scientists and university teachers, fewer than one in 20 are black. The ratio is only slightly higher in the construction trades. The plain truth is that the number of blacks at the professional or skilled labor levels is simply too slight to produce much competition, let alone discrimination against white men.
The remedies that have helped a small number of blacks have helped a larger number of women. Almost a quarter of the nation's doctors and lawyers are women. But that simply means that professional schools that once sliced the admissions pie in favor of white males have been forced to hand out more equal slices.
Does anyone doubt that this happened because of Federal law and enforcement pressure? The city of Richmond, Va., shows what happens when that pressure disappears. Under its minority contracting rules, more than 30 percent of city construction contracts went to minority companies. With revocation of those rules, minority participation fell to 5 percent of the contracts in 1993 in a city that is 55 percent black. A new outreach program is now driving the numbers back up, though nowhere near the original accomplishments.
Richmond points the way toward a new national reality if the war on affirmative action succeeds. Today, young activists from privileged backgrounds and elite universities argue that America's poor, its minorities and women deserve no advantage to correct past discrimination at work and school. Theirs is the callow wisdom of the political-science class, not the ennobling, real-world wisdom produced from our nation's long struggle against racial and gender inequity.
Mr. Kristol likes to say that affirmative action has been poisonous for race relations in America. There is poison in the air these days, but not the one he imagines.

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
The Road Not Taken

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL 

 BODY:
See them smile and toast each other before their little Chinese Communist and American flags. The negotiators from Beijing and Washington are celebrating their happy day of commercial accord.
And why not? They have done their job well. The Americans said that unless the Communists stopped stealing United States intellectual property like movies, compact disks and computer software, Washington would retaliate with sanctions on more than $1 billion of Chinese exports to the U.S. 
 Being no fools, the Communists, after making all kinds of horrible faces, gave in at the last moment -- as predictable from the first moment. That is, the first moment that the Clinton Administration showed that this time it really meant its warnings, really.
So maybe the rest of us should be toasting each other too, because Beijing and Washington have shown we were right -- that on important disputes between the two countries the Chinese Communists would give ground in the face of economic pressure from the United States.
But we do not toast. We see the road not taken. We know where it might have led had Mr. Clinton had the courage, if not of his conviction then at least of his promises.
We asked the President to use tariff pressure against Communist China to persuade Beijing to lessen the use of its three key instruments of rule: torture, imprisonment and slave labor. No revolution was demanded, no abdication by the Communists, just that they display restraint in practicing their theology of force as government.
Mr. Clinton agreed but broke his word. There are two reasons to bring all this up -- and again tomorrow and tomorrow.
One is simply that Chinese are still tied to torture boards in political cells and made to work in factories based on slave labor, and Tibetans are still beaten, arrested, deported from their own land. Of all Americans who should continue to fight for China, none have a greater obligation than those who voted for Bill Clinton. The second reason we will deal with in a moment.
Soon after taking office, he signed an executive order: If the Communists did not lessen oppression in China and occupied Tibet by spring 1994, the U.S. would raise some tariffs on Beijing's exports to the U.S.
It was sensible to believe it would work. It was simple decency to try. Economic pressure had been carried out, effectively, against the Soviet Union and South Africa.
And for Communist China, the U.S. was and is an essential customer. Beijing sells us four times as much as it buys, runs a $30 billion trade profit with the U.S. and counts on our technology to strengthen and smarten its huge war machine.
Last year, up to almost the last moment, top members of the Administration said Mr. Clinton would never go back on his executive order.
But as they spoke, the China lobby -- American businesses operating in China -- had already gotten to Mr. Clinton. The President, knowing his executive order was not fulfilled, just tore it up. What's more, he said he did not want to hear about human rights anymore as a China-trade issue. When he turned, he whirled all the way round. State Department officials who had said he would never do such a thing, what could you be thinking of, meekly changed their public minds, as did a sad collection of Democratic senators.
Now, the State Department report on human rights shows that oppression is as pervasive and cruel as when Mr. Clinton made his promise, and when he broke it.
That second reason to remember: Political appeasement and economic strengthening of dictatorships are related and contagious diseases.
Politically and morally there is a connecting line between forgetting the tortured in the Chinese cells, sales of high-tech military equipment to the Chinese by America, Europe and Israel, European pressure to lift the embargoes against Saddam Hussein and Fidel Castro, and Russian contracts with Iran for nuclear plans.
The democracies cannot destroy all tyrannies. But they should not empower them. Yet they do, they do.
Maybe, after the President betrayed himself and human rights pledges last year, the political bosses of the gulag in Beijing's Politburo offices raised happy little toasts of their own. I think so.

HEADLINE: Pataki's Budget Could Lift Bond Rating, Agency Says

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ALBANY, Feb. 27

 BODY:
Gov. George E. Pataki's proposed budget received unusual support today from a leading state credit agency, which suggested that it would raise New York's bond rating if the Governor's budget is passed on time and if it is in balance for half a year.
Standard & Poor's, which rates New York's general obligation bonds at A-, the lowest of any state, said in a report that passage of Mr. Pataki's budget by April 1, the start of the fiscal year, "could provide the impetus for a rating upgrade." But with a month to go before the deadline -- and a 10-year record of failing to pass a budget on time -- negotiations between Mr. Pataki and the Legislature are in only a nascent stage. 
 The Standard & Poor's report noted that Mr. Pataki had proposed to reduce state spending for the first time in recent history while also cutting income taxes.
"This budget must be based on realistic economic assumptions and passed in a timely manner," the report states. "If that is achieved, S. & P. will consider it another step toward the state's fiscal recovery that could lead to an increase in the state's G.O. bond rating to 'A' in the near future."
An increase in the bond rating would allow New York to borrow at lower interest rates, thereby saving money for future budgets. It also would provide a psychological boost for the state's business climate and a political victory for Mr. Pataki.
Richard J. Marino, a director of Standard & Poor's, said it was uncommon for the agency to hint publicly about a rating increase before it was ready to grant one. He said the firm would not act until at least October, when it would be able to judge if the state budget remained in balance after six months.
The state's other primary credit rating agency, Moody's Investors Service, issued a report last week that was less encouraging. It made no suggestions about the possibility of a rating increase.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bonds Gain On Failure of British Bank

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Government securities gained in thin trading yesterday, after the collapse of a venerable British investment bank helped to attract funds to the market as some investors sold stocks to buy short-term securities.
Early in the day the failure of Barings P.L.C., the 228-year-old British investment house, sent ripples through the world's financial markets amid fears that the crisis would spread. While trading steadied in Europe, the United States bond market still benefited from a flight to quality as investors sought a safe haven. 
 But traders cautioned that yesterday's gains, the second consecutive advance, should not be seen as the start of a longer rally, especially because the short end of the market had been vulnerable to reports about the health of the economy.
William A. Hornbarger, associate vice president, fixed-income research, at A. G. Edwards & Company in Cleveland, said the market's move was made in thin trading, "and thin trading will exacerbate any move the market makes."
The price of the 30-year bond rose 21/32 , to 10029/32 . The yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 7.47 percent, from 7.52 percent on Friday, and is still at a five-month low. The rates of shorter-maturity bills were also down.
At the Treasury's regular weekly auction of new three- and six-month bills yesterday, the rates declined for the third consecutive week. The rate for three-month bills dropped a basis point, to 5.73 percent, while the rate for a six-month bill fell 11 basis points, to 5.90 percent. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
The market is likely to be tested in two weeks, when the Government releases the latest employment data. But ahead of that is the Purchasing Management report, to be released tomorrow. The market will focus on the prices paid and prices received components, and look for inflationary pressures as signs of whether the Federal Reserve's policy of increasing short-term interest rates has kept inflation from accelerating.
Mr. Hornbarger said the chairman of the Federal Reserve, Alan Greenspan, was scheduled to speak to the National Association of Business Economists in Washington tonight. As a result, trading activity may be limited today, as the market waits to see if Mr. Greenspan will say anything beyond his remarks in Congressional testimony last week.
"We think the bond market right now is carving out a new trading range," Mr. Hornbarger said, "and the current yield may be the low end of that trading range."
The Treasury market was also supported by a steady dollar, which recovered from declines caused by a surging German mark. A stronger dollar makes dollar-denominated investments like Treasury securities very attractive to foreign investors, especially when there is turmoil in other markets.
Elsewhere, municipal bond prices rose, as a decline in new sales, down 50 percent from last year, helped increase the demand for outstanding issues. This week the dealers are expecting to sell $1.42 billion of new paper, but that will be down $680 million from last week.
The largest sale of the week, a $500 million bond offering by New York State tomorrow, is expected to do well. Lehman Brothers will manage the offering, in which a New York 10-year bond could yield 5.50 percent, according to traders.
Following are the results of the Treasury's auction of three- and six-month bills yesterday:

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Shell and Exxon in North Sea Oil Venture

BYLINE: By AGIS SALPUKAS 

 BODY:
Two of the world's largest oil companies, the Royal Dutch/Shell Group and the Exxon Corporation, announced yesterday that they had received approval from the British Government for an $800 million project to squeeze more oil and gas out of developed fields in the North Sea.
A series of such projects has helped keep the flow of oil in the North Sea at much higher-than-expected levels over the last several years -- a trend that has made it more difficult for the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries to force world oil prices higher. 
 Last November, OPEC decided to extend its production quota of 24.5 million barrels a day through the end of this year, in the hope of keeping supplies tight and pushing up prices as world demand rose.
The strategy has worked to some degree; prices have recovered from the mid-December level of $16.50 a barrel. Yesterday, the price of the benchmark West Texas intermediate crude was $18.66 a barrel.
Adam Sieminski, an energy analyst for NatWest Securities, said the increase in North Sea production had moderated the rise in prices. Although many big fields in the North Sea are reaching maturity, he said, projects like the one announced yesterday have enabled oil companies to tap into smaller surrounding fields and keep production from falling.
"They just keep squeezing out more oil and gas," he said.
Production in the North Sea rose from 4.48 million barrels a day in 1993 to 5.3 million barrels a day in 1994. It is expected to climb to about 5.8 million by end of this year.
The two companies will develop three fields in the central North Sea, known as Teal, Teal South and Guillemot A, more than 100 miles east of Aberdeen, Scotland. A medium-sized tanker will be used for loading and storage.
Production is scheduled to begin in about two years. The venture will be able to process 55,000 barrels of oil a day and transport 30 million cubic feet of gas a day through the nearby Fulmar Gas Line to St. Fergus, Scotland.
While the oil reserves of the three fields, at 90 million barrels, are not huge, the venture will be among several modest projects that have helped production in the North Sea. Horizontal drilling has enabled oil companies to tap fields from existing platforms, and three-dimensional seismic surveys have enabled them to pinpoint deposits, greatly reducing the chance of dry wells.
The three fields in the venture will be developed using underwater wells, linked by flow lines to the tanker.
Heinz Rothermund, the managing director of Shell U.K. Exploration and Production, which will operate the venture, said the use of such a platform system "will provide higher profitability than any alternative options and offers the potential for the economic development of other North Sea fields in the future."

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Conseco Wants to Buy All of 2 Insurers

BYLINE: By MICHAEL QUINT 

 BODY:
Conseco Inc., which tried unsuccessfully last year to acquire the much larger Kemper Corporation, said yesterday that it wanted to spend almost $728 million to buy shares of two life insurance companies it controls but does not own entirely.
Because the shares of the two affiliate companies -- Bankers Life Holding and CCP Insurance -- have been depressed along with many other insurance stocks, analysts saw the move as way for Conseco to buy cheaply shares that could help improve its faltering earnings and perhaps be sold at a profit some day. Conseco, a insurance holding company, knows the operations of the two companies inside out, as it reorganized them in the early 1990's and continues to provide much of their management. 
 Michael A. Lewis, an insurance analyst at Dean Witter, estimated that the purchases would "significantly" increase Conseco's per-share earnings, even if it had to issue new securities to raise the $727.6 million it proposed to pay for the shares. He added that owning 100 percent of the two companies could give Conseco more flexibility in managing them than the current arrangement in which Conseco owns about 60 percent of Bankers Life and a little less than half of CCP.
Conseco's business strategy of buying life insurance companies, fixing them to improve profits and then selling shares to the public was very successful in the 1980's and early 1990's. The company, based in Carmel, Ind., is continuing that approach, though higher interest rates have hurt profits of most life insurers and Conseco's fourth-quarter profits were 35 percent less than in the comparable period a year earlier.
The morning announcement of Conseco's plans caused a quick rise in the company's stock, which closed yesterday at $35.25, up 75 cents. But even with that gain, and recent purchases by Conseco of its own shares, its stock remains below the $45 of two weeks before it announced fourth-quarter earnings that were weaker than expected. The company's share price is well below its all-time high of $75.75, reached in October 1993.
Because of the close ties among Bankers Life, CCP and Conseco, the offers of $22 a share for Bankers Life and $22.50 a share for CCP are expected to be approved. Shares of Bankers Life rose $2.50 yesterday, to $21 a share, a price that is only slightly below the $22 price set in 1993, when a Conseco-led investor group publicly offered the shares, and well above the low of $16.50 last December. CCP rose $1.25, to $20.625, well above the $15 set at its public offering in 1992 and the $13.625 low set last December.
Steven Hilbert, chairman of Conseco, is also chairman of Bankers Life and CCP. The Conseco offer will be considered by independent directors of those two companies.
Bankers Life, based in Chicago, specializes in the sale of supplemental medical insurance, long-term- care insurance and annuities, using its own network of sales agents. CCP, based in Carmel, specializes in the sales of annuities to educators using a sales force of independent agents.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Another Cut In Production By Chrysler

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: DETROIT, Feb. 27

 BODY:
In another signal that demand for new vehicles may be weakening, the Chrysler Corporation plans to idle the Canadian assembly plant that produces its midsize and full-size sedans for a second week, beginning Monday.
The plant, in Bramalea, near Toronto, had operated two shifts continuously since Chrysler introduced its LH sedans in 1992. But last Wednesday, Chrysler said it would shut the plant this week, because too many unsold vehicles had accumulated on dealers' lots. 
 Then, after examining sales estimates on Friday, Chrysler executives decided they would forgo another week's production in hope of thinning dealers' inventories.
"Inventories just aren't where we want them to be, and we've said before we won't overproduce," Michael Aberlich, a Chrysler spokesman, said today in confirming the production cut.
The Bramalea plant produces the Chrysler Concorde, New Yorker and LHS; the Dodge Intrepid, and the Eagle Vision.
The cutbacks reflect an industrywide trend. Since December, dealer inventories have been rising relative to the levels a year earlier, when demand was unseasonably robust.
Climbing interest rates have held back demand, the auto makers say, although they still predict sales will increase this year and next. While sales remain strong for pickup trucks and sport-utility vehicles, other segments of the market, like mini-vans, appear to be weakening.
Chrysler has reined in its optimism about the market. Last spring, it projected record industry sales in 1995 of 16.6 million cars and light trucks. But as interest rates rose, Chrysler's economists reduced that number to 15.6 million, which would be about a 3.3 percent increase over 1994 sales of nearly 15.1 million.
Auto industry analysts have been expecting Chrysler and other auto makers to cut back their aggressive production schedules. On Friday, Jack Kirnan of Salomon Brothers trimmed his earnings estimates for Chrysler, mostly because of production cuts and rising incentive costs. Last week, Chrysler doubled rebates on some mini-vans.
Saying that most of the problems are temporary, Mr. Kirnan continues to rate Chrysler's stock a buy. Still, he has reduced his forecast of 1995 per-share earnings by 50 cents, to $9.50, and his 1996 forecast by $1, to $11. Chrysler shares closed today on the New York Stock Exchange at $44.25, down 12.5 cents.
Mr. Kirnan predicted that Chrysler would also have to cut a week of production of its Neon subcompact car. And he said the Ford Motor Company, which has already made some production cuts, would make further reductions.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Rebounds as Fears Over Barings Crisis Fade

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar rose against most leading currencies yesterday, rebounding from early losses as concern faded that the collapse of the British investment firm Barings P.L.C. would lead to an international financial crisis.
Early in the day, in Asian and European trading, currency dealers flocked to buy German marks, fearing that the Barings crisis could spill over to other institutions and depress financial markets around the world. In recent years, the mark has become a haven for many investors in financial crises because the mark has maintained its value better than the dollar. Investors also have great faith that the Bundesbank, more than any other central bank, will keep inflation under control, thus preserving the value of mark-denominated deposits and assets. 
 "We're basically seeing a flight to quality into the mark with the shocking news from London on Barings," Karl Halligan, a trader at the CIC Bank New York, said yesterday morning.
But fears that the Barings crisis would spread eased later in New York trading. The dollar was also bolstered by early gains in American stocks and bonds, although stocks fell later in the day.
Because the Barings crisis is "an isolated incident for a single U.K. institution, it didn't have a lasting impact on the dollar," said Vicki Schmelzer Alicea, who handles currency sales for Westdeutsche Landesbank Girozentrale in New York.
After falling to a 28-month low of 1.4530 marks early in the day, the dollar recovered to 1.4660 marks late in New York trading yesterday, up from 1.4612 marks on Friday. The dollar also recovered from an early low of 96.58 yen, bouncing back to 97.07 yen, up from 96.50 yen on Friday. The dollar touched a record low of 96.12 yen on Nov. 2.
The mark has been steadily strengthening in recent months, benefiting by political uncertainty in Italy, Spain, France and Britain. The surge of buying in marks yesterday further battered many of these weakened currencies. The mark rose to a record highs against the Italian lira and the British pound yesterday.
The pound also fell against the dollar, ending at $1.5812, from $1.5885 on Friday. In other trading, the dollar fell to 1.2430 Swiss francs, from 1.2450 francs on Friday.

HEADLINE: Car Bomb Kills At Least 54 In Kurdish Iraq

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: ANKARA, Turkey, Feb. 27

 BODY:
A car bomb exploded in a crowded market street in the Iraqi city of Zakho today, killing at least 54 people and wounding up to 80, Western and Turkish officials said.
The city is in the area of northern Iraq controlled by Kurds, who are under the protection of an allied Western force that operates from the air base at Incirlik, Turkey. It was not clear who was behind the explosion. Past bombings have been blamed by local Kurdish groups on the Iraqi Government, which claims sovereignty over the area. 
 American military officials at Incirlik said the early-morning explosion occurred in the financial district of Zakho. They said that at least 54 people were dead but the death toll could rise. Up to 80 people were injured, 15 seriously, a spokesman said. Western aid workers said they feared the death toll could go higher.
Northern Iraq has had fighting since mid-December between two factions, the Kurdistan Democratic Party and the Patriotic Union of Kurdistan.
A statement from the Patriotic Union said initial reports from Zakho indicated that as many as 80 people were killed and dozens wounded.
 Relief workers reported from the region that the explosion was in a crowded commercial street lined with traders, many from neighboring Turkey. A Turkish Foreign Ministry official confirmed the explosion and said he was concerned that Turkish truck drivers who regularly pass through Zakho could be among the casualties.
Zakho, 12 miles from the Turkish border, and the towns of Dohuk and Salahuddin are controlled by the Democratic Party. The Patriotic Union holds Erbil and Sulaimaniya further south.
Turkey and the United States have recently warned both leaders that their fighting could endanger the flow of relief aid to the region.
Turkey supports the truck trade into Iraq to boost its regional economy, which has been affected by the 1990 United Nations embargo punishing Iraq's invasion of Kuwait. Turkish trucks bring food and medicine, allowed under the trade sanctions, and return with diesel fuel to sell on the local market.
Lucrative tolls collected by the Democratic from the Turkish trucks lie at the heart of the present conflict with the rival group. The Democratic Party refuses to share the revenue. The two factions formally share power in the local parliament and administration, based in Erbil.
